Chapter I

The Headmaster's Office

"I've had enough trouble for a lifetime."

The words hung in the quiet of the Headmaster's office, and in his frame above the desk Albus Dumbledore went on beaming at Harry through his tears, and around the walls the portraits dead headmasters and headmistresses applauded on and on, as though they could not stop.

Harry stood with the Elder Wand in one hand and his own wand in the other, and found that now the decision was made he was almost too tired to move.

Dawn was coming up in the long windows. The office had survived the battle almost untouched: the silver instruments still whirred and smoked on their spindly tables, and Fawkes's perch stood empty by the door, gleaming, as though somebody had polished it just in case. The light came in low and gold and lay across the desk, and the desk, Harry noticed with strange specificity, still had a tin of sherbet lemons in the corner of it. Snape had never moved them.

"To be clear," said Ron, who had sunk into a spindly chair and looked like a man who had been awake for a year, "you're putting the most powerful wand in the world back in a tomb."

"That's the idea."

"The unbeatable one."

"It's only unbeatable if someone beats me," Harry said. "And I'm planning on dying in my bed about a hundred years from now. If I die undefeated, the wand's power dies with me. That's the end of it. That's the whole point."

"He's right, Ron," said Hermione, though her voice wobbled with exhaustion. "It's the only way to break the chain. People have been murdering each other over that wand for centuries."

"I know he's right. I just want it on the record that you had the chance to be the most powerful wizard alive, and you're giving it up." Ron tipped his head back against the chair and closed his eyes. "Put it in the history books. He had the option."

Dumbledore nodded. "You keep finding, Harry," he said, and his painted voice was gentle, "that the only safe place for power is away from your own hand. That is the rarest instinct in this room, and every portrait on these walls knows the cost of lacking it. Myself first."

"You're going to make me say goodbye to you twice in one night," Harry said, to the frame, and his voice cracked on it, and Dumbledore smiled and shook his head.

"No, dear boy. That is the privilege of portraits. From now on, you only ever have to say hello."

Ron looked from one to the other of them, then shrugged with the last of his strength. "Mental," he said. "Both of you. Always have been." But he got up, and the three of them walked down the moving staircase together, past the wreckage of the gargoyle, and out into the wounded castle.

The corridors were not empty, but they were quiet, in the way of a house at the end of a long illness. The portraits had crowded themselves three and four to a frame to watch the three of them pass, and some bowed, and a knight in rusted armour stood at attention all the way down one gallery with his sword reversed; somewhere overhead Peeves was composing a victory ode of spectacular vulgarity, the same four rhymes round and round, and even McGonagall had not yet had the heart to stop him. In the Entrance Hall the four great hourglasses had been smashed, and nobody had swept; rubies and emeralds and sapphires and topazes lay mixed together across the flagstones in drifts, house against house all spilled into one treasure, and a small Hogwarts elf was going along the edge of it on her knees, gathering gems out of the rubble into her tea-towel, crying steadily and without any sound, and putting every stone back regardless of colour into whichever glass was nearest.

Through the doors of the Great Hall, Harry could see the families at the long tables — those who had stayed, those who could not yet go home to houses with one bed too many — and McGonagall moving down the rows with a teapot, of all things, refilling cups, one hand on a shoulder here, a word there. He did not go in. There would be time, and the Hall would still be there, and he had one more thing to do while the grounds were empty.

They went to the white tomb at first light. The lawns on the way down were a map of the night written in the grass: a giant's footprint, pooled with dawn rain, big enough to bathe in; a scorch like a fallen flag; chunks of the Astronomy Tower lying in the grass as though the sky had shed. The Whomping Willow stood unmoved and unbothered, having apparently won all its own engagements. Ron and Hermione stopped at the beech tree by the water without being asked — there are things you can tell about your friends after seven years, and one of them is when a thing is yours to do alone — and Harry walked the last fifty yards by himself.

The tomb had been broken open, and Harry laid the Elder Wand inside on Dumbledore's breast, where it had lain before, finished with the world. Then he mended the tomb, and that felt right, somehow, that the last spell of the war should be a repairing one. The marble knitted itself under his wand like ice forming, the crack closing without a seam, until the tomb lay whole and white as the day they had built it. Harry stood for a moment with his hand flat against the stone. The marble was cold, and then, under his palm, slightly less cold.

The lake was the colour of pewter. Somewhere up in the castle, fifty families were waking — those who had slept — to the first full day of a world without Voldemort and without their dead, and Harry could not yet tell which of those two facts was bigger.

"All right?" said Ron, when he came back up the bank, which from Ron meant a great many things, and Harry said, "Yeah," which meant a great many things back, and Hermione took an arm of each of them, and they went up to the castle three abreast, too tired to talk, walking in step without noticing they were doing it, the way they had walked across half of Britain.

Kreacher found them in the Entrance Hall. The old elf had a bandage round one ear and Regulus's locket bouncing on his chest and a tray of sandwiches that he thrust at Harry.

"Master will eat," Kreacher croaked. "Master defeated the Dark Lord and Master will eat."

"Kreacher —" Harry began, and the elf drew himself up to his full thirty inches, bandage and all, and Harry sat down on the marble staircase and took a sandwich. "You led them," he said, around it. "The elves. I saw you — you came up out of the kitchens with a carving knife, you were shouting about Regulus —"

"Kreacher led the elves of Hogwarts in the name of Master Regulus, in the name of Harry Potter and his friends - the defenders of house elves," said Kreacher, with tremendous dignity, the locket swinging. "The Dark Lord's followers learned to fear the kitchens of this castle." He paused, and something ancient and crabbed in his face moved aside for a moment, like furniture being shifted. "Master Regulus would have wished to be there. Kreacher wishes Master Regulus had been there."

"He was," said Harry. "Take it from me. I had it on good authority last night that they don't miss things like that."

Kreacher looked at him for a long moment. Then he turned his attention fiercely to the tray, as if it had misbehaved. "Master has eaten one sandwich. There are nine sandwiches."

Ron, lowering himself onto the step beside Harry, looked the old elf in the eye and said, with complete seriousness, "You were brilliant, mate," and Kreacher's ears went down and then up, like something taking flight, and Hermione said nothing at all except "Thank you, Kreacher," and wiped her eyes with the heel of one hand. Kreacher stalked off toward the kitchens muttering that Mistress Hermione was a civilised witch.

Harry ate two sandwiches sitting on the marble staircase, discovered he was hungrier than he had ever been in his life, and ate four more. Then he drew out the Invisibility Cloak and pulled it over himself, because he could not, that morning, bear to be congratulated one more time, and climbed unseen to Gryffindor Tower. The Fat Lady's gown was torn at the shoulder and she wore it like a medal; her friend Violet had come visiting with a bottle of something celebratory and was already asleep against the frame. Harry slipped the Cloak off, and the Fat Lady let him in without a password — "As if I'd ask you, dear boy, get in, get in" — and the common room beyond was empty and warm, the fire lit by some kind hand, everyone still down in the Great Hall with their families.

The dormitory had kept its shape — five four-posters, the smell of dust and old wood — but the year showed everywhere he looked: Neville's toad-tank standing empty on the sill, Dean's bed stripped bare, waiting out the year of its owner's exile, Harry's own bed by the window with the blankets squared away by elves who had kept squaring them away all year, apparently, against the day. Somebody — the same kind hand that had lit the fire, he supposed — had left a folded pair of pyjamas on the pillow. He looked at them for a while from very far away, and did not manage them.

He cast Muffliato on the bed-hangings before he lay down. He did it without thinking, the way he had done it every night for a year in the tent, the way you check a door you already know is locked; and then he took off his glasses, and lay down in his old bed in his filthy clothes, and went under like a stone into a pond.

He dreamed, of course.

He was in the forest, and the forest was also the corridor at the Ministry, and the corridor was also the drawing room at Malfoy Manor, the way places are in dreams, and Hermione was screaming somewhere overhead, through the floorboards above him, and ahead of him between the trees the red eyes opened, and a voice that came from inside his own scar said, as before, Harry, you cannot help them — and this time there was no castle behind him to save, the castle was already burning, he had got it wrong, he was always exactly too late — and the green light came at him like the sea coming in, and it did not stop at the surface of him this time, it went in, it went through his chest like a tree of fire growing all its branches at once —

Harry woke up screaming.

Chapter II

Silence With Edges

At nine that evening, Mrs. Weasley loaded a plate and announced that Harry had been asleep since noon and that boy needed feeding. Ginny volunteered to carry it up before her mother had finished the sentence.

The Fat Lady let her through without a password ("He's up there, dear, hasn't stirred") and the common room stood empty and wrong in the way the whole castle was wrong, every familiar thing wearing dust. The fire had burned down to a red murmur. Somebody's chess set stood mid-game on the table by the window, abandoned since — she did the arithmetic without wanting to — since the night the Carrows fell, ten days and one world ago, both kings still standing. She crossed the room quietly, balancing the plate, rehearsing nothing; she had given up rehearsing things to say to Harry Potter somewhere around her fifth year, when she had finally understood that the rehearsals were the problem.

She climbed the boys' staircase, past the worn places in the stone where six years of feet she knew had climbed it, and at the door of the seventh-years' dormitory she stopped.

It was silent. And Ginny Weasley had spent a year at Hogwarts under the Carrows, where children silenced their own dormitories at night so that no one would hear who was crying, or who was screaming their way out of the nightmares left over from the detentions; and she had learned the one thing about silencing charms that nobody who casts one ever seems to understand.

Silence has a texture. A room where nothing is happening sounds like nothing. A room sealed by Muffliato sounds like held breath. It has edges.

This silence had edges.

"Finite," said Ginny, and the screaming hit her like a wave.

She got the curtains open and got hold of him — he fought her for a second, blind without his glasses, his hand clamped to his chest as though he had been speared — and she did what you learned to do in the Carrows' year, which was not to shake and not to shout but to hold on and say the name, low and level, over and over: Harry. It's Ginny. You're in the Tower. It's over. It's me. Until his eyes found her, and the screaming stopped, and he came back into himself in pieces, the way people did.

He was soaked through. His shirt stuck to him, half its buttons gone since the battle, and as he sat up gasping, hand still pressed over his heart, Ginny took his wrist and — gently, the way you open a letter you are afraid of — moved it aside.

And saw.

She did not gasp. Afterward, that was the part Harry remembered: she did not gasp, and did not flinch, and did not look away. The moonlight through the window was enough. Over his heart, stark on the skin, lay a lightning bolt — larger than the one the whole world knew, and crueller — and out of it spread fine branching lines, up toward his collarbone, down along his ribs, like the limbs of a winter tree, like frost grown across a windowpane, like lightning photographed at the instant of the strike. She put her fingers on it. It was neither hot nor cold. Beneath it his heart was going like a Snitch in a fist.

"Harry," said Ginny, very quietly. "What did this."

He could have lied. He had been planning, in the formless way of the very tired, to manage this — later, after the funerals, when they've all slept — but it was dark, and it was her, and her fingers were already resting on the proof.

"The Killing Curse," he said. "In the forest. It hit me, Ginny. He didn't miss, and I didn't dodge. It hit me."

The stillness went all the way through her. For a long moment she did not move at all, her fingers still resting on the scar, and he watched it land — watched her eyes go from the mark to his face and back to the mark, watched her breathing come apart. She had stood at the edge of the crowd while Hagrid carried him out of the forest; she had done her grieving once already, in half a minute, in front of everyone. Now she was learning it had been true after all, under her own hand, and there was nowhere to put a thing like that. Her eyes filled, and she let them, not looking away from him, not wiping her face, as though tending to herself was a thing that could wait its turn.

Then she stood up, pulled the blanket off the next bed, and put it round his shoulders, tucking it close with hands that were not quite steady.

"Hospital wing." Her voice shook. She steadied it by plain force, and said it again. "Hospital wing. Now."

"Ginny, it doesn't hurt, it's not — there are people up there actually injured, Madam Pomfrey's been awake three days, I'm not taking a bed over a bad dream —"

"Harry." She crouched so they were level, and her voice was the gentle one almost nobody got, which was somehow more immovable than any shout. "You were hit by a Killing Curse, and you have been walking around for two days with nobody knowing but you. You are going to see a Healer tonight, with me standing next to you, or I am going to go and wake my mother. Those are your options. Choose quickly. She isn't sleeping deeply."

They went down through the sleeping castle together, Harry in the blanket because Ginny had simply kept hold of one corner of it, steering him with it like a sail. The corridors at night were full of small repairs: trestles and rope, neat pyramids of salvaged stone, a suit of armour laid out in pieces on a dust sheet like a patient awaiting surgery. On the fourth floor they passed a window with no glass in it, the night coming through soft and cold and smelling of the lake, and Harry stopped without meaning to.

"I keep doing that," he said. "Stopping at the broken bits."

"Everyone does," said Ginny. "You should see Filch. He goes round all night with his lamp, just — visiting them. Mrs. Norris won't leave the trophy room; something about the way the cases smashed offends her personally." She tugged the blanket. "You can visit the window tomorrow. It'll still be broken."

The hospital wing at ten o'clock was rows of sleeping wounded and one lamp burning at the far end. Madam Pomfrey came out of her office at the sound of the doors with her wand already lit and her face already arranged into the expression that had been terrorising malingerers for three decades.

"Whatever it is, Potter, unless you are actively bleeding it can wait until —"

"Show her," said Ginny.

And Harry, feeling foolish, opened the ruin of his shirt.

Madam Pomfrey stopped as though she had walked into a wall.

She came forward slowly. She raised her wand, and the light at its tip went soft as a candle, and she moved it over the scar — the bolt, the branches, the whole silver-red tree of it — and said nothing for the better part of a minute. One hand had come up to cover her mouth without her seeming to know it was there. Harry had been her patient for eight years; he had watched her meet regrown bones and basilisk venom and the wreckage of a hundred bad nights without so much as a raised eyebrow, and so he realized he was seeing something he had never seen before: Madam Pomfrey going through everything she knew — every salve, every counter-charm, every clever piece of mending in thirty years of matron's work — and coming out the other side with empty hands. The wand-light trembled, and steadied. Her eyes, when they came up to his, were wet, and she did not trouble to hide it. Then she gathered herself, visibly, the way she must have done a thousand times outside the curtains of some terrible bed, and when she finally spoke, it was not in the matron's voice at all. It was a voice Harry had heard from her perhaps twice in eight years, at midnight bedsides, when she thought her patients were asleep.

"Sit down, Mr. Potter."

She examined him for half an hour and was gentle the whole time, which unsettled Harry more than anything else that had happened all week. She asked her questions quietly — when, and which curse, and how far from him were you standing, and you woke where — and took the answers without one exclamation, though her wand-hand was not quite steady; and Ginny stood at the foot of the bed like a sentry through all of it, and was not asked to leave.

"There is nothing here to mend," Madam Pomfrey said at last. She had drawn up a chair, which in eight years Harry had never once seen her do. "Dittany will not touch it, and nor will anything else, and I want you to hear me clearly, because you will spend the rest of your life with people flinching at it. It is not an injury, Mr. Potter. It is a record. The curse went through you looking for a life to take, and what it found to kill" — her voice frayed; she paused, and mended it — "was not you. The mark is only where it passed. There is no Dark magic in it. There is nothing in it at all. It is the truth documented, and the truth is that you are still here."

She looked at it a moment longer. Then she did something she had never done in eight years of basilisk venom and regrown bones and Dementor attacks: she put her hand on Harry's head, briefly, the way you would with a much smaller child.

"Eight years I have patched this boy," she said — to Ginny, as though Harry were not in the room. "He has come to me bitten, burned, broken, and once with no bones in his arm whatsoever, and never once in eight years has he come on his own legs, of his own accord. He didn't tonight, either." She looked at Ginny over her spectacles. "Keep doing whatever it was you did."

Then she stood, matron again, and produced a small bottle. "Dreamless Sleep. One dose, tonight only — it is not a solution, Potter, and I will not be handing you a habit; we will talk properly about the dreams when the funerals are done. Bed. That one. No, I am not sending you back across the castle to a tower full of empty beds, don't be absurd."

And Madam Pomfrey — who in eight years had ejected every after-hours visitor she had ever caught, without exception and without mercy — looked at Ginny Weasley settling into the bedside chair like a permanent installation, said nothing whatsoever about it, and brought her a blanket.

Harry slept without dreaming, for the last time in a long while. When he woke, very early, Ginny had gone back to her family — but propped against the empty bottle was a note, in the round hand he would have known anywhere:

Great Hall, half six. We're going to talk, and you're going to eat.

Chapter III

The Hollow Morning

The Great Hall at half past six the next morning was full of people and almost silent. Sunlight came through the shattered windows in long dusty columns. The house tables had been pushed to the walls to make room for the wounded and, at the far end, beneath where the staff table used to stand, the dead, decently covered now with banners of all four houses, because the houses had stopped mattering somewhere around midnight two days before. Professor Flitwick stood on a chair, mending a window with small tired movements of his wand, and the glass knitted itself together with a sound like distant chimes, and that was the loudest thing in the castle.

Harry sat alone at the end of the Gryffindor table with a fresh plate of Kreacher's sandwiches and found he could not eat.

"You should eat that."

He had not heard her come in. Ginny sat down across from him, not beside him, took half a sandwich, and pushed the plate back. Her eyes were red-rimmed and dry. Her hair was tied back with what looked like a strip torn from a Gryffindor scarf, and a healing cut along her jaw had gone silvery with Madam Pomfrey's salve.

"Mum's properly asleep at last," she said, in the flat, careful voice. "Real sleep, not the kind where she sits up at every noise. Dad's sitting with... Dad's sitting with Fred. Percy won't leave either of them. George went somewhere and Bill went after him. So here I am."

"Ginny —" he began, and the apology rose in him like floodwater, I'm sorry, it's my fault, every name under every banner —

"Don't." She said it quietly, but it stopped him as surely as a Shield Charm. "I've watched you do it already. When you look at Colin Creevey. I watched you carry the weight of his death like you'd cast the curse yourself. Colin, and Fred, and Remus and Tonks, and probably Mad-Eye and Dobby and Hedwig and everyone back to Cedric Diggory." Her voice cracked on the last name. "You've been gone eleven months, Harry. I have waited a very long time for quiet mornings with you, and I am not spending them listening to you confess to murders Tom Riddle committed."

It was the Tom that undid him. She was the only person he knew besides Dumbledore who had ever used Voldemort's school name, and she had earned the right to it at eleven years old, in a chamber under the lake.

"I watched Hagrid carry you out of that forest," Ginny said. "I heard somebody screaming and it took me a second to work out it was me. So you'll forgive me if I'm not interested in whose fault anything is. You're alive. I believed you weren't for half an hour, and that was enough for one lifetime."

Harry looked at her, really looked the way he had not let himself look at anyone in months, because looking at people meant loving them, and loving them had felt like painting targets on them. Something in his chest gave way.

"What you saw last night," he said slowly, "is part of something bigger. I'm going to tell everyone — your whole family, McGonagall, Kingsley — properly, in a few days, because you've all earned it and I can't get through it twice. But you get this part first, because it's yours. I didn't go into that forest with a plan, or a trick, or one of Dumbledore's loopholes in my pocket. I walked out there to die. Actually to die. And the last thing I thought about was not the prophecy or Tom, it was you."

Ginny went very still. Out of the corner of his eye, Harry saw Flitwick mend another window; the chimes drifted the length of the Hall.

"That," she said at last, unevenly, "is the single most infuriating thing you have ever said to me, Harry Potter." And then she was around the table and her arms were round his neck and her face was in his shoulder, and she was crying at last, the way he suspected she had not let herself cry in front of her mother, in great silent shudders, and he held on. He had spent a year holding on to a wand, a locket, a sword, a stone. It was a revelation to hold something that held him back.

When she finally let go, she did not go back around the table. She sat down beside him, hip to hip, took the rest of his sandwich out of his hand, divided it with the precision of the youngest of seven children, and gave him back the larger half.

"Eat. We have logistics to discuss."

"Logistics?" Harry repeated.

"Logistics. One." She held up a finger. "When the — when things here are done, Mum is going to ask you to come to the Burrow. She's been rehearsing it all night; I've caught her at it twice when she thought nobody was listening, working out a casual voice, as if there's any chance you'd say no, as if there's any chance she'd allow you to say no. When she asks, you're to act surprised. It matters to her that it's an invitation and not a — a custody arrangement. Can you do surprised?"

"I've been practising surprised since I was eleven."

"Two." Another finger, but her voice changed; she was watching him now. "Andromeda Tonks wrote to McGonagall yesterday. She's asked if you'd come — when you're ready, she said, no sooner. Harry. You do know, don't you, that you're —"

"A godfather," said Harry. The word landed in him the way the tomb had felt under his hand: enormous and cold and then, beneath that, strangely warm. "I have a godson. Teddy. He's — Merlin, Ginny, he's a month old and his parents are lying under that banner because they came to fight for my —"

"Careful," said Ginny.

Harry stopped. Breathed. Started again, and this time the sentence went somewhere different. "I didn't even know I had a godfather until I was thirteen — and I spent most of that year being told he was a mass murderer who was coming to kill me. Then one night in the Shrieking Shack the whole thing turned inside out, and there he was: the first grown-up who was ever just mine. He asked me to come and live with him about an hour after I'd met him properly, and I said yes before he'd finished asking. I had him two years." He looked at his hands. "Teddy gets me from the start. Whatever I'm worth, he gets all of it. I'll need — I don't know anything about babies. I don't know which way up they go."

"I'm the youngest of seven," said Ginny, "so I've held precisely as many babies as you have. But Mum's raised seven and Andromeda's raised one, and between the two of them we'll be taught which way up he goes. We'll go Thursday. You'll be appalling at it for ten minutes, and then you'll be fine, because the only thing babies actually require is somebody who turns up, and turning up is the one subject you've never failed." She paused. "Three. There are going to be funerals. A lot of —" and for the first time that morning her list-voice failed her, one breath's worth, and he watched her catch it and go on. "A lot of them. You'll be asked to speak at some. You're allowed to say no. You're also allowed to say yes. But whichever you say, you don't go to a single one alone. That one isn't a request, it's policy. New policy. As of now."

"Whose policy?"

"Mine," said Ginny. "I find I'm full of them this morning."

Down the Hall, McGonagall passed with her teapot on its third circuit, paused behind them for a moment, said, "Potter. Miss Weasley," in a voice that had been crying and did not care who knew it, put one hand briefly on each of their shoulders, and moved on before anything could be said back; and Flitwick mended another window, and the chimes drifted down.

"And four." Ginny took the last bite of the smaller half, which she had been quietly eating all along. "Half six. Tomorrow. Here."

"That's all four is?"

"That's all four is," she said. "We do this every morning, Potter. Quiet first. Talking if it wants to happen. Eating regardless. I spent a year imagining you in that horrible tent — don't look at me like that, Ron told me about the tent — and somewhere in there I worked out what was coming for you if you won. Medals. Speeches. Casseroles. Everyone giving you everything except an ordinary morning." She stood, and collected the plate, and looked down at him with exhaustion drawn on her face — the red rims, the silvery cut, the strip of scarf, the unbearable steadiness. "So that's what I'm giving you. Half six, every day, until further notice."

"Ginny." She stopped. The sunlight had reached the end of the Hall by now and stood in a column between them, full of slow gold dust. "I told you the last thing I thought about in the forest was you. I never told you what the thought actually was. It wasn't anything grand. It was a morning like this one — a table, and food, and you across from me, and nothing left to fight." He looked at the empty plate in her hands, and then at her. "So you haven't just worked out the one thing nobody else would think to give me. You've worked out the one thing I wanted."

Ginny held his eyes for a moment, and nodded once, as if something had been settled that would not need saying again. Then she carried the plate away down the length of the Hall, back to her family, and Harry sat a while longer in the moving sunlight, while Flitwick's windows sang themselves whole one by one.

Chapter IV

The Whole Truth

He told them on the fourth day.

He had thought hard about who should hear it, and in the end the list made itself: the Weasleys, every one of them, because they had bled for it without ever being told what it was; Hermione and Ron, who knew most of it but deserved to hear it whole; Professor McGonagall, because she had defended the school believing she was buying time for a plan she had never been shown; Kingsley, because somebody in the new Ministry had to know the truth, and Harry trusted exactly one person in the new Ministry; and Andromeda Tonks, because her husband, her daughter, and her son-in-law had died for the cause and she was owed the full story.

They gathered in the early evening in McGonagall's office, with tea that nobody drank. She refused, still, to call the office hers — "the Headmaster's office," she said, and her eyes went to Dumbledore's frame. Fifteen people, and the portraits, who for once did not even pretend to be asleep.

Harry told it all.

He told them about the prophecy, and the night at Godric's Hollow, and what Voldemort had accidentally made him when he chose to attack Harry. He told them what a Horcrux was, and watched Mrs. Weasley's hand go to her mouth, and told them there had been seven, and watched Bill — the curse-breaker who had met that word before in the worst tombs in Egypt — go grey. He told them about the locket and the cup and the diadem; about the diary.

He told them about the snake. He told them about the seventh.

"The one he never meant to make," Harry said. His voice had gone strange in his own ears, very level. "The night he killed my parents, the curse didn't just rebound. It tore him apart — he was unstable by then, he'd already split his soul too many times — and a piece of him broke off and lodged in the only living thing left in that room. That's why I could speak Parseltongue, and see into his mind, and feel his moods like weather. For sixteen years, I was carrying a piece of Voldemort's soul in me, and as long as it there, he couldn't die."

The room had stopped breathing.

"You need to know about Snape first — the rest of it makes no sense without him." Harry looked around the room. "What I didn't say in the Hall is how I came to know any of it. I was there when Voldemort killed Snape, and with his last moments, he gave me his memories. That's how I found out he was on our side."

"Severus Snape," said McGonagall, very carefully, like a woman setting her feet on ice. "I heard you in the Hall, Potter. I have turned the words over every day since — and still I cannot lay them beside the man I drove out of a window four nights ago, naming him coward as he fled."

"He fled so he wouldn't have to hurt you," Harry said. "He wasn't allowed to be anything else, not in front of anyone. That was the price of the job: everyone had to believe it. Voldemort most of all." He took a breath. "And it killed him anyway. The night of the battle, Voldemort decided the Elder Wand wouldn't answer to him while Snape lived — he had it wrong, the wand was never Snape's, but he believed it — and he set the snake on him. I watched it happen and I couldn't stop it. And Snape, dying, with the last few minutes he had, gave me his memories, which included a message. Dumbledore had left some information with him a year ago, to be delivered at the very end, and Snape spent his last breath delivering it." He found he could not look at anyone, so he looked at the fire. "That the piece of Voldemort in me couldn't be destroyed the way the others were. That as long as I was alive, he couldn't die. And that I had to let him kill me — walk to him, not defend myself, let the curse land — because there was no other way to make him mortal."

It took the room a moment. It took Hermione, of course, about three seconds; Harry heard her breath go in. It took Bill Weasley perhaps five, and what Bill said, very quietly, into the hush, was: "A Horcrux can't be unmade while the vessel survives. There's no extraction. There has never been an extraction." He looked up at the portrait above the desk, and his scarred face was terrible. "You knew. How long did you know?"

"He suspected from the diary," Harry said, because someone had to, and because the portrait had gone very still. "He was certain by my fifth year. I saw it in the Pensieve — Dumbledore told Snape himself, near the end, what I was being kept alive for, and Snape asked him to his face whether all of it — the lessons, the protections, the tests — had only ever been about delivering me to the right death at the right moment." Harry's voice stayed level, at a cost everyone in the room could hear. "Dumbledore didn't deny it."

Mrs. Weasley came out of her chair.

Afterward, nobody could quite agree on what she said first, because it arrived all at once — and every word of it was aimed at the portrait. That Albus Dumbledore had sat at her table, and eaten her cooking, and looked her in the eye, knowing. That she had fed this boy and patched him and worried about him as he embarked on the Hogwarts Express every single September. That he had been ELEVEN. That an entire Order of grown witches and wizards had spent a decade dying to protect a boy that one old man had been quietly raising like livestock to be slaughtered. And then, somewhere in the middle of a sentence, the anger ran out from under her, and she turned from the portrait to Harry and simply stood there crying, and reached down and gripped his hand in both of hers as though somebody might still try to take him, and what was left when the fury had burned off was only grief. Mr. Weasley was on his feet beside her with a hand on her arm — not to stop her, anyone could see that; only so that she would not be standing in it alone.

"Albus told no one," Kingsley said into the crackle of the fire, in the silence that followed. "Not the Order. Not Moody. Not me. We guarded that boy for years without once being told what we were guarding him for."

And then McGonagall rose, and the room went quiet for her the way rooms always had, and she turned to face the portrait of Albus Dumbledore, and when she spoke it was in the coldest voice Harry had ever heard her use.

"I knew you fifty years," she said. "I did things on your word that I would have done on no one else's, because I believed — we all believed — that whatever you were not telling us was for the best interest of our students. And you let me stand on those walls in May believing I was buying time for some plan of yours, some last clever thing — and the plan" — her voice did not rise; it dropped, which was far worse — "was that a child I had watched grow up would walk out of my castle, alone, in the dark, and be murdered on schedule. I have sat in this office every day since the battle and asked your counsel, Albus. I am telling you now, in front of witnesses, that I do not know when I shall ask it again."

The portrait did not produce one of its twinkling deflections. The old man in the frame looked down at his folded hands for a long moment, and when he spoke he sounded every year of his great age.

"Everything you are thinking of me, Minerva, I have had sixteen years to think of myself, and I assure you I thought it first, and oftener, and in less forgiving terms. I offer no defence. I ask only that you let Harry finish — because the end of the story is the part I had no right to count on, and counted on anyway, because I knew him."

They all looked at Harry. He discovered, somewhat to his own surprise, that he was not willing to let the room burn the old man entirely — and said so, awkwardly, with the fire on his face.

"I'm not going to tell anyone here not to be angry. I've been angry at him plenty these last 11 months, and I don't expect I'm finished. But, Dumbledore hoped. He built outs — the blood protection, the wand, things I'll get to — every door he could leave unlocked without Voldemort noticing there were doors, he left unlocked. He just couldn't tell anyone. Not the Order, not Snape till the end, not me — especially not me, because Voldemort could read me like a window, and the whole thing only worked if I walked out those gates meaning it." He took a breath, and the next part he said slowly, because he was only ever going to say it once.

"So hear this part properly. I didn't go to the forest with a plan in my pocket. I wasn't being clever, and I wasn't counting on a loophole, because I didn't know there was one. I heard the message and I agreed with it. The arithmetic was right. My life, to give everyone in this castle a chance to defeat Voldemort. So I put the Cloak on, and I walked out of those gates to die. Not to risk it. To do it."

Hermione made a sound like something tearing. "There was no guarantee," she said, and her voice — which had argued in front of teachers and Ministers — was climbing and cracking like ice in spring. "Whatever brought you back — whatever he was counting on — it couldn't have been a certainty, even HE couldn't have known, you walked out on a — on a conjecture —"

"I didn't know," Harry agreed quietly. "I found out when I woke up. Hermione — I'm not telling you I gambled. I'm telling you I folded. I thought it was the end, full stop, no station, no train, nothing — and I went anyway. I'd rather have you all angry about the true thing than comforted by a false one."

And the room broke.

Not loudly — that was the strange thing Harry would remember about it afterward; it broke the way a dam at the bottom of a valley breaks, deep, and almost quiet. Mrs. Weasley was weeping without appearing to know it, both hands pressed flat over her heart as though holding something in behind her ribs. Charlie, who wrestled dragons for a living, sat with his knuckles white on his knees and his jaw working and said nothing at all. Fleur said something low and fierce in French; Bill did not translate it, yet visibly agreed with every word.

Ron stood up so fast his chair went over.

"You walked past us." His ears were scarlet; it came out at parade-ground volume and cracked clean down the middle. "Under the Cloak. We were RIGHT THERE, in the corridor — and you stopped for Neville, you gave NEVILLE the snake, the only goodbye you had in you was a tactical instruction to NEVILLE LONGBOTTOM — and you walked past me and Hermione like we were — like —" and there the anger ran out from under him all at once, the way it always did, and he stood in the wreckage of his own sentence with his fists opening and closing, and finished, hopelessly, "you absolute — mate," which was not the word he had been building toward and was somehow far worse than any he could have built toward, and then his legs were done and he sat back down and gripped Harry's shoulder with one hand, hard enough to leave a mark, and left it there, and did not take it back for the rest of the evening.

"If I'd stopped," Harry said — to Ron, but for all of them — "for any of you. For any of you." He shook his head, once. "I'd still be standing in that corridor."

It was Andromeda Tonks who asked, then, the question Harry had been dreading and hoping for in equal measure. She had sat through all of it very upright, Teddy asleep against her shoulder, and her voice, when it came, was conversational in the particular way of a person holding a very great weight perfectly level.

"You keep saying you walked out alone. Did you?"

Harry looked at her, and understood that she already suspected, the way the bereaved suspect things, and that there was nothing to do but give it to her whole.

"No," he said. "There's an object — after tonight, as far as anyone outside this room is concerned, it doesn't exist — that lets the dead walk with you. Not come back. Just — walk with you. A little way." The fire popped; nobody in the room appeared to be breathing. "My mum came. And my dad. And Sirius. And Remus." He held her eyes. "I told Remus I was sorry — that Teddy had just been born, that I was the reason he'd — and he said he was sorry too. That he'd never know his son. But that Teddy would know, one day, what his parents died for, and that he hoped he'd understand it: they were trying to make him a world he could live a happier life in." He had to stop for a moment. "He walked me nearly to the clearing. None of them told me to turn around — not once. They just made sure I wasn't cold on the way. Andromeda — I think it's the proudest I have ever seen anybody look."

Andromeda Tonks closed her eyes. When she opened them she was not crying, which was somehow more frightening than if she had been; she pressed her lips once to her sleeping grandson's hair, and said, with perfect steadiness, "Thank you. That is the only account of my son-in-law's death that anyone has thought to bring me" — and then she did cry, with tremendous economy, three tears and finished, while the entire room pretended valiantly to be studying the carpet.

And Ginny — who had known since the first night that the curse had truly struck him, who alone of everyone in the office had seen the proof; but who had not known about the message, or the Cloak, or the corridor, or the dead who walked him to the clearing — Ginny let go of Hermione's hand, stood, crossed the Headmaster's office in front of her entire assembled family, sat down on the arm of Harry's chair, and took his hand in both of hers, chin up, daring comment. None came. Mr. Weasley looked at the two of them, and then at his shoes, smiling slightly.

"He cast the curse," Harry said at last, into the quiet, with Ginny's hands around his, "and I didn't raise my wand, and it hit me. And — I died. Or as good as. And the piece of him that had lived in me for sixteen years died there instead of me — because my mother's protection was in my blood, and my blood was in his veins, and he'd tethered me to life without ever understanding what he'd done. I was somewhere else for a while. A station. Dumbledore was there" — above the desk, the portrait inclined its head, gravely, and did not quite meet anyone's eye — "and I got a choice. I could go on — and I know where on goes now, a little, and it's nothing to be afraid of. Or I could come back. To the war, and the grief, and the cost of finishing it." He looked around the office — at Molly with her hands over her heart, at Ron's hand heavy on his shoulder, at McGonagall bolt upright with tear-tracks she had stopped attending to, at the portrait, at Ginny.

"I came back."

The silence afterward was the longest Harry had ever sat through. It was George — George, who had barely spoken in four days — who finally broke it.

"What was it like?" he said. His voice was rusty. "Being dead."

Mrs. Weasley made a small sound, but Harry held George's eyes and answered him straight.

"Warm," he said. "Quiet. Like the moment after you put down something heavy. There was a station, and a train that would've taken me — on. I don't know where. But it wasn't dark, and it wasn't lonely, and I wasn't scared. If that's where Fred is, George, then he's all right. I'd swear it on anything you like."

George looked at him for a long moment. Then he nodded, once, and got up and walked out of the office, and Bill half-rose, but Mr. Weasley shook his head: let him. They heard his footsteps going down, and then, very faint, from the spiral stairs, a sound none of them had heard in four days, which was George Weasley crying like he had finally been given permission.

"Mr. Potter." McGonagall's voice was not quite steady. Behind her square spectacles her eyes were wet, and she was making no effort about it. "You walked into that forest, at seventeen, on the word of a message in a Pensieve, believing you would not come back."

"Yes."

McGonagall took off her glasses, polished them with terrible thoroughness, and put them back on. "Fifty points to Gryffindor," she said, in a voice like cracking slate, "for sheer bloody-minded nerve. And yes, Potter, I am aware that the hourglasses are in pieces and that you will never sit in a classroom of mine again. You will permit an old woman the only currency she has." Which was, from Minerva McGonagall, approximately a sonnet.

It was Hermione — of course it was Hermione — who asked the question Harry had been waiting for. "Harry. The curse hit you. The Killing Curse doesn't leave a mark — that's the one thing everybody knows about it — except that once, it did." Her eyes went to his forehead. "It tore a piece off him the night it gave you that. And this time there was a piece of him in you when it landed, so —"

"There's a mark," Harry said.

And because it would never be easier than now, in this room, with these people, he stood and unbuttoned his collar and pulled his shirt open at the chest.

Over his heart, stark against the skin, lay the second lightning bolt and its branching silver-red tree — the path the curse had taken as it burned through him searching for something to kill, and found the piece of Voldemort, and took it instead. It reached from his collarbone down along his ribs, and it was absolutely permanent.

Ginny did not look at the scar. She had seen it already, by moonlight, with her fingers resting on it. She watched the others see it instead — chin up, steady as a sentry, daring anyone in the room to make it worse than it was.

"Madam Pomfrey's had three looks at it," Harry said into the ringing silence. "It won't fade and it won't close, because there's nothing to close." He shrugged the shirt back into place, suddenly self-conscious. "It doesn't hurt. Neither of them do, anymore. That's how I know it's really over."

Mrs. Weasley crossed the room and took his face in both hands, the way she had at the end of his fourth year, and looked at him a long time and said nothing at all, which from Molly Weasley said everything. Kingsley sat back in his chair with the air of a man quietly rebuilding his understanding of the last seventeen years.

"The Elder Wand is in that tomb, and as far as the Ministry of Magic is concerned it does not exist and never did.", Kingsley said at last, "The Horcruxes — the Wizengamot will need the broad shape of it for the trials, but the method dies in this room. Agreed?" Heads bowed around the circle. "And Harry — the country is going to want to put you on every front page and every balcony for the next year. You're entitled to refuse all of it. But there's one thing I'd ask you to do publicly, because no one else alive can do it."

"Snape," Harry said.

Kingsley nodded slowly. "Snape."

Chapter V

The Prince's Due

The hearing was held in Courtroom Ten — the same room where the old Ministry had tried Muggle-borns for the crime of existing. Harry decided that it was a good room in which to start telling the truth.

The chair in the centre of the floor still had its chains.

Harry had sat in it once — fifteen years old, summoned at eight in the morning for the crime of saving his cousin's soul, while a hundred people in plum robes looked down at him and Cornelius Fudge read the charges. The Ministry had offered him a witness stand this time, up on the dais, beside the Chief Warlock. Harry looked at the stand, and looked at the chair, and walked to the chair, and sat down in it, and the whole chamber went very still.

"For the record," he said, "I've sat here before. Most of you were in the room. I was fifteen, and the charge was a Patronus." He let that sit. The chains stirred once against the stone, like a dog recognising somebody, and lay still. "I've been told all summer that it's a different Ministry now. All right — today you can prove it. You're going to hear the whole story of a man most of you would have crossed the street to avoid, and some of it is going to be hard on people in this room. Listen anyway."

In the press pen, Rita Skeeter's acid-green quill had been confiscated at the door by order of the Chief Warlock and replaced with standard Ministry issue, which she held like a dead fish. Beside the dais sat Hermione, with a crate of certified parchment: she had spent two weeks shuttling between Grimmauld Place and the Department of Mysteries having every memory authenticated against tampering, and the seals on the crate said so in three colours of wax, and her expression invited absolutely anyone to raise the question anyway.

He gave the Wizengamot four hours of it. He gave them the memories, decanted into the Ministry's great Pensieve and projected above the chamber like weather: a thin boy in a too-big coat watching a red-haired girl on a swing; a young Death Eater on a hilltop, wrecked with terror, begging Dumbledore to hide the Potters; always; the silver doe in the frozen forest, leading Harry to the sword. He gave them his own testimony, under oath, of the night on the Astronomy Tower — a mercy planned between two old men, the timing of a death already certain — and of the memory of the plan itself, laid down years in advance: Severus Snape, headmaster of Hogwarts, taking the post to stand between the Carrows and the students, hated by everyone he was protecting, dying without ever once being thanked.

"He was the bravest man I ever knew," Harry told the chamber, and the quills along the press bench wrote it down exactly as he said it. "He spent seventeen years on the most dangerous assignment in the war, alone, with no one to talk to and nothing to gain. Voldemort killed him believing Snape was his man to the end. He fooled the greatest Legilimens in history, and he did it for love of my mother. He saved my life more times than I can count while making absolutely sure I never felt grateful for a second of it." A ripple of something that was nearly laughter, quickly stilled. "Lily Evans was his best friend, and my father bullied him, and I have his memories to prove both. He wasn't a kind man, but he was a great one. Put it right."

There was one challenge. An elderly warlock far up in the tiers rose and observed, silkily, that memories could be altered, that the chamber was being asked to acquit a man on the strength of images, and that the boy, after all, was known to love a good story. He was a Nott cousin by marriage, Hermione murmured later — a man with his own reasons for wanting memories ruled unreliable in a courtroom. Calling Harry boy was a mistake; a low murmur of disapproval went round the tiers, and Harry waited it out.

"The Department of Mysteries certified every memory in that crate; the seals are on the table; Miss Granger will walk you through the authentication charm by charm if you've got another four hours." He stood up out of the chained chair, and faced the tiers. "But fine — set the memories aside. All of them. Then explain the sword of Gryffindor arriving in a frozen pond in the Forest of Dean, guarded, placed, exactly when we were lost. Explain a Patronus that led us there and asked for nothing. Explain why the headmaster of Hogwarts spent his last year inventing detentions with Hagrid for children the Carrows wanted to chain. Explain why a man with Voldemort's complete trust, who could have had anything, died in a shack with no escape plan and no exit, and spent the last minutes of his life giving his memories to me." He looked at the objecting warlock until he sat down. "Memories can be tampered with, sir, you're quite right. But a whole life can't be. Everything I've shown you today points the same way, and everything he ever did points the same way. Judge him on that."

They put it right. The verdict was posthumous exoneration in full, with a commendation entered into the record. It made the front page of the Daily Prophet under a headline Rita Skeeter had certainly fought against and lost: SNAPE: DUMBLEDORE'S MAN TO THE LAST. Harry framed it. He was not entirely sure why.

The portrait was a different fight. By ancient charm, the office painted only those headmasters who died in harness, and the castle, it seemed, had its doubts about a headmaster who had fled out a window. Harry went up to the office three times that summer to argue with a wall, which McGonagall observed was excellent practice for the Ministry. She sat at her desk through each attempt, marking parchments, declining to help on the stated grounds that the castle was older than both of them and would do as it pleased; but on the third visit, as Harry stood in the middle of the carpet getting angrier at fourteenth-century masonry, the portraits began to weigh in, and it became a hearing of its own.

"The precedent is against you, Potter," said a sour-faced headmaster in a ruff. "The charm paints those who die in office, in service —"

"He died in service, you embroidered fossil," snapped Phineas Nigellus Black, who had been waiting, it was clear, for exactly this fight. "He held this office a full year for the sole purpose of standing between the students and the people he was pretending to serve, which I would invite this wall to compare with certain headmasters who held it for the salary." He drew himself up in his frame. "This office has hung thieves, frauds, two outright cowards, and one woman we have all agreed never to discuss. It can stretch to a Slytherin of genius. Hang the man."

"The castle hears everything said in this room," added Dumbledore quietly, from above the desk, when the shouting had died down. "It always has. It is not argument it waits for, Harry. Tell it what you told the Wizengamot. Tell it what he did here."

So in the end it was not argument that did it but testimony: he stood in the middle of the office and told the room — the portraits, the rafters, the castle itself — everything Snape had done under that ceiling, the sword smuggled out under the Carrows' noses, the students steered into detentions with Hagrid instead of worse, the year of walking a knife-edge in a school that spat when it said his name.

The next morning there was a new frame beside Dumbledore's, and in it, black-haired and sallow and looking as though he found the entire arrangement distasteful, sat Severus Snape.

Harry came up alone when he heard. For a long moment they looked at each other, the boy and the painting.

"Thank you," Harry said finally. "For all of it. I know you didn't do it for me."

Snape's black eyes glittered. "Correct, Potter," he said.

"There's something you should have, then. Two things." Harry came forward to the desk, beneath the frame. "The first is about the wand. The Elder Wand — he killed you for it, and it was never yours. It changed allegiance the night Draco disarmed Dumbledore on the Tower. You died for a wand that had already moved on, and I'm sorry, and you deserve to know the rest: it answered to me, in the end. I used it for exactly one repair, and then I put it back in his tomb, and when I die it dies. The thing you were murdered over is finished. It's out of the world."

The portrait was silent for a long moment. Something moved behind the black eyes that Harry had seen once before, in a dying man's face, asking to be looked at.

"So he killed me for nothing," Snape said at last, slowly. "And it bought him nothing. And the wand is beyond every hand that will ever reach for it." A pause. "There are worse ways for it to end. And the second thing, Potter? You said two."

"The doe," said Harry. "In the forest. I never saw the caster, but I followed it without even thinking, straight through a wood full of Snatchers, because some part of me knew it. I'd have followed it anywhere. I thought you should hear that."

"Do not," said Snape, with totality, "make my Patronus a subject of conversation," which was, both of them understood, an acceptance. Then, with great reluctance he said: "She would, however... not be ashamed of you."

Chapter VI

Fifty Funerals

May became a month of black robes.

The morning of the first one, the Burrow was up before the sun, and the kitchen was already at work. Molly had been ironing dress robes since five — all of them, including a set for Harry, who could have bought himself a hundred sets and had not thought of it once; his own had been fitted for the Yule Ball, three years and four inches ago, and everything else he owned had spent the past year at the bottom of a rucksack. The new set appeared on his bed brushed and ready, with a black tie of Arthur's, and was never discussed, because in Molly Weasley's grief there was room, somehow, to notice that a boy had nothing fit to wear. Nobody could eat the breakfast and everybody was made to try. George came downstairs last, in his good robes, wearing underneath them a jumper with an F on it that fit him exactly; and the kitchen went quiet, and Molly put down the teapot and crossed the room and straightened the collar of it as though it were the most ordinary thing in the world, and that was how the family got out the door.

They buried Fred on the hill behind the Burrow, under the tree the twins had once bewitched to pelt Percy with crab apples. Half the wizarding world wanted to come and the Weasleys allowed almost none of it; it was family, and Harry and Hermione counted as family, which Harry understood properly for the first time when Mrs. Weasley seized his arm at the graveside and held it through the whole service like a banister. Ginny took up his other side without a word and laced her fingers through his — clause one of the funerals policy, ratified in the Great Hall over sandwiches — and so Harry stood through Fred Weasley's funeral holding on to two Weasley women, or being held on to, the direction of it never afterward clear to him and never mattering. Lee Jordan spoke, because George could not. He told the story of the Portable Swamp, and the day the twins flew out of Hogwarts on confiscated brooms with Peeves saluting, and people laughed — actually laughed, in wet, broken bursts — and Harry thought Fred would have approved of that more than any amount of solemnity. When it was over, George stood with his hand flat on the coffin and said, to nobody in particular, "He always went first. Born first. First through every door." A long breath. "First again. Typical." And then he took out his wand and set off a single Whiz-bang, silent, gold, in the shape of a fox that ran twice around the hill and went to ground in the sunset, and that was the only eulogy he ever gave, and the only one Fred needed.

The family went down the hill in twos and threes as the light failed. Percy did not go down. He stood at the grave with his hands behind his back, in the posture of a man at a hearing, and George — who had not been alone all day, by the family's quiet engineering — looked back from the gate, and went and stood with him, and the two of them stayed up there a long time in the dusk. But Ginny, watching from the orchard fence with her hand still in Harry's, saw the moment when Percy's parade rest finally broke, and saw that it was George who caught him; and she let go of a breath she had been holding all day, because if George could still catch somebody, George was going to be all right.

Remus and Tonks were buried side by side under an oak on the edge of the Hogwarts grounds, within sight of the Whomping Willow, which Harry had suggested to Andromeda with his heart in his mouth and which had made her laugh — one short, shocked, real laugh — for the first time since the battle. "He and Dora would find it extremely funny," she said. Kingsley spoke for Tonks, who had been his colleague and his friend — about a trainee who had knocked over his coffee on her first morning and arrested a dark wizard by lunchtime, about hair that told you everything and a heart that hid nothing — and then Harry spoke for Remus, the last Marauder, the best teacher he had ever had.

"The first spell he ever taught me was the one for fear," Harry told them. "I was thirteen. He stood a classroom of us in front of a wardrobe with the worst thing in the world inside it, and he taught us that the counter-charm is laughter — that you look the thing dead in the eye and find the angle that makes it absurd. It's the only Defence lesson I've ever seen that the whole class still casts." He looked at the coffin under the oak. "He spent his life being told he was the dangerous one. He sat apart, at the edge of every room, but he died in the middle — shoulder to shoulder, in the open, defending a school that sacked him and a world that wouldn't hire him, next to a wife who never once let him sit apart again. I want it on a record somewhere that he was the gentlest man I ever met, and that the last thing he asked me, the night he died, was whether I'd seen his son." His voice held nearly all the way through it, and where it didn't, nobody minded; and he held five-week-old Teddy through the rest, the baby's hair cycling slowly through every colour it had, as if he were trying them against the sky.

Colin Creevey was buried as a Muggle, in a churchyard in the West Country, with a headstone that said BELOVED SON AND BROTHER and could say nothing else, because his parents could not put he fell in the greatest battle of the wizarding age on a stone the neighbours would see. The whole of Dumbledore's Army came anyway, dressed as Muggles, standing awkward and heartbroken among the yew trees while a vicar who could not be told the truth did his kind, baffled best.

It was Dennis, afterward, who gave them the idea, although he didn't mean to. He was sixteen, and he stood at the grave turning something over and over in his fingers, and Harry saw that it was Colin's DA Galleon — the coin Hermione had charmed in their fifth year, the one Colin had kept in his pocket through everything, because Colin kept everything.

"He'd have wanted it with him," Dennis said. "Only I can't — if I bury it, it's just gone, isn't it. And it's the best thing he ever — he was so proud —"

Hermione knelt at the headstone for a long time with her wand out, working with the tip of her tongue between her teeth, the way she did. When she stood up, Colin's Galleon was set into the base of the stone as if it had grown there, gleaming, protected by charms that would shrug off weather, thieves, and time itself.

"It's still connected," she said quietly. "To all of ours. They were made as a set; they'll always be a set." She showed them: she pressed her own Galleon, and the coin in the stone grew warm and shone briefly gold, like a candle behind a curtain. "So whenever any of us thinks of him — wherever we are — he'll know."

Dennis Creevey cried like a child then, into Harry's shoulder, and Harry held on to him and looked over his head at the coin shining in the churchyard shade and could not have spoken to save his life.

After that it became the thing they did, unofficial and unbreakable. A DA Galleon, set in charmed gold into the stone, for every member they buried — Colin, and the others — and on into the years ahead, decades distant, at funerals where the mourners would arrive as elderly witches and wizards and stand around a grave like the old soldiers they were. Visiting Colin's grave that autumn, Dennis would find the coin warm more often than not. The Army never did get round to disbanding.

Chapter VII

Tea and Thestrals

By June, Harry was living at the Burrow, but he came back to Hogwarts two or three days in any given week. There was always a reason — the rebuilding, where every able wand was welcome; McGonagall and the governors planning the memorial. Underneath the reasons sat a plainer truth: the castle was the first home he had ever had, it had nearly been destroyed the same night he had nearly died, and he needed to watch it mend.

He went down to Hagrid's on one of those Tuesdays with a tin of Mrs. Weasley's fudge.

The hut had taken a curse or two in the battle and been rebuilt in a weekend by Hagrid and Grawp, which meant it was now slightly larger and noticeably crookeder, and Harry loved it. Fang hit him at the door like a hairy Bludger. The kettle went on. The rock cakes came out. And Hagrid — who had hugged him after the battle, and wept on him at Fred's funeral, and roared himself hoarse at the victory feast — sat down opposite him, filled two mugs the size of buckets, and could not look at him at all.

"Hagrid?"

"S'nothing. Tea's hot."

"Hagrid."

The great hands went flat on the table. "I carried yeh," Hagrid said, to the teapot. "He made me carry yeh. Bound me up an' made me — an' I held yeh, an' yeh were so light, yeh've always been too light, I used ter tell Madam Pomfrey so — an' I gave yeh to 'em, Harry. I laid yeh down at his feet, on the ground, like — an' I keep thinkin'. I carried yeh once before, see. Night yer mum an' dad — Dumbledore gave yeh ter me an' I flew yeh ter yer aunt's, this little bundle, yeh slept the whole way over Bristol. An' I thought, that night in the forest, I thought: both times. Both times someone's given me this boy ter carry after You-Know-Who was done with him. On'y the second time yeh weren't —" The rest of his words were drowned by deep sobbing.

Harry came around the table — there was no point trying to get an arm all the way across those shoulders, so he did what he could, which was lean against him — and said the thing he had been thinking all morning.

"Hagrid. I was awake."

The shaking stopped.

"In the forest. The whole time. I couldn't move, and I couldn't tell you — if he'd known I was alive it was all finished, I had to let him believe it — but I heard you. I heard every word you said while you carried me." Harry's own voice was going now, and he let it go. "Everyone else found out I was alive an hour later, and they got to be glad, and that's how they'll remember that morning. You're the only person who ever mourned me to my face. I heard exactly what I was worth to you, Hagrid, out loud, the whole way up from the forest, and I'm going to be hearing it for the rest of my life. I'm sorry I couldn't tell you. And thank you. Both times you've carried me, I was alive at the end of it. You have never once put me down anywhere that wasn't safe."

Hagrid cried then, hugely and without embarrassment, into a tablecloth-sized handkerchief, and Fang howled companionably, and from somewhere up the hillside came a vast interested rumble of "HAGGER? HARRY?" — and the tea went cold and was rebrewed twice, and Harry ate three rock cakes entire, out of love, and his jaw was sore for 3 days.

Walking back up in the long evening light, he went by way of the trees at the edge of the forest. He could not stay out of it, that summer; the place where he had died kept drawing him back, as though some part of him needed to stand at its edge, again and again, and be sure that nothing in it was coming for him any more. That was where he found Luna, standing in a clearing with a bucket of raw meat, surrounded by thestrals.

"Hello, Harry," she said, as though they had arranged it weeks ago. "I'd offer you some, but it's not really for people."

The herd stood around her, black and patient, folded up like umbrellas. A foal was going through her pockets with its face. Harry watched the great dragonish heads swing toward him and remembered being fifteen, seeing them harnessed to the school carriages for the first time, and believing something had gone wrong with him.

"There are more of them than there were," he said.

"No," said Luna serenely, "there are more of us." She held a strip of meat out to the foal. "Everyone can see them now. The whole school. Hagrid says it's the largest visible herd in Britain." She regarded the thestrals with great affection. "I think they'll enjoy the company, you know. They've been here all along, being beautiful, and everyone walked past them for centuries. It must have been like being a very good book that nobody reads. And now hundreds of people will look at them every day and know they're real. Almost everything that happened is terrible," said Luna, in precisely the voice she might have used to discuss Wrackspurts, "but not that part."

Harry helped her feed them. The leathery muzzles took the meat from his palm with shocking delicacy.

"How's your dad?" he asked, carefully, because the last time he had seen Xenophilius Lovegood, the man had been trying to sell him to the Death Eaters.

"He cries at breakfast, mostly," said Luna. "He thinks I don't see. We're rebuilding the house and he keeps stopping in the middles of rooms to apologise. I've told him we're not going to carry it. They had me in a cellar, and he's my father, and nearly everyone did one desperate thing or another for somebody before the end. Ours simply happened in front of you." She wiped her hands on her robes and considered Harry with those pale, protuberant, kind eyes. "You died, didn't you. People keep saying nearly, but they weren't there — and the thestrals have been following you about all evening as if you're one of the family. They're never wrong about that sort of thing."

There was, Harry thought, absolutely no one else alive who could have said that sentence, in that tone, and made it a comfort.

"For a bit," he said.

"Was it nice?"

"Yeah," said Harry, and found that he was smiling. "Yeah, it was, actually."

"I thought it would be," said Luna, nodding. "Mum will be there. And your people. I expect it's like the voices behind the veil — you remember, in that room — not gone. Just in the next part." She picked up her empty bucket. "I'm glad you came back, though. You'd only just got Ginny sorted out. It took you ages."

She walked with him as far as the lawns, talking the whole way about her plans for repainting the Ravenclaw common room ceiling, extracted his promise to visit before September, and wandered away toward the gates; and Harry stood a moment in the blue dusk between the castle and the forest — one lit up behind him, one dark — and found that the forest frightened him a little less than it had that morning.

Chapter VIII

What the Dead Leave Behind

The owl from the Ministry's Office of Wills and Probate arrived at the Burrow on a wet morning in June, and Harry went in to the reading expecting nothing, because everything Remus Lupin owned in the world had famously fit in one shabby suitcase.

It nearly did. The will was short. To Andromeda, the contents of his Gringotts vault, "with apologies for the amount." To Teddy, everything else, held in trust — and one item to be given on his thirteenth birthday, "not before, Andromeda, I know you": the Marauder's Map. ("I solemnly swear," Harry said, when he heard, and had to stop and look out the window for a while.)

And to Harry, two things.

The first was a battered wooden case, the size of a small accordion, lined in felt, holding two dozen crystal vials in racks — each one filled with the silver-white wisp of a memory, each one labelled in Remus's tidy, exhausted handwriting. Harry read the labels with the case open on his knees in the Burrow's sitting room, while the probate clerk pretended very hard to be reading paperwork.

James — the first full moon after they managed it. (Start here.)

Lily, age 15, hexing your father in front of the whole common room. He deserved it. Note how hard Sirius is laughing.

Your parents' wedding. Sirius loses the ring for nine minutes. I have never told anyone before now.

Lily and you, October 1981. She is teaching you to clap.

Dora. For Teddy, when he asks what she laughed like.

The second item was a letter. Harry read it alone, in the orchard, and never showed anyone the whole of it, though he told Ginny most. You are holding, I hope, the only kind of wealth I ever managed to store up, Remus had written. I spent years believing my memories were a vault of grief, and it took your mother, and then Sirius, and then — late, too late, I am sorry — Dora and Teddy to teach me they held inexplicable joy. Visit them, Harry. That is an assignment from your professor, and I will know if you skip it.

Look after my son. Not from duty — he will have Andromeda for raising and the Weasleys, I suspect, for spoiling — but the way Sirius should have been allowed to look after you. Take him on his first broom. Teach him how to use the map when he leaves for Hogwarts and how to cast a Patronus at thirteen. And when he is old enough to ask why his parents went to the battle when they had a son at home — and he will ask — tell him the truth: that we went because we had a son at home. We fought for a morning we knew we might not see. If you are reading this, the morning came. Show it to him.

P.S. — The last vial is labelled in Dora's hand, not mine. She did it the night before the battle when she thought I wasn't looking. I haven't viewed it. It's marked "For Remus, if I'm slow." I am trusting you to make sure my son sees it one day, because I find — forgive me — I haven't the courage to write about it further.

P.P.S. — In the side pocket you will find my emergency chocolate. I suspect you now need it more than I do. Eat it. Professor's orders.

The chocolate was Honeydukes, very old, perfectly preserved under a Stasis Charm. Harry ate one square in the orchard with the rain coming on, and it worked the way it had in a train carriage full of Dementors five years before, which is to say it helped immensely, but was not quite enough.

Sirius's bequest had come to Harry years before, of course — a house, a vault, and a hippogriff — but it was that same June, with Remus's case of memories under his arm and nowhere that felt right to keep it, that Harry finally understood what he had actually been left. He had been bequeathed a home, and had simply never moved into it.

He and Kreacher took Grimmauld Place back room by room, and they did it, by unspoken agreement, not by erasing the Blacks but by choosing among them. The troll's-leg umbrella stand went. The shrunken heads came down (Kreacher was permitted, after negotiation, to keep them in his den, where he polished them on Sundays). Walburga Black's portrait defeated every counter-curse Bill Weasley knew — the Permanent Sticking Charm had been cast with real feeling — until Harry stopped trying to remove her, and instead hung on the facing wall, where she could not avoid looking at it for all eternity, a large photograph from his parents' wedding: James and Lily and Sirius and Remus, young and shouting with laughter, Sirius's head thrown all the way back, every one of them waving. Walburga screamed for a week. Then she fell silent, permanently — defeated, in the end, by joy — and never said another word.

Sirius's bedroom Harry did not change at all. The Gryffindor banners and the Muggle motorcycles and the posters Sirius had put up at sixteen specifically to enrage his mother were fixed with the same Permanent Sticking Charm, which meant the room would belong to seventeen-year-old Sirius forever, which Harry decided was exactly right. He aired it, and mended the window, and put a cot in the corner that would keep for a few years, and told Andromeda that whenever Teddy was old enough for sleepovers, there was a room waiting in London: his godfather's room, in his godfather's godfather's house, under the eyes of every motorcycle Walburga Black had ever hated.

And in Kreacher's den, one evening, without ceremony, Harry hung a small gold frame. Inside it was a scrap of parchment that had cost one life to write and another to retrieve: To the Dark Lord — I know I will be dead long before you read this — signed, in a cramped and furious hand, R.A.B.

Kreacher stood in front of it for a very long time, his ears trembling.

"Master Regulus's note," he croaked at last. "In a frame of gold. In Kreacher's room."

"He was the first person who ever fought back from inside this house," said Harry. "Seemed to me the house ought to know about it."

The cooking improved beyond all recognition after that.

Fred's will was not read at the Ministry, because Fred had lodged it where Fred lodged everything: in the back office of Weasleys' Wizard Wheezes, in a drawer marked DO NOT OPEN (NO REALLY) (GEORGE THIS MEANS YOU). George opened it in July, the week before the shop reopened, with Ron and Harry there because he could not do it alone, and read it aloud in a voice that wobbled.

"Being the last will and testament of Fred Weasley, the better-looking one. In the event of my heroic, tragic, and devastatingly handsome death: everything to George, obviously, you absolute weeping mess, stand up straight. The shop is yours, but the name stays plural. To Ronnie — my share of the flat, and the strong recommendation that you stop pretending you don't want to work here, the customers will love you. To Ginny, my broom, and the Bludger we kept in the cellar; she knows why and Mum had better not. To Harry Potter, our founding investor, who handed two skint nobodies a thousand Galleons in a fit of what we can only assume was head trauma: lifetime free merchandise, not to exceed what Verity can carry, a one-third share of every laugh this shop ever sells, and the following instruction, which is binding and enforceable: laugh at least once a day, Potter. That's an order from a dead man. They're very hard to refuse."

There was a silence in the back office.

"Well," said George, wiping his face with the heel of his hand, "you heard him. Once a day. We're opening Monday, and the pair of you are working the till, and Harry's wearing the staff robes, the magenta does wonders for the Boy Who Lived."

They opened Monday. The queue went down Diagon Alley and round the corner, and the first thing every customer saw, hanging over the till where Fred could keep an eye on the money, was a portrait-sized photograph of two identical grinning redheads, under a sign in gold letters that read: FRED WEASLEY DIED LAUGHING. THE LEAST YOU CAN DO IS JOIN HIM. — MANAGEMENT.

Chapter IX

The Trials of Summer

The trials ran all summer, a slow tide through Courtrooms One to Ten, and Harry attended more of them than he wanted to and far fewer than the Prophet implied.

Dolores Umbridge was tried in June, before a full Wizengamot and a packed gallery, on charges that took the court scribe eleven minutes to read into the record: her direction of the Muggle-born Registration Commission; the wands snapped; the families broken; the Dementors she had stationed inside her own courtroom for the comfort of the accused.
