She arrived in pink, with a little black bow in her hair, and surveyed the chamber with the bright, unbothered confidence of a woman who had been useful to every regime in living memory and visibly expected to be useful to this one too.

Then the prosecution called Hermione Granger.

Hermione testified for two hours with a stack of the Commission's own files at her elbow — she had, it emerged, lifted an entire drawerful on her way out of the Ministry disguised as Mafalda Hopkirk, on the grounds that "evidence was clearly going to be wanted eventually" — and she was calm, and she was precise, and she was entirely without mercy. She read the names of the convicted into the record, every one. She described Mary Cattermole's hearing: the Dementors along the walls, the silver cat pacing the rail, and the locket — a stolen locket, Madam, looted from a murdered man, though you told that courtroom it was an old family heirloom; the family was the House of Slytherin, and it was a Horcrux, and you wore it for weeks without noticing anything amiss, which the court may weigh as it sees fit — glittering at Umbridge's throat while she processed a mother of three toward the Kiss for the crime of owning a wand.

"Hem, hem," said Umbridge, into a pause.

"I yield no portion of my allotted time," said Hermione, without looking at her, "to interruptions," and returned to the files; and the sound from the gallery was one the Chief Warlock chose, leniently, to have recorded as a cough.

Umbridge was convicted on every count on a Thursday afternoon and taken down to begin a life sentence still explaining, in that sweet girlish voice rising by degrees toward something a great deal less sweet, that every single thing she had done had been authorised — which was, Hermione said afterward, white-faced over tea at Fortescue's reopened ice cream parlour, the true horror of her, and the reason the verdict mattered less than what came next: "She'll die believing she was a good employee, Harry. The thing that has to burn isn't her. It's the paperwork that made her possible." She began drafting that same evening, on the back of the sundae menu. Kingsley is said to have kept the parchment.

The Malfoys came up in the third week of July.

Narcissa was tried first, and the charge sheet was thin — a Manor lent to Voldemort had also been a Manor occupied by him, and prosecutors with sons of their own read the file and understood what it described — and the trial turned, in the end, on four minutes in the Forbidden Forest. Harry testified to them himself. He described lying in the leaf-mould with his heart slamming against a brand-new scar while Voldemort sent Narcissa Malfoy forward to confirm the kill; the cool hands; the fingernails; the whisper — is Draco alive? Is he in the castle? — and then the lie she had told, kneeling at her master's feet, with his snake circling her shoulders and the greatest Legilimens in history a wand's length away.

"She had exactly one piece of information left that Voldemort wanted," Harry told the Wizengamot, "and she spent it on her son. If she answers honestly, I'm dead where I lie, and most of the people in the castle die after me. Every person who walked out of Hogwarts that morning walked out through a door Narcissa Malfoy held shut with her bare nerve. I'm not asking the court to like her. I'm telling the court what's owed."

Probation; wand restrictions; reparations on a scale that would quietly rebuild half of Hogsmeade. Narcissa Malfoy received the verdict standing, with no expression whatsoever, and then looked across the chamber at Harry and inclined her head perhaps an inch — the bow of one creditor to another, both accounts closed — and Harry inclined his, and that was the entire conversation, and it was sufficient.

Draco's trial took a single morning. He had been sixteen when Voldemort assigned him an impossible murder as a slow death sentence for his father's failures, and the defence barely had to argue the coercion; it only had to read it aloud. Harry testified that Draco had lowered his wand on the Astronomy Tower before the Death Eaters ever reached it. He testified that a boy who had shared a school with him for six years had stood in his own drawing room, looked directly into his swollen face, and said he couldn't be sure. Ron — with the air of a man performing surgery on himself without anaesthetic — confirmed it. Probation; supervision; wand to be returned upon release from evidence.

Which is how Harry came to be standing in the corridor outside Courtroom Eight afterward, while Narcissa signed parchment at a clerk's desk, holding a wand of hawthorn and unicorn hair that had been in his keeping, one way and another, since March.

Draco was alone by the lifts. He was thinner, and the year had carved something out of his face that was never coming back, and he watched Harry approach the way you watch weather arrive.

"This is yours," said Harry, and held it out, handle first.

Draco looked at the wand and did not move. The last time it had changed hands, Harry had torn it bodily out of his grip in a drawing room full of screaming. "It won't —" he began, and stopped, and tried again with less voice in it. "It changed allegiance. Everyone knows what it —" what it did was win the war; the wand that had been taken from Draco Malfoy had gone on to master the Elder Wand itself, though barely a dozen people living knew the chain entire. "It won't answer to me anymore."

"It will," said Harry. "I asked Ollivander. Wands come back when they're given back. Taking is what breaks the allegiance. Giving is what mends it." He kept his hand out, steady, in the empty corridor. "Take your wand, Malfoy."

"Why?" It came out cracked and furious and about fifteen years old. "You hate me. I'd have let it happen, at the Manor — I didn't say your name, but I didn't stop anything either — I have never once stopped anything — why would you —"

"Because it's yours," said Harry. "Because your mother lied to Voldemort's face with his snake on her shoulders, and I'm standing here because of it, and I don't want to owe your family anything ever again — so take it, and we're square." And then, because it was true, and the war was over, and he was so tired of the old arithmetic: "And because you were sixteen, and nobody came for you. People came for me my whole life, Malfoy. Whole queues of them, taking turns. Nobody once came for you."

The corridor was very quiet. Somewhere down the row a lift arrived, stood open, and left empty.

Draco took the wand. Colour came back into his face at the touch of it — or perhaps that was only the light — and something came back with it: half an inch of spine; the ghost of the boy on the train who had stuck out his hand before either of them knew anything at all.

"Potter," he said. It was not thank you. It was as close as he could get, and both of them knew the exchange rate.

"Malfoy," said Harry, and nodded, and walked away.

It was not forgiveness, exactly, and neither of them would have called it friendship under Veritaserum. It was a door left unlocked between two houses — which both of them privately intended never to use, and which both of them, in the years that came after, would more than once be quietly glad of.

Chapter X

The Burrow in Summer

The summer arranged itself, without anyone deciding, around two poles: Hogwarts and the Burrow.

Three days a week they went up to the castle, where the rebuilding had become the largest voluntary gathering of wizards in living memory. McGonagall ran it like a military operation crossed with a village fête; Flitwick re-charmed the enchanted ceiling, which had stuck, like a broken clock, on the storm of the battle; Hagrid and Grawp moved fallen masonry that whole teams couldn't shift; and Bill Weasley led the curse-breaking crews through the corridors, flagging the scars of Dark magic that couldn't simply be mended, because some spells leave marks that stone remembers. Harry worked on Bill's crew. He told everyone it was because he was good at it — which was true; a year of hunting Horcruxes had given him a strange portfolio of skills — but the real reason, which only Ginny had worked out, was that the castle was the first thing he had ever loved, and it had been wounded answering for him, and he slept badly on the days he hadn't put some piece of it back.

He slept badly most days, in truth.

The nightmares had a rotation, like a duty roster. The forest. The Manor, Hermione's screams through the floor. The locket's voices. Walking into the clearing again, except in the dream nobody was waiting at the castle to be saved, the castle was already burning, he had got it wrong, he was always exactly too late. He learned to wake without shouting, mostly. He learned to cast Muffliato on his bed-hangings at Grimmauld Place and at the Burrow both — knowing, now, that Ginny could hear the edges of it, and casting it anyway — and to be downstairs early with tea already made, looking rested, because the Weasleys had buried a son and the last thing they needed was to carry him too. He had a sentence for the bad mornings, a small lie with excellent posture: it's over, so I'm fine.

To Ginny — who had known since the first night, who asked, every time, directly — he reported the weather: better this week. Hardly anything now. Honestly.

It worked for six weeks. It worked right up until the July night Ron — who had spent eleven months sleeping six feet from Harry in a tent and could read his breathing like a clock — stood outside the Muffliato and counted, and then went downstairs and got his sister.

Ginny didn't knock. She came in, sat on the edge of the bed while Harry was still surfacing from the forest with his hand clamped to his chest, and put her palm flat over his — over the scar — and waited, saying nothing at all, until his breathing came down out of the trees.

"Better this week," she said then, evenly, into the dark. "Hardly anything now. Honestly."

"Ginny —"

"You lied to me." She said it without heat, which was far worse. "Madam Pomfrey told us we'd talk about the dreams when the funerals were done. The funerals are done, Harry. They've been done for weeks. And instead you've been up at dawn making tea so you'd look rested, and silencing your bed in a house full of people who love you, and telling me the one thing you knew would make me stop checking. So, no. We're not doing that anymore. New rule, and it's the only one I'm ever going to give you: you don't have to be all right with me. Not ever. You just have to be honest."

"Everyone in this house is grieving," Harry said to the canopy, because he could not say it to her face. "Your mum buried a son nine weeks ago. I'm not adding —"

"If you finish that sentence with some arithmetic about whose pain weighs more," said Ginny, "I am going to fetch the Bludger Fred left me." But her hand stayed where it was, steady on the lightning-tree over his heart, and when she went on it was in the gentle voice almost nobody got. "Harry. I had a year of nightmares after the Chamber. Riddle's voice, every single night, telling me which parts had been my fault. You know what I learned? They get smaller when you say them out loud. They hate being said out loud. It takes the dark out of them, like opening curtains. You taught a room full of us to drive off Dementors with a memory — and you can't aim one honest conversation at your own boggarts?"

"It's not the same —"

"It's exactly the same. You're just only allowed to do it for other people, and never for you." She lay down on top of the covers, shoulder to his shoulder, the way she had in another life, in a sunlit corner by the lake. "So. The forest. Walk me through it — the real one, not the version you told the office. What did it smell like? What did your feet sound like? Where was I, when you thought about me?"

And Harry, staring at the dark canopy of the bed, with her hand over the scar and her hair against his arm, opened the curtains.

It was not a cure. The dreams came back — they would come back for years, thinning slowly, the way floodwater goes down. But that was the night the war stopped being a thing Harry carried alone in a locked room, and by August, when Andromeda — who had buried a husband and a daughter in one year and had opinions about grief — quietly gave him the name of a Healer at St. Mungo's who specialised in curse-trauma and the survivors of the war, Harry took the card. And, eventually, used it. He made Ron come too. Ron complained the whole way there and was silent the whole way home, and the following week said, with elaborate carelessness, "Same time Thursday, then?", and that was that.

Up at the castle, meanwhile, there was one stretch of the rebuilding that nobody would touch.

The seventh-floor corridor had been repaired around — the work crews simply flowed past it, the way water flows past a stone — and by July it stood alone in the mended castle: a ragged gap of blasted wall and open sky, swept clean of rubble, swept daily in fact, but not rebuilt; because every witch and wizard on the crews knew exactly what had happened on that spot, and not one of them was willing to be the person who made it look as though it hadn't.

George came on a Tuesday. He came with his sleeves rolled and one of Fred's flat caps jammed on his head, and he brought Percy.

He declined all offers of help, including Harry's, with perfect courtesy — "Family wall, this one" — and the crews found reasons to work within sight of it all day without once looking directly at it. The brothers did much of it the slow way, by hand, stone lifted onto stone, Percy in his shirtsleeves with mortar to the elbows; and Percy talked the whole time. That was the thing the people who were there carried away with them afterward: Percy Weasley — who had not made a joke in anyone's hearing for three years, and had broken the drought roughly thirty seconds before the wall came down — talking, on and on, low and steady, while his brother set stones. He talked about the new legislation and a memo that had bitten him; about their father's plug collection and their mother's clock; about a Tuesday in 1989 when Fred had transfigured his prefect badge to read PINHEAD and he had worn it half the day without noticing. And George worked, and said almost nothing, and listened the way you lean on something.

It was Harry, working down the corridor on Bill's crew, who heard the end of it, because the end of it was nearly silent.

"He died laughing." Percy's voice had stopped being steady. "At me. At a joke I made. I gave them three years of nothing, and the very last thing he ever heard was me being funny, and I keep — George, I keep needing it to mean something, and I've been afraid to ask you whether it does, because you're the only person alive who would actually know —"

George set down the stone he was holding. He looked at the wall, at the last gap in it, which was about the size of a man.

"You want to know what it means," he said. "It means the last thing that ever happened to him was a surprise from someone he loved. That's the whole of what a joke is, Perce. That's the entire trick, there isn't any other part. He went out on the best one there is." And he took from his jacket something small that gleamed — one Weasleys' Wizard Wheezes product, of a type the watchers down the corridor never afterward agreed on — kissed it the way you kiss a Snitch, set it into the gap, and sealed the last stones over it with his wand.

"There is now," announced George, to the corridor at large, in something approaching his shop voice, "a Weasley product inside the fabric of Hogwarts castle for the rest of time, and the Headmistress need never know."

"The Headmistress," said Professor McGonagall, from the end of the corridor, where she had been standing for some minutes with her hands folded, "knows everything that becomes part of her castle, Mr. Weasley. I shall expect it to remain dormant."

"No promises, Professor."

And Minerva McGonagall looked for a long moment at the smooth new wall with Fred Weasley's last laugh sealed inside it, and said, "No. Quite right. None of us can make those anymore" — and walked on, and let it stand.

The rest of the Burrow summer was made of smaller things, which turned out to be the things that mattered. Mrs. Weasley cooking for twelve every night because feeding people was how she stayed upright, and nobody dreaming of stopping her. Percy, awkward as a heron, mowing the entire orchard by hand without being asked, because penance needs somewhere to go. The ghoul restored to the attic, banging companionably. The clock on the wall, all nine golden hands swung at last to home — and the family standing in front of it the day Mr. Weasley quietly took down Fred's hand, and Mrs. Weasley took it from him, and put it not away but on the mantelpiece, facing the room, where it could keep an eye on the money.

It was Ginny who got George back on a broom. The paddock matches had restarted in July — two-a-side over the orchard, charmed apples for Bludgers, Mrs. Weasley pretending not to watch from the kitchen window the way she had been pretending for twenty years — and George had watched four of them from the top of the wall, commentating like Lee Jordan, flying in none. Nobody pushed. Beaters fly in pairs; everyone could do the arithmetic.

On the fifth evening, Ginny walked over to the wall and held out Fred's old bat.

"We are one apple down the chimney away from losing to Ron and Hermione," she said. "Hermione, George. She flies like a deckchair. This is now a family emergency."

George looked at the bat for a long moment. Then he took it, and kicked off, and the first crack of apple off willow went echoing away over the orchard like something splitting open that had badly needed to; and when they all landed an hour later, sweaty and shouting, George stood holding the bat with an odd, listening expression, and said to Harry, quietly, under the noise of Ron disputing the score: "Thought it'd feel like he was missing worse up there than anywhere. It doesn't. It just feels like flying." He spun the bat once and caught it. "He's not up there, is the thing. He's in the shop, and in the wall, and in about half of everything I'm going to say for the rest of my life. Turns out the sky's just the sky." And then, swinging back up onto the wall, in his full shop voice: "RIGHT. Best of three, and somebody confiscate Hermione's broom before she injures a tree."

And up at the castle, in greenhouse three, something was growing that had nothing to do with plants. Neville Longbottom had taken over the rebuilding of the greenhouses with a quiet authority that nobody questioned, because nobody questions the man who beheaded Voldemort's snake with the Sword of Gryffindor in front of a thousand witnesses while on fire. And every day at noon, the lunch deliveries from the Leaky Cauldron arrived by the trundling crate-load — and every day, somehow, the crate for greenhouse three was carried up personally by Hannah Abbott, who had left school in their sixth year when her mother was killed, and had come back for the battle, and was now helping old Tom run the pub.

"She does know," said Ginny, watching from the courtyard with deep scientific interest as Hannah explained, for the fourth consecutive day, that she just happened to be passing, "that the greenhouses are not on the way to anywhere."

"Leave them alone," said Harry, watching Neville present Hannah with a flutterby bush cutting in a pot, going steadily redder, explaining its care at considerable length while Hannah held it like a crown jewel. "It took us six years."

"It took you six years. I was ready in four." Ginny narrowed her eyes. "Five Galleons says he's planted her something by August and proposes within five years."

"No bet," said Harry, who had seen the careful row of seedlings in greenhouse three labelled, in Neville's round hand, H.A. — and the following week, when Hannah's mother's grave in the churchyard at Godric's Hollow gained a flutterby bush that rippled gently even in still air, he kept that to himself, because some things you were allowed to see and not say.

Chapter XI

Monica and Wendell

Hermione went to see the Minister for Magic in the second week of June, and was shown up to Level One by a secretary who kept sneaking looks at her, and found Kingsley Shacklebolt behind a desk drowning in parchment — the Wizengamot's restructuring to one side, the trial schedules to the other, and somewhere underneath it all, presumably, a government.

"Miss Granger." He cleared a chair for her himself. "Tell me what you need."

She had prepared a speech with subsections. What came out instead was: "I modified my parents' memories last August. Total identity reconstruction — they believe they are Wendell and Monica Wilkins, no daughter, lifelong dream of moving to Australia. I built the work to withstand interrogation, which means I built it to withstand me, and I —" the subsections deserted her entirely — "I know they landed in Melbourne. I don't know where they are now. The reversal has to be done by the original caster, so it has to be me, but I can't find them, not quickly, not from here — and every month it stays in their heads, the harder it comes out."

Kingsley wrote two lines on a fresh sheet, sealed it, and sent it off with a tap of his wand. "Three things, then," he said, and counted them off with the calm that had steadied a year of Potterwatch. "First: the Australian Ministry's Office of International Magical Cooperation owes this office several favours, and is about to repay one. Muggle records, professional registries — if Wendell Wilkins has so much as renewed a fishing licence, you will have an address within a fortnight. Second: there will be an International Portkey authorised in your name, and a second one, keyed to emergencies, to bring you home — because you are not going to the far side of the planet to perform delicate reversal work with no way back. Third." He looked at her over the wreckage of his desk. "There is a question you have been very careful not to ask me. Let me answer it anyway. The statutes governing the use of memory charms are enforced by this Ministry. The Ministry that might have prosecuted a Muggle-born girl for hiding her family from a death squad no longer exists; you had a hand in burning it down. The one sitting in its place considers the matter" — he pressed a stamp down with finality — "closed. Go and get your parents, Miss Granger."

Hermione — who had argued with examiners, Ministers, and on one notable occasion a dragon — found that she could not speak at all, and nodded instead.

She declined the outbound Portkey in the end, to almost everyone's bafflement and Ron's open horror. "An international Portkey chain is eleven jumps and a full day of feeling like you've been put through a Muggle washing machine, and I need my wand-hand steady at the other end," she said, which was true; and then, which was truer: "And they crossed the world as Muggles, because I sent them as Muggles. I want to arrive the way they live. It seems owed."

So on a Tuesday at the end of June, Ron Weasley walked into Heathrow Airport carrying his worldly possessions in a rucksack with an Undetectable Extension Charm, surrendered his belt to Muggle security with the expression of a man being asked to hand over his wand, and gripped both armrests through the takeoff while the most brilliant witch of her age held his hand and watched England tilt away beneath them with her heart in her mouth.

The flight was twenty-two hours, and it changed two lives, though only one of them noticed at the time. Ron passed through terror somewhere over France and arrived — by way of the drinks trolley and the discovery of in-flight films ("it's a Pensieve, but with dragons that aren't real, Hermione — do Muggles have any idea how good they have it?") — at a kind of evangelical wonder. And somewhere over the Indian Ocean, around hour fifteen, with the cabin dark and Hermione staring sleeplessly at the seatback in front of her, rehearsing reversal theory she had known cold for a year, Ron put down his third film and said the wisest thing she ever heard him say.

"Stop revising the spell. The spell's going to work; you built it. It's after that you're scared of." He let that sit a moment. "So tell them all of it. Not the Prophet version — the real one. Write it down the way you'd want to be told. Everything. Even the bits you've never said out loud to anyone but me. Because your dad's not going to be angry about a charm, is he. He's going to be angry about every single thing that happened to you that he wasn't allowed to stand in front of. You can't give him those years back, Hermione. But you can let him know. It's the only thing you've got that he actually wants."

Hermione looked at him for a long moment in the dim cabin light.

"When did you get like this?" she said.

"Tent," said Ron, and went back to his film.

She wrote for the rest of the flight. By the time the wheels touched Australia she had filled three school exercise books, and her hand ached, and Ron had watched the dragon film four times; and at arrivals a liaison witch from the Australian Ministry stood holding a sign that said GRANGER, with an address in a Melbourne suburb folded discreetly behind it.

The dental practice was on a quiet street, with a jacaranda out front and a brass plate that said WILKINS FAMILY DENTAL, and Hermione stood looking at the brass plate for four and a half minutes before Ron took her hand.

"Worst case," he said, "it doesn't work, and we research until it does. You're you. There's no spell you can't take apart."

"That is not the worst case," said Hermione, in a small precise voice. "The worst case is that it works."

It worked.

She did it in their sitting room that evening, having got herself inside with a story about a university survey, because there are doors even war heroines have to talk their way through. The reversal of a memory modification that deep was, she had warned Ron, less like opening a curtain than like restoring a flooded house — the memories came back in surges, out of order, each one arriving with its full original weight. Mrs. Granger — Monica — Mrs. Granger sat down very suddenly in the middle of standing. Mr. Granger gripped the back of the sofa and stared at the brown-eyed girl in their sitting room while nineteen years rose around him like water: a bushy-haired baby; a letter delivered by owl; a platform between platforms; a daughter, a daughter, they had a daughter —

"Hermione," her mother said, in a voice that cracked down the middle, and held out her arms, and for one whole minute it was everything Hermione had imagined on the worst nights in the tent.

Then her father straightened up, and his face changed.

"You did this," he said. "To us. You — our own daughter — went into our heads —"

"Dan," said her mother.

"A year, Hermione. More! We've been here over a year, we sold the house, we left — Christ, we left the country — and none of it was real, none of it was us —" He was not shouting. Hermione told Ron afterward that shouting would have been easier. "You took our choice away. We're your parents. It was our job to protect you, and you decided we couldn't, and you reached into our minds and you took it. Like we were — furniture. To be put in storage."

"Yes," said Hermione.

The word stopped him. She stood in the middle of the sitting room she had charmed herself out of, eighteen years old, and did not defend herself at all.

"Everything you just said is true. I took your choice away. I did it because there was a war, and because of who I am in that war, and because the people we were fighting torture the families of people like me for information and then kill them — and I knew that if I asked you, you'd refuse to go. You'd have stayed, because it was your job to protect me. And then I'd have spent the war knowing you were a target I'd painted myself, and I had — Dad, I had to be able to think." Her voice was shaking now. "I'm not asking you to say it was all right. It wasn't all right. It was just the only thing I could live with. And I told myself that if we won, I'd come and give it all back and let you be as angry as you needed to be. So." She lifted her chin, and Ron, standing uselessly by the mantelpiece, thought she had never looked more like the girl who had walked into a troll's bathroom. "Be angry. I'm not leaving this time. I'll be at the hotel on Collins Street, every day, for as long as it takes."

It took four days.

For four days there were stilted lunches and silences with edges on them, and Mrs. Granger looking at her daughter with her heart in her face while Mr. Granger looked mostly at the table. It was on the fourth evening that Hermione took the three exercise books out of her beaded bag — where they had sat since the landing like something live — and did the thing Ron had told her to do over the Indian Ocean.

She left them on her parents' kitchen table after dinner on the fourth night, with a note that said This is what happened to me. I would rather you heard it from these than from the newspapers, which have most of it wrong, and you WILL be reading the newspapers, because our photographs are in them. All my love, regardless. — H.

It was all there. The troll, told funny, because it was. The basilisk in the pipes the year she was twelve, and the part where she lay petrified in the hospital wing for weeks — which you were never told about, and there is an apology owed there, and not by me; the school will be receiving a letter. The Department of Mysteries. The year on the run: the tent, the hunger, the radio names of the dead, the locket that whispered. And then, because she had promised herself the whole truth or nothing, Malfoy Manor: the drawing-room floor, and the woman who had carved through her arm and her mind for the better part of an hour wanting to know how they'd got into her vault, and the knife at her throat afterward, and the chandelier, and the elf who died getting them out — written plainly, without one adjective of self-pity, in the same round handwriting that had been on every school report they'd ever signed.

Hermione and Ron came back the next morning to find the kitchen door already open. Her mother was standing in it. She had clearly been up all night; she had the third exercise book still in one hand; and she looked at her daughter for a moment, and then took Hermione's left forearm, very gently, in both hands — the arm Hermione had kept in long sleeves through four days of Australian winter sun — and pushed the sleeve back, and looked at the thin silvered line of the scar there, and at the word it spelled, and did not flinch, because she had decided, somewhere around four in the morning, that her daughter would never again watch a parent flinch at it.

"You will tell me," said Mrs. Granger, "what that word means, and why anyone thought it applied to you. And then you are going to let your mother make you breakfast every morning for as long as you are on this continent, and you are going to eat it, because you have clearly missed about four hundred meals, and we have a great deal of catching up to do." Her voice held until almost the end. "All of us."

Behind her, Mr. Granger appeared in the hall. He had the first exercise book under his arm and he had aged and softened in some way overnight, and he stood looking at his daughter — the brightest witch of her age, the war heroine, eighteen years old in his doorway hoping to be let in — and what he finally said was:

"The troll. You went and hid in the toilets?"

"I was twelve," said Hermione, and then she was laughing, and then she was crying, and then she was in her father's arms with her mother's around them both, and Ron stood in the garden examining the jacaranda with tremendous diligence until Mr. Granger's voice said, gruffly, over Hermione's head: "You. Weasley. It says here you came back. In the middle of winter, against — what's the word — Disapparition, no map, no idea where they'd gone. But you came back and you found her."

"She came back for me first," said Ron. "Second year. I had — there were these spiders —" He stopped. "It's a long story, sir."

"We've got nineteen years," said Hermione's father. "Come inside."

The Grangers sold the practice in September. They were home — the real home, the one with Hermione's height-marks on the larder door, repurchased at what the estate agent called a sentimental premium and Mr. Granger called daylight robbery — by Christmas, where they hosted a dinner attended by a number of redheads that Mrs. Granger never did manage to count accurately, and at which Mr. Granger and Arthur Weasley discovered one another with a joy so total — "He wants to know how AEROPLANES stay up, Dan, NOBODY ever wants to know" — that their wives eventually had to confiscate the whiteboard.

Chapter XII

The Kissing Gate

They went to Godric's Hollow on a Sunday at the end of July, the four of them, because Harry had been three times in his life and could remember almost none of it: once as a baby, being carried out of a ruin; once at Christmas, in the snow, with a snake waiting; and once — though this hardly counted — on the front of a thousand postcards he had never asked anybody to print.

"I've never been at all," Ron said, as they walked down from the Apparition point with the hedgerows ticking with crickets. He said it quietly, like an apology. "Eight years. Best mate. Never once thought to —"

"You were busy," said Harry. "There was a list."

The village in summer was a different country from the village in the snow. The lane came down past gardens in full riot, washing on lines, a tabby asleep on a warm wall; somewhere a wireless was playing through an open window, and the church tower stood up out of the green like it had grown there. The last time Harry had walked this lane it had been Christmas Eve, under snow, wearing someone else's face, with carols leaking out of the church and a horror waiting in a cottage; and he discovered, walking it now in the smell of cut grass, that you could give a place back to yourself. You just had to come in daylight, with the right people.

In the square, the war memorial felt them coming. The obelisk shivered as they crossed toward it and became the other thing, the true thing: a man with untidy hair and glasses; a woman with long hair and a kind, tired, lovely face; and a baby boy sitting in his mother's arms.

Ron stopped dead in the road. He looked at the statue, and then at Harry, and then at the statue.

"That's them."

"Yeah."

"That's you."

"Yeah."

Ron studied the marble baby with the air of a connoisseur. "You were a fat baby," he said at last, with deep satisfaction — and Hermione made a scandalised noise, and Ginny laughed out loud in the quiet square, and the laugh didn't land wrong; it landed the way Ginny's laughs always landed, like a window opening. Harry looked at his mother's stone face under the summer sky and thought she would have liked the sound of it.

The cottage they came to last on the way out of the square, and the gate did what the gate did: the moment Harry's hand touched it, the ruin swam up out of its concealment for the four of them — the cottage still standing but broken-backed, ivy holding the walls together, the whole right side of the upper storey blown outward and open to the sky. The room where it happened. The room where both things happened: the worst thing, and the thing that had never happened before in the history of the world.

The sign rose out of the ground by the gate, golden letters on wood, the monument-marker telling why the house had been left as it stood. And around the official words, over them, under them, filling every inch of the wood and overflowing onto the gatepost, was the handwriting — years of it, layers of it, the messages travellers had left. Harry had seen it at Christmas and been moved by it then: good luck, Harry, wherever you are. But since May there was new ink over the old, a whole spring tide of it, and the new ink said other things.

HE DID IT.

Thank you. Thank you. Thank you — one hand, over and over, filling a corner.

My daughter came home because of this family.

And down low, near the grass, small and green and careful: It's over. Sleep well.

Harry read the gate for a long time. Nobody hurried him.

"When this is a museum someday," Ron said finally, his voice not entirely level, "I'm going to tell them I knew you when you couldn't do a Summoning Charm. I feel the record should be kept honest."

The churchyard gate was a contraption Ron had to be shown how to use: a little half-moon pen with a swinging gate inside it, so that it could never stand open, and you had to step in and shuffle round and let it close behind you before you could pass.

"It's called a kissing gate," said Hermione.

"Why?"

"Because only one person can come through at a time," said Ginny, going through ahead of Harry and then turning and holding the gate shut against him from the other side, "so the one in front can demand a toll." She tapped her cheek. Harry paid the toll. Ron, arriving at the gate behind him, looked at Hermione with the expression of a man discovering that the wizarding world had been withholding critical information from him for years.

They walked the rows slowly, in the bee-loud green. Kendra and Ariana Dumbledore, under where your treasure is, there will your heart be also — and Harry, who had stood here once feeling cheated of the whole story, stood now knowing all of it, and found the anger had gone wherever the rest of that year had gone, and what was left read like what it was: a mother, and a sister, and a boy who had carried them both to the end of his long life. Ignotus Peverell, under the worn triangular mark, the stone so old the name was mostly weather. "Family, technically," Harry said, and put his palm flat on it, one owner of the Cloak to the first.

By the yew, a small grave neither he nor Hermione had marked at Christmas had something growing on it that did not belong to the churchyard: a flutterby bush, young and glossy, planted with visible care, rippling gently in air that wasn't moving. The headstone said ABBOTT. Harry caught Ginny's eye and said nothing, and Ginny caught his and said nothing back, and they let it ripple.

And then the white marble, two names on one stone, and the line beneath:

THE LAST ENEMY THAT SHALL BE DESTROYED IS DEATH.

"At Christmas," Harry said, "I thought it sounded like something they'd say. Death Eaters. Conquering death — that was his whole — I stood here in the snow and I couldn't work out why anyone would put that over my parents." He could hear Hermione beside him, the careful quiet of someone who remembered exactly what she had answered then. "Hermione said it meant living beyond death. Living after it. I nodded, and I didn't believe her, because it was December, and we were losing, and it just looked like a hole in the ground with a riddle on it."

He knelt down and set his hand on the grass, where the sweet peas were going — Ginny had carried them from the Burrow wrapped in damp newspaper, a great armful of Molly's own, because Molly had stood at the kitchen door that morning, taken one look at where they were going, and gone for the scissors without a word.

"It's not a riddle," Harry said to the stone, very quietly. "I checked."

And then it came, and he let it come. He had cried for his parents before — at two in the morning at ten years old, in front of a mirror at eleven, into Hagrid's photograph album more times than he would ever tell anyone — but all of that crying, he understood now, kneeling in the warm grass, had been for himself: the boy missing them, the boy robbed. This was different, and it was enormous, and it didn't frighten him. This was grief with the panic finally gone out of it. He cried the way you cry when something is finished: for James, who got twenty-one years; for Lily, whose hand he had held in a forest she'd been dead in for sixteen of them; for everything they bought and never saw delivered.

"It worked," he managed, to the marble, because it was the message he had been carrying since May and there was nowhere else to deliver it. "Everything you did. I'm supposed to tell you — it worked. He's gone, and I'm not, and the house is full of people, and —" and that was as far as words went.

Ginny was already down on the grass beside him, both arms around him, her cheek against his back, directly over the tree of scars, holding on the way she held on, like fact. Ron's hand arrived on the top of his head, heavy and brotherly, and stayed there a moment, and Ron said nothing at all, which from Ron was fluent. Hermione knelt and laid out the sweet peas along the base of the stone — pink and white and that deep Lily-red — taking her time about it, getting them right, crying without any fuss, the way she did everything.

The church bell counted some hour or other. Nobody checked which.

Afterward — washed out, wrung dry, strangely light — they didn't leave the churchyard straight away. The other three drifted up the path ahead, Ron pretending to develop an interest in seventeenth-century headstones, and Harry and Ginny ended up at the kissing gate, Ginny sitting up on the frame of it with her heels hooked on the bar, Harry leaning beside her, the village going about its Sunday evening beyond the wall. Swifts were hunting midges over the lane. Somebody's dinner was cooking; somebody was calling a dog; an upstairs window lit up gold.

"I could live in a place like this," Ginny said, to the lane in general.

Harry looked at the cottages with their lights coming on, and felt the future arrive in him quietly, the way the village had arrived around the memorial: something that had been there all along, waiting for him to touch the gate.

"Someday," he said, and heard, for once, no flinch anywhere in it, "when the list is short. A place like this. Garden big enough for the gnomes your mum exiles. Kitchen the right size — small, I mean. Kitchens should be small enough that you bump into people." He considered. "A clock."

"With hands on it," said Ginny.

"With hands on it."

She looked down at him from the gate, hair on fire in the last of the light, and her voice when it came had no joke anywhere in it, which was how he knew to stand very still. "I'm holding you to all of that, you understand. The village, the gnomes, the stupid small kitchen. I have a very long memory and I am told I'm relentless."

"Whatever the house is," Harry said, "it's you. You know that part, right? It's been you for —" he gave up counting honestly — "longer than I noticed."

"Knew before you did," said Ginny. "I've been planning this since I was ten. You're the only one here acting surprised." And she held the gate shut against him with one foot until the toll was paid, and paid back, and paid again with interest — and from up the path came Ron's voice, deeply weary, informing Hermione that he was never showing Harry another door of any kind as long as he lived.

They walked up the lane in the long gold light, the four of them, past the square where two stone parents sat with a stone baby and watched them go. The village had been the place where everything was taken from him. They walked out of it in the smell of cut grass and other people's dinners, and it was just a place where people lived — which was, Harry thought, with Ginny's hand in his and the gate creaking shut behind them, exactly what it had cost everything to make it.

Chapter XIII

Ordinary Time

It took Harry most of July to put a name to the strange thing that was happening to him, and in the end it was Hermione — back from Australia with sunburn across her nose and her parents' letters arriving twice a week — who named it for him. They were shelling peas on the back step of the Burrow; Mrs. Weasley had discovered that grief went easier with tasks, and ran the household that summer like a benevolent work camp. Harry said that everything felt unfamiliar lately. Even the good things. Especially the good things.

"Because they're finally ours," said Hermione, not looking up from the peas. "Think about it. Every good thing any of us has ever had came with a clock running on it. We all fell for each other in wartime, Harry. There was always a third person in the room, and the third person was the war. Now it's just us — and nobody ever taught us what to do with just us." She frowned at a pod with tremendous seriousness. "I find it terrifying," she added, "and I would not trade it for anything."

So they taught themselves, that summer, the one subject that had never been on any timetable: ordinary time.

Harry took Ginny to Muggle London for their first real date, because it was the only place on earth where neither of their names meant anything at all, and the anonymity was the most extravagant gift he could think of to give her. He bought tickets to a Muggle cinema mostly to see what she would do, and what she did was watch the audience as much as the screen, delighted, and lean over in the dark to whisper, "It's a Pensieve. They built themselves a Pensieve for made-up memories, because nobody handed them the real kind. Harry. I love them." On the way home she rode the Underground escalators twice for pure pleasure, and Harry laughed on a public street with his head back, unrecognised, unremarkable, and the photograph nobody took of that moment was his favourite of the entire year.

They learned each other in peacetime, which turned out to be a different subject from loving each other in wartime — slower, and made of absurdly small things. He learned that she took her tea stewed to tar like Bill; that she talked Quidditch tactics in her sleep; that she could not cook so much as toast and was magnificently unrepentant about it. She learned that he could cook — really cook, a Dursley inheritance he was years from being able to laugh about — and hummed while he did it without knowing; that he kept a running list in his head of what people liked, and quietly stocked the world accordingly, her favourite quill, Ron's favourite sweets, Hermione's preferred ink, without ever once mentioning it; and that he was hopeless, genuinely hopeless, at being given anything, going stiff and wary as a stray cat, so that loving him was partly a long patient lesson that affection was not a debt being run up against him.

"You flinch when I'm nice to you," she told him on the orchard wall one evening, conversational and terrible. "Did you know that? You flinch politely. Nicest flinch I've ever seen. We're going to fix it. I'm going to be good to you until you stop bracing, Potter, if it takes me sixty years."

"Is that a threat?"

"It's a training plan. I'm extremely goal-oriented. Ask anyone."

The scout's letter had come in early July: the Holyhead Harpies, who had been watching the red-haired Chaser who tore up the D.A. reunion match, inviting Miss Weasley to trials — following, the letter said pointedly, the completion of her N.E.W.T. year, which this club regards as non-negotiable in respect of a generation whose education has been stolen from once already. So Ginny was going back to Hogwarts. Hermione was going back too — "the laws are rotten, Harry; the werewolf statutes, the elf statutes, the Muggle-born Registration files still sitting in a Ministry basement — and if I'm going to tear it all out by the roots from the inside, I need every N.E.W.T. they will let me sit" — and so, on a blanket in the orchard with the lamps of the Burrow coming on, Harry and Ginny negotiated the year apart like the veterans they were.

"Say it," said Ginny. "Whatever it is you're not saying. New rule, remember."

"It's a year," Harry said. "We only just — Ginny, it's a year."

"It's thirty-eight weeks, broken up by Hogsmeade weekends, Christmas, and Easter," said Ginny, who had counted. "Harry. We did eleven months with no letters, no map, and no idea whether the other one was alive — while broken up, I'd remind you, which was your idea and the worst one you have ever had — and we kept anyway. We kept without even being allowed to say so. Now I get parchment, and owls, and a boyfriend with a Ministry salary who can stand in Hogsmeade High Street holding both my hands in front of God and Rita Skeeter." She lay back and considered the first stars with an air of immense satisfaction. "This isn't the hard year. We've already had the hard year. This is the year we get to write it all down."

Kingsley came to the Burrow himself in July — as the Minister this time, with apologies to Mrs. Weasley for the formality — and laid it out plainly at the kitchen table: the Auror Office had lost a third of its people in the war, and half of what remained had spent a year taking orders from Yaxley; Gawain Robards was rebuilding it from the studs; the N.E.W.T. requirement was waived for veterans of the battle who could pass the assessments. "I'd like you both. Not as trophies — Robards will work you like dray horses, and the old guard will resent every shortcut you'll be perceived to have taken. I'd like you because you spent a year successfully hunting the most protected Dark wizard in history, and because the list of his followers still at large is long, and some of them have nothing left to lose."

Mrs. Weasley had gone very still at the sink. Harry watched Ron watch his mother's back, and think it all the way through, and answer for himself.

"I'm in," Ron said. "On one condition, which I'm setting now, in front of witnesses. When the list gets short — and it will — I'm out, and I go and work with George. Fred's half of that shop has to come from somewhere, and it isn't coming from a stranger." His ears had gone red, but his voice never wavered. "A few years catching the worst people in Britain. Then I make people laugh for a living. That's the whole plan, start to finish."

"That," said Kingsley gravely, "is the most sensible career trajectory I have heard since taking office." And at the sink Mrs. Weasley wiped her eyes with her wrist and announced that nobody was joining anything until they had eaten seconds.

Ron, meanwhile, planned his and Hermione's first official date with the thoroughness of a military campaign and the judgement of a Niffler. He booked the most fashionable wizarding restaurant in Britain — a place with floating candles, a wine list in French, and, it transpired, a maître d' who tipped off the Prophet — and the evening lasted eleven minutes past the arrival of the photographers, at which point Ron stood up, dropped a Galleon on the table for the bread, said, "Wrong venue, my mistake, c'mon," and took her out through the kitchens.

They ended up on the Embankment with fish and chips out of the paper, watching the Muggle boats go down the Thames; and Hermione — shoes off, feet tucked under her on a public bench, stealing his chips, eighteen years old and free — informed him that this had been, for the record, the correct restaurant all along, and that next time they should simply start here.

"Noted," said Ron. "I panicked. I read this book — don't laugh — Twelve Fail-Safe Ways to Charm Witches —"

"You did not —"

"Chapter four says take her somewhere impressive! Chapter four has clearly never met you. You're impressed by footnotes."

They had their first proper peacetime row in August, over something so gloriously unimportant that neither of them could afterward agree what it had been — Hermione maintained it was the correct organisation of George's stockroom; Ron maintained, to his dying day, that it was the Chudley Cannons' prospects, about which she was wrong — and halfway through it, in full flight, both of them started laughing at the same instant and could not stop, and had to sit down on the stockroom floor among the Skiving Snackboxes, wheezing.

"We used to row like the world was ending," Ron said, wiping his eyes, "because it was, basically, all the time. You know what I've worked out? We fought because we were scared. All those years. Now we just fight because you're wrong about the Cannons."

"I am never wrong about the Cannons. They are statistically the worst team in the league."

"See, that's the thing about love," Ron told the stockroom ceiling, with the serenity of a philosopher. "You can know all that, and stay anyway."

Chapter XIV

Hands on the Clock

Andromeda took an afternoon off in late July — her first since May, arranged for her by a conspiracy consisting of Mrs. Weasley, a tearoom in Diagon Alley, and Molly's flat refusal to accept no — and so it was that Harry and Ginny found themselves alone in Grimmauld Place with one three-month-old Metamorphmagus and a confidence that lasted, by Ginny's later sworn testimony, eleven minutes.

Teddy opened proceedings charmingly, turning his hair to match whatever held his attention: Ginny-red, then kitchen-copper, then — fixing on his godfather with an expression of delighted recognition — jet black and deliberately untidy, which made Ginny laugh so hard she had to sit down on the floor. He then sneezed away both of his eyebrows, Summoned Ginny's earring from clean across the room with a shriek of triumph (it was recovered, eventually, from inside his left sock), and produced, during a nappy change that Harry afterward declined to discuss with anyone, what Ginny could only describe as weather.

By the time Kreacher appeared, the kitchen looked like the site of a small localised duel, and Teddy was wearing one sock, no eyebrows, and Harry's glasses.

"Master is holding the young master," Kreacher observed, "upside-down."

"He likes it!"

"He is a Black," said Kreacher, with sudden dark pride, taking possession of the baby with the offhand competence of a hundred and fifty years, "and the Blacks are born contrary. Kreacher raised Master Regulus from this size. Master will fetch the milk — warm, not hot — and Master's Weasley will stop laughing at Master, as it does not help."

("Master's Weasley," said Ginny, wiping her eyes, "is going on my Chocolate Frog card. I want it in the first sentence.")

Andromeda returned at six to find the kitchen restored, dinner underway, and all three of them asleep in the drawing-room armchair in a heap — godfather, baby, and Ginny — with Teddy's hair gone jet black on one side and Weasley red on the other; and she stood in the doorway for some time, looking at the only photograph nobody took that summer that mattered more than all the ones they did, before she could bring herself to wake them.

The letter came two days before Harry's birthday, by a baffled-looking post-office owl, addressed in a large careful hand Harry had last seen scrawling DUDLEY'S — KEEP OUT across a bedroom door.

Harry —

The wizard who moved us back said it's over and you won. He had a purple top hat. Mum cried at the kitchen table for about an hour. Don't tell her I told you. Dad didn't say anything but he bought a paper next morning, which he never does, and you were in the normal papers, did you know? One line. Gas explosion thing. We knew what it meant.

Is it over for real? Do we still need the protection people? Because Hestia's all right, but the other one keeps testing me. He says it's CONSTANT VIGILANCE. I think he just doesn't rate Smeltings.

Anyway. Glad you're not dead. I mean it. If you're ever in Surrey there's a normal coffee place by the station. No wobbling teacups or anything.

— D

P.S. Happy birthday for Thursday. I had to ask Hestia when it was. Which is bad, because it means we never asked. Sixteen years and we never once asked.

Enclosed was a birthday card — Smeltings purple, a Muggle photograph of a boxing glove on the front, blank inside but for a large D — and Harry sat looking at the first birthday card ever to make the journey from Privet Drive for a long time, while Ginny read the letter over his shoulder with her chin resting on his head.

"You're going to go to the normal coffee place by the station," she said.

"Yeah," said Harry, who had not known it until she said it. "I am, actually."

"Good. Bring him a Chocolate Frog card when they're out. Tell him the picture leaving the frame is supposed to happen this time."

Harry turned eighteen on the last day of July and counted, privately, the birthday cakes of his life: a squashed chocolate one at eleven, delivered by a giant in a hut on a rock in a storm, with HAPPEE BIRTHDAE spelled wrong and meant entirely; and Mrs. Weasley's every year since, taller annually — this year a frankly architectural construction with a golden Snitch on top that actually flew. Ginny's present came last, on the orchard wall, after the others: a small flat parcel, and inside it a photograph in a plain frame — a Muggle photograph, taken by Dennis Creevey on his brother's camera, of Harry and Ginny by the paddock, her head thrown back mid-laugh, his face unguarded, the two of them going absolutely nowhere.

"It doesn't move," Harry said.

"It doesn't move," Ginny agreed. "Wizard photographs move because the moment keeps going — and that's lovely, but everything in our lives has kept going, Harry. Usually at a dead sprint. Usually with something chasing it. I wanted you to have one moment that just — stays." She knocked her shoulder against his. "Happy birthday. You flinched. We're working on it."

He put it on the mantelpiece at Grimmauld Place, where there was nothing else at all — the first thing he had ever chosen for a mantel of his own.

The next piece of the summer arrived in the second week of August, when Ginny frogmarched Harry into Eeylops Owl Emporium on the grounds — delivered in the street, with bystanders — that the war hero responsible for half the Ministry's owl traffic had spent fifteen months borrowing other people's birds.

"I'm not replacing her," Harry said, at the door, with his feet planted.

"Correct," said Ginny. "You're not. Hedwig isn't replaceable. She was the first creature that was ever yours and she died flying with you, and when we're ninety you'll still go quiet sometimes in October, and I'll still know why. Nobody is replacing her. But you promised me a letter every week, Potter, and I am not entrusting my private correspondence to the Ministry owl pool, who read everything — don't argue, Dad's confirmed it. This is not a replacement. It's a colleague."

The colleague chose them, in the manner of owls: a small tawny with opinions, who ignored Harry's outstretched arm entirely, landed on Ginny's shoulder, examined Harry for a long moment with eyes like two cups of strong tea, and then stepped, deliberately, across onto him — the full transfer of allegiance conducted, witnessed, and notarised inside a minute. He then bit Ron, who had come along to advise, in a manner the shopkeeper described as "affectionate."

"He needs a name," said Harry, out on the street, the cage swinging.

"He's got one," said Ginny. "Tuesday."

"...Why Tuesday?"

"Because that's the day his letters will come." She said it like a fixture in the calendar of the universe — already arranged, unalterable, filed somewhere official. "Every Tuesday, wherever I am, whatever's happened, for the rest of our lives. So that when you hear him at the window, you'll already know it's me before you look up." She scratched the owl under the chin; Tuesday accepted this as tribute. "First rule of being loved, Potter — we've been over this. It keeps a schedule. It shows up on a day."

And the last piece came in the third week of August, when Mr. Weasley called the whole house into the kitchen after supper with the particular shuffling casualness he reserved for things that mattered more than anything else.

He had been out in the shed for three evenings running. They had all assumed it was a Muggle radio.

It was the clock. The family clock — the one with the nine golden hands that had hung in the kitchen telling the truth through two wars — except that there were no longer nine hands on it. There were ten. Two new ones, hand-cut and hand-engraved, the workmanship a little homelier than the originals and the lettering, if anything, a little prouder: HARRY, and HERMIONE.

"Should have done it years ago," said Mr. Weasley, into the sudden ringing silence, polishing his glasses for something to do with his hands. "Years ago. The clock's been wrong for years, is the truth of it. It's meant to show the whole family — that's the entire point of the magic, it doesn't work properly otherwise — and I kept, well. There was a war on. There isn't, now." He fitted the two hands with a tap of his wand, and they trembled, and caught, and swung — clean across the face, neither of them hesitating for an instant — to home, where eight others already pointed; and from the mantelpiece, Fred's hand stood watch over the whole arrangement; and Hermione made a sound into Ron's shoulder that she would be denying for years, and Mrs. Weasley said "ARTHUR" in a voice that obliged her to sit down, and Harry looked at his own name keeping time on the Weasleys' kitchen wall and could not have produced one word under torture.

"Right," said Mr. Weasley, very pink, hanging it back on its nail. "That's that, then. Who wants pudding?"

And late that night, out on the Burrow's back step, with the gnomes scuffling in the hedge and Ginny's head on his shoulder, Harry asked the question that had been sitting in him all summer.

"Is this what it's like for everyone? All the time? Just — days?"

"Mostly they complain it's boring," said Ginny.

"Imagine," said Harry, with his whole heart, and over the orchard the stars came out above a world with nothing chasing it.

Chapter XV

Diagon Alley

It happened on the last Saturday of August, which was also the day half of wizarding Britain was in Diagon Alley buying school supplies, which was, of course, the point.

They came out of Knockturn Alley at ten past noon: Thorfinn Rowle, huge and blond and eight weeks past sane; Selwyn; and two Snatchers with nothing left to lose, which Kingsley had warned was the most dangerous kind of wizard left in the country. They were not there with a plan. They were there because the Dark Lord's world had ended and taken theirs with it, and because the Prophet had printed that Harry Potter would be at the reopening-month celebrations at Weasleys' Wizard Wheezes, and because if everything was already over, then they were going to end loudly.

The first curse took the front window out of Slug & Jiggers and the street dissolved into screaming.

Afterward — in the Prophet, in the Auror Office report, in the testimony of forty witnesses — people kept reaching for the same word, and the word was calm. The survivors of that year were many things when wands came out: brave, fast, ferocious. Harry Potter, stepping out of the joke shop into a street full of panicking families with a Death Eater's curse already splashing off the Shield Charm he'd thrown over a sweet stall, was calm — the dense, transferable calm of someone who has been here before, in worse company, with less help, and knows precisely what happens next, in what order.

"Ron — left side, shield the shopfronts, keep the queue inside George's. Ginny — kids into Flourish and Blotts, ward the door behind you, nothing through it but me. Neville, with me, take the right wall and watch the rooftops." He didn't shout it. He said it the way you'd read a shopping list, walking forward into the open street, and it was somehow more frightening and more steadying than any shout: the four of them moved like a hand closing.

His first spell wasn't aimed at anyone. He swept his wand the length of the Alley and cast the Anti-Disapparition Jinx over the whole block — nobody leaves — and Harry felt rather than saw the two Snatchers understand, in that moment, exactly how their afternoon was going to go.

Rowle's Killing Curse came at him green and screaming and Harry didn't block it, because you can't; he stepped, exactly enough, the economical sidestep of a man who had been ducking that particular curse since before he could talk, and his answering Disarmer took Rowle's wand at the wrist while the big man was still mid-syllable in his second spell. Selwyn got a Shield up; Harry's next spell wasn't at Selwyn, it was at the cobblestones under him, which rippled like a shaken rug and put him on his back, and the Incarcerous arrived before he did.

The younger Snatcher bolted. Neville took him at twenty paces with a Full Body-Bind that would be discussed admiringly in Auror training for years.

The older Snatcher grabbed a child.

She was perhaps nine, white-faced, a Flourish and Blotts bag still hooked over one arm, and the Snatcher hauled her against his chest with his wand at her throat and screamed something about safe passage, and the whole Alley went silent, and Ginny, at the door of the bookshop, went still as stone.

Harry lowered his wand.

He walked forward slowly, both hands easy, talking in a low, level voice — look at me, that's it, you don't want her, she's heavy and she's slowing you down, what you want is a way out, so look at me, I'm the way out, I'm worth more than the whole rest of the street — and the Snatcher, sweating, tracked him, exactly as he was meant to, and never saw Harry's wand hand begin to move because Harry had learned wandwork from duelling Death Eaters in the dark and there was nothing in the world faster than he was across the last six feet of a fight. The Levicorpus took the Snatcher up by the ankle; the child dropped eighteen inches into the cushion of air Harry had laid under her with his off hand before anyone else in the street had registered the first spell; the Disarmer and the ropes were a formality.

And then — this was the part the witnesses told and retold, more than any duel — Harry Potter knelt on the cobbles of Diagon Alley with his back to four trussed Dark wizards as though they had ceased to exist, which functionally they had, and asked the nine-year-old her name, and told her she had been brilliantly brave, and walked her by the hand to her sobbing mother, and only then looked up to find Ginny across the wrecked street, and the look they exchanged was its own entire conversation: all right? — all right.

Gawain Robards arrived four minutes later with eight Aurors to find the threat neutralised, the casualties nil, the street already being repaired by volunteers, and the four most wanted men left in Britain hanging — in one case literally — outside a joke shop, beneath a window display that George had, with great presence of mind, re-enchanted so that the WONDER WITCH products appeared to be pointing and laughing.

Robards looked at the prisoners. He looked at Harry, four days from his first day of training. He looked, slowly, around the street at forty witnesses bursting to testify, and back at Harry with the expression of a man revising a lesson plan.

"Cadet Potter," he said at last. "Anything to report?"

"Quiet morning, sir," said Harry, "until it wasn't," and from the doorway of the shop came the unmistakable sound of George Weasley deciding the mourning period was over and laughing until he had to hold on to the till.

That night the nightmares were bad — the child's face kept becoming Ginny's, becoming Colin's — and that was the week Harry finally used Andromeda's card, which is in the file at St. Mungo's to this day, and is nobody's business but his.

Chapter XVI

The Other Side of the Door

Harry stayed at the Burrow the night after the Diagon Alley attack, in Bill's old room, and woke at two in the morning from the dream in which the child's face kept becoming other faces, and lay still for a while, listening to the house.

The Burrow at night was a creature of noises — the ghoul's pipes, the stairs settling, the garden conducting its gnome politics — and he had learned its whole vocabulary that summer, which was how he knew, passing Ginny's door on his way down for water, that something was wrong before he could have said what. He stood on the dark landing with the glass in his hand, listening, and then he understood. It was the door. There was no sound behind it at all. Not breathing; not bedsprings; not the muttered Quidditch tactics she ran in her sleep.

Nothing. A nothing with edges. Held breath.

You learn to hear silencing charms, she had told him once, at Hogwarts, under the Carrows — and Harry stood outside the second silenced door of that long year, on the other side of it this time, and felt his heart turn over.

He knocked; the charm ate it. So he did what she had done in May, and ended the spell, and said her name into the dark — low, and level, the way you do — and there was a long pause, and then her voice came, quick and bright and false as a fairground.

"Can't sleep? I'm fine. Go back to bed, you've got training."

Harry opened the door.

She was sitting up against the headboard with her knees drawn in and her wand still in her hand from the Muffliato, and the moonlight did to her face what it had done to his scar in May, which was show it plainly. She had been crying for a long time with the doors of herself shut — the way you learn to cry in a year when being heard gets other people hurt.

"Better this week," Harry said quietly, from the doorway. "Hardly anything now. Honestly."

Ginny gave a wet, defeated laugh. "Using my own ammunition. Filthy tactics, Potter."

"Learned them from the best Chaser in the family." He crossed the room and sat where she had sat — in May, in July, every single time — and put his palm out flat on the quilt, and after a moment she took it. "New rule," he said. "You'll know it; you wrote it. You don't have to be all right with me. You just have to be honest."

It came slowly, and then all at once, the way water comes through a wall.

The girl in the Alley first. "I froze, Harry. I had my wand on him and the shot was there and I froze — because for half a second she wasn't a stranger. She had Aggie Sloper's face. Aggie was a first-year last year. The Carrows kept first-years chained in the dungeons when the older students stepped out of line — Cruciatus practice for the seventh-years, on whoever they'd caught — and we ran the D.A. out of the Room of Requirement, and every operation I planned, I was choosing. Me. Sixteen. Choosing whose detention it would be if it went wrong; who could take it; who'd break. And I got good at it. That's the part, Harry — I got good at that arithmetic. And I still see their faces in the dungeon light, and yesterday one of them had a wand at Aggie's throat except it wasn't Aggie, and the famous Harry Potter handled it while his girlfriend stood in a doorway like a statue —"

"Stop." He said it the way she said it. "Neville told me about last year. Properly told me, at the castle, over the greenhouses. Do you know what he calls you? The general. He says the D.A. survived the Carrows because you never once spent anyone you couldn't afford, and that the most remarkable thing that happened all year is that everybody came home. And yesterday you put a bookshop between thirty children and four Dark wizards and warded the door from the inside. The statue in the doorway is the reason there was no one inside Flourish and Blotts for that man to take. Your arithmetic held, Ginny. It has never once not held."

She cried then, properly, against his shoulder, and somewhere in the middle of it the rest of it came out — the thing underneath the thing, in pieces. "— and the night of the battle, when Hagrid carried you out — I was screaming, you know that part — and an hour later, Fred. And Harry, I couldn't find the grief. It was queued. There was so much terror ahead of it in the line, and all of it was spent on you, and my brother was lying dead in the Great Hall and what I felt was not him too — like an afterthought, like a second thing — and what kind of sister —"

"The kind who'd had her heart in two places for years and lost half of everything in one hour," said Harry, into her hair. "Grief queues, Ginny. Mine's been queued since I was one year old; I'm still being handed things from the back of the line. It doesn't mean you loved him an afterthought's worth. It means that night overdrew the whole account, and you've been quietly paying it down ever since, every day, alone, behind a silenced door — because heaven forbid the people who love you should have to carry you too." He felt her go very still against his shoulder, hearing it. "Yeah," he said. "It sounds exactly that stupid from the outside. Wish somebody had told me that in June."

"Somebody did tell you that in June," said Ginny, muffled. "She's brilliant. You should listen to her." And she laughed, and broke, and laughed again, in the manner of a person setting something heavy down one piece at a time.

They talked until the window went grey — about Aggie Sloper, who wrote to Ginny every fortnight from a farm in Wales and was getting a Kneazle for her birthday; about Fred, the good inventory of him, including the Bludger in the cellar and the why of it, which Harry was finally told, and swore on his wand to keep from Molly; about nothing; about all of it — and somewhere before dawn they fell asleep where they sat, on top of the covers, entirely dressed and entirely ruined, her head rising and falling on his chest, over the lightning-tree.

Mrs. Weasley found them at seven. She stood in the doorway of her daughter's room for a long moment, taking in the scene with the eye of a woman who had raised seven children and missed nothing whatsoever in thirty years; and Molly Weasley — who had duelled the most dangerous witch of the age into dust, and whose opinions on propriety predated several Ministers for Magic — looked at the two of them: at her daughter's face, slack and unguarded and at peace in a way it had not been all summer, and at the boy she had been putting extra food in front of since he was twelve. And she went back downstairs, and put two more rashers in the pan, and said nothing about it to anyone, ever — except once, years later, to Arthur, very early on the morning of a wedding.

Chapter XVII

The Order of Merlin

The ceremony was held on the first of September's eve, in the Ministry Atrium, where the Fountain of Magical Brethren — and the obscene black statue that had briefly replaced it — was gone, and in its place stood a plain monument of white Hogwarts stone bearing fifty names, with room left, pointedly, for none.

Kingsley had fought the planning committee to a standstill on every point that mattered. There were no banners with Harry's face on them. The press were admitted but penned. And the dead came first — "In this Ministry," Kingsley said from the podium, in the voice that had read the names on Potterwatch, "the dead will always come first, because for one year, in this building, they came last."

Order of Merlin, First Class: Fred Weasley. Molly and Arthur went up together, and Molly held the medal in both hands all evening and did not put it down once, and somewhere near the back George said, wetly and audibly, "First Class. He'd be unbearable," and the laugh that went round was the kind that waters the eyes.

Order of Merlin, First Class: Remus John Lupin; Nymphadora Tonks Lupin — "and she'd thank you not to read the first part twice," said Kingsley, off-script, to a ripple of broken laughter from everyone who'd known her. Andromeda carried Teddy up to receive both. Teddy, four months old, seized his mother's medal in one fat fist and refused absolutely to surrender it, and his hair, in front of two thousand witnesses and every photographer in Britain, turned exactly the gold of the ribbon — and that was the picture on every front page in the wizarding world the next morning, under headlines about the future, and not one editor chose the picture of Harry instead, which Harry considered the first unambiguous evidence that the world had actually changed.

Order of Merlin, First Class: Severus Snape. Kingsley read the citation himself — for seventeen years of intelligence work at continuous risk of death, for the protection of the students of Hogwarts, and for fidelity beyond proof — into a silence with history in it, a hall full of people renegotiating their memories. It was Harry who stood up first. He stood and applauded into that silence, alone, for an entire honest five seconds — and then McGonagall stood, and then the staff table together, and then the D.A., and then it took the Atrium like weather, and up at Hogwarts that night, the portraits being connected to portraits, a sallow man in a black frame heard about it before midnight and called it, on the record, "a tasteless display," and was observed by Albus Dumbledore to sit somewhat straighter for a week.

Colin Creevey's medal — First Class, for valour, in defiance of an evacuation order, aged sixteen — went to Dennis, who walked up with his brother's camera around his neck, and at the podium, instead of speaking, lifted it and took a photograph of the entire hall, the flash like a small sun. "He'd never have forgiven me," he said into the microphone, by way of explanation, and two thousand people laughed and wept at once. The photograph hangs in the rebuilt Gryffindor common room. Everyone in it is waving.

Then the living: the defenders of Hogwarts, Orders of Merlin in their classes, called name by name — the D.A. and the Order, the teachers, the house-elves of the Hogwarts kitchens (a precedent over which three Wizengamot members resigned and were, in Ron's assessment, no loss), Horace Slughorn looking frankly amazed at himself, Mrs. Longbottom in her best hat watching her grandson receive a First Class medal with an expression that finally, finally, contained no comparison to anyone. Neville crossed the stage the way he had crossed the courtyard in May — unhurried, certain, slightly amazed at himself somewhere underneath — and Kingsley, shaking his hand, departed from the script for the only time that evening: "The sword is in a case at Hogwarts, Mr. Longbottom. The Ministry has agreed it is on permanent loan to the school — but the school has agreed it is to be understood, by everyone, forever, whose hand it answered." And on the way back to his seat, passing his grandmother, Neville heard Augusta Longbottom say to the witch beside her, in the carrying voice of a woman who had never once needed a Sonorus, "His father's son, you know" — and there was nothing in it, for the first time in his whole life, but plain ferocious pride, no measuring, no but; and Neville Longbottom sat down holding his medal and looked at it for a long time. He got the second-loudest cheer of the night.

The loudest was short, because Harry had bargained it down personally. First Class, for Hermione Jean Granger — who heard her full name in a Ministry hall that had once tried her for theft of magic, and walked up with her chin high; for Ronald Bilius Weasley — who shook Kingsley's hand and could be lip-read by the entire front section saying "Bilius? Was that necessary?"; and for Harry James Potter, who at the podium declined the speech, took out a piece of parchment, and read, instead, the fifty names off the white stone, every one, into a silence you could have built on; and then said, "Everything else was all of us," and sat down.

He was, the Prophet sniffed the next day, no orator. The Prophet also printed all fifty names for the first time. Hermione framed that, and gave it to him for Christmas.

Chapter XVIII

Cards and King's Cross

The owl from the Chocolate Frog people arrived at the Burrow two days before the ceremony, with three contracts and a tin of free samples, and Ron's reaction was audible from the orchard.

"WE'RE GOING TO BE ON CARDS."
