"Ron —"

"CARDS, Harry. Do you understand — Dumbledore is on a card. AGRIPPA is on a card. I have wanted — since I was five, mate, since before I knew you existed —" He stopped, vibrating, holding the parchment like scripture. "I used to have six hundred of them. I never had an Agrippa. And now —" his voice went hushed and holy — "kids are going to swap me."

"You're going to be the rare one," said Ginny, reading over his shoulder. "It says collectors' first edition. Some poor child is going to have eleven Harrys and no Ron and cry about it."

"Don't," said Ron, fanning himself with the contract. "I can't take it."

The cards came out the same week as the September Hogwarts Express, which meant Platform Nine and Three-Quarters on the first of September was full not only of owls and steam and trunks but of children — Muggle-born children boarding openly, their parents unafraid on the platform, a thing that had not been true twelve months before — clutching brand-new Famous Witches and Wizards cards, and it was there, in the crowd, that fame finally arrived in the only shape Harry had ever found it bearable.

A boy of eleven, first robes too long, planted himself in front of the trio with the absolute fearlessness of someone who has not yet learned that you don't, and held up a card. "You're him," he said, with rigour, comparing. The card-Harry, Harry had been mortified to discover, ducked shyly out of frame after about four seconds and had to be coaxed back; the photographer had sworn it captured his essence. "Mum! MUM! It's — look — will you sign him? I mean it? I mean you?"

"Only," said Harry gravely, "if you've got a Ron Weasley in there."

The boy riffled his stack, anguished. "I've got three Nevilles —"

"Keep the Nevilles," said Harry, signing, "he's the one who killed the snake. But you want to find a Weasley. Ask him about the chess game on the back of the card; it's all true; I was there." And as the boy tore off through the steam, evangelizing, Ron stood beside him on the platform with an expression Harry had seen only once before, in a mirror in an empty classroom, seven years ago: the look of a boy seeing his deepest, oldest wish, the one from before everything. I am on the card too.

"All right?" said Harry quietly.

"Best one," said Ron. "Out of all of it. Don't tell Hermione."

"Don't tell Hermione what?" said Hermione, appearing with her trunk and prefect-straight in her travelling clothes, and "Nothing," they both said, in the old harmony, and she looked between them and decided, visibly, to allow it.

The goodbyes were long, and they were not sad — that was the strange grace of that platform, that year. Hermione hugged Ron for an age and instructed him to write actual letters, with verbs in them; Ron promised that Auror Cadet Weasley's correspondence would be checked for spelling by a professional; Mrs. Weasley cried, and made Ginny promise to eat, and made Hermione promise to make Ginny promise. Teddy, on Harry's hip with his hair turquoise for the occasion, grabbed Hermione's card stack and had to be bought off with the spare Neville.

And then the whistle, and Ginny took hold of Harry's jacket with both hands in front of the entire platform, every photographer from the Prophet, and at least two hundred students, and kissed him in a way that settled, permanently and in print, any public questions about the matter.

"Christmas," she said, on the train step, hair like a banner. "Hogsmeade in October. Letters every week — and Harry. The dreams. When they're bad, you write me that night, not the cheerful version three days later. I'll know. I always know."

"Every week," Harry agreed. "And — yeah. All right. The real version."

"And try not to be too noble while I'm gone. If you must save the world again, leave some for me. I'm only seventeen. I'd like a career."

The train took the curve. He walked alongside it a few steps, the way people always had, the way his own children would one day make him swear not to and he would do anyway, and the last he saw was Ginny's hand at the window and Hermione's beside it.

Ron appeared at his shoulder, and they stood in the thinning steam: two eighteen-year-olds with Ministry identification in their pockets, training at eight sharp, a wanted list with four names already crossed off it, and a standing appointment on Thursdays that neither of them mentioned and neither of them missed.

"Funny," Ron said at last. "First time we ever stood on this platform, you were about to fly my dad's car into a tree."

"You were about to fly your dad's car into a tree. I was a passenger."

"That," said Ron with dignity, "is not how it reads on the back of my card." He clapped Harry on the shoulder, and they turned together toward the barrier, toward London, toward all of it — the trials and the rebuilding; the shop with one twin behind the till and two names over the door and a binding instruction to laugh once a day; the case of silver memories waiting in a cupboard at Grimmauld Place with an assignment from his professor attached; the letters in Ginny's slanting hand that would arrive every Tuesday without fail, and the harder, truer ones he would send back; the godson whose hair couldn't make up its mind; the world that fifty people had died one night in May to leave them — unfinished, unwritten, and theirs.

The scar on Harry's forehead had not pained him since the war ended, and never would again.

The one over his heart had never hurt at all. It lay there under his shirt, the bolt and all its branches, Ginny's by claim and proof by nature — proof that he had walked out, and proof that he had come back — and beneath it, steady as Tuesday letters, his heart went on doing the only thing it had ever really been asked to do.

It kept time.

✦  End of Book One  ✦

Chapter I

The First Tuesday

The Auror Office training annexe occupied a scarred stretch of Level Two that still smelled faintly of the previous administration — burnt parchment, and something underneath it that the cleaning crews had given up on — and at eight o'clock on the second of September, fourteen cadets stood in it at various interpretations of attention while Gawain Robards looked them over like a man pricing timber.

He was a big, grey, unhurried man with a quill behind his ear and the permanent squint of someone who had survived three Ministers and one occupation, and he let the silence run a long time before he spent any words on them.

"Some of you," he said at last, "fought at Hogwarts. Some of you fought other places that didn't get songs written about them. One of you" — he did not look at Harry, which somehow had the effect of everyone else not looking at Harry with great intensity — "has already done the single most difficult thing this office has attempted in my lifetime. Here is what that buys you in this room." He held up a thumb and forefinger, a quarter-inch apart. "May was a battle. This is a job. A battle forgives you almost anything if you win it. The job forgives nothing, ever, because the job is what happens on all the days when there is no song. You will forget what you were in May. I will teach you what to be on a wet Tuesday in March when the warrant is wrong and the wards are sour and nobody is watching. Dismissed to kit. Not you, Longbottom."

Harry, filing out, caught four seconds of it through the closing door: Neville, standing easy in a way the boy from the train never had, and Robards saying, "Your application essay was one sentence long, cadet," and Neville saying, "It only needed one, sir," and Robards looking at him for a moment and then down at the file, and the door clicked shut.

The sentence, Neville told them that night in the barracks, between Ron's bunk and his own, had not been complicated. There is one name still on your board, and I have been preparing to meet its owner since I was eight years old.

"Rabastan Lestrange," Ron said. Not a question.

"They never caught him in May." Neville said it the way he said things about plants — factual, patient, certain. "Bellatrix is dead, Rodolphus is in Azkaban, Crouch is gone. Rabastan was the fourth wand in my parents' sitting room. He's the last one, and he's out, and I find I'm not able to be a herbologist yet." He turned over and put out the lamp with a word. "After. Not yet."

And from the next bunk, in the dark, Harry heard Ron — who had set his own after at a kitchen table in July, in front of witnesses — say quietly, "Fair enough, mate. One list each."

Ginny2 September 1998

Potter —

Report, as ordered, so pay attention.

The castle is mended. I don't know what I expected — I watched them rebuild it all summer, I carried stones — but mended and whole are different things, and last night it was whole: every window lit, the ceiling doing a clear black sky with about a million stars, first-years coming in over the lake looking exactly as terrified as we did. There's a boy in Gryffindor this year who fell in the lake and got fished out by the squid and is therefore already a legend, which is the fastest anyone's ever done it. You took four years to be a legend. He managed it in four minutes, and he can't do a Summoning Charm either.

McGonagall opened the feast. She didn't do a speech, not really. She stood up and said the fifty names — just that, all of them, into the quiet — and then she said, "You are what they purchased. Be worth the price. Eat." And we ate. It was the right way to do it, Harry. Everyone cried about a normal amount and the puddings were aggressive.

Two things you should know about the seventh floor. One: the corridor is beautiful, you can't tell at all, except that you can tell exactly, because the new stones are half a shade lighter, like the castle wanted to remember where. Two — and I don't know who started it, a Hufflepuff probably, it has Hufflepuff energy — people touch the wall when they pass. Just lay a hand flat on it, walking by, the way you'd touch a teammate's shoulder. Nobody talks about it. Everybody does it. There's a patch going smooth, and it's exactly the shape of a hand, and George cannot ever be told or he'll come install a tollbooth.

Three things you should know about the new Defence professor, and I'm saving two of them for a proper letter, because they deserve room. The short version: his name is Wrenn, he's American, he was an Auror over there for thirty-one years, he is genuinely good — and he walked into his first N.E.W.T. lesson with a plan for a class of frightened children, and he is about to be the most confused man in Scotland.

Badge arrived at breakfast. Captain. Demelza nearly took my eye out celebrating, so morale is adequate. Trials Saturday, at which I will be merciless, because I have a club watching and a year to be worth it.

Speaking of being worth it: you'll have done your first day by the time Tuesday lands on your windowsill, if the barracks have a windowsill, which they'd better. Write me the real version. Not the Prophet version, not the cheerful version. The rule travels, Potter. It's the whole reason there's an owl.

Hold out your arm. He bites people he respects.

— G.

P.S. The wall thing. I touch it twice. Once for Fred. You can guess the other one, but I'll deny it.

Harry8 September 1998

Gin —

The real version, then.

The barracks have a windowsill. Tuesday landed on it at six in the morning looking extremely pleased with himself, bit Ron, accepted half my sausage as tribute, and has been sitting on the kit shelf all evening supervising me writing this with an expression I can only call editorial.

First week, honestly: my hand hurts from writing, my everything else hurts from everything else, and I'm happier than I know how to put down on paper, which is a strange sentence to write about a place where a man called Savage made me do the Disillusionment ladder forty times because my collarbone "flickered." (It did flicker. He was right. I'd have liked him to be less right.) The old guard is about what Kingsley warned us. Nobody says N.E.W.T.s out loud, but the word is around all the time, the way the smell of the old Ministry is around — Savage looks at Ron and Neville and me like we're something the Minister tracked in on his shoe. Fine. Robards told us to forget May, and I'm trying, because he's right too: this is harder than a battle. A battle has him in it, and your blood does the work. This is learning — wards, law, evidence, the right way to enter a room, the seventeen wrong ways, the paperwork that makes an arrest stick so the next Umbridge can't unstick it. Hermione would be top of the class inside a month and the worst part is everyone here knows it; her name came up twice in Magical Law and both times the instructor got a faraway look like he was checking his citations.

Neville told us about his application essay. I'll save it for the holidays — it's his to tell — but Gin, the hat tried to put him in Gryffindor for six years before he let it, and I'm only now seeing the whole shape of why it was so sure.

Nightmares: twice. (The rule travels.) Once the usual, once a new one with the Diagon girl in it. Wrote them down like the Healer says, felt stupid, slept after. So it works, and I'm doing it, and you can stop composing the letter you were already composing.

The wall — I had to put this letter down for a bit after that. Touch it once for me. You know which one for.

Tell the legend in first year the squid did the same for Dennis Creevey's brother once, and that it's the best-judging creature at the castle.

Train hard. Be merciless. The club's right to watch.

— H.

P.S. He does bite people he respects. The duty sergeant is now respected. There's an incident form. I have framed my copy.

MINISTRY OF MAGIC — AUROR OFFICE — INCIDENT REPORT No. 44-B
7 September 1998

Nature of incident: Avian.

Location: Cadet barracks, Level Two annexe, window three.

Reporting officer: Duty Sergeant T. Gimlet.

Account: At 06:02 an owl (tawny; small; bearing of a much larger owl) effected entry at window three and presented one leg, bearing correspondence, to Cadet Potter. Per Regulation 12 (Visiting Owls, Registration and Tagging of), the reporting officer approached the bird with the standard wing-tag. The bird looked at the wing-tag, looked at the reporting officer, and bit the reporting officer.

The reporting officer wishes it recorded that the bite was not unprovoked, in the sense that Regulation 12 was the provocation, and the bird had clearly read it.

Recommendation: that the owl ("Tuesday," prop. Cadet Potter) be granted standing barracks clearance, all hours, in the interests of departmental morale and the reporting officer's remaining fingers. Further recommendation, underlined twice in the original: nobody else touches the leg. The leg is for Cadet Potter.

Appended below, in the Head Auror's hand: Granted. Standing clearance, all hours, all sites. If the bird ever wants a wing-tag, I have no doubt it will requisition one. — G.R.

Chapter II

Cadet Potter

M.O.M.
Auror Office — Training Division
From: Snr. Auror M. Savage
To: Head Auror G. Robards
Re: Cadet intake — week two assessment
Date: 9 September 1998

Sir,

Per your request, candid evaluation of the September intake follows. The cohort is keen, half-trained by war, and bad in the precise ways war makes people bad: they clear rooms like the rooms are full of Death Eaters, they treat cover as a personal insult, and they Disarm first and document never.

Regarding the cadet you did not ask about by name. The boy is talented; nobody disputes the boy is talented. He is also nineteen — eighteen — I have checked twice and decline to check again. There is a reason this office has asked for N.E.W.T.s since before either of us held a wand, and the reason is not the potions paper. It is that the qualification proves a candidate can do a slow, boring, necessary thing for two years on schedule. Heroics we have. Heroics are what got half the old office killed. I will train him without fear or favour, but I wish it noted that I consider the waiver a political decision, and political decisions are how this department got Dawlish.

— M. Savage

M.O.M.
Auror Office
From: G. Robards
To: M. Savage
Re: Re: Cadet intake — week two assessment
Date: 9 September 1998

Noted, as is your custom, in writing, for the file, with copies.

Train him without fear or favour. Report back in eight weeks. I'll keep this memorandum somewhere safe in case either of us needs to eat it later.

— G.R.

The Concealment and Disguise practical, in week six, was a Savage production, and Savage productions were famous by then: he built scenarios the way other men built gaol cells, and the October exercise was his masterpiece — a mock village street in the training hall, a "fugitive" to be observed and taken, a dozen examiners watching from the gallery. Cadets went in pairs. Pairs came out, one after another, having pounced on the fugitive swiftly, cleanly, and — they were informed, to their bafflement — fatally: every pair had walked the same hundred yards of staged street, and not one of them had noticed the second wand, the watcher in the upper window covering the fugitive's back, who had "killed" each arresting officer the moment they moved.

Harry and Ron went last. They walked the street once. Then Harry stopped, in the middle of it, and said — not to Ron, but upward, to the gallery, in a conversational voice that carried — "The washing's wrong."

A pause, from above.

"It's been on the line all day, sir, and it isn't moving — there's a Stilling Charm on the whole yard so nothing flaps and spoils somebody's sightline. The fugitive's reading yesterday's Prophet with today's headline showing through from the back page, the pub sign's been silenced, and the only window in the street with the curtain hooked back two inches is the one with the angle on the postbox where you've put the dead-letter drop. You've staged it too clean. The street where somebody's waiting to kill you is the street where nothing's wrong — that's the tell, that's always been the tell; it's how we knew, all last year. May we do the exercise where the village is allowed to be a village? Because I'd rather train on that one."

There was a long silence from the gallery, of a texture the other cadets discussed for weeks. Then Savage's voice, dry as a wand in a holster: "Cadet Weasley. Does he do this often?"

"Constantly, sir," said Ron. "You get used to it. I'd take the window man into custody, though — Harry's been holding the street ten seconds and that's usually about the limit before something regrets it."

They took the window man into custody. The exercise was rebuilt over the weekend. The new version was discussed admiringly, alongside a certain Full Body-Bind, for years.

Ron20 October 1998

Hermione —

Verbs, as promised, professionally spell-checked (by me) (the professional).

Training's brilliant and horrible. Mostly brilliant. We did Duelling Assessment Thursday — Proudfoot runs it, he's the one with the moustache that enters the room before he does — and he had the bright idea of demonstrating "the gap between battlefield habits and professional standards" on an actual cadet, and the actual cadet he picked was, of course, Harry, because the old guard have been wanting a look at him with the gloves off since September the second.

Hermione, it lasted nine seconds. I counted. Everyone counted, separately, and we've compared, and the consensus is nine. Proudfoot's a proper duellist, all the footwork — and Harry just did the thing he does, where he's somehow already finished and waiting for the rest of the room to arrive. Disarm, bind, wand presented back to Proudfoot handle-first, "thank you, sir, very instructive" — and the WORST part, the part I'll treasure into old age, is he meant it. He wasn't being smart. He genuinely took notes after. Proudfoot stood there holding his returned wand looking like a man who's been handed his own hat by a hurricane.

Savage watched the whole thing from the door and didn't say a word, but I was nearest, and I saw the memo he was holding, and I saw him fold it in half and put it back in his pocket.

Mum's sent socks for the entire intake. The entire intake, Hermione. Fourteen cadets. There's a note says "for any cadet whose mother is too far away," which is all of them, which she knew. Susan Bones cried. Susan Bones fought at the battle and a pair of grey socks with one gold stripe finished her off completely. Tell your mum and dad the jumper situation will be assessed at Christmas and they should declare a size honestly, because Mum's asked, and resistance just gets you a bigger jumper.

Miss you like mad. Two and a half weeks to Hogsmeade leave. I've done the sums on it twice, which Harry says is rich, coming from me, re: a certain mirror. Long story. Eight years old. I'll tell you over butterbeer.

— R.

P.S. How's the legislation? Has Slughorn introduced you to "just a very few people you absolutely must meet" yet? Use him shamelessly. He'd be offended if you didn't.

Chapter III

Remedial Defence

Here is what going back actually looked like, for the ones who went back.

McGonagall had invented a whole new category for them — "the eighth years," twenty-odd students who should have finished in the year the school was stolen, returning now to sit the N.E.W.T.s they'd been robbed of. They kept their old dormitories, were excused from curfew on the grounds that you could not reasonably give a curfew to people with Orders of Merlin, and answered to exactly one authority figure, who was Hermione Granger, because McGonagall had taken one look at the cohort, invented the post of Head of the Eighth Years, and pinned a badge on her before the feast ended. ("It's not a real position," Hermione said, pink with pleasure, wearing it everywhere.) Every Thursday, two Mind Healers came up from St. Mungo's and kept hours in a sunny room off the hospital wing, and McGonagall announced it at the first breakfast in the plainest words anyone had ever heard her use: "Half this school fought a battle in these corridors. Talking to someone is not a weakness. It is maintenance. I go on Thursdays myself." Then she sat down and ate her porridge while the entire Great Hall absorbed that, and by October the Thursday room had a waiting list.

Ginny and Hermione shared three free periods a week and spent them together, by unspoken treaty — sometimes working, sometimes walking the rebuilt corridors getting used to the castle being whole, sometimes saying nothing at all in the way of people who had wintered together. They walked past the seventh-floor wall most days, and touched it, the way everyone did.

And into the middle of all this, on the first of September, had walked the new Defence Against the Dark Arts professor.

His name was Jonah Wrenn. He was sixty-three years old, built like a dock piling, and had spent thirty-one years as an Auror for MACUSA — the American Ministry — before retiring to fish in Maine, where McGonagall's letter had found him. (He showed it to the N.E.W.T. class once, the whole text of it: Professor Wrenn — My students have seen a great deal. I require someone who has seen more. — M. McGonagall. "Eleven words," he said. "I'd packed before I finished it.") He arrived with a syllabus built for what he expected to find: a school full of traumatized children who would need to be coaxed gently back toward defensive magic, starting with theory, building carefully toward a Patronus by June.

The first N.E.W.T. lesson is now part of school legend, and the legend is accurate, because Hermione was there taking notes.

"By the end of this year," Wrenn told them, pacing the front of the classroom in his long grey coat, "my goal is to have each of you producing a corporeal Patronus. It's the hardest charm I'll ever teach you. Most grown wizards never manage it, so nobody should feel —"

Ginny's silver horse came up the center aisle at a canter, circled him twice, and went out through the wall.

Wrenn stopped pacing. In the silence, Luna Lovegood raised her hand and asked, with genuine courtesy, whether he would like to see a hare, and produced one without waiting; Seamus's fox went up and over the desks; and within thirty seconds the classroom was full of silver light and patient teenage faces, and Professor Jonah Wrenn stood in the middle of his ruined lesson plan with his arms folded, looking from animal to animal like an accountant finding the books already balanced.

"Who taught you this," he said at last. It did not sound like a question. It sounded like a man requesting a name for the report.

"Harry Potter, sir," said half the room, in unison, without irony.

It became the running answer of the term. Nonverbal Shield Charms that Wrenn tested personally, discreetly, with his full strength, and could not break — who taught you that — "Harry Potter, sir." A fifth-year who disarmed a seventh-year in a sanctioned demonstration with a wand movement Wrenn had last seen used by a MACUSA duelling champion — where in God's name — "The D.A., sir. There are hand-me-down notes." He took to grading with his head in one hand. He was heard to remark, in the staff room, that awarding "Exceeds Expectations" felt insulting to a class that had exceeded his by the second Tuesday of September.

But here is the thing the school worked out about Jonah Wrenn before September was over, and the reason they came to love him: he was a genuinely good teacher, and good teachers adapt. The day he wheeled in a boggart for what should have been routine review, and a quiet seventh-year girl's boggart turned out to be nothing but a closed dormitory door — a door, closed, with edges of silence around it — Wrenn looked at it for two seconds, dismissed the boggart, put the kettle on the corner stove with his wand, and taught the rest of that lesson sitting on the front of his desk, talking about what Aurors in his old office did after the fights: who they talked to, what they wrote down, how the good ones got old. "Defence against the dark arts," he told them, "is mostly not wandwork. The wandwork you people have. We'll spend the year on the rest of it." And he rebuilt the entire syllabus around what they didn't know — magical law and the rules of engagement, dark-artifact handling, MACUSA forensic technique, the testimony that makes an arrest stick, and the spell you don't cast — and he put Ginny Weasley in charge of the practical duelling drills, on the grounds, entered formally in his lesson plans, that she ran them better than he did.

M.O.M.
Hogwarts School — Defence Against the Dark Arts
From: Prof. J. Wrenn
To: Headmistress M. McGonagall
Re: Syllabus — request for full revision
Date: 30 September 1998

Headmistress,

Formal request to discard the approved syllabus, attached, on the grounds that it has been overtaken by events, the events having occurred last May.

Supporting facts. One: I assigned the Patronus Charm as a June objective and it was demonstrated to me, corporeally, by a third of the class, before I had finished the sentence. Two: I have cross-checked my N.E.W.T. register against the Order of Merlin honours list, and there is substantial overlap. I am not too proud to state in writing that I require guidance, which is a sentence I last wrote in 1971.

Request permission to teach them the matters they are actually behind in: law, procedure, restraint, and what a peaceful year is for.

— J. Wrenn

M.O.M.
Office of the Headmistress
From: M. McGonagall
To: Prof. J. Wrenn
Re: Re: Syllabus
Date: 30 September 1998

Granted. That is precisely why I hired a man who would notice.

— M.M.

The Quidditch trials were held the first Saturday of September, and two hundred students turned up, which was a problem, because Gryffindor House contained nowhere near two hundred students; a good half of the crowd had come purely to watch Ginny Weasley fly, and she cleared the stands of spectators inside ten minutes with a megaphone and a tone the team would come to know extremely well.

What she actually had to work with, when the tourists were gone, was this. Demelza Robins, the only other survivor of the old side, match-fit and loyal to the bone. Jimmy Peakes and Ritchie Coote, the Beaters from Harry's championship team — but Quidditch had been cancelled under the Carrows, so they had not swung a bat at a moving target in sixteen months, and it showed. And then nothing. No Keeper, no Seeker, no third Chaser: a team to be built from raw timber.

She built it. Keeper went to Cora Pike, a silent fifth-year with astonishing reflexes and no confidence whatsoever, who saved four of five penalties at trial and then apologised for the fifth. Third Chaser went to Natalie McDonald, a fifth-year with soft hands and a hard head who had spent the Carrows year as one of Ginny's D.A. runners, and flew like she had something to repay. And Seeker — Seeker went to Josie Kirke, a third-year nobody had ever once seen fly, the younger sister of the Andrew Kirke who had beaten for the house in Harry's day, who turned up to trials on her brother's cast-off Cleansweep and flew it like the broom owed her money. She had been too young for the team before the war, and there had been no team during it; what there had been, it emerged, was an orchard at home, a hand-me-down broom, and four years of flying alone against imaginary internationals every evening until it got too dark to see. She caught the trial Snitch three times in forty minutes — the third time coming out of a dive so late that Demelza put her hands over her eyes and watched through her fingers — and when Ginny, signing the team sheet, asked her where on earth she had been, Josie shrugged and said, "In the orchard. There was a war on," which was the entire biography of half her generation, and won her the Seeker's spot and a permanent place in her captain's heart in the same four seconds.

Then Ginny pushed them, and October was brutal. Dawn sessions before lessons, the lake fog still on the pitch; bludger drills until Peakes and Coote got their timing back from wherever the Carrows year had buried it; penalty after penalty against Cora Pike, who flinched at praise more than at quaffles. Ginny drove them the way she drove herself, which was the problem, and it was Demelza — only Demelza could have — who finally said it to her face in the changing room: "They're not scared of losing, Gin. They're scared of disappointing you. It's not the same fear and it doesn't make anyone fly faster."

Ginny went away furious, and came back the next morning different, because she was her mother's daughter and she could hear a true thing through a wall. What she came back with, though she'd have hexed anyone who said it aloud, was Harry's method — the D.A. method, the one she'd watched at thirteen and fought under at sixteen: you make them feel brave first, and teach them second, because courage is the thing skill sticks to. She benched the drills for a week and made Cora Pike save penalties from her — from Ginny Weasley, full speed, no quarter — and roared with delight at every save until the girl stopped apologising; she put little Josie Kirke in front of the whole team and had her outfly her for the Snitch twice out of five, which was two more times than anyone outflew Ginny Weasley at anything; she told Natalie McDonald, publicly, that she flew like a Harpy, and watched it land like a knighthood.

By Hallowe'en they were a team. By the first match they were dangerous. The first match was Slytherin, in November, and what happened in that one made the Daily Prophet — but that news reached London by owl, the way all the best news did that year, and it belongs a little further on.

Chapter IV

Hogsmeade, By Permission

The first Hogsmeade weekend of November fell, by a coincidence in which Molly Weasley declined to believe and Gawain Robards declined to be questioned, on the same Saturday as the cadets' first leave; and so at ten in the morning, at the village end of the Hogwarts lane, in cold bright air that smelled of woodsmoke and the first frost, Harry Potter stood waiting at the gates of a place he had last entered through a portrait hole with a war on, and watched a crowd of students come down the lane, and picked the red head out of it from a quarter mile, the way you pick out a lit window.

She saw him from fifty yards, left the lane at a flat run, and kissed him — both fists in the collar of his coat, up on her toes, two months of letters cashed in at once — and Harry kissed her back with his hands in her hair and the cold bright morning going on around them entirely unattended. It lasted long enough to draw applause from a knot of third-years, whom Ginny, without fully detaching, dispersed with a look she had been developing all term for exactly such emergencies.

The village was mended the way the castle was mended: whole, and half a shade brighter where it had been broken. Honeydukes had reopened in June — there was a small plaque by the till for old Ambrosius Flume's nephew, who had hidden students in the cellar passage during the Carrows year and paid for it — and Ginny moved through the shop with the focused efficiency of a professional athlete supplying a dormitory, while Harry paid for it all over her objections on the grounds, he said, of back wages: "Six years of you watching me buy sweets for everyone else. I've done the sums." (He had not done the sums. Ron had done the sums, in September, unprompted, with a quill, and presented them to Harry as evidence in the matter of Weasley v. the Density of His Best Friend, 1992–1997.)

They did not go to Madam Puddifoot's. Ginny proposed it with a straight face; Harry got as far as the curtained window, read the sign — UNDER NEW MANAGEMENT, SOMEHOW — and they stood on the pavement laughing like idiots until it became necessary to move along for the dignity of the Auror Office. The Three Broomsticks gave them an hour, and butterbeer, and Madam Rosmerta's fierce gladness; and half the bar found a reason to walk past the table, and nobody bothered them, because that was the contract the wizarding world was slowly learning to honour: look, be glad, leave them be.

The one exception arrived when they were a butterbeer deep: a tall, weathered wizard in a long grey coat, sixty-odd, with a jaw like a dock piling and the unmistakable bearing of law enforcement, who stopped at their table, looked down at Harry, and said, in a flat American drawl:

"Mr. Potter. Jonah Wrenn. Defence Against the Dark Arts. I'd like to lodge a complaint."

"Professor," said Ginny, delighted, not even pretending to hide it. "You found him."

Wrenn pulled out a chair and sat, uninvited, in the manner of Aurors on every continent. "Thirty-one years with MACUSA's Auror office, Mr. Potter. I retired. I was fishing. Your Headmistress wrote me eleven words and I crossed an ocean to teach Defence to a school of traumatized children, and I built a syllabus accordingly — gentle, gradual, theory first. First lesson, I assign the Patronus Charm as the goal for June." He leaned forward. "This young woman's horse was through my classroom wall before I finished the sentence. A girl with radish earrings produced a hare and asked if it could do tricks. I have fifth-years holding nonverbal Shields that I could not break — and I tried, son, discreetly, with everything I had, and I could not break them. And every single time I ask this school 'who taught you that,' the room says your name like it's the answer to all possible questions. So here is my complaint." He sat back. "I was hired to teach Defence to the only year-group in recorded history that doesn't need it, because a teenager with no training, no budget, and a price on his head ran the most effective Defence programme this castle has ever seen, out of a magic room, in secret, twice, the second time under actual torturers. I'd like your notes. I am not joking about the notes."

Harry had gone steadily redder throughout this. "It wasn't — teaching, really, it was just — everyone was going to have to fight, so —"

"Kid." Wrenn held up one weathered hand. "I trained Auror cadets for ten years. You can make a person able to fight in six months; that's a syllabus. What you did is make them want to stand next to each other while they fought. That part has no syllabus. I've seen it maybe twice in my life, and both other times the man had grey hair." He produced a napkin and a stub of pencil. "One professional question, and then I'll buy the round and leave you to your Saturday. The Patronus. Thirteen-year-olds. How."

Harry thought about it properly, because the man had asked it properly. "Happy memory first," he said. "Wand later. Get them living inside the memory before they ever touch the incantation — most people teach it backwards, and then the charm fails because there's nothing behind it. And let them fail safe. Nobody casts anything worth casting while they're afraid of looking stupid."

Wrenn wrote it on the napkin, word for word, folded it into his breast pocket like evidence, and stood. "Miss Weasley runs my N.E.W.T. duelling drills better than I ran them at the academy, and Miss Granger has corrected the American statute citations in the margins of my own textbook, and I have mailed the margins to MACUSA." He put coins on the table for the round. "Last thing, and then I'm saying it to McGonagall as well, so it's on the record on two continents: you should teach, Mr. Potter. Properly. A classroom, a register, the whole dull glory of it. Because the plain truth about this school — and everyone in it knows it, and nobody has said it to your face — is that the best Defence Against the Dark Arts professor Hogwarts ever produced has never once actually held the position. When you're done catching the ones that need catching, the post stays open. It's traditionally cursed. You've got prior experience." And he was gone through the crowd, leaving Harry with his ears scarlet and Ginny wearing the expression of a woman whose two favourite worlds had just shaken hands.

And then, because Harry had asked for it to be the two of them, they walked out past the last houses to the hill above the Shrieking Shack.

The fence was new. The Shack was not — the Ministry had wanted to restore it, and then to monument it, and McGonagall had ruled, in a letter the Prophet printed in full, that the building required neither: it has stood crooked and honest through everything; it is the most truthful structure in Britain; leave it be. There was only a small bronze plate on the fencepost now, and the plate said: HERE SEVERUS SNAPE GAVE THE LAST OF SEVENTEEN YEARS. PASSWORD PROTECTED TO THE END.

"George wrote the inscription," Ginny said. "Nobody knows that. The committee asked the family of every defender for suggestions, expecting, you know, committee things — and George sent that, and McGonagall read it and closed the discussion." She put her hands in her pockets, looking down the slope at the sagging roofline. "He says Fred voted by proxy."

Harry stood at the fence for a long time. He had told her all of it by then — the Pensieve, the doe, the look that asked for Lily's eyes at the end — but telling and standing are different things, and she let him stand, the way he had learned to let her, until the cold got into their boots.

"I keep doing this," he said eventually. "Going back to the places. Godric's Hollow. The forest, in June. Now here. Ginny, honestly — is it morbid? You can tell me it's morbid."

Ginny considered the Shack with her head tilted, in the way she considered a defence before deciding where it was weakest.

"When we mended the seventh floor," she said, "nobody could walk past the gap. All summer. People went three corridors round. And then George built the wall back, and now nobody can walk past it without touching it — same corridor, same people, same dead. The difference isn't the wall, Harry. It's that somebody went and stood in the gap on purpose, and did the work, and turned it from a hole into a place. That's all you're doing. You're going round to all your gaps and standing in them till they're places." She knocked her shoulder against his arm. "It's the opposite of morbid. Morbid is going three corridors round for the rest of your life."

The walk back to the gates took twice as long as the walk out. At the lane's end, with the castle going gold-windowed above them, she backed him up against a beech tree that had been providing exactly this privacy since the founders, and kissed him goodbye slowly and thoroughly, one cold hand at the back of his neck, until the prefects' whistle went up the lane; and when she finally pulled back, she didn't say goodbye. She put her palm flat on his chest, over the coat, over the tree of scars, the way the school touched the wall, and said, "Tuesday."

"Tuesday," said Harry; and that was their word now, and it meant what whole letters meant, and he watched her run up the lane into the crowd and could not have explained to a single examiner in the gallery why a man recently rated Outstanding in Concealment was standing at a public gate wearing an expression that could be read from the Astronomy Tower.

Chapter V

Operation Ghyll

M.O.M.
Auror Office — EYES ONLY
From: G. Robards
To: All section heads; Operation list
Re: Courier raids — attribution — case designation GHYLL
Date: 11 November 1998

At 03:10 Tuesday the Gringotts bullion courier from the Newcastle sub-vault was taken on the moor road by a party of three. Method matches the Carlisle raid of 29 October: layered anti-Disapparition cast in advance along the route, wards inverted against the escort, precision, no bodies. They are not raiding for gold. They are raiding for fare. Goblin liaison estimates the two raids together at the price of exactly one thing on the open market: an unregistered long-sea Portkey, with silence thrown in. In plain terms, for every desk this memo reaches: Rabastan Lestrange is robbing Gringotts couriers to buy himself an untraceable passage out of Britain, and the day he has enough gold, he is gone, and we will not get him back for twenty years.

Curse-breaker analysis of the ward inversions is attached. The signature is taught, not improvised — the old Lestrange method, and of the three wands that ever used it, two are accounted for: one dead at Hogwarts, one in Azkaban.

The board in the operations room has carried eleven names since May. As of this morning ten are crossed through. The file on the last is reopened and designated OPERATION GHYLL. Rabastan Lestrange is in Britain, he is funded or nearly funded, and he intends to stop being in Britain. Once he is over water we lose him for twenty years.

Briefing Thursday, 08:00. Cadets Potter and Weasley are seconded to the operation under my personal authority, and before the first wag of the first jaw about it: they have more recent field experience of the subject's social circle than this entire office combined, and I am too old to pretend otherwise for the comfort of the wage scale.

— G. Robards, Head Auror

The briefing room on Thursday held thirty people and one piece of furniture that mattered: the board. Harry had passed it every day since September — eleven photographs, ten of them crossed through in red, the office's whole purpose hung on a wall — and it was different to sit in front of it, under the last uncrossed face. Rabastan Lestrange looked out of the photograph with the Lestrange look: the heavy-lidded patience of a family that had waited out one fall of their master already and regarded the law as a kind of weather.

Neville sat at the back. He had come in early and chosen the seat, and he watched the entire briefing without taking a note, without moving, with a stillness that Harry — who had learned stillness the hard way, in a tent, in a forest — recognised at a glance and could not stop glancing at.

When the room emptied, Neville didn't move, and Harry and Ron didn't either, and so they were both witnesses to it: Neville walking to the front, standing before Robards at his full quiet height, and saying, "Sir. I'm formally requesting assignment to Ghyll."

"No," said Robards, not unkindly, and kept stacking parchment.

"Sir —"

"Cadet, I have read your file, and your application essay, and the St. Mungo's visitor log for the Janus Thickey ward, which has your name in it every Sunday for eighteen years." Robards set the parchment down. "That's precisely why it's no. The day we take him, the wand across from his needs to be the steadiest wand in Britain, and no wand on earth is steady eighteen inches from its own oldest wound. I will not hand you to him as a weakness, Longbottom. He'd find you in a crowded room. His family always could."

The silence went long. It was Harry who broke it, and he was as surprised as anyone.

"Sir — with respect — I stood across from the man who killed my parents fourteen times before I was eighteen, and the file you've read says how that ended. Steady isn't the opposite of wounded. Most of the steadiest people I know are wounded everywhere. If it goes to a fight, you want Neville Longbottom in the building — I've seen the evidence, you've cited it in the curriculum." He took a breath. "But more than that. There's a version of this where it's done right, where the last name comes off the board with every form filed and nothing for any defence counsel to touch ever — and at the end of that version, the person reading Rabastan Lestrange his rights is the son of Frank and Alice Longbottom, by the book, in front of witnesses. Not revenge, sir. The opposite of revenge, performed where the whole country can see the difference. I can't think of anything this office could do that would say more about what it is now."

Robards looked at Harry for a long moment, the squint doing its slow arithmetic. Then he looked at Neville.

"Research and support," he said. "Intelligence cell, full case access, no entry team — and the arrest, Longbottom, if we take him breathing, is yours, on the conditions the cadet has just laid out, which I am adopting wholesale and intend to claim as my own at the inquiry." He picked his parchment back up. "Dismissed. And Potter — the next time you feel a speech coming on, warn an old man. I'd have sat down."

The operation's first real action came two weeks later, on a moor road in Northumberland, and here is plainly what happened — because the Prophet never got it right, and Harry's letters were not allowed to say.

Intelligence said Lestrange's runners would hit the Gringotts courier route again on the Thursday. So the Aurors replaced the courier with a decoy — Savage, under Polyjuice, carrying a strongbox full of nothing — with the rest of the team concealed along the road, and the two cadets posted to an observation point on the ridge with orders to watch, log, and touch nothing. The runners took the bait at four in the morning: two men rising out of the bracken to stop the decoy, exactly as predicted. What nobody had predicted was the third man, dug into the heather forty yards back as insurance, who stood up behind Savage with a curse already leaving his wand while every concealed Auror in the county was watching the other two.

Harry was off the ridge before the observation log could record him leaving it. He broke cover at a dead sprint, threw a Shield Charm over Savage's back at thirty yards — the curse cracked off it like a stone off ice — and put the third man down with a Stunner and a Disarmer so close together that the official report listed them as a single spell. The two runners bolted for their escape Portkey; the team rose up out of the road and took them before they reached it. Total duration, start to finish: ninety seconds. Prisoners: two couriers and one gunhand, all delivered to holding by dawn, all three eventually talkative. Casualties: none — because a cadet under standing orders to touch nothing had decided, correctly, in under a second, that the orders were wrong.

Savage said nothing to Harry on the ride home. But the next morning the duty roster had been quietly amended, and Cadet Potter no longer appeared under observation.

Ginny17 November 1998

The Prophet says "renewed Auror activity in the north." Your last letter says training is "varied at the moment." Tuesday arrived flying like he'd been fed meat and purpose.

I can do these sums, Potter. I'm not asking what. The rule's the rule and I know it has a shape around your work — we'll find the edges of that when we have to, I expect. I'm only saying: I've noticed, I'm with you, and the how clause is now in effect. However varied things get — the real version. Scared is allowed. Scared in writing is positively encouraged.

Match Saturday. Slytherin. First one since — well. First one. Their captain came to me Tuesday, Harper, you don't know him, and proposed both teams walk out together. Slytherin proposed it. I said yes before he'd finished the sentence, partly because it's right and partly to watch the look on his face, which suggested he'd prepared four further arguments and a fallback position and now had nowhere to put them.

Touch the wall for me Saturday morning, wherever you are. Metaphorically. I'll do the real one.

— G.

What happened on the moor road, four nights later, went into the case file as Incident GHYLL/7, and into Auror Office legend as something shorter.

The intelligence said Lestrange's runners would hit the Tuesday bullion courier a third time, on the high stretch where the road ran between two becks; so Savage took eight Aurors out to lie in the heather and take them mid-raid, and the two cadets came along under the doctrine Savage had repeated four times in the briefing room and once more, with eye contact, on the ridge: you are observers. You will observe. You are equipped with a wand for the sole purpose of defending the hole you are lying in.

Harry lay in the wet heather next to Ron for five hours, watching the road through Omnioculars, learning the great unadvertised truth of Auror work, which is that it is mostly lying still in weather. The courier convoy came through at three. The raiders hit it at six minutes past, exactly as the intelligence promised — two figures rising out of the eastern beck, wards inverting along the road with that signature ripple like heat off iron — and Savage's eight came up out of the heather in a closing crescent, clean as a drill, and it was all going exactly to plan, which was the thing that bothered Harry.

He swung the Omnioculars off the fight entirely. It was the lesson of the too-clean street, the one he'd shouted at a gallery in October: when it's going exactly to plan, find out whose plan. He tracked the dark ground the attack had pulled every eye away from — the western beck, the dead angle behind the command position — and found it: a third figure, flat to the bank, forty yards behind Savage, wand already up and braced on a stone. Not raiding. Waiting. The raid was the bait; the prize was a senior Auror's back.

There was no time for the link. Harry came up out of the hole shouting Savage's name with the Shield Charm already leaving his wand — a Protego thrown forty yards to cover another man, which Proudfoot would spend the spring teaching as a thing that could apparently be done — and the killing-coloured curse out of the western beck broke over it in a spray of light that lit the whole moor like a flare. After that it was very fast. Harry's Disarmer took the third man's wand while the flare was still falling; Ron's voice came down the command link from the observation post, flat and rapid, vectoring two Aurors onto the western bank by grid square before anyone else had fully turned around — the net, Savage would call it afterward, the first time anyone used the word — and the man came up out of the beck wandless and ran, and was brought down in the heather, and the two original raiders, finding their evening's geometry suddenly upside down, surrendered with an alacrity that suggested they had not been paid for any of this.

The ninety seconds that followed were the frightening part, and Harry put them in no report and one letter. The third man — cornered, soaked, bound hand and foot in the light of nine wands — was laughing. He laughed up at the circle of Aurors, and at Harry longest, and what he said, conversationally, with the rain running into his mouth, was: "He knows your shapes now. All of you. He's not running, you know. He's building." And nothing in three weeks of interrogation ever improved on it, because the man was a hired wand who knew one true thing, and that was it, and a certain kind of man gets simpler the closer you get to the centre, and simpler is worse.

On the ridge, afterward, Savage stood in front of Harry for a long moment with the rain coming down between them.

"You were ordered to observe, cadet."

"I observed a wand braced on your back-line, sir," said Harry. "Then I commented."

Savage looked at him for another moment, and then at the prisoners going up the road, and then said, "Debrief at six. Both of you. Bring the Weasley grid-call, written out — it's going in the manual," and walked off down the hill; and that was all, except that the next morning a memo went up from the Training Division requesting that Cadets Potter and Weasley be seconded to Savage's own field rotation, effective immediately, and in the box marked justification Savage had written one line: Ask anyone who was on that moor.

Harry24 November 1998

The how: cold, careful, long hours, and last Thursday, for about ninety seconds, genuinely frightening — everyone's whole, including me, and that's the truthful weather report. Scared in writing, as encouraged. It helps that you asked for it. It helps more than the writing-down, actually, and I do both.

(What I can say, because forty witnesses and the Prophet will: we brought three men in, off a moor road, before dawn. Runners and a gunhand, not the prize. The prize is still out there and knows we're close, which is the part that was frightening, because a certain kind of man gets simpler the closer you get, and simpler is worse.)

Sloper — you'll like this. Jack Sloper's in our intake. Aggie's brother, the Beater, the one who knocked Katie out with his own bat that year, which he brings up himself before anyone else can, which tells you most of what you need to know about him. He's solid, Gin. Slow on theory, dead reliable in the field, the one who checks the corner everyone else has decided is clear. Aggie writes him every fortnight too. He reads the letters out at supper if the kneazle features. The kneazle is named Filch. The entire intake is emotionally invested. Savage asks after the kneazle now in the manner of a man requesting a status report.

The match made the Prophet, did you see? Photo of the two teams walking out together, and the caption didn't mention me even once, which I believe makes it the finest piece of sports journalism of the age. Three hundred and ninety to twenty, you merciless creature. The Harpies scout was seen "smiling, briefly, possibly." Robards read the report at breakfast and said, with feeling, "THAT's house-clearing discipline," and put the clipping on the operations-room wall, where it remains, next to eleven photographs, improving the place.

Tuesday's asleep on the kit shelf with one foot in my ink. The how, again, so it's in writing twice: tired, careful, hopeful, yours.

— H.

Chapter VI

What George Sends

WEASLEYS' WIZARD WHEEZES — OWL ORDER FORM No. 2,481
Received 1 December 1998

CUSTOMER: H. Potter, Auror Training Annexe, Ministry of Magic (the irony has been NOTED and FILED)

ORDER: Skiving Snackbox, Deluxe — QUANTITY: fourteen (14)

CUSTOMER REMARKS: One per cadet. Christmas. Do NOT tell Robards.

— — — — — — — — — — — — —

FOR OFFICE USE (George's handwriting, increasingly large):

Order APPROVED with AMENDMENTS. Quantity revised to FIFTEEN (15) — you will require one for Robards, on the house, because a man who has run that office since May without a Fainting Fancy is a man in DENIAL.

Enclosed FREE with order: one (1) Punching Telescope, veteran's edition, for Savage, with our compliments — the WWW intelligence network (Verity, mostly) reports he has earned it.

Discount applied: Family rate (100%). Do not argue with the form, Harry. The form is enchanted and has heard every argument. Money returned under separate owl. The form would also like to remind you that Fred's half of this shop signed off on the family rate personally, in the will, in writing, and the form does not intend to be the first thing in this building to defy him.

— W.W.W. ("Every joke in this shop is half his. The funny half.")

Daily Prophet — Advertising Standards Notice5 December 1998

The Advertising Department wishes to clarify that the full-page notice in Thursday's edition reading "U-NO-POO — THE CONSTIPATION SENSATION — YES, WE'RE STILL SELLING IT, HE'S DEAD AND WE'RE NOT, SOME JOKES YOU EARN" was paid for in full, met all legal requirements, and will not be withdrawn, the management having concluded, upon review and upon receipt of four hundred and twelve supportive owls, that it is in fact extremely funny.

Ron8 December 1998

Hermione —

Something I want to say on paper first, because on paper I can get it in the right order, and because you think better with things in writing, and because — all right, mostly because it's easier. Bravery's got a paperwork department too. Who knew.

Plain English first, so there's no mistaking me, and then the working: when Lestrange is caught — not before, not instead, after — I'm leaving the Aurors, and I'm going to work with George at the shop, permanently. That's the decision. It's made. The rest of this letter is me showing the working, because you think better when you can check the working.

I'm good at this job. That's the strange part, and I need you to believe I know it, so that you'll believe the rest. I'm not Harry — nobody's Harry, watching Harry work is like watching weather decide things — but I'm good. Strategy module, top of intake. Savage says I read a room like a chessboard, which from Savage is roughly a parade. If I stayed, I'd make Auror, proper Auror, and probably a good one.

And I keep doing sums with it, late, when the barracks have gone quiet. Not the leaving sums — you know my condition, I set it at the kitchen table in front of Mum and the Minister for Magic and I meant every word: when the board's clean, I'm done. The list isn't short yet. One name left, and it's the worst one, and I'm not going anywhere while it's up there — Harry doesn't get to do that wall without me, and that's the end of that sum.

It's the after sums. Because here's what I worked out, doing the strategy module, top of intake: the thing I'm actually best at — the thing I've been best at since I was eleven and gave myself up on a giant chessboard — isn't catching the worst people in Britain. It's looking at a board and seeing which piece should be spent and which piece matters. And Hermione, I look at the after-board, and the piece that matters is the shop. George says "from us" now, did you notice, at the Burrow he signs things "from us" — and there's only one way that stays true, and it's a Weasley behind that counter who can carry the other half of the joke. Percy can't. Bill and Charlie have lives. Ginny's got a broom with her name on it.

It's me. It was always going to be me. I just wanted to tell you the whole shape of it now, in December, so that when the day comes you'll know it wasn't a retreat. It's the move the board wants. Grand strategy. Kingsley said so himself, basically.

Anyway. Four days to the Hog's Head. I've been informed by the coin that attendance is mandatory and that you wrote the protective enchantments on the invitation, which explains why it corrected my spelling when I RSVP'd.

— R.

P.S. I've just reread this and it's all chessboards, so here's the plain version, on the record, because you deserve it in plain: I'm staying in the Aurors until we take Lestrange. The day the board is clean, I resign, and I go and run the shop with George, for good. That's the whole plan, start to finish. You're the first person I've told it to straight.

Dudley10 December 1998

Harry —

Coffee was all right wasn't it. You drink it wrong by the way. Three sugars is a kid's drink, I said so at the time and I've thought about it since and I stand by it.

Boxing's going well. Coach says I could do regionals if I keep my feet sorted. I told him my cousin does law enforcement and he said "runs in the family then, discipline" and I didn't correct him. It half does though doesn't it. Run in the family I mean.

Mum asks about you. That's the thing I mainly wanted to write. She doesn't ask straight out, she does it sideways, "I suppose your cousin is busy" while looking at the kettle. I tell her what you said I could tell her. She nods and does the kettle. It's not much but Harry it's more than sixteen years so I thought you should know the direction of travel.

Bring the girlfriend next time if you want. Ginny. You said her name nine times, I counted, it was embarrassing mate. There's a pizza place does normal pizza.

P.S. The frog card came out then. Bloke at the off-licence collects normal football ones and I showed him yours and said I know him and he said "course you do Dud" so cheers for that, made me a liar in my own postcode.

Chapter VII

The D.A., Reconvened

The coins went warm on the second Saturday of December, all across Britain, with a message that fit the old way around the rim: HOG'S HEAD. 7PM. BRING NOTHING. OWE NOTHING. — D.A.

Aberforth had refused all payment and all thanks, in that order, loudly, and then closed the bar to the public, cleaned it to a standard that suggested deep personal conflict, and hung over the fireplace — with no comment whatsoever — a framed photograph of a girl with long blonde hair, smiling, eight years old forever. The portrait's frame upstairs stood empty; Ariana, everyone understood without being told, was attending.

They came through the door in twos and threes out of the snow: Seamus and Dean, arguing about football, which was Dean's revenge for seven years of Quidditch; Katie Bell and Alicia and Angelina, who arrived holding George's hand with an air of having concluded a long negotiation with grief and won; Michael Corner and Terry Boot; Padma and Parvati; Lavender Brown, scarred down one side of her face and jaw, who paused half a step in the doorway — and Parvati simply took her arm and walked her in like weather, and by eight o'clock Lavender was holding court by the fire telling the story of the scars to a circle of people who had earned the right to hear it, which, she said, was the entire difference: "Strangers stare like it's their question. You lot look like it's my answer."

Dennis Creevey came with the camera around his neck, because he was sixteen and it went where he went.

Luna ran the toast. Nobody had asked her to; it was simply understood, the way thestrals understand things, that it would be Luna. She climbed onto a chair with a glass of Aberforth's mead and waited for quiet, and got it instantly.

"Hello, everyone," she said. "Before we drink anything, I think we should say that this is not really a reunion, because we never disbanded. You can't disband the D.A. We checked the founding enchantments — well, Hermione wrote them, so I asked her, which is the same thing." Laughter, and Hermione going pink. "So this is just a meeting. Our membership is currently seventy-one. Twelve of the members are dead, which doesn't end a membership; we checked that too." The room went still, but it was a warm stillness; this was why it had to be Luna. "Their coins are in their headstones now, and Hermione never disconnected them from the set, so when this one in my hand went warm on Saturday, so did theirs, wherever they are. Colin got the message. I find that I like knowing that more than I like almost anything." She raised her glass. "To the members in the next part. The meeting has come to order, and you're all marked present."

"Present," said the Hog's Head, with one voice, and drank; and Aberforth turned to face his shelves for a while, and no one took the slightest notice, out of courtesy.

The evening then became what such evenings become: louder, warmer, fragmenting into orbits. And at the bar, helping Aberforth with a competence that suggested the Leaky Cauldron's training, was Hannah Abbott — who had come up from London "to lend a hand," carrying a crate of bottles, wearing a sprig of flutterby blossom pinned to her dress that rippled gently in the unmoving air of the room, and fooling precisely nobody.

"Watch this," murmured Ginny, materialising at Harry's elbow with two butterbeers. "He's been working up to it for an hour. He's done two laps of the room with her drink. There he goes — no — he's stopped to explain the drink to Seamus — Neville, you tragedy — there he goes."

Neville reached the bar. He set down the drink. He was seen to inhale. And then, in front of the better part of Dumbledore's Army, Neville Longbottom — who had crossed a courtyard alone toward Lord Voldemort and a burning hat — was rescued by Hannah Abbott, who took the drink, and his hand with it, and said something that made him laugh his real laugh, the unguarded one, and did not give the hand back; and a sound went around the room that was half cheer and half sigh, and George called out, "SIX YEARS, LONGBOTTOM, WE HAD A POOL," and Hannah, without turning, called back, "Who had December?" and Aberforth, gravely, raised one finger, and was paid out of the till to scenes of disorder.

It was somewhere in the middle of all this that Harry, queuing at the bar, found Cho Chang beside him.

"Hello, Harry."

She was down from Edinburgh — Healer training, second year, the blue trim on her cuffs — and she had grown into one of those people whose composure is a finished room; but there was a flicker under it when she said his name, an old window briefly lit, and Harry, who had become very good at reading rooms and remained famously hopeless at reading this particular kind, smiled at her with uncomplicated gladness and said, "Cho! Healer training — that's brilliant, that's — sorry, hang on —" and turned his head.

He had not meant to turn his head. Nothing had happened across the room to turn it. Ginny was only standing by the fire with Luna, mid-story, lit up gold, doing nothing remarkable except existing in his field of awareness like a compass fact; and Harry's sentence had simply followed his attention home, the way it had been doing all evening, the way — Cho watched it happen — it evidently did all the time. He caught himself, came back, "— sorry. Second year, you were saying. Do you get a specialism yet?" and was, for the rest of the conversation, kind, present, interested, and so completely, structurally elsewhere that there was nothing in it for anyone to mourn.

Cho talked with him about spell damage rotations and the Edinburgh snow, and watched his eyes make two more involuntary trips across the room, and felt the old window go dark with hardly a draught — it had been so long lit out of habit, mostly, the way you leave a lamp on in a room you've moved out of. When Ginny's path brought her over and Harry's arm went around her with the unconscious accuracy of long practice, Cho lifted her glass a careful inch — to Ginny, woman to woman, the full sentence of it: I see it, it's real, stand down, and honestly, well done — and Ginny, who missed nothing and had been missing nothing all evening, lifted hers the same inch back, and the entire treaty was negotiated, ratified, and archived without a word, the way the important ones are.

("She's lovely," Ginny said later, on the walk up to the castle gates. "I was all braced to be gracious. Years I've kept that graciousness oiled, Potter, and you went and made it obsolete by being incapable of finishing a sentence when I'm in a room." — "I finish sentences." — "You've stopped four tonight. Dennis has photographic evidence." — "Of sentences?" — "Of your face, Harry, during the sentences," said Ginny, with enormous satisfaction, "and I've ordered copies.")

At the end of the night Dennis stood on a table with his brother's camera and took the photograph: all of them crammed together under Ariana's picture, Aberforth scowling magnificently in the back, Luna's radish earrings mid-swing, Neville and Hannah's hands not even pretending. He took it the Muggle way, no charm on the film, so that the moment would stay; and a print of it hangs to this day behind a certain bar in Hogsmeade, next to a girl who is eight forever, marked along the bottom in Dennis's careful hand: D.A. — ALL PRESENT.

Chapter VIII

Eleven Hands at Christmas

The Burrow at Christmas held more people than the Burrow could hold, which was the point of the Burrow.

The Grangers came for the whole of Christmas Day, their second visit since Australia, and the alliance between Arthur Weasley and Daniel Granger was now a settled feature of both families' lives: they spent the early afternoon in the shed in a state of mutual scientific rapture over a set of Muggle fairy lights ("they work on a principle, Molly, the bulbs are wired in series, if one goes the lot go — it's the most courageous design philosophy I've ever encountered") while Mrs. Granger taught Fleur a Christmas pudding hard sauce that Fleur pronounced "almost French," the highest honour ever conferred in that kitchen.

Teddy Lupin, eight months old, attended his first Christmas in a Weasley jumper the size of a teapot cosy — midnight blue, white T — and conducted the day from lap to lap like a small visiting monarch, his hair working through the room's whole palette: Weasley red on Molly's knee, bushy brown for Hermione, a heroic attempt at Fleur's silver that came out the colour of tinsel and was retained, by acclamation, for the rest of the day. The wolf came last, after the brooms-that-weren't-really (a four-inch hover, Molly had ruled, and not one inch higher) and the singing blocks and the small avalanche of soft things every war-emptied heart in two families had bought one baby. Harry had cleared it with Andromeda in November — had stood in her kitchen and said, halting, "I want to get him — I don't want it to be a sad thing, I want it to be a true thing, but if it's too soon, or too much, you're the one who —" and Andromeda had looked at him and said, "Edward Lupin is going to grow up knowing exactly what his father was, Harry, in every sense, and the sooner the better, and a wolf with a friendly face is precisely where you start." In the toyshop he had stood at the shelf for a long quiet minute all the same, and the witch behind the counter had had the grace not to recognise him out loud.

Now he sat on the hearthrug and held it out — grey and soft, with kind amber eyes and one ear already flopped, as though it had been loved in advance — and the room, which knew what it was looking at, went quiet around them without quite stopping its business, the way the Burrow could. Teddy regarded the wolf. The wolf regarded Teddy. Teddy reached out, seized it by the ear, hauled it into a headlock of total acceptance, and bit it; and his hair, which had been Christmas-paper gold all morning, slid without any fuss at all into a soft sandy brown that nobody in that room had ever seen on him and everybody in that room recognised; and Andromeda put down her tea and looked into the fire for a moment, and Molly suddenly needed something from the kitchen, and George said, hoarsely, "Well, that's that, then. He's keeping it," which gave the room permission to laugh, which was George's whole job now and he was getting good at it.

He loved it immediately, devotedly, by chewing its ear to felt; and Andromeda, watching her grandson asleep at last in the wreckage of the wrapping paper with the wolf in a headlock, said, to nobody in particular, in the tone of a woman reporting a fact about the weather, "Dora would have re-coloured her hair to match every single person at this table by now," and the table lifted its glasses without anyone needing to propose it.

George came with Angelina and signed every gift from us, the way he had since the summer, and nobody remarked on it, because remarking was not how the Weasleys did it; but Harry noticed that the tag on his own gift — a framed, enchanted photograph of the Diagon Alley shopfront, in which the WONDER WITCH display pointed and laughed at the viewer on a seven-second loop — was signed from us, for the calm, and he put it on the Grimmauld Place mantel next to the photograph that didn't move, and the two of them kept each other company, the still one and the laughing one, which was about right.

Dudley's Christmas card arrived on the twenty-third (a Muggle robin; Merry Christmas. Mum picked it. That's the news really. — D.) and a card went back to Privet Drive by the last owl post: the first Christmas card, it was generally agreed at the Burrow table, ever to fly in that particular direction in the history of the world.

And the clock had all eleven hands home.

It happened at around four o'clock on Christmas Day and lasted nearly two hours, and Mr. Weasley was the only one who saw the moment itself: Hermione's hand swinging from travelling to home as the Grangers' hired car came up the lane, the last of the eleven, the whole golden family fanned on home like a hand of winning cards — Fred's hand watching from the mantelpiece, included by position, which had been the design — and Arthur Weasley stood looking at his clock in a houseful of noise for a long moment, and then went and found Molly in the kitchen and didn't say anything, just turned her by the shoulders so she could see it too; and whatever passed between them then was theirs, and stayed theirs, and the potatoes were only slightly late.

Molly3 January 1999

to Ginny, Hermione, Harry, Ron, and yes, Neville dear, you as well

My darlings —

The house is too quiet and I have made three pies about it, so you may expect the pies by the next post, and I will hear no nonsense about training-camp nutrition from ANY cadet of MINE, Harry, dear — I have read the Auror Office dietary guidance, your father obtained it for me through official channels, and it was written by a bachelor.

Now. I have counted the jumpers in the photographs Dennis sent and I am SHORT ONE JUMPER, and the missing jumper is Hannah Abbott's, and that is MY error and not hers, and it is being corrected in yellow with an H as we speak, because any girl who can make our Neville laugh like that in front of forty witnesses is getting a jumper if I have to invent a new size of needle. Neville, dear, you will deliver it and you will pass on the sizing honestly. Mothers always know, and I am informed her own is not here to do it, so the matter is settled and we shan't speak of it again except at the wedding.

Arthur has not stopped talking about the series-wired fairy lights. Daniel has promised him a soldering iron. I have hidden the soldering iron. This information is shared on a need-to-know basis and you all need to know in case he asks.

The clock looked very fine on Christmas Day. Your father pretends he built the new hands for the magic to work properly, and the magic does work properly, but between us five: he checks it last thing every night now, before bed, the way he used to check on all of you. Some men say their prayers. Your father reads the clock.

Eat. Sleep. Write to your mothers, all of you; I am everyone's, it's been decided.

— Mum

Chapter IX

The Missed Tuesday

Harry11 January 1999

Gin —

This letter is doing a job I'd rather it didn't, and I'm going to write it plainly because we promised plainly.

Starting sometime in the next ten days, there will be a stretch — I'm told three weeks, could run longer — when I can't write. Not won't: can't, by order, in writing, the kind with consequences. Where we're going, an owl is a flare, even Tuesday, even at night. I can't tell you where, or what, or with whom, and I can't even tell you exactly when it starts, and Ginny, I have sat here for an hour trying to find the version of the rule that survives this, and what I've got is: the how, in advance, for the whole stretch, paid up front like rent.

The how is: trained, ready, frightened the right amount, surrounded by the most careful people I've ever worked with, and coming back. That last one isn't information, I know. It's intent. It's the only thing I'm allowed to send and the only thing that matters anyway.

When it starts, Tuesday will stop coming. That's the only notice you'll get, and I hate it, and I couldn't think how to spare you the morning you realise it's begun.

I'm sorry. The job has a hole in it where the rule should go. I didn't cut it and I can't fill it, but I can tell you it's there, which is the most honest thing left on the table.

Yours, every Tuesday there is, including the silent ones —

H.

Ginny13 January 1999

Noted, Cadet.

(That's the steady opening line. I drafted six. One was steady. You're welcome.)

The real version from this end: I read your letter at breakfast, took the Quidditch practice of their lives out on my own team — Demelza has asked me to convey that whatever you did, you should apologise — and then sat in the stands by myself for a while doing the thing you do, putting the fear in writing. It's in my trunk. It's three pages. You may read them when you're back, as proof of how the rent was spent.

Here's where I've landed, and I want it on paper before the silence starts, so you can carry it in:

I knew. The night you came back from the dead with grass stains on you and I decided — and it was a decision, Potter, on the staircase, about four in the morning, you were asleep — that I was keeping you: I knew what you were. You run toward it. You always have. Eleven years old, toward it. The girl in the Alley, toward it, before anyone else's feet had even chosen. I am not the sort of fool who falls for a man because he runs toward it and then spends fifty years asking him to stand still. I love the thing that frightens me. They're the same thing. I've checked, and they don't come separately.

So: go. Be exactly what you are, all the way, with my whole blessing — that's the part I needed you to carry in, in writing, in your kit, where the cold can't argue with it.

And then COME BACK, and we'll invent the rest of the rule together, because apparently we're the first two people stupid enough to need a clause for "beloved is also a weapon of the state."

The wall gets touched twice a day until Tuesday flies again.

— G.

The silence began on the nineteenth of January.

What the operation actually was — declassified long afterward, plain enough to say now — was this: twelve Aurors went into the high north under concealment for three weeks, to take Lestrange's remaining network apart piece by piece: the runners, the forgers, the quartermaster who bought his supplies, each one lifted so quietly that the others never knew. The mail silence had one simple reason behind it. An owl can be followed. A single followed owl would have unravelled three weeks of work and put every hidden Auror under a Death Eater's wand — so Harry was not allowed to write, because a letter could literally have killed him.

She was ready for it, and being ready helped the way a railing helps on ice — something to grip while the thing happens anyway. The first silent Tuesday, the owl simply did not come, and she taught herself to eat breakfast while not watching a window.
