The second Tuesday was worse, because the owl did come: Tuesday arrived at the Gryffindor table at eight in the morning with nothing on his leg at all — he had, the family later pieced together, simply refused to skip his day, presenting himself at the Ministry owlery, being turned away empty by a sympathetic duty officer, and flying the route anyway, on principle, on schedule — and an empty-legged owl, it turned out, was the loudest letter ever sent. Ginny fed him bacon with hands that wanted very much to shake, and did not let them, because half the Great Hall had worked out by then what the famous owl meant, and a captain is watched.

It was that evening that McGonagall kept her back after Transfiguration — ostensibly about the N.E.W.T. coursework, actually about nothing of the kind. She let the classroom empty, straightened a stack of essays that did not need straightening, and said, to the window, "I sat where you are sitting, Miss Weasley, in two wars. Both times there was somebody in the field, and a silence with rules to it, and a castle full of people watching my face for the news. The waiting is the whole tax. There is no spell for it; I looked." She turned, and her face had nothing of the classroom in it. "My office keeps a fire, a kettle, and a chessboard, most evenings after eight, and conversation is not required or particularly encouraged. The gargoyle has been given your name." Ginny went twice that month, and they played chess almost entirely in silence, and McGonagall beat her with ruthless courtesy both times, and it helped in a way that nothing with words in it could have; and it is part of why, five weeks later, when the Head Auror's Patronus arrived before dawn with the worst kind of news, it was the Headmistress herself who climbed the tower stairs, and why Ginny, dressing with steady hands in the lamplight, was not entirely unrehearsed at being watched by Minerva McGonagall while the news was bad.

It was Hermione — up at the school, three tables' worth of N.E.W.T. notes to her left — who did the thing that helped most, because it was Hermione, and her love arrived disguised as engineering. She appeared in the common room that night with a small flat box and the expression she wore when a problem had finally agreed to be a problem instead of a feeling. "Protean Charm," she said. "Same as the coins. There are two of these buttons; the other one's gone to Ron by tonight's post, and Ron is not under mail silence, he's on the support rotation at headquarters, I checked the regulations, all forty pages, there is no rule against a button." When the operation's field teams reported in — which they did, she had established, on a schedule Ron was cleared to know existed but not to describe — Ron would press his once if the reports were green. The button in Ginny's pocket would go warm for the space of a breath. One press, once or twice a week, meaning nothing the Ministry could object to and everything else: still green. still coming back. — R.

"It's not the rule," Hermione said, watching Ginny turn the button over. "I can't get you the rule. It's a — handrail."

"It's my brother and my best friend smuggling me the how," said Ginny, "through forty pages of regulation, on a schedule," and hugged her so hard that the N.E.W.T. notes suffered, and neither of them mentioned the state of anyone's eyes, by treaty.

The button went warm eleven times in twenty-three days. Ginny kept a tally on her trunk lid in chalk, like a prisoner, like a lighthouse keeper, like exactly what she was, which was a nineteen-year-old girl in a castle learning the oldest curriculum there is: how to love someone the world also needs. Her game that month, the school agreed, reached a level not seen at Hogwarts in a generation. ("Where do you put it all?" Demelza asked, after the Ravenclaw match, with awe and two black eyes. "In the hoops," said Ginny.)

And on the eleventh of February, a Thursday, the wrong day entirely, the silence ended the way she had not let herself imagine it: not with a letter but with the owl himself arriving at dinner flying like thrown gold down the length of the Great Hall, with a black thread tied to his leg — the signal they had agreed meant safe, silent, soon — and behind the thread, no letter, because no letter was cleared yet; and behind the no-letter, three days later, a letter; and behind the letter, at the Hogsmeade gates on the last Saturday of February, by special petition to two separate authorities, the cadet himself — thinner, paler, a healing line above one eyebrow, his entire heart in his face — and Ginny came through the gates at a run and kissed him before either of them had said a single word: cold mouth, snow in her hair, five weeks of silence ending against a wrought-iron gate, his arms wrapped all the way around her and her feet not entirely on the ground. The third-years present that day report that the hello lasted, by conservative estimate, a fortnight, and that nobody begrudged them a second of it.

The rule, as amended that evening, in the snow, on the road below the castle, and ratified by both parties:

You can't always tell me what. You will always tell me how. And when you can't tell me anything — Tuesday flies anyway. The day holds even when the letter can't. Love keeps a schedule, especially in the dark.

Chapter X

The Ghyll

In the end, it was the greenhouses that found him.

The January operation — the long silence — had taken Lestrange's network apart wire by wire: four runners, two forgers, and a quartermaster lifted so quietly, over three weeks of frozen surveillance, that each one believed the others were still free. What it had not taken was Lestrange, who had gone to ground with his Portkey money somewhere in the north so completely that the maps ran out of pins; and the case might have sat there until the spring, except that the quartermaster's boots came through the evidence room, and the evidence room had standing instructions, and the standing instructions were one cadet's whole assignment, and the cadet was Neville Longbottom.

Neville spent two days with the boots, a microscope of Hermione's recommending, and the patience of a man who had spent eighteen years of Sundays learning that some things only give themselves up slowly. Then he walked into Robards' office without knocking, which had never once happened, and laid out on the desk, on white parchment, a smear of crushed leaf-mould and three seeds the size of ground pepper.

"Mire-moss," he said. "And the seeds are dwarf bog-myrtle, the charmed strain — it was bred two centuries ago for exactly one purpose, screening properties from Muggle ramblers; it confunds a compass and it makes you remember an urgent appointment elsewhere. It doesn't grow wild, sir, it has to be planted and it has to be renewed, and it only thrives where the water's acid and the limestone's been cut — gorge country. Becks and ghylls." He put a map on the desk on top of the parchment. "Mire-moss narrows it to high country. The myrtle strain narrows it to a wizard's garden wall. And the Floo Network archive narrows it to this: every property in the Yorkshire dales whose connexion was severed by request between the two falls of You-Know-Who. There are four. Three are visible from public footpaths." His finger came down on the contour lines where the land folded shut. "One is at the head of a ghyll a compass can't find."

Robards looked at the map for a long time.

"The operation's been named Ghyll since November," he said at last.

"Yes, sir," said Neville. "I thought that was tidy of you."

The forward staging post was a requisitioned barn in the hills above the dale, and on the last night, after Robards had walked the plan for the final time — every man's position chalked on the floor, every contingency lettered, his closing words the entire philosophy of his office: "Nobody is brave tomorrow. Brave is for when the plan fails, and the plan will not fail. Sleep." — the lamps went down, and the teams turned in, and three people did not.

They ended up at the map table, the way they had ended up around fires in a tent, in a tower, in a forest: Harry, and Ron, and Neville, with the gorge's contour lines between them and three mugs of cocoa that Ron had produced from somewhere with the air of a man who had learned the important parts of quartermastering from his mother.

"One list each," Ron said quietly, after a while — the barracks line, fourteen months old now, from the first night of all of this. "And the whole thing's down to one name, and the name's down to one morning." He turned his mug. "Eleven years old, I sat on a train and the worst thing in my world was corned beef. Mental, when you do the sums."

Neville had the warrant out on the table. He had folded and unfolded it so many times that the creases had gone soft as cloth, and Harry watched him square it against the map's edge, and asked the question straight, because they had earned straight years ago.

"How are you? The real version."

"That's the strange part," said Neville, slowly. "I keep checking for the anger. Eighteen years I've had it banked, and I keep going down to the vault tonight and it's — it's not gone, it's changed. It's gone all quiet and orderly. Like the greenhouse before a storm." He smoothed the warrant flat. "I went to St. Mungo's on Sunday. Told them it might be done by my next visit — I always tell them things, the Healers say it matters even if — and Mum gave me a sweet wrapper." He brought it out of his breast pocket: a small square of foil, smoothed to softness like the warrant. "She's given me hundreds. Gran used to tell me to bin them, and I never once did, I've kept every one, and tomorrow I'm arresting the man who — " his voice stayed perfectly level, which was somehow worse and better than breaking — "with one of them in my pocket. So that something of hers is there for it. That's the whole answer, Harry. I'm not going down that gorge angry. I'm going down it kept. There's a difference, and it took me eighteen years and one application essay to find it."

From the dark behind them, Robards' voice said, "Map table closes in five minutes, gentlemen," with no reproach in it whatsoever, and a moment later, "Longbottom. Your parents' file sits on my desk, not in the archive. It has sat on every Head Auror's desk for eighteen years. Tomorrow it moves." And the lamps went out properly, and none of the three of them slept much, and it did not matter in the slightest.

They went in on the fourth of March, an hour before a moonless dawn, in sleet: twelve Aurors and two cadets, Potter on the entry team at Robards' own order ("the file is the file"), Weasley running coordination from the ridge, Longbottom at the support post in the rear — by the book, by his own agreement, with the arrest warrant in his breast pocket where another man would carry a photograph.

The house at the ghyll-head was dark, low, and wrong, and Harry knew it was wrong from two hundred yards, the old tell singing in him like a struck rail: too clean. No wards on the approach. No myrtle-flinch at the boundary. A back door an inch ajar, in sleet, in March.

"He's not in the house," Harry said, on the whisper-link, flat and fast. "The house is the postbox. He's behind us — the ghyll's the —"

The ghyll lit up.

Lestrange had not built a refuge; he had built a killing-ground and slept in it for three months, and the anti-Disapparition wave that came down the gorge with the first curses was his own family signature, the inversion, the hunters' road home cut behind them — eight years of war distilled into one piece of geometry: twelve Aurors in a stone throat, attackers above on both lips of the rock, and the only cover a frozen beck and the dark.

What happened in the next four minutes exists in eleven sworn statements, and the statements disagree about everything except Harry.

The statements agree that the voice on the link never rose and never stopped: shields on the south lip, he's right-handed off the rock — Proudfoot, the overhang, MOVE — lights down, he wants you lit — the dense, transferable calm, colder than the beck, the sound of someone who has been here before, in worse company, with less help. They agree that the team re-formed around that voice the way iron filings re-form around a magnet. They agree that the two wands on the north lip were down inside ninety seconds, taken by curses that came up out of the dark gorge with horrible precision, and that the voice assigned the prisoners' binding to others and kept moving, because it had already done the arithmetic the whole night turned on: two on the north lip, the inversion needs three, where is the —

And they agree about the moment, because every one of them saw it, lit stark white by the curse itself.

Jack Sloper had gone down — a shattered kneecap from a ricochet off the rock, nothing worse, but down, in the open shale at the bottom of the gorge, in the killing-light of someone's flare, dragging himself toward the beck with his wand arm not answering. And the third wand, the missing wand, Rabastan Lestrange himself, rose up out of a fold of rock not forty feet from the boy and took his time — took his time, the statements repeat, levelling the curse at the crawling cadet with the unhurried, heavy-lidded leisure of a man feeding something — because that was the Lestrange signature older than any ward-work: they had always, always preferred the ones who couldn't fight back.

The curse was green-edged and it was never identified, because it never landed where it was aimed.

Harry crossed the open shale faster than the witnesses could agree on and was simply there, between, shield up — and the curse hit the shield, and the shield held, and the overspill did not: it broke over the top edge like a wave over a sea wall and opened him from the left shoulder to the collarbone, a dark cutting curse, later catalogued, of the kind that does not stop bleeding for spite. The statements agree that he staggered one half-step, planted, and did not go down. The statements agree that he put his wand-point on Rabastan Lestrange across forty feet of sleet, bleeding through his kit in the flare-light, standing over Jack Sloper the way a wall stands, and that what he said, conversationally, carrying, was:

"Me instead. It's a better story."

Lestrange came down onto the shale to finish it personally, which was his vanity and the end of him.

The duel lasted eleven spells, and the eleven sworn statements reconstruct it like scripture, and Proudfoot teaches it now, every autumn, with diagrams: how the boy fought the whole of it one-armed with his off shoulder dropping blood; how he gave ground twice, both times on purpose, walking Lestrange's feet onto the iced beck-stone an inch at a time like a chess problem; how the first word anyone heard him cast aloud — after ten silent exchanges that lit the gorge like a storm — was the same first word it had ever been, the disarm, Expelliarmus, the signature the whole country knew, thrown not as a question but as a sentence, with everything behind it; and how Rabastan Lestrange's wand left his hand at the exact moment his back foot found the ice, so that the last free Death Eater in Britain went down on a frozen stream in his own fortress, wandless, on his back, under the wand-light of a nineteen-year-old who had out-thought him on his own ground in under three minutes.

Harry stood over him, swaying slightly, bleeding steadily, and did not cast anything else. The gorge went quiet, the way places do when something old finishes.

"Cadet Longbottom," he said, up the link, in a voice eleven statements describe as courteous. "Your warrant, please. We'll wait."

Neville came down the ghyll path through the sleet without hurrying — the statements are unanimous; he never once hurried — and the support team's lights came down with him, so that the bottom of the gorge filled slowly with brightness, and Rabastan Lestrange, propped on the ice in the wreck of his last plan, watched the heavy, mild, unhurried figure resolve out of the weather and arrive at last above him, and recognised the face, because it was rounder but it was the face, and the statements record what he said, which was a thing about Frank and Alice Longbottom and the noises they had made, pronounced with the heavy-lidded leisure, the feeding look, designed in the moment of his ruin to do the only wounding left in his reach.

Neville looked down at him, and did not bleed.

"Rabastan Lestrange," he said — and his voice, the statements agree, was the voice he used on seedlings: even, unhurried, certain of time — "you're under arrest, by warrant of the Wizengamot. Murder. Torture. Forty-one further charges. The clerk will read you the full list at your committal — it takes about an hour, and you'll hear every word of it sitting still."

He bound him by the book. Checked the restraints; checked them twice; called the medi-wizard over for the prisoner — every step of it witnessed, every step of it filed, because by the book was the point. By the book was the whole revenge. And then, when it was done and signed for, Neville Longbottom knelt down on the ice so that the two of them were eye to eye, and said the only part that belonged to him.

"My mum and dad are alive. They beat you before I could walk, and they've never known it — so I'll know it for them. Every Sunday, for the rest of your life, somebody who loves them is going to walk out of their ward into the daylight. And you'll be in a cell, knowing the Longbottoms won." He stood, and straightened his soaked kit, and his face when he turned into the lights was wet and entirely at peace, like a greenhouse roof after rain. "Take him up."

Harry watched the lights carry the last name on the board away up the ghyll path. He felt the night let go of him, all at once, the way a rope lets go. And then he noticed — distantly, politely, the way you notice weather happening to somebody else — that the gorge had begun to tilt.

"Good arrest, Cadet," he said, with enormous clarity, to Neville; and then his knees went, and Jack Sloper, one-legged on the shale beside him, was the only one close enough to break his fall.

Here is what the wound had been doing while Harry Potter fought an eleven-spell duel one-armed and then stood at parade rest through a formal arrest: bleeding. The curse had been built for exactly that — a dark severing charm engineered out of spite, that fights every attempt to close it — and it had been emptying him, quietly and steadily, from shoulder to collarbone, the entire time, and he had simply declined to be interrupted. The field medi-wizard got one look under the soaked kit and stopped being calm. "He's lost far too much, and I cannot close a cursed wound in a riverbed. He goes to St. Mungo's now. Not soon. Now."

Now was the problem. Lestrange's anti-Disapparition inversion still hung over the whole gorge — the trap that had sealed twelve Aurors in was now sealing one bleeding cadet's way out — and so the next nineteen minutes happened at a dead run. Savage and Proudfoot carried Harry up the ghyll path on a conjured stretcher with Robards himself lighting the way; Ron's voice came down the command link from the ridge stripped of every joke he had ever made, coordinating two curse-breakers as they tore a window in the ward wide enough for a Portkey to work through; Sloper hopped the entire path on one leg and a borrowed shoulder because he flatly refused to be evacuated ahead of the man who'd been cut open standing over him. Savage kept both hands pressed on the dressing the whole way up and kept up a low, vibrating, continuous stream of orders — "stay awake, cadet, that is an order, there's paperwork, you have never once missed paperwork" — and partway up the path Harry, grey to the lips, his eyes half shut, said one sentence with perfect clarity that every Auror present entered into their statements, and that none of them could read aloud at the inquiry without stopping:

"Somebody tell the owl."

The ward came down at the ridge at 5:51 in the morning. The emergency Portkey was a coil of rope; it took Harry, the medi-wizard, Savage, and Robards, and it left behind it, on a dark hillside in Yorkshire, eleven Aurors, one prisoner transport, and Ron Weasley — who was required by after-action protocol to hold his post for nineteen more minutes, with his best friend's blood drying down one side of his kit, and who spent them, by his own later account, learning exactly how long a minute can be made to last.

Chapter XI

The Real Version

The knock came at twenty past six in the morning, and it was not a knock anyone had delegated. Minerva McGonagall stood in the doorway of the seventh-year girls' dormitory in a tartan dressing-gown with a travelling cloak thrown over it, a lamp in her hand, and she crossed straight to Ginny's bed and spoke before Ginny was fully upright, plainly, the way she did everything that mattered.

"Miss Weasley. I need you to listen to all of it before you stand up. There was an Auror operation in Yorkshire tonight. It succeeded — Rabastan Lestrange is in custody; the war's last name is crossed off. Harry is alive." A breath, exactly one. "He is also badly hurt. He shielded another cadet from a cursed wound and lost a great deal of blood, and he is at St. Mungo's now, and the word the Head Auror's Patronus used was serious, and I will not stand here and pretend to you it used a different one. Get dressed. Warm clothes. Miss Granger is already waiting in my office — your brother is at the hospital and your parents are on their way. You have four minutes, and you may use the time I am giving you to be frightened in, because once we are downstairs we are going to be brisk."

Students were not permitted to leave the grounds in term. The school's Floo connexions were sealed by the Headmistress's own standing order, written in her own hand, in September. Minerva McGonagall crossed her office at half past six in the morning, took the sealing-ward off her own fireplace with three precise strokes of her wand, and said, to the question Ginny hadn't asked: "I have written seventeen rules for this school this year, Miss Weasley, and I stand behind every one of them. I will fill out the forms on Monday. Walk faster."

The Floo took them to the visitors' atrium of St. Mungo's, and the corridor outside the Fourth Floor curse ward took the next hour of their lives.

Ron was there. He had come straight from the hillside the moment protocol released him, and he was still in his field kit, and one whole side of it had dried dark, and Ginny looked at the colour of it and understood whose it was and had to put a hand against the corridor wall. Hermione went to him without a word and he held on to her like a man holding a railing. And then — because he was Ron, and because their family did not do mysteries at hospital doors — he sat the two of them down on the bench and told it plainly, in order, all of it: the trap in the gorge; Sloper down in the open with a shattered knee; Lestrange taking slow aim at a boy who couldn't move; Harry crossing forty feet of open shale to stand in the way, and the curse opening him shoulder to collarbone over the top of his own shield. "And then he beat him anyway, Gin. One-armed, bleeding, eleven spells, walked him onto the ice like a chess problem and took his wand off him — and stood there through Neville's whole arrest like it was a passing-out parade, and then he went down, because that's the order he does things in, the job first and the bleeding after." Ron's jaw worked. "He was talking when we got him up the path. Mostly orders. And one —" his voice did the thing voices do, and steadied — "he said, somebody tell the owl. So you'd know the silence wasn't him breaking the rule. He was going under on a stretcher and he was worried about your Tuesday."

Ginny put her head down on her knees for a while after that, and nobody hurried her.

Molly and Arthur had arrived before them — Ginny walked into her mother's arms and stayed there — and Arthur, who hugged her second and longest, said the thing that put the whole night in one image: "His hand's been on mortal peril since five o'clock, love. Your mother was dressed before the Patronus came. We've been watching it the way you watch a kettle." It moved off mortal peril, he did not say, while you were in the Floo — because it hadn't yet, and the Weasleys did not lie to each other in corridors.

So they waited, and the waiting was the worst hour any of them spent that year. Healers went in; Healers came out; nobody would say anything except that he was "being worked on," which Hermione — who coped, always, by knowing things — translated into precise questions fired at a cornered trainee: what class of curse, what counter-sequence, what was the replenishment rate, was the wound responding, until the trainee fled and Hermione ran out of questions to hide inside, and came and sat down and held Ginny's hand instead, and the two of them watched the doors, and the tea McGonagall conjured went cold in everyone's hands, untouched, the way tea always did in that family at the worst moments. At one point Ginny said, to nobody, in a voice of pure fury, "He is going to be fine, and then I am going to kill him," and Molly said, "That's the spirit, dear," with complete sincerity, and that was the only laugh the corridor got.

At half past seven, the doors opened properly, and a senior Healer named Okafor came out pulling off her gloves, read the corridor in one glance — six Weasleys' worth of red hair, a Headmistress, a Head Auror standing at parade rest by the window — and had the mercy to lead with the verdict.

"He's going to be entirely well." She let that land before anything else. "Plainly, since you've earned plainly: the curse was a dark severing charm designed to keep a wound open, and it fought us for three hours, and it lost. The wound is closed and it will stay closed. He's taken enough Blood-Replenishing Potion to float a rowing boat, he keeps the arm, he keeps the shoulder, and he keeps a thin white scar from here to here that will fade to a silver line. He woke four minutes ago. His first sentence was a question about whether the arrest paperwork had been filed correctly." She consulted the ceiling. "His second was a list of your names. You can go in — walking, Miss Weasley."

Ginny did not entirely walk.

He was propped up in the bed by the window, grey as porridge, strapped from shoulder to ribs, hooked to a softly glowing bottle of something crimson — and awake, and turning toward the door before it finished opening, because some instrument in him had never once needed adjusting either. Ginny crossed the ward, took his face in both hands the way her mother did it, looked at him for one long, ferocious, inventory-taking second —

— and kissed him. Properly. Carefully at first, because of the strapping, and then not carefully at all: a long, fierce, unhurried kiss with her forehead pressed to his after it and both of them breathing like the end of a match, in front of her parents, her brother, Hermione, the Headmistress of Hogwarts, the Head Auror, two Healers, and the entire curse ward — from the far end of which Jack Sloper, his leg in a sling-frame, started the applause, and the night shift joined in.

"Hi," said Harry, somewhat winded, when he was allowed.

"You're on a chart," said Ginny, "that says boring kind of fine. I've read it. I read it twice." Her voice wobbled exactly once, on the second read, and was forgiven for it. "You are never doing this to me again, and we both know that's not true, so here's the version I actually mean." She sat down on the edge of the bed, took his good hand in both of hers, and said the thing she had worked out in the corridor, plainly, because the year had taught her plainly.

"I priced it tonight, Harry. Sitting on that bench, with Mum's arm around me and your blood dried on my brother, not knowing — for one whole hour, not knowing — I made myself look straight at it and price it. The job. The whole career. Forty years of Tuesdays, and some of them silent, and one or two of them, apparently, like tonight." She tightened her grip. "And here's where I landed, eyes open, full price, in a hospital, which is the only place it counts. The boy who would have stood on that ridge and watched Jack Sloper die in the open — I've never met him. I never loved him. There is no version of you that stands back that is still you, and I am not in love with a hypothetical, I'm in love with the idiot who jumped on the troll. So: go. All the way. My whole blessing, same as January, except now I know exactly what the words cost and I'm saying them anyway." She lifted his hand and kissed his knuckles, businesslike, like a seal on a document. "The forehead belongs to history. The chest is mine. This one" — she nodded at the strapping — "I'm assigning to Aggie Sloper's brother, who is going to hear from me about his footwork in writing. Any future additions get assessed item by item, by me, in person, over a chart."

"You got through the whole speech without crying," Harry managed, who had not.

"I'm an athlete," said Ginny, with immense, wet dignity. "We compartmentalise."

The rest of the day arranged itself around the bed. Molly fed the ward, the corridor, and "those poor exhausted Healers." McGonagall stayed until noon, and on her way out paused at the foot of the bed and said, "Fifty points to Gryffindor, Potter. The standing total is now embarrassing"; and back at the castle she filed the required forms for an emergency student absence, and in the box marked reason she wrote, in her upright hand, school morale — which, as she observed to nobody, had the rare virtue of being perfectly true. Tuesday the owl arrived at the ward window at one o'clock, having located Harry by whatever instrument owls use, carrying nothing at all; he had simply reported for duty, and he refused to leave, and by evening the ward staff had issued him a perch and a saucer and an unofficial staff position, which he accepted as no more than his due.

Daily Prophet — FRONT PAGE5 March 1999

POTTER GRAVELY WOUNDED FELLING LAST LESTRANGE

Boy Who Lived in "desperate duel" at secret moor fortress — St. Mungo's silent — nation holds its breath

In scenes that sources describe as "the Battle of Hogwarts in miniature," Harry Potter, 19, was last night gravely — some say mortally — wounded while single-handedly storming the stronghold of Rabastan Lestrange, the last Death Eater at large, who is now in Ministry custody. The Auror Office, in a terse statement, confirmed only "a successful operation with no fatalities," a formulation healers consulted by this newspaper called "consistent with grave concealment." Mr. Potter's condition is unknown. FULL COVERAGE: pages 2, 3, 4, 5, 6, 8 and the special commemorative pull-out, prepared, our readers will understand, purely as a precaution.

The Prophet's version, it will be noted, had Harry storming the fortress alone, omitted Neville and the moss and the net entirely, got the curse wrong, the county wrong, and the duel's length wrong, and came with a commemorative funeral pull-out for a man who was at that moment eating his third helping of Molly Weasley's shepherd's pie. Hermione filed the formal complaint before visiting hours ended; it ran to four pages and had schedules. The press office's corrected statement — drafted at the bedside, by committee, with Ginny holding the parchment steady — was eleven lines long, named every Auror and both cadets, credited the case break to "the botanical analysis of Cadet N. Longbottom," and was printed in full the next morning beneath the photograph a man in a tree opposite the window had taken of Ginny asleep in the chair with her hand over the strapped shoulder like a second dressing; and for once in its long disreputable life the Prophet wrote a caption nobody could fault, because it was four words long and simply true: THE BOARD IS CLEAN.

Robards came at four, declined a chair, and stood at the foot of the bed with a small box and the expression of a man performing surgery on himself.

"Two pieces of business. One." He set the box on the blanket. It held a plain bronze badge: the open eye of the office, with no cadet's bar across it. "The commission board met this morning. Three years is the requirement; the requirement stands; you'll serve every day of the remaining two and a half, because shortcuts are how this office got Dawlish. But the cadet bar comes off as of last night, by unanimous vote, on the grounds — I am quoting Senior Auror Savage's written motion, which I intend to have framed — that 'the title was making the rest of the profession look bad.' And for the record, Potter, since you'll be too polite to repeat it: it was Longbottom's moss, and Weasley's net, and your wall. I have run this office for ten months on the assumption that the three of you were a political decision. The minutes will show I have revised my assessment."

"And two," said Robards, at the door, not turning around, in the voice of a man leaving the last word where he could disown it: "there is a standing order in the operations room as of this morning, Miss Weasley, posted over my signature. Whatever else moves, the owl flies." And was gone, before anything could be done about anyone's face.

Chapter XII

After the Only Case

Ron2 April 1999 — by hand, across the barracks, four feet, because some letters need to be letters

Mate —

You'll have seen me writing this all week. Savage definitely has; he's started leaving the room with the tact of a hippopotamus.

The board's clean. That's the whole letter, really. You were standing next to me at the kitchen table in July when I set the terms in front of Mum and the Minister: when the list gets short, I'm out, and I go and make people laugh for a living. The list isn't short, Harry. The list is FINISHED. We finished it — you, me, Neville, the moss, the net, the wall — and I've been waiting three weeks to feel the catch, the "but maybe one more year," and it isn't coming, because there isn't a but. There's just the next right move, and I've been able to see it since December, and you know I don't move till I've seen it, and then I don't miss.

George needs the other half of the counter. Not for the stock or the books — Verity runs those better than either of us ever will. For the BIT, Harry. The shop runs on a double act and he's been doing it solo for a year, signing things "from us," doing both voices. I sat in the back room at Christmas and watched him test a new line on a kid — brilliant stuff, the kid was crying laughing — and George was watching the space next to himself the whole time, checking the timing with someone who wasn't there. I can't be Fred. Nobody's auditioning for Fred. But I can stand in the spot, and feed him the lines, and be a Weasley behind that counter, and that's the piece the board wants moved, and I'm the only piece that fits the square.

Resignation goes to Robards Monday. Wanted you to have it first, in writing, because we started this in writing — a kitchen table, witnesses, conditions — and because of the other thing, which I'll say once and then we're never speaking of it again and if you bring it up at the wedding I'll deny it:

it was an honour, Auror Potter. The whole eight years of it. Every board we ever played.

Now come to the shop Saturday and help me carry boxes, the new line launches in May and it's got your name on it. Not legally. Mostly not legally. We'll talk.

— R.

Neville's resignation went in the same Monday, which neither of them had planned and neither of them was surprised by; and his, being Neville's, was four sentences long, and Robards read it twice and filed it without one word of argument, because the first sentence was My application essay said there was one name on your board, and the board is clean, and you cannot argue a man out of a finished sentence.

What Robards did instead was walk him out — the full length of Level Two, past the operations room, where somebody had pinned to the board, in the empty space where eleven photographs used to hang, a single pressed sprig of dwarf bog-myrtle — and shake his hand at the lifts, and say, "The offer stands open for life, Longbottom. Both your parents' files say the same thing in the assessor's column, did you ever know that? One word. Thorough." And Neville said, "Yes, sir. I'm told I take after them," and went down in the lift with his face doing something complicated and good.

The letter from Hogwarts was waiting on his bunk that same evening, which was McGonagall's timing, which is to say: perfect, and never explained.

Pomona Sprout5 April 1999

to Neville Longbottom

Dear Neville,

I am writing to you before the notice goes to the Governors, because you are the reason for the notice and you ought to hear it from me, over a cup of something, except that I cannot wait for the cup.

I am retiring. Not this year — the year after next, in the summer of 2001, when the replanting of greenhouses one through six (YOUR replanting, I have told the Headmistress whose design it was, do not write back disputing it) is established enough to hand over. Herbology at Hogwarts requires a professor. I require an apprentice for two years who will then BE that professor. I have, in thirty years, written this letter once.

I watched you in the greenhouses last summer, dear boy — a war just over, the whole world wanting to give you medals, and you with your sleeves rolled, splinting a Whomping Willow sapling and talking to it. McGonagall tells me you then went and did the other thing, the necessary thing, the one with the warrant; the staff room read the Prophet aloud at breakfast and Filius stood on his chair. And now I am told the necessary thing is done, and so I will say what I have known since you were thirteen and the only child in a century to get a true answer out of a Mimbulus mimbletonia:

the Aurors borrowed you, Neville. The growing things had you first.

Come home. The greenhouses miss you. Greenhouse three in particular — I have kept a certain row of seedlings alive, the ones labelled H.A., and I should warn you they have flourished into a small hedge and will want explaining to someone.

With great pride, none of it surprised —

Pomona Sprout

He read it in the barracks with Harry and Ron reading it shamelessly over both shoulders, and the only thing he said for a long time was "Two years' apprenticeship. Starting September." And then, going slowly, magnificently red: "The Leaky's an easy Floo from Hogsmeade. For — logistical reasons. Hannah's taking over from Tom in the autumn; it's not announced, don't —" and the rest was lost in the noise, which carried to the duty desk, and was logged, by Senior Auror Savage, as celebration, justified.

Daily Prophet — page 430 April 1999

WIZENGAMOT PASSES GRANGER PROVISIONS AT FIRST READING

The Registration Repeal and Magical Persons (Equal Standing) Bill — informally, universally, and to its drafter's visible irritation known as "the Granger Provisions" — passed its first reading yesterday by a margin described as "thumping." The bill's drafter, currently sitting N.E.W.T.s, was excused testimony by the committee chair, who noted the supporting documentation was "four hundred pages and frankly conclusive." Minister Shacklebolt, asked whether an eighteen-year-old drafting landmark legislation from a school library set a concerning precedent, replied that the precedent had in fact been set some time ago, "and we are all simply living in it."

Harry stayed.

He went with Ron to the shop on the Saturday and carried boxes, which is what he had been invited to do, and witnessed something that was none of his business and that he would remember all his life, which is what he had actually been invited to do.

It happened in the middle of the morning rush, with the new line half-shelved and the queue out the door. A boy of about ten brought a Skiving Snackbox to the till and asked the question children had been asking since 1996 — did the Fainting Fancies work on teachers, like if a teacher ate one — and George, ringing it up, began the bit, the old bit, the one that needed two voices: "Ah, now, that would be an irresponsible use of our products, which we cannot condone —"

"— in writing," said Ron, from the next till, without looking up, in exactly the right register, on exactly the right beat. "We cannot condone it in writing. Verbally, between friends, with the receipt as your alibi: half a Fancy in the staffroom tea covers a whole Charms class, and we'd know."

"We tested it," said George.

"Extensively," said Ron.

"On Percy," said both of them, in chorus, without rehearsal, without ever once having done it before; and the boy went out the door glowing like a lamp, and the queue laughed, and George stood very still at his till for a moment with his hand flat on the counter. Harry, watching from the stockroom door with a crate of Punching Telescopes, saw him glance — quick, involuntary, twenty-one years of habit — at the space beside himself, the space he had been checking the timing with all year; except that this time the space had Ron in it, red-haired and solid and restocking the shelf with his ears going pink, and George looked at him for a second, and then up at the photograph over the till, and said, under the noise of the shop, something Harry was too far away to hear and did not need to.

("He said 'all right, fine, the trustworthy-looking one stays,'" Ron reported later, deeply pleased. "It's going on my next card.")

Harry stood in the King's Cross of it, the platform feeling, watching the two of them go off toward their right lives; and then on Monday morning he went back down to Level Two, to the operations room with the clean board and the pressed sprig of myrtle, and found that the room felt the way the Burrow felt, or the castle: like a place he was from now. Savage had the new rotations up. There was a stack of cold files — the small evils, the ones with no songs — and a memo about a cursed-object ring working the Welsh borders, and a seventeen-year-old in the corridor (Sloper, returned, limping, luminous) asking if there was room on anyone's team.

The how (he wrote that night, the Tuesday letter, the shoulder long healed and the writing back in the right hand): everyone's gone to their after, Gin, and it turns out I'm already standing in mine. Ron's got a counter and George's timing. Neville's got a greenhouse with a hedge to explain. Hermione's got a country to renovate. And I've got a clean board that won't stay clean — it never stays clean, that's the job, the job is what happens on the wet Tuesdays — and a badge with no bar on it, and Robards is teaching me warrants, and Savage called me 'Potter' today without making it sound like a diagnosis. I'm not staying because someone has to. I'm staying because when I walk past the board I feel the way you say you feel at the top of a penalty run: HERE. This one's mine. Three months till the train. Counting in Tuesdays now — it's only thirteen.

Chapter XIII

The Second of May

HOGWARTS SCHOOL OF WITCHCRAFT AND WIZARDRY — ORDER OF REMEMBRANCE
2 May 1999

Sunrise — The names. Read at the Memorial Stone by the Headmistress. The school will stand. The school will not be asked to be silent; those who need to weep are invited to consider it participation.

Morning — No lessons. The castle is open: every corridor, every tower, the grounds, the pitch. Walk where you need to. Staff will be where staff are needed, which the staff have been told is everywhere.

Midday — Luncheon on the lawns, weather permitting, and (Professor Flitwick wishes it recorded) weather has been arranged to permit.

One o'clock — In the Great Hall, for those who wish it: the photographs. Mr. D. Creevey of Gryffindor has, with the families' blessing, hung one hundred of his brother's pictures. The exhibition is titled WHAT COLIN SAW. It will become permanent.

Sunset — The thestral paddock. Miss Lovegood asks us to say only: come and see.

There will be no minute of silence this year. By vote of the survivors' families, there will instead be, at the minute of victory, one minute of noise. You will know the minute. The Headmistress requests that visitors with Weasley products deploy them OUT OF DOORS, and the Headmistress further notes, for the record, that she has been reliably informed there is one INSIDE her castle wall, and that it had better remain dormant, TODAY OF ALL DAYS, Mr. Weasley.

They were all there.

The Ministry had wanted a state occasion at the Ministry and had been overruled by an alliance of McGonagall, the families, and the Minister himself, who settled the matter in one sentence: "They didn't fall in the Atrium." So the state came to the school instead, and was made to queue like everyone else; and at sunrise on the second of May, one year on, the names were read off the white stone into a cold gold morning by Minerva McGonagall, who had buried students before and never once let her voice be anything but level for them, and was level for all fifty, and then sat down rather suddenly, and was handed, by Kingsley Shacklebolt, in front of four thousand people, a tartan handkerchief, which she took, and the four thousand loved them both for it.

Harry stood for the names between Ginny and Ron with a hand held in each, and the coins did what Luna had told the Hog's Head they would do: at the last name, fifty Galleons went warm at once — in pockets and on watch-chains and in headstones from Yorkshire to the Hebrides — Hermione's charm-work carrying one message around the rim, set by a vote of the membership: ALL PRESENT.

The day itself was the strange, holy, ordinary thing the order of service had built it to be. People walked. That was most of it: thousands of people walking the mended castle, laying hands flat on a certain stretch of seventh-floor wall until the queue for it ran down the stairs — Percy stood at that wall a long time, and George stood with him, and what they said was theirs; but witnesses report that at the end of it George knocked twice on the stones, gently, the way you knock on a door you have the right to, and said, "Behave in there," and walked on lighter. In the Great Hall, under WHAT COLIN SAW, Dennis stood all afternoon beside picture forty-one — Fred and George, mid-fireworks, 1996, heads thrown back — receiving the stories people needed to give him, banking them, sixteen years old and conducting a nation's grief with a kindness that made Healers in the crowd exchange professional glances. George's Hogsmeade stall, staffed by George, Ron, Angelina, and Verity in rotation, sold out entirely by three under the banner EVERY GALLEON TO THE FIFTY — and the till, counted that evening, contained roughly four times the value of the stock, because people had paid what the day was worth instead of what the merchandise cost, and George looked at the heap of it for a while and then had to go and stand behind the tent.

At the minute of victory, the school made its minute of noise, and it could be heard, the village swears, from Dervish and Banges to the Hog's Head: four thousand voices, every Weasley product out of doors deploying at once over the Black Lake in a barrage of golden foxes and silver dragons, the Gryffindor team flying formation through the middle of it with their captain's hair like a banner — and from inside a certain seventh-floor wall, at the precise minute, one single, muffled, unmistakable whizz-POP, followed by silence, followed — four thousand witnesses, sworn — by the castle's own deep stones somehow giving the impression of laughter; and Minerva McGonagall, on the lawn, was seen to look up at the seventh floor, and say something to herself that the people nearest insist was "Quite right. None of us can make those anymore," and dab her eyes with state tartan.

And at sunset, Luna led whoever followed — hundreds followed — down to the thestral paddock, where the herd stood waiting, black and patient and folded like umbrellas; and she said, in the carrying, unbothered voice of the only person who could: "A year ago today, lots of us learned to see them. I thought we should come and tell them we don't mind anymore." And the strangest, truest thing happened of that whole strange true day, which is that hundreds of people who had each thought their sight of the thestrals was a private wound stood together at one fence, all seeing the same beautiful animals in the last of the light, nobody alone in it, the herd moving gently among them taking strips of meat from a thousand hands with shocking delicacy; and Harry stood at the rail with Ginny's head on his shoulder, in the place where everything had been lost and everything had been won, on the day it all came back to be looked at, and found, when he checked — and he checked, the way you press a bruise — that it held. All of it held.

"One year," Ginny said.

"One year." The foal — a yearling now — was going through his pockets with its face. "Gin. The morning after — that first morning, in the Great Hall — if you'd told me about today. The stall, the wall, the noise —"

"You'd have believed me," said Ginny. "That's the thing about you nobody puts on the cards. You always believe me." She straightened up and faced him in the dusk, the castle lit gold behind her, every window. "Eight Tuesdays to the train, Potter. Race you to the rest of it."

Chapter XIV

The Train Coming Home

The Quidditch Cup final was played on the last Saturday of May, Gryffindor against Hufflepuff, under the first properly blue sky of the year, and the whole season came down to it like a sum coming down to its last line. Gryffindor arrived unbeaten: Slytherin demolished in November, Ravenclaw beaten in February in driving snow by a margin the Prophet called "frankly unsporting." Win this one, and it would be a perfect season — every match, no defeats, no draws, never once behind — the first that anyone could find in the school's records, and Madam Hooch, who kept the records, had spent the week looking.

The stands were full of people who had no business fitting into them. Harry was there on official leave, signed by Robards personally with morale written in the reason box, which was becoming the Ministry's word for Weasley; Ron and George came up together, having shut the shop for the day under a sign reading CLOSED FOR FAMILY FIXTURE — BACK BY THREE, NO REFUNDS ON THE SCORE; Hagrid took up most of a row; Professor Wrenn, who had announced in September that he did not understand Quidditch and did not intend to learn, had by May acquired a rosette, a scarf, and a complete loss of professional composure, and could be heard from the opposite stand. And in the top tier of the visitors' box, in plain green robes with her arms folded, sat Gwenog Jones, captain of the Holyhead Harpies, who had come to see one player and made no secret of it.

What she saw was the team Ginny Weasley had built from raw timber carrying her, and being carried, in roughly equal measure — which is the truest thing that can be said about a great captain. Cora Pike, who had apologised for goals in September, kept a clean sheet for the first hour and saved a penalty with her boot. Peakes and Coote, sixteen months rusty in autumn, played the Hufflepuff Beaters to a humiliating standstill. Natalie McDonald scored twice and made the pass of the match. And Ginny — Ginny scored six of Gryffindor's first seven goals, flying the way the school would describe it to younger students for years afterward, with the relentless, joyful, slightly frightening economy of somebody who has decided exactly what she is for; and then, with the game broken open, she stopped shooting and started giving the goals away — to Natalie, to Demelza, lobbing them the kind of passes that arrive as gifts — because a scout watches what you do when you've already won. Gryffindor were a hundred and ninety points up when Josie Kirke, thirteen years old and five stone soaking wet, came out of an orchard-honed dive with the Snitch held over her head in both fists, screaming, and was caught out of the air by her own captain before she remembered to pull up.

Final score: three hundred and forty to fifty. Played three, won three. A perfect record, the Cup, and a roar off the pitch that they heard in Hogsmeade.

McGonagall presented the Cup herself, and shook Ginny's hand a beat longer than ceremony required, and was seen to say something that made Ginny laugh and then blink hard — McGonagall confirmed the substance of it, years later, as: "The last Weasley to win me this Cup chose dragons over the league afterward, Miss Weasley, and I have spent eight years being gracious about it; I shall write to Romania tonight, and I shall enjoy it. And the rest of what I said was this: Miss Kirke caught that Snitch and looked for her captain before she looked for the crowd. They all do that, your team. Thirty years of presenting this Cup, and I have rarely seen it. Take it to Holyhead." And then Ginny Weasley, holding the Quidditch Cup, took a victory lap with the whole team strung out behind her, and at the teachers' stand she peeled off, dropped to a dead hover at the rail, took two fistfuls of Harry's jacket, and kissed him from broomback — one-handed, the Cup still in the other, in front of the entire school, the staff, the Hufflepuff team, the press, and Gwenog Jones — and four thousand people lost their minds, and the photograph went on the front page under the headline PERFECT, which everyone involved agreed covered the season, the kiss, and the general situation.

In the visitors' box, Gwenog Jones wrote one line in a small notebook, closed it, and left before the crowds. The line — Ginny learned it much later, framed, as a gift on a contract anniversary — read: Stopped scoring when the match was won. Sign her twice if allowed.

Ginny14 June 1999

RESULTS ENCLOSED. Read the parchment before this letter, I'll wait.

Read it? Good. Yes. SEVEN. Yes, the Defence one. The examiner — little wizard, Tofty, ancient, lovely — asked me to demonstrate "a Patronus, if you're able, no shame if not, it's N.E.W.T.-level work," and I gave him the horse at full gallop round the chamber, and he watched it the whole way round and then said, "Miss Weasley, in fifty-one years of examining I have learned to ask the next question, so: what is the memory?" And I said, "The morning after the battle, sir. Someone kept a promise." And he wrote something on his parchment for quite a long time and when I came out Professor McGonagall told me the practical examiners are not actually PERMITTED to award marks above Outstanding but that Tofty had attempted it.

(O in Defence, since you'll want it in writing. The most uncomfortable forty minutes of the examiner's career was apparently Michael Corner's Boggart. Long story. Buy me dinner.)

So that's that, Harry. That's school. Seven years — eight if you count the one we don't count, and we should count it, I earned that one twice — and on the last night I did the full circuit: the common room, the pitch at dusk, the wall (twice, you know the protocol), and the Great Hall ceiling doing a clear night absolutely covered in stars, and I sat where I sat that first morning when you came in grey as a ghost and I made room on the bench. I made room on the bench for the rest of my life, it turns out. Best tactical decision of my career and I include the Ravenclaw feint.

The scout's letter said the club regards the N.E.W.T. year as non-negotiable. PAID IN FULL. Trials July 9th. Holyhead. Be there or hear about it forever.

Last letter of the year, Potter. The next thing Tuesday delivers is ME.

— G.

P.S. He knows, somehow. The owl. He's been strutting along the Gryffindor table all week like a foreman at the end of a job. Forty-one Tuesdays, one black thread, zero misses. Give him the whole sausage on the 21st. He's earned the whole sausage.

The first of September belongs to the leavings, in every life that touches that platform; but the twenty-first of June belongs to the comings-home, and it is the better day, and nobody writes songs about it because the people who know are all busy being there.

Harry came early. He had a standing arrangement with the stretch of wall between the barrier and the coffee stand — third pillar, good sightline up the track, discovered the previous June — and he stood at it in the steam and racket with Tuesday on his shoulder (special dispensation, vigorously self-granted; the owl had simply attended) and a year of the platform's regulars nodding at him the way regulars do, leaving him be, the contract holding.

Ron arrived at ten past, in the magenta staff robes, carrying — "don't ask" — a long parcel that was visibly a broomstick wrapped badly, the shop's homecoming gift; Mr. and Mrs. Weasley at twenty past; Andromeda with Teddy on her hip at half past, Teddy's hair doing scarlet-engine red in honour of the occasion. Harry took the small familiar weight of him and pointed up the track.

"Watch the bend, Ted. Any minute."

The Hogwarts Express came round the bend at 5:58, gleaming, on time, inbound — the whole long scarlet length of it sliding in under the iron arches in a rolling wall of steam and noise and faces at windows — and somewhere in the crowd a hundred families surged forward, and the doors went down the train like applause.

She came out of the steam the way she had come down the Hogwarts lane in November, the red picked out of the crowd by some instrument in him that had never once needed adjusting: trunk handle in one fist, Quidditch case over her shoulder, captain's badge still pinned on out of seven years' habit, scanning — and finding, third pillar, instantly, like a compass fact. She left the trunk where it stood. (Ron rescued the trunk. Ron spent a portion of every great moment of his life rescuing the trunk, and would say at the wedding that he considered it his sacred office.)

She hit him at a flat run, and the kiss went on long enough for the applause to start, swell, and politely finish — a knot of third-years, two grandmothers, and a porter — and this time neither of them dispersed anybody, because school was over, and the year was over, and there was no rule left anywhere that applied.

"Hi," said Ginny, eventually, from approximately no distance.

"Hi." Harry found his voice had gone somewhere and sent the rest after it: "Welcome to the rest of it."

"You're here. This is the real version," she said — checking, the old joke with the whole year inside it — and Harry said what was simply, finally, administratively true:

"I'm here. This is the real version."

And Tuesday the owl, on his shoulder, having surveyed the platform, the steam, the family converging with the trunk, the two of them, and the general satisfactory completion of the route — forty-one deliveries, one black thread, zero misses — put his head under his wing, on duty, in public, at six o'clock in the evening, and went conspicuously, conclusively, off the clock; and that, Ginny maintained ever afterward, was the moment the year officially ended: not the kiss, which was for them, but the owl clocking off, which was for everyone.

Chapter XV

The Trial

The trial was on a Friday, at the Harpies' ground above Holyhead, with the Irish Sea hammering away at the bottom of the cliffs like a crowd warming up; and the only spectators permitted at a closed trial were club staff, which is why the figure leaning on the rail of the empty stand in a plain travelling cloak had been signed in, in Gwenog Jones's own hand, as equipment.

There were eleven triallists. The morning was drills — laps, gates, the bludger corridor, sprint-and-stop until two of the eleven were sick over the side — and Ginny flew it all the way she had flown the whole year, the way the school had stopped being able to look away from: with the relentless, joyful, slightly frightening economy of somebody who has decided exactly what she is for. The afternoon was a match, triallists salted into the first team; and somewhere in its second hour, the watching coaches stopped making notes on the others.

The thing that did it — the thing that made Gwenog Jones, who had seen everything twice, come up out of her seat — was not the hat-trick (though there was a hat-trick), and not the Ravenclaw feint executed at full speed against an international Keeper (though the Keeper discussed it for weeks). It was a nothing moment in the seventieth minute: a loose quaffle, a fifty-fifty ball, two fliers converging at combined speeds that make the professional game the professional game — and the other triallist flinched out of it, and Ginny did not, and took the ball, and scored; and as she came back round the hoops, Gwenog turned to her seconds-in-command and said the sentence that ended the trial as a competition: "She's been in worse. You can't coach been in worse."

The contract meeting afterward was four people in a wood-panelled office smelling of liniment and sea: Gwenog, the club secretary, Ginny with her hair still wet from the showers, and — by the player's request, noted in the minutes — the equipment, standing by the door.

Gwenog Jones did not sit so much as occupy. She put her boots on the desk, opened her small notebook, and read from it without preamble.

"Three things I watch at a closed trial, Weasley, and none of them is goals. One: what you do in the fifty-fifties. You went in level every time, including against Morgan, who has put two internationals in St. Mungo's, which you knew, because you do your reading." She turned the page. "Two: what you do when it goes wrong. Seventieth minute, your wing collapsed, and you didn't shout — you moved. Reorganised the whole attack mid-air without a word, which tells me the shouting you did at Hogwarts was a choice, captain to children, and you can take it off like a kit bag. Three." She closed the notebook. "What you do when you've already won. You stopped scoring. Started giving them away — proper gifts, wrapped, with the defender drawn off first. Twenty minutes of making other people look good for an audience of one scout. You did the same thing in your school final — I was in the stands, and it's in this notebook twice now, which makes it a pattern, and patterns are what I sign." She let the silence sit. "Skill I can buy by the yard, girl. Every trial, eleven of you turn up with skill. What I can't coach is somebody who's been in worse and knows it, and plays like the score is a fact instead of a feeling. You flew today like the worst thing that can happen on a Quidditch pitch already happened to you somewhere else. That's the whole signing. Morgan!" — this last in a bellow toward the door — "tell the secretary to bring the parchment before the Hogwarts one changes her mind."

"I won't be changing my mind," said Ginny. "I've wanted this since I was six."

"I know," said Gwenog. "I've read your letters. You wrote to this club eleven times between the ages of six and nine, and somebody in the front office had the wit to keep them, and when your name came up for trials I had them fetched out of the archive and read them all in one sitting." For one moment the granite face moved, somewhere around the eyes. "The fourth one informs me that you already had a broom, your brother's old Shooting Star, and were not afraid of bludgers or of boys. The club's position is that the scouting file was complete in 1988 and the rest of this has been paperwork."

Then she looked past Ginny, at the door, at the equipment.

"You. Potter." Gwenog took her boots off the desk and leaned forward. "I watched the stands today as much as the sky — that's the job too. You sat on your hands for four hours and you never once looked away from the match to check who was looking at you, which for a man with your problem is an Outstanding on the only exam I set. So hear the terms of your contract, which is unwritten but enforceable. The first morning the back pages decide her season is a story about you, I lose a Chaser and Britain loses the best signing of the decade. Clause nine exists so that morning never comes. The club will spend what it must. Your job is one thing: be exactly what you were today — present, glad, and silent in the press — for about two seasons, until her name stands on its own ground. Can you do two seasons?"

"I can do forty years," said Harry. "Sign it in blood if you like. I've got a quill that does that."

Gwenog Jones looked at him for a moment, and then at Ginny, and delivered her verdict to the room at large: "He'll do. Secretary! The parchment."

HOLYHEAD HARPIES Q.C. — MEMORANDUM OF TERMS — G. M. WEASLEY (CHASER)
9 July 1999

1. Reserve squad from 1 August 1999; full training rights; the management's stated expectation, recorded at the player's insistence as an expectation and not a promise, is first-team debut within the season, ON MERIT.

2. Wages per the standard reserve schedule, Clause 4. (The player is advised the club has noted, with approval, that she did not ask about Clause 4 until page two.)

3. CLAUSE 9 — inserted by the Captain, non-negotiable from the CLUB's side: No press obligation of any kind shall attach to the player's private life. The club's press office will answer all enquiries touching upon any individual or individuals connected to the player with the club's standard formulation ("She's here to play"), indefinitely, at the club's own cost. The Captain wishes the player to understand that the Harpies signed a Chaser, the Chaser's name is Weasley, and the club will spend whatever it must to keep the back pages about the front of the shirt.

4. The player's owl shall be accommodated at the club owlery. The club notes the owl appears to believe he holds a staff position. The club has elected not to contest this.

Signed: G. Jones (Capt.) — G. M. Weasley — (witnessed: H. J. Potter, listed per club sign-in sheet as "equipment," a designation the witness has asked to keep)

They had the celebration dinner at the Burrow, because of course they did — the table outside under the apple trees, everyone, the whole clock's worth: Ron and George straight from the shop still smelling of gunpowder, Hermione down from a Wizengamot committee with ink on her ear, Neville and Hannah up from London with a crate of the Leaky's best and a small ferocious hedge-cutting in a pot ("housewarming," said Neville, "for eventually; it travels well"), Andromeda and Teddy, Percy with the Ministry's official congratulations and his own separate, shyer ones, Fleur and Bill, the Grangers; and Molly cooked for thirty and cried into none of it where anyone could prove it, and Arthur read the clock last thing, the way some men say their prayers, and found all eleven hands at home, and went up to bed a rich man.

And late that night, in his old room at the top of the house out of pure habit, Harry sat down at the window with a quill and started the Tuesday letter — Gin — the real version of today — and got eleven words in before the fact of it arrived, and he laughed at himself, alone, quietly, at one in the morning: she was one floor down. There was no distance left to cross. For the first time in forty-one Tuesdays the letter had nowhere to go.

He wrote it anyway. Some habits you keep because they made you.

Harry9 July 1999 — the letter Tuesday never carried

Gin —

The real version of today: you flew like the reason brooms got invented, Gwenog Jones said a thing about you I'm saving for your slow days, and I stood at a rail above the Irish Sea signed in as "equipment" and couldn't tell, for whole minutes at a time, where being proud of you stopped and just being glad started. I don't think there's a border. I've looked.

It's one in the morning at the Burrow. You're asleep one floor down — I can hear the house doing its noises, and none of the doors have edges, I checked on the way up, old habit, the good kind now. The clock in the kitchen says HOME eleven times. Tuesday's on the perch in the kitchen window with his head under his wing, off the clock since June, milking retirement, pretending he doesn't hear the mice.

A year ago you stood on a platform and told me to write you the real version on the nights the dreams were bad, and I want the accounting on paper, so: forty-one Tuesdays. Some of them were the real version of bad — the silence, the ghyll, the ward. Most of them were just days, which you taught me was the whole prize. And every one of them, the letter went and the day held, and somewhere in the middle of all that scheduled honesty I stopped bracing, Gin. That's the year's news, under all the other news. The flinch you said we were working on. I had to go looking for it tonight to be sure. It's gone.

So here's the last entry of the year, for the file, in writing, witnessed by an owl:

I'm going to marry you. Not yet — I know the plan, the season first, the flat near the ground, first-team debut ON MERIT, I've read your contract more times than my own commission — but it's not a someday anymore, it's a date I haven't learned yet, and you deserve to have it in ink the way we put everything that matters in ink: there's a village in the West Country with a gate that creaks, and a kitchen that needs to be small enough to bump into you in, and a clock wanting two hands to start with and then we'll see, and a wall somewhere that our kids will touch without knowing why until we tell them. I've seen it the way you see the goal before the move. Every Tuesday between now and then is just the route.

The forehead one's history's. The chest one's yours — by claim, and by proof, and (I had it checked at St. Mungo's, you'll want the documentation) by nature: still keeping time, steady, all year, every year.

It kept time. It keeps time. So do I. So does the owl.

Steady as Tuesday —

H.

(unsent — folded small, and tucked into the torn lining of the trunk lid, where a boy had once kept a shard of mirror and half a map: the place he kept the things he wasn't finished with.)

Chapter I

Removal Vans

The van was Hermione's idea, which surprised nobody, and a Muggle van, which surprised everybody, and her father drove it, which turned out to be the entire point.

"You can fit a flat's worth of furniture in a beaded bag," she explained to Ron, on the first Saturday of September, supervising the loading of a perfectly ordinary hired Transit outside her parents' perfectly ordinary house, while her father and Mr. Weasley wrestled a bookcase down the path in a state of joint bliss. "I've done it. I once fit a tent in a handbag, along with a portrait, a chandelier's worth of books, and your entire wardrobe, which took up less room than the books and smelled worse. That's not the point of moving house. The point of moving house is that your dad carries something heavy, and complains about the stairs, and stands in the empty room at the end with his hands on his hips saying well, that's that, then — and mine has missed every single one of my movings. Eleven years of them. Trunks onto trains, trunks off of trains, a tent across a country. He gets this one." She consulted her list. "Also, Dad, the bookcase goes on its back, or the doors swing."

"She labelled the boxes by subject," Mr. Granger told Mr. Weasley, with the weary pride of a man displaying a wonder of the world. "Then alphabetised the subjects."

"We had to stop her sister doing the same with the gnomes," said Mr. Weasley companionably, and the two of them disappeared into a conversation about load distribution that lasted, on and off, four years.

It was the month of removal vans. All over the wreckage-cleared, two-summers-mended world, the generation that had won the war was doing the thing nobody writes songs about, which is carrying its childhood up other staircases. And because this is the story of all of them, it is worth setting down plainly where everybody woke up on the first Monday of that September, the first Monday of the rest of it.

Ginny Weasley woke at half five in a narrow bed in Holyhead, in the top-floor room of a tall salt-streaked boarding house kept by a Mrs. Prosser, who had been letting rooms to Harpies for forty years and had opinions about all of them. The room had a slanted ceiling, a view of grey Welsh sea, a wardrobe that smelled of other people's seasons, and — as of Sunday evening, installed by its owner with a spell-o-taped diagram and a quantity of language — a perch by the window, bolted to the sill, with a brass plate that Ron had had engraved as a moving-in present: TUESDAY. ALL HOURS. CLAUSE 4.

She lay still for one minute, listening to the sea and the gulls and the entire unfamiliar weight of it — professional athlete, six o'clock kit run, two hundred miles from everyone whose footsteps I know — and then she got up, because she was Ginny Weasley, and the minute was the ration.

On the washstand was Monday's letter, which had arrived Sunday so as to be there for exactly this, and which she had saved for exactly this, and she read it standing in the cold light while the kettle went.

Gin —

By the time you read this you'll have heard the sea all night and be about to run six miles of Welsh headland before breakfast, and I want the record to show I knew the order of operations: read first, then run. Some things about you are fixtures of the calendar of the universe.

The house did the thing last night where it's too quiet, so I went down and ate toast with Kreacher at midnight and he told me about Holyhead. He says the Blacks considered the Harpies "the only acceptable club" on the grounds that a great-aunt of Sirius's once sent a Howler to the Minister for Magic over a disallowed goal and got a public apology. Gin, I checked the family tree. It's TRUE. Her name was Dorea. Fly like a Black, apparently, is a compliment.

New rule for the new age, proposed for ratification: the letters keep Tuesday, whatever the fixture list does to us. Everything else in both our diaries is about to belong to other people — Robards owns my Thursdays, Gwenog owns your everything — but Tuesday stays ours, and when the season gets loud we defend it like a hoop.

Run well. Mind the headland. Make them all sorry.

— H.

P.S. — Ratify by owl. He's sulking on the new perch like a retired colonel. He'll cheer up the moment there's a route.

She wrote RATIFIED across the bottom in handwriting still warm from sleep, added three lines a future biographer was never going to be shown, and gave it to the colonel, who took one look at the grey two hundred miles between himself and London and visibly remembered why he existed.

Then she ran the headland in the rain, first of the squad out, and Mrs. Prosser, watching the small fierce figure go up into the weather, made the assessment she had been making about Harpies for forty years and was never once wrong about, and put an extra rasher on for the girl's return.

Hermione Granger arrived at the Ministry of Magic at eight o'clock sharp, which she had calculated was early enough to show willing and late enough not to look frightened, and was directed by a welcome-witch with a securely suppressed smirk down to Level Four, along a corridor that got progressively further from the lifts, around a corner that the architecture itself seemed to have forgotten about, to a door bearing a fresh brass plate.

The plate said: H. GRANGAR — BEING DIVISION (ASSISTANT, PROBATIONARY).

The office behind it had been, in living memory, a broom cupboard, and the brooms had left grudgingly and not entirely. There was a desk that touched three walls at once. There was a chair, and most of a second chair. There was a filing cabinet whose bottom drawer was an inch open, immovably, against something inside that had given up its shape years ago; and on the desk, squarely centred, with a note in a senior hand — Miss Granger. A starting brief, to learn the files. No urgency attaches. — O. Gamp — sat a single folder, thin as a sympathy card, labelled HOUSE-ELF WELFARE: STANDING MATTERS.

Hermione hung up her coat on the hook (the hook came off the wall; she repaired the hook; the hook would not dare again), sat down in the whole chair, and opened the folder. It contained four items: a regulation from 1811; an amendment to the regulation from 1816, withdrawing it; a memo from 1923 proposing a committee; and a memo from 1924, regretting the committee.

She read all four. Then she sat for a moment in the broom cupboard at the forgotten end of Level Four — the brightest witch of her age, the woman whose legislation had passed its first reading to a thumping majority while she was still sitting school exams, thirteen days short of twenty years old — and understood, with perfect clarity, what this room was. It was not an oversight. It was a burial plot. Somewhere upstairs, men like the senior hand on that note had read her file, and seen her coming, and prepared, with the unhurried craft of their kind, a place where relentlessness goes to be slowly filed to death.

Hermione Granger took out a fresh sheet of parchment, wrote across the top of it REQUISITION: COMPLETE REGISTRY OF BONDED HOUSE-ELVES, ALL HOLDINGS, 1811–PRESENT, and smiled the smile that three Death Eaters and one Dolores Umbridge had learned about too late.

"No urgency," she said, to the brooms. "Wonderful. I have years."

Ron Weasley opened the shop.

It does not sound like much, set down like that, and it was the whole of everything: at half past eight on Monday morning, George Weasley handed his younger brother the keys to Weasleys' Wizard Wheezes — the actual keys, the founding keys, one of which was a Muggle key that fit nothing and had been on the ring since the beginning because Fred liked the weight of it — and announced that he had a supplier to see in Birmingham and would not be back before two.

"There's no supplier in Birmingham," said Ron.

"There's no supplier in Birmingham," George agreed. "There's a café in Birmingham where I'm going to read the paper for five hours. Verity's on the till at ten. The new line's in the back, the Floo's listed, and the answer to the boy who comes in at nine fifteen every Monday and asks what's the most annoying thing a Galleon buys is the Whistling Wart, and you upsell him the batteries, which it doesn't take, which is what's annoying about it." He put on his coat with the air of a man boarding a ship. "Shop's yours, Ronnie. Mondays are now structurally your problem. I'd intended to ease you in gradual, but Angelina pointed out, Saturday, that I've been easing you in gradual since April, and that gradual is just scared wearing a waistcoat. So." He stopped at the door; the bell hung off its bracket, waiting. "He'd have given you the keys in May, you know. I'm the careful one. Nobody ever once believed it, but I'm the careful one."

He was gone before anything could be done about it, which was a family trait, and Ron Weasley stood alone in the best joke shop in the world — his name over the door in a plural that now included him, the morning sun coming up the Alley and in through the great bow window, the Pygmy Puffs starting their day shift of being adorable, the portrait of two grinning identical boys watching him from above the till — and turned the keys over in his hand once, and said, to the portrait, "Right. Don't help."
