He took nine Galleons in the first hour, upsold the batteries at nine fifteen on schedule, talked a frightened witch through a refund she was owed and too polite to demand, and rearranged the window display on a hunch — the new line front and centre, the classics in a pyramid behind, a hand-lettered card reading ASK US WHY THE BATTERIES — that lifted the morning's take forty percent before Verity ever arrived. He did the sums at noon, twice, because he liked them. The shop, the keys, the till, the whole improbable architecture of his after — and underneath the floorboards of the morning, faint but present, like the smell of gunpowder in the walls: the other thing. The thing he had not said to anyone except Hermione, and to her only sideways. Half this shop is a dead man's mind. Every joke I sell is his. Sooner or later I find out whether there's a Weasley brain in here that's mine.

That reckoning has a chapter of its own, further along. On the first Monday it stayed under the floorboards, where reckonings keep; and at two o'clock George came back from Birmingham smelling of coffee and newspaper, took one look at the window display, and stood very still in the doorway for a moment in a way Ron pretended not to see.

"The batteries card," George said at last.

"Too much?"

"Fred tried for two years to find the line for that window," said George, going through to the back, voice perfectly level, leaving the bell still ringing behind him. "Ask us why the batteries. Two years, and the answer was a question. He'll be unbearable about it wherever he is. Put your sums away, the till believes you, nobody likes a gloater."

And Harry Potter went to work, in the manner of the other three hundred people who went to work in the Auror Office that Monday, except for one thing, which happened at the lifts.

He was twenty-five days past the end of his probationary field year. The bronze badge on his chest had no cadet's bar across it and had not since March; the commission still had two years to run, and Robards still owned his Thursdays, and the cold-case stack on his desk was still the small unsung evil that the job actually was — a cursed-object ring working the Welsh borders, a vanished warlock in Kent, the slow paper war against the world's leftover cruelty. Nothing about the Monday was remarkable. That was the remarkable thing.

At the lifts, a wizard he had never met — elderly, Magical Maintenance by his coverall, a spanner that was visibly more than a spanner in his belt — looked at the scar, the way half of Britain looked at the scar, and then did the new thing, the thing that had been slowly replacing the old thing all year: looked at the badge instead, and nodded, one workman to another, and said, "Morning, Auror," and went back to his paper.

Auror. Not the Boy Who Lived. Not even Potter. A job, nodded at, by a man with a job.

Harry stood in the lift with his case files under his arm, grinning like an idiot at the brass grille, all the way down to Level Two.

Chapter II

The Being Division

The registry arrived on the fourth day, and it arrived the way Odo Gamp intended everything in his Division to arrive, which was as a punishment.

Two wizards from Records brought it on a hover-trolley: one hundred and nine ledgers, leather-bound, each the size of a paving slab, comprising the complete registry of bonded house-elves in the British Isles — opened in 1811, maintained with imperial thoroughness until about 1830, and then kept up, for the following century and a half, in the spirit of a diary abandoned in February. The trolley would not fit through the cupboard door. The ledgers were stacked, instead, in the corridor, in a wall, with a memo on top.

Miss Granger — Your requisition is fulfilled. Do return them in order. The Division's resources regrettably do not extend to clerical assistance for probationary staff, and I must ask that the corridor remain passable for those with urgent duties. — O.G.

Hermione stood in the corridor and read the memo twice. Then she looked at the wall of ledgers for a long moment, with an expression that anyone who had hunted Horcruxes with her would have recognised and stepped well back from, and went to find out exactly who Odo Gamp was.

It took her one lunch hour, the Ministry archive, and two cups of tea with the welcome-witch (whose smirk, it turned out, had been aimed at the nameplate, not the witch, and who became an ally for life the day Hermione had it corrected by owl-order at her own expense rather than file the form that would have crossed Gamp's desk). Odo Gamp: sixty-eight years old; Head of the Being Division for twenty-six of them; a Gamp of the Gamps, the old pure-blood line, too canny ever to have touched the Death Eaters but bred from the same deep water; survivor of nine Ministers, two regimes, and every reform ever pointed in his direction. He had never once said no to anything. That was the entire secret of him. He said in due course. He said when resources allow. He said a committee might fruitfully consider. He was, the welcome-witch said, with the precision of twenty years at the front desk, "the place where ideas go to get old."

"He approved my requisition in a day," said Hermione. "If he wanted to bury me, why not sit on it for a year?"

"Because the requisition is the burial, dear." The welcome-witch stirred her tea. "A hundred and nine ledgers and no clerk. You're meant to spend two years cataloguing, file a report he'll thank you for beautifully, and be reassigned somewhere harmless by a man with a record of having given the famous Miss Granger everything she ever asked for. He's done it to eleven reformers. I've watched every one come down that lift on their first Monday, all lit up." She looked at Hermione over her cup with something that was not quite hope yet, but was checking its calendar. "They were all clever. Not one of them was organised."

Hermione went back down to Level Four, and stood before the wall of ledgers, and did the thing none of the eleven had done: she read the punishment all the way to the end.

It took her five weeks. She did it methodically, evenings included, with a system of colour-coded slips that eventually required its own box; and somewhere in the third week, in Ledger 61, in the brown ink of 1849, she stopped taking notes for organisation and started taking them for ammunition — because the registry, it turned out, was not a record of elves. It was a record of transfers. Bonded elves conveyed with houses, like fixtures; conveyed in wills, like furniture; conveyed, in four entries she copied out with a perfectly steady hand and read to Ron that night in a perfectly unsteady voice, in settlement of gambling debts, with the elf's name misspelled differently on each side of the transaction because nobody involved had thought it worth checking against the only being who knew it.

And the law that governed every one of those transfers — the load-bearing statute under the whole edifice, cited in margin after margin for a century — was the Regulation of 1811. The one in her sympathy-card folder. The one that had been withdrawn in 1816, by the amendment in the same folder, and reinstated by no Act she could find, because there wasn't one: somewhere in the 1820s a clerk had simply gone back to citing the old regulation, and every clerk since had cited the clerk before, and the entire legal architecture of elf bondage in Britain had been resting, for one hundred and eighty years, on a paperwork error.

Hermione sat in her broom cupboard at nine o'clock at night with Ledger 61 open under the lamp and both hands flat on the desk, and breathed.

"You gave me the foundations," she said, to the absent Mr. Gamp, quite calmly. "You buried me in the foundations of your own house, and nobody's ever read them, and the foundations aren't there."

She did not, of course, simply announce it. She had been at the Ministry five weeks, and she had spent seven years watching what happened to true things announced without groundwork. What she did instead was go and see Percy.

Percy Weasley, twenty-three, had spent the year since the war doing penance the only way Percy knew, which was competence: he had attached himself to the Minister's office as the man who actually read things, and Kingsley — who had learned in the Order to value any wand that did what it said it would do at the hour it said it would — had quietly made him indispensable. He kept his brother's photograph on his desk. He came to Sunday dinner every Sunday, early, and helped his mother set the table, and the family had stopped holding their breath about it. And he received Hermione in his small immaculate office, listened to forty-five minutes of registry evidence with his fingers steepled, and then said the sentence that founded an alliance which would, over the following decade, quietly renovate an entire government.

"You've found a hole in the law," Percy said. "Now you must on no account fall into it yourself. May I tell you how this building actually works?"

"Please."

"You are thinking of going to a committee and proving that the regulation lapsed. Don't. The moment you prove the foundations are missing, every interested party in Britain unites to pour new ones — and they will be worse, Hermione, they will be 1811 with modern draftsmanship, passed in a panic at speed, and your name will be on the panic. Gamp would help you do it. He'd thank you in the minutes." Percy took off his glasses and polished them, and for a moment he looked very like his father, which would once have horrified him and now would not. "Here is what you do instead. You do not attack the hole. You occupy it. You draft the replacement first — complete, costed, boring, with a schedule of transition that gives every great house a face-saving decade and every elf an exit that no one can frame as theft of property, because you will have established, from their own registry, in their own ink, that they never lawfully owned anything. You build the entire new floor in private. And then you invite the Ministry to notice the hole — at the hour you choose, in the week the press you choose is hungry, with the only available solution already written, in your handwriting." He put the glasses back on. "It will take you a year, perhaps two. Gamp will believe the whole time that you are cataloguing. Let him. The Sorting Hat wanted me in Slytherin, you know," he added, with the ghost of the only joke Percy Weasley ever made about himself, "and I have decided to stop wasting it."

Hermione looked at him for a long moment, with the registry slips fanned in her lap like a winning hand nobody else could read yet.

"Percy," she said, "I could kiss you."

"Please don't. My brother is enormous, and I've only just got the family back."

She drafted all winter. The broom cupboard filled, slip by slip and clause by clause, with the quiet architecture of the thing that would one day be called the Elf Welfare and Standing Act — though the folder on her desk, the one Gamp's eye would slide across on his stately quarterly visits to commend her diligence among the ledgers, was labelled, in her roundest, most probationary hand: REGISTRY CATALOGUING: INTERIM NOTES, VOL. I.

There was one more scene that winter that belongs to this chapter, and then the chapter can close.

On a Friday evening in late November, Hermione went down to the Hogwarts kitchens — she had written ahead; she was, after all, doing a registry survey, and it was even true — and sat at the long scrubbed table where four house tables' worth of food was made, with a hundred elves working around her in the firelight, and asked the question her seven-year-old campaign had never once thought to ask, because S.P.E.W. had been founded by a fourteen-year-old who knew everything about injustice and nothing yet about people.

She asked what they wanted.

It took three hours and four pots of tea, and an elderly elf named Pomona's Tilly doing most of the talking once it was established that the Miss truly meant the question; and Hermione walked back up to the entrance hall afterward with eleven pages of notes and her entire bill half-rewritten in her head — freedom to, not just freedom from; the bond reformed before abolished, wages and exit and standing first, dignity defined by the dignified — and Winky's name on a list of her own making, under a heading that read, simply, FIRST.

Dobby would have come with her to the kitchens, she thought, standing in the dark of the carriage road with the castle lit up gold behind her. Dobby would have heckled.

"It's going to be slow," she said out loud, to the memory, to the cold air, to the long game ahead. "It's going to be slow, and boring, and built out of paperwork, and nobody will ever write songs about a transition schedule. And it's going to work, and they're going to call it inevitable afterward, the way they always do." She pulled her scarf up. "You'd have hated every minute of the meetings. I'll do those. You did the hard part."

And she went home to the flat that didn't exist yet, which is to say to her parents' house, where her mother had kept dinner warm, and her father wanted to hear about her week, and there was a removal-van's worth of childhood up the stairs in alphabetised boxes, waiting, like everything that winter, for the rest of it to start.

Chapter III

Reserve Squad

The first thing professional Quidditch took off Ginny Weasley was the title.

At Hogwarts she had been the captain, the general, the name on the board; at Holyhead she was the reserve squad's newest Chaser, which meant she was the one who carried the ball crate, pumped the bludger restraints before training, and answered to "new girl" from anyone with a first-team shirt — a system Gwenog Jones maintained with complete impartiality and had survived herself, and which Ginny accepted without one word of complaint, because she had grown up the youngest of seven and knew exactly what the system was for. You carried the crate so that everyone could see what carrying it did to you. Some girls carried it like a grievance. Ginny carried it like a warm-up.

The second thing it tried to take was harder, and it had a voice.

The voice belonged, mostly, to Carys Pugh — first-team Chaser, thirty-one years old, fourteen seasons, twice capped for Wales, with a face full of weather and a left-handed reverse pass that Ginny had cut out of the Prophet and pinned to her wall when she was eleven. For the first month Carys Pugh did not speak to her at all. Then came the October press day, and a reporter from Quidditch Weekly asked the room at large whether the club had signed the Weasley girl for her flying or her photographs — given, he said, pencil already moving, the obvious commercial appeal of a certain connexion — and the club's press officer was opening her mouth around the famous formulation, she's here to play, when Carys Pugh got there first.

"Took us eleven trialists and four hours to pick her," said Carys, lacing a boot, not looking up. "If she'd come in the door dating a troll we'd have signed her ten minutes sooner, that's all the difference the boyfriend makes." And then, as the room relaxed and the quills caught up, she finished the lacing, stood, and added, with fourteen seasons of flat Welsh finality: "Mind, she's not done it yet. Reserve hat-tricks against Caerphilly seconds aren't doing it. Ask me in March whether she's a Harpy or a headline. I'll tell you honest."

It was, Ginny wrote to Harry that Tuesday, the single most useful thing anyone had said about her in print all autumn:

— because she's RIGHT, Harry, that's the thing. The reporter wanted me to be a story, and the press office wants me to be a non-story, and Carys is the only one in the building talking about me like a CHASER. "She's not done it yet." I've pinned it over my bed, next to her reverse pass, which she still won't teach me, which is also correct, because you don't give the new girl the pass, you make her steal it. I'm going to do the thing, and then I'm going to ask her to say it again in March, and then I'm going to buy her a drink and make her teach me the pass. This is the plan. Approve the plan.

(Approved. — H., by return owl, with the further note that he had once spent a year being talked about as a story by an entire country and the only cure he ever found was a redhead who treated him like a Seeker.)

The doing of the thing arrived, as such things do, out of a clear sky and somebody else's bad luck, on the second Saturday of January.

It had been a hard, strange autumn underneath the crate-carrying: the six a.m. headlands, the tactical school of Gwenog's sessions — Hogwarts Quidditch, Ginny discovered, was to this what a garden is to a farm — the Tuesday letters holding the line of her like a handrail; one league match watched entire from the reserve bench with her hands going white on the rail; Christmas at the Burrow squeezed into thirty-six hours of leave, where she had slept eleven hours, eaten her bodyweight, kissed Harry under her mother's mistletoe to general applause, and been informed by George that her crate-carrying was now the shop's third-best-selling photograph, which was a lie that did her more good than most of that month's truths. And underneath it all the ledger she kept where Harry kept his: they think I'm a headline. Page intentionally left blank. To be completed in March.

It completed itself early. On the second Saturday of January, away to the Appleby Arrows in a crosswind that grounded the morning's racing, the Harpies lost both first-team wing Chasers inside an hour — one to a bludger that broke her arm in two places, one to a hamstring on the frozen turf — and Ginny Weasley came on at the hour mark, in sleet, two hundred and forty miles from anyone whose footsteps she knew, with the Harpies ten points down.

What happened in the next ninety minutes was set down soberly in the match report and is best given soberly here, because it needs no help: she scored four and made two, into a crosswind, against the league's second-best defence; she took the Arrows' famous mid-pitch press apart so methodically that their captain changed it twice and then abandoned it; and in the eighty-fourth minute, ten feet above the gorse with the rain going sideways, she played a left-handed reverse pass — Carys Pugh's own pass, stolen off fourteen years of newspaper cuttings and four months of watching at training, executed first time in match conditions — to put Carys Pugh herself in alone for the goal that broke the game.

The Harpies won by two hundred. The crowd, what Appleby weather had left of it, stood for her. And in the changing room afterward, while the medi-witch worked down the line of them and the sleet hammered the roof, Carys Pugh crossed the room with two mugs of tea, handed one over, and delivered, in front of the squad, the full and entire retraction of Carys Pugh:

"That was my pass."

"It was," said Ginny. "I stole it. You wouldn't teach me."

"No," agreed Carys, sitting down beside her, "and look how well that worked. We'll fix your release point Tuesday — you're a quarter-beat early, it's why it wobbled." She drank her tea. The room had gone very quiet around the two of them, in the way of rooms that know they are watching a door open. "I'm asking to be quoted, mind," Carys added, to the room, in her flat press-day voice. "March came early. She's a Harpy."

Quidditch Weekly17 January 2000

WEASLEY ANNOUNCES HERSELF AT APPLEBY — AND THE HARPIES' DRESSING ROOM ANNOUNCES HER BACK

Whatever else was said about Holyhead's most-photographed reserve before Saturday — and this publication said its share — the record now shows the following: four goals and two assists on a full debut, in conditions that had senior internationals asking for the anemometer reading, including a reverse pass in the Pugh manner that drew from Pugh herself the rarest tribute in Welsh sport, namely a sentence. "March came early," the fourteen-season veteran told this reporter, on the record, by her own request. "She's a Harpy." The club's press office, asked for further comment on the player's much-discussed personal connexions, repeated its standard formulation. For the first time in this correspondent's experience, it appears to be the whole story: she is, on this evidence, very much here to play.

The clipping went to London by the next owl, annotated in the margin in Ginny's hand — page completed, two months ahead of schedule — and it hangs today, framed, in a kitchen in the West Country, next to a photograph that doesn't move; and the release point was fixed on the Tuesday, as promised, and the drink was bought, and by April, when the Prophet attempted one last "boyfriend signing" item ahead of the Harpies' London fixture, it was answered not by the press office but by an unprecedented joint letter to the editor, signed by the entire Holyhead first-team squad, which read, in full: "Come and chase her, then."

Chapter IV

The Ideas Ledger

There was a drawer in the back office of Weasleys' Wizard Wheezes that neither brother opened, and they did not talk about it, and it ran the shop.

Not the famous drawer — the will drawer, DO NOT OPEN (NO REALLY), long since opened, its instruction now framed on the office wall where the binding clause could supervise (laugh at least once a day, Potter; that's an order from a dead man). The other one. Bottom left of the partners' desk, brass handle gone green, and inside it — Ron knew, because George had told him exactly once, on Ron's first day, in the voice of a man fencing off a minefield — the ledger.

"Fred's ideas book," George had said. "Every notion he ever had, the second he had it. He wrote them down half-finished on purpose — said finishing them was my job, said he was the lightning and I was the — anyway. It's in there. I put it in there in May, the year of, and I know exactly what's in it, which is about nine products and a hundred and forty beginnings, and I can't —" He had shut the subject the way he shut the drawer, with his foot, gently. "It's not yours and it's not mine. It's ours, and half of ours isn't here, so it stays in the drawer. Stock's this way."

So the ledger sat in the dark all autumn, the shop's sleeping conscience, while above it the shop itself did something nobody had planned for, which was boom — and boom, specifically, along lines that kept tracing back to the younger brother.

It was Ron who noticed that the Hogwarts owl-order forms spiked every Sunday night and proposed the Weekend Wheeze, a rotating Sunday-only special that turned the spike into a flood. It was Ron who looked at the queue data — there was queue data; he had started keeping it in a notebook of his own, columns ruled with a wand, because a queue was just a chessboard where every piece was bored — and split the till in two, classics left, new lines right, cutting the Saturday wait in half and the Saturday walkouts to nothing. It was Ron who renegotiated the Honeydukes wholesale arrangement by the simple expedient of working out, three moves in advance, what Honeydukes' new owner needed more than money (a Hogsmeade ally against the Zonko's revival) and trading it. George, watching this last performance from the doorway of the back office, was heard to say, to the portrait over the till, "He's doing gambits, Freddie. On confectioners."

And it was Ron, on a wet Thursday in February, restocking the chess sets — they carried a standard line, enchanted but ordinary, the pieces argumentative in the usual way — who stood very still in the aisle for a long moment, holding a white knight that was loudly criticising his grip, and had the idea.

He took it to George the way you take things to a Weasley, which is sideways, over chips, at nine o'clock, with the shop shut and the rain on the bow window.

"Chess sets," Ron said. "Ours, not the bought-in line. Hear the whole thing before you do the face. Every piece in a standard set will fight for anyone, right, that's the design, they're soldiers. Ours learn you. Charm-work in the base of every piece — it watches your games, builds a book on you, and then it does the thing no chess set in the world does." He moved the vinegar to e4. "It coaches. The pieces start telling you the truth. Not moves — anyone can charm a set to play; the Ministry'd have it banned from tournaments by Tuesday, and rightly. Habits. 'You always castle scared.' 'You've hung your queen's knight every Thursday this month.' 'You attack when you're losing and it's the only time you attack, and your sister worked that out about you in 1992.' A set that makes you better, because it's the only opponent in the world that's on your side." He put the chip down. "Grandmaster Sets. Three tiers. The top one learns openings off you and names them after you. Kids who got nobody to play with get a set that wants them to win eventually. George. Say something."

George had done the face anyway — the doing-the-sums face, the both-brothers face — but what he said, after a long moment, was: "Margins."

"Forty percent at the mid tier, I costed it Tuesday. The charm-work's the expense, but it's a one-time enchantment, no consumables, and the word of mouth is the advertising, because every single buyer ends up telling somebody what their chess set said about them." Ron had the figures out of his apron pocket, two pages, ruled columns. "And before you ask: it's not a joke product, I know it's not a joke product, I know what the sign over the door says. But the sign says Wheezes, George, and I've been on that floor a year now, and I'll tell you what we actually sell, because I've watched their faces on the way out. We don't sell jokes. We sell the feeling of being on the inside of the joke. Confidence, basically, at three Galleons a box. Well — this is that. This is confidence for the kids who don't get picked for Quidditch. I was one. I'd have slept with it under my pillow."

There was a silence with the rain in it. George looked at the two ruled pages for a while, and then at his brother, and then — Ron told Hermione afterward that he watched it happen, watched George decide something much bigger than a product line — he got up, went to the partners' desk, and opened the bottom-left drawer.

The ledger was black, swollen with use, the corners gone soft. George put it on the chip paper between them and stood with his hand flat on it for a moment, the way you stand at certain walls.

"Page forty-something," he said. "Forty-three, forty-four. I'm not going to watch you read it. I'm going to make the tea, and you're going to read pages forty-three and forty-four, and nothing else, that's the deal, the rest is still ours-and-half-of-ours-isn't-here. Forty-three." He went and filled the kettle, and kept his back turned, and his voice came over the running water almost steady. "April of '96. He woke me up at two in the morning to write it down. I told him to go back to sleep."

Page forty-three, in a fast slanting hand, said: CHESS — why does it have to be WAR? what if the pieces were on YOUR side — teaching set?? — pieces that know your game — \"the only opponent that wants you to win\" — PROBLEM: I'm rubbish at chess. SOLUTION: Ron??? — and then, double-underlined, with the violence of two a.m. certainty: THIS IS A RON PRODUCT. wait till he's done being a hero. ask Ron.

Ron Weasley sat in the back office of his dead brother's shop with the ledger open under the lamp and the kettle roaring and read the last three words about eleven times.

"He wrote that three years ago," George said, still to the kettle. "Before the Department of Mysteries, even. I found it again in June, the year of, when I couldn't sleep, and I shut the drawer on it, because it was one more conversation we were never going to —" The kettle clicked off. The shop was very quiet. "And then you walk in tonight with the same idea off your own bat, except finished, except costed, with the tiers and the margins and the thing about confidence, which is better than anything on that page, that's the part he couldn't do, the finishing was always —" George turned around. His eyes were wet and his voice had given up pretending, and what he said was the thing the whole year had been carrying toward: "I'm not putting your name under his on a memorial, Ron. That's not what this is. He picked you. Three years ago, two in the morning, before any of the rest of it — for the brain, not the grief. So we're doing it, and the box is going to say DESIGNED BY R. WEASLEY, FROM A NOTE BY F. WEASLEY, and the two of you can argue about billing somewhere down the road, a long time from now, a very long time from now, when I get there and referee."

The Grandmaster Set launched at Easter. It sold out its first run in nine days; the second run had a waiting list with two hundred names; the Prophet's business page, in genuine bafflement, ran the headline JOKE SHOP CORNERS EDUCATIONAL MARKET, which George had framed and hung in the window with a card reading WE THOUGHT IT WAS FUNNY. By summer there were Grandmaster Sets in the Gryffindor common room and the Auror Office break room and the Minister's study; an entire generation of children too small for the war learned the truth about their castling from small marble voices that wanted them to win; and one set, the very first off the line, tier three, was not for sale, because it stood on the partners' desk in the back office, beside a black swollen ledger that lived above the drawer now instead of in it — and its pieces, by special enchantment, on the firm's own time, were slowly building their book on a player who was going to need, in chapters to come, an opponent that told him the truth.

The first thing it ever said to Ron, four moves into the first game, in the voice of a marble queen with strong opinions, was: "You're better than you play when anyone's watching. Curious. We'll work on it."

George laughed for ten minutes. Order from a dead man: filled, with interest.

Chapter V

Start Here

The case had been in the cupboard under the stairs for twenty-one months, and Harry knew exactly how long, because he had told the cupboard joke to Ginny when he put it there — first thing I've ever volunteered for the cupboard under the stairs — and the joke had been a way of not opening it, and they had both let it work.

It was Andromeda who ended the truce, the way she ended most things, by being briefly and devastatingly factual at Sunday lunch.

"Remus's birthday is Thursday," she said, passing Harry the roast potatoes. "The tenth of March. He'd have been forty. Teddy and I are taking flowers to the oak, and you're not invited — it's a grandmother thing, we have our own arrangement — but I thought you should have the date, because you didn't have it, did you." It was not a question. "You can't keep a man's memories in a cupboard and not know his birthday, Harry. He'd find it funny, but you should still stop."

So on Thursday the tenth of March, in the year 2000, Harry Potter stayed late on Level Two, signed the after-hours log for the case-review Pensieve in the small chamber off the Auror Office — purpose of review, the form asked, and he wrote personal, and then, because Robards had built an office where you wrote true things on forms, assignment from a teacher, overdue — and set the battered wooden case on the table beside the stone basin.

Two dozen vials in their felt racks, each with its label in that tidy, exhausted hand. He read them all again, the way you check a staircase you already know. Lily, age 15, hexing your father in front of the whole common room. He deserved it. — Your parents' wedding. Sirius loses the ring for nine minutes. — Dora. For Teddy, when he asks what she laughed like. And in the first slot, the first position, the one the felt had been holding out to him for twenty-one months:

James — the first full moon after they managed it. (Start here.)

"Right," said Harry, to the empty chamber, to his professor, to the assignment. "Starting."

He tipped the silver thread into the basin, and went in.

— Dawn, and dust, and slatted light. The Shrieking Shack, but younger: the wreckage of it newer, the claw-gouges in the wainscot still pale, a boarded window letting morning through in long bars full of floating gold. Harry stood in the corner of the upstairs room, inside another man's remembering, and the first thing the memory gave him was the cold and the ache of it — Remus's own body, fifteen years old, every joint rung like a bell — and the second thing it gave him was his father's voice.

"Hold still, Moony, I'm doing the inventory."

James Potter was fifteen. He was sitting cross-legged on the ruined floorboards in a jumper with a hole in one elbow, hair like a hex had hit it, glasses mended at the bridge with Spellotape, and he was — Harry's heart did something enormous and complicated — he was bossy, he was officious, he was conducting an examination of Remus's arms and shoulders with the strutting precision of a small doctor, turning the thin wrists over in the light, counting.

"Left arm, nothing. Right arm, nothing. Collarbone — that one's old, that doesn't count — Padfoot, wake up and witness this, you great idle carpet — nothing." James sat back on his heels. The look on his face was the look Harry had seen exactly once before, on a much older James, in a photograph, holding a baby. "Moony. There are no new ones. Do you understand what I'm telling you? Eleven moons we've watched you come back chewed, and the first time — the first time out with us —" He grabbed Remus by both shoulders and shook him, gently, the way you shake a thing you can't believe you're allowed to touch. "You're clean. It works. We fixed it."

"You didn't fix it." Remus's voice, young and hoarse and trying very hard for steadiness, came from all around Harry, from inside his own borrowed ribs. "It's still — James, next month it's still —"

"Next month we'll be there again," said James Potter, fifteen years old, with the total unexamined certainty that his son would one day learn had held three boys to a fourth through everything, "and the month after, and every month, forever, until they invent something better, which they won't, because there's nothing better than us. Padfoot! Wormy! He's CLEAN! First moon ever! This calls for —"

And the great black dog on the bed unfolded itself and became a boy mid-laugh, gorgeous and careless, already talking; and by the window a small plump boy who had been keeping earnest watch turned around glowing with the pride of having been useful, of having been included, of having been one of us; and Harry stood in the gold morning bars and looked at Peter Pettigrew's young unbetraying face and could not breathe.

That was where he came out. Not at the joy — he could have stayed in the joy all night, in his father's voice, there's nothing better than us — but at the rat in the sunlight, the traitor at fifteen with no treachery in him yet, beaming, loved; and the awful undertow of knowing the ending of everyone in the room. He came up out of the basin with his hands gripping the stone rim and stood for a long time in the dark chamber, shaking, the silver light playing on the ceiling.

"One," he said finally, out loud, to the assignment. His voice came out wrecked. "That's — one, Professor. I'll need a minute before two."

He decanted the memory back with steady-enough hands, settled the vial in its slot, and looked for a while at the second label — Lily, age 15, hexing your father. His mother, next in the felt. His mother's voice, which he had heard exactly once that he could remember, screaming.

He closed the case.

But he did not put it back in the cupboard under the stairs when he got home; that much had changed and stayed changed. It went on the mantelpiece in the drawing room at Grimmauld Place, beside the photograph that didn't move, in plain sight, with the latch undone — open, the case said now, to anyone who knew its history; not yet, but open — and Kreacher, dusting around it next morning, studied the new arrangement and adjusted his ministrations accordingly, and never once let the latch fall shut.

He didn't go home directly, though. That is the other half of this chapter.

He came up out of the Ministry into the March night meaning to take the Floo, and his feet declined, and he walked instead, and somewhere on the Embankment with the river going by he stopped fighting it and turned on the spot; and the dark swallowed him and gave him back two hundred miles west, in the lane by the church, in Godric's Hollow.

The village was almost asleep. Lit windows, woodsmoke, a dog complaining somewhere about a hedgehog. He went the old route without deciding to — past the war memorial that did its quiet trick for him alone, the obelisk becoming the statue, the woman with the long hair and the baby; through the lychgate into the churchyard, where he stood a while at the white marble with his hands in his pockets and told the two of them, in no particular order, about the inventory, and the Spellotaped glasses, and there's nothing better than us, and being able to hear, now, where James Sirius Potter's certainty would one day come from. The kissing gate at the churchyard's far corner stood in the dark being a gate. He put his hand flat on the top bar as he went through — the toll, paid in the only coin he had on him tonight — and it creaked its old creak, and he stood stricken for a second at how much the sound was hers now, how completely two summers had rewritten it.

And then, because he was not ready to be indoors yet, he walked the long way round, down the lane that ran behind the church, where he had no errand and had never had one.

The cottage stood at the bend, behind a low stone wall, behind a garden gone two winters wild. It was small — smaller than the Burrow, bigger than a keeper's lodge — stone-built, slate-roofed, crooked in the spine the way old honest houses go crooked, with an apple tree holding up the dark at the garden's end and a deep-silled kitchen window facing the gate so that whoever stood at the sink would see you coming up the path. The windows were dark and had been dark a while. On the wall by the gate, white on weathered green, a board: FOR SALE — FREEHOLD — APPLY MESSRS. WITHE & PENNY, DIAGON ALLEY & GODRIC'S HOLLOW, EST. 1622.

Harry stood at the wall in the dark with his heart going strangely, and conducted, without meaning to, an inventory of his own.

A village where everyone already knew, and had spent nineteen years not making a fuss. Check. A garden long enough for gnomes in exile and one de-gnoming derby a summer. Check. A kitchen window over the sink, a kitchen that would hold six people and no more, because — her voice, on a wall, in the orchard light of the summer after the war — big kitchens are for staff, small kitchens are for families, I want the one where everyone's in the way. Check. Room on the chimney breast, his own eye added, where a clock could hang — a clock with hands on it, hand-cut, two to start with.

The gate was the kind that creaked.

He stood there for a long time, and the village slept around him, and the FOR SALE board ticked gently against its post in the small wind; and then Harry Potter did one of the hardest things he did that whole year, which was nothing. He did not copy the agents' names. He did not pace the garden. He did not press his face to the dark kitchen window like a Boy Who Lived in a Christmas story. The season had four fixtures left and the Auror Office had a succession problem nobody was saying out loud, and there were two careers still half-built and a marriage that did not exist yet, and the gate was not his to creak. He memorised the lot of it instead — which, he admitted to himself on the walk back to the square, was considerably worse — and went home to Grimmauld Place, and moved a wooden case from a cupboard to a shelf, and undid the latch.

The Tuesday letter that week ran four pages on the memory, because the rule was the rule and she got the real version, always, first.

About the lane behind the church it said only this, down in the postscript, where the important things lived:

P.S. — Walked in the village after. Found something I'm not going to tell you about yet, because it isn't time, and because some things you have to be able to afford in more ways than the Gringotts way. But Gin — when it's time, you're going to laugh at how completely you already described it.

And two hundred miles west, in a salt-streaked room above the Irish Sea, Ginny Weasley read the postscript four times, looked at it sideways, looked at it straight, wrote back a single line under her signature — Noted, Potter. The list was always real. — G. — and pinned his letter over her bed, next to a reverse pass and a sentence about March, where she kept the things that were coming true.

Chapter VI

The Second Second of May

The second anniversary fell on a Tuesday, which everyone agreed was somehow correct.

The morning belonged to Hogwarts, as it always would: the walk up from the gates in spring sunshine, the white stone with the fifty names, the minute of noise at the minute of victory — already, in only its second year, as fixed and necessary as the minute of silence it answered, the bang and the roar and the music rolling down over the lake while the merpeople boomed underneath. Harry stood the minute with Ginny's hand in his and Teddy on his shoulders — Teddy Lupin, two years and four weeks old, turquoise for the occasion, conducting the noise with both fists, the single loudest mourner on the grounds, exactly as the day intended.

But the evening — the evening, as of the year before, belonged to the Hog's Head.

They came down the hill in the long light in twos and threes, the ones who had been on the coins: Neville with greenhouse dirt still in the seams of his hands and Hannah on his arm; Luna straight off an international Portkey with a rucksack full of Swedish moss and her wand behind her ear; Seamus and Dean arguing about the West Ham result; Cho with a ring on her finger and a Healer's exhaustion worn like a good coat; Padma and Parvati; Michael, Terry, Anthony; Dennis Creevey, taller now than Colin ever got, granted evening leave by a Headmistress who had signed the form before he finished asking, with the camera on its strap.

And at the door of the Hog's Head, Aberforth Dumbledore stood waiting with his sleeves rolled, having spent the afternoon, with much complaint, on the operation that would in later years require a fortnight and a marquee: he had pushed his two long tables together, end to end, down the middle of the back room, and covered the join with a cloth.

"More of you than last year," he said, by way of welcome, glaring at the door as it kept opening. "There'll be more again next year, I suppose. People keep getting born and married and apprenticed, no consideration. Sit down, the goat's been at the cheese, mind the third stool."

The long table filled. That is what the evening was, mostly — the thing it had been invented to be, the year before, when the first reunion had ended with everyone agreeing it must never again take a war to gather them: an annual census of the living, conducted over Aberforth's stew and Rosmerta's donated oak-matured mead, in the back room where the whole thing had started. News went up and down the boards like bread. Neville was a year into the apprenticeship and had opinions about compost you could not stop with a Shield Charm; Hannah had the Leaky's accounts singing and a plan for the upstairs rooms; Luna, returned from four months in the Swedish ranges, reported that the Crumple-Horned Snorkack remained theoretical — "which Daddy says is the best possible thing for it; the moment something's proven it gets a committee" — and that she had met a wizard out there called Rolf who could sit still longer than she could, news she delivered serenely into a sudden total silence of held cutlery, and declined to expand upon. Cho's fiancé was inspected, approved, and informed by Seamus of his extraordinary luck in terms that made Cho cry laughing. Dennis moved up and down the table all evening with the camera that had been his brother's, not asking anyone to pose, catching people mid-laugh and mid-listen, building what nobody yet knew would be the first panel in the great photographic record that hangs today where everyone can see it.

Ginny, on the bench beside Harry with her boots up on the famous third stool, had come off the back of the best Harpies season in a decade — fourth in the league, a cup quarter-final, eleven goals after Christmas, and a Quidditch Weekly profile that had not mentioned Harry until the ninth paragraph, which she had cut out and framed, the ninth paragraph specifically. Ron and Hermione sat across from them, and Ron was jumpy — passing things before they were asked for, laughing a half-beat early, checking the inside pocket of his jacket with his elbow in the manner of a man confirming, every four minutes, the continued existence of something small — a performance to which Hermione appeared serenely oblivious, and about which George, arriving late from stocktake with Angelina Johnson and squeezing in opposite, leaned across to Harry and murmured: "Three weeks he's been carrying it. It's ruining his posture. If he doesn't ask her by June I'm putting it in her cracker at Christmas."

(George and Angelina had arrived together. "Not like that," said George, to the table's raised eyebrows, hanging her coat. "Like what, exactly?" said Angelina, dangerously. "Like nothing," said George, "there's no like, we're extremely — Aberforth, two of whatever stops this conversation." It was the spring they were still telling everyone, including each other. The table let it lie, because the table was wise, and because that story has its own chapter, further along.)

And then, at nine, came the toast.

It was Luna's office, by the precedent of the first reunion, and would be Luna's office forever, and she stood with her glass and waited for the quiet in the way that only Luna could — not asking for it, simply being so completely still that the noise felt overdressed and left. The lamps guttered. Down the long table, the faces: every one of them had been a child soldier in this very room, mustering through a portrait hole; and Aberforth leaned on the bar at the back with a glass of his own, under the painting of a girl with a kind smile, and waited.

"Two years," said Luna. "We are all still extremely alive, which I notice nobody predicted. Some of us are professors now, nearly, and some of us are famous at Quidditch, and some of us are engaged to Healers, and one of us met a man in Sweden who can sit still" — serenely, over the renewed outbreak — "and the ones who aren't here are also doing very well, I find, if you know how to listen, which I do. So." She raised her glass to the room, to the painting over the bar, to all of it. "The toast is the same as last year, and it will be the same every year, because it is the only roll-call we ever passed. Dumbledore's Army —"

"— ALL PRESENT," said the long table, every voice, the old answer.

And it was precisely then — glasses still aloft, the words still in the air — that the door of the back room banged open hard enough to scandalise the goat, and a silver weasel made of light came through the wall, stood on the long table among the stew bowls with its whole body quivering, and spoke in the voice of Arthur Weasley, transported far beyond grammar:

"BORN AT SEVEN O'CLOCK — a girl — everyone is — Molly says to say everyone is PERFECT — Bill says — Bill is crying, Bill says her name is — " the weasel paused, gathered itself, delivered the news with the full weight it deserved — "Victoire. Because of the day. They named her for the day. Born on the second of May, the second of May has a BABY —"

The Hog's Head back room came apart. Glasses everywhere, Weasleys upright, Ginny with both hands over her mouth and then both fists in the air, Aberforth bellowing for quiet and pouring with the other hand; and Luna Lovegood, still standing, still serene at the eye of it, simply amended the toast — raised her glass one inch higher and said, "All present, plus one," — and the long table drank to Victoire Weasley, aged two hours, the first of the after-children, born on the day itself as though the calendar had finally decided to apologise.

Dennis got the photograph. Of course Dennis got the photograph: the whole table mid-eruption, mead in the air, Ginny's fists up, George's head thrown all the way back in the manner of a boy in an older picture, the silver weasel still standing among the bowls with its mouth open. It hangs first in the line of them — the long-table photographs, one per year, the row that runs on and on down a corridor you will visit at the end of this book — and under it, in Dennis Creevey's careful hand, the caption that named the tradition:

The Second Second of May. All present, plus one.

Chapter VII

Chapter Four Has Never Met You

Ron Weasley presented himself at the Grangers' front door on the second Saturday of June, at eleven in the morning, carrying a toolbox.

The toolbox was the pretext. There was a shelf in the upstairs study that wanted rehanging, and Ron had let it be known he'd see to it — "no magic in the fittings, Dan likes a Rawlplug" — and Hermione was at the Ministry until two because the Ministry had discovered, as everyone eventually discovered, that her Saturdays could be had for the asking. All of this Ron had arranged with some care over a fortnight, and none of it survived contact with Mrs. Granger, who opened the door, looked at him for one second, and called back into the house: "Dan. He's here about Hermione."

"I'm here about the shelf," said Ron.

"Of course you are, love. Kettle's on."

So it happened at the kitchen table after all, which was where it had always been going to happen — the table where four days of silence had ended over three exercise books, in a kitchen Hermione had charmed her parents out of and back into, with the larder door six feet away wearing its pencilled ladder of heights, H.G. age 5, H.G. age 6, H.G. age 7, recommencing, after a gap that explained itself, with H.G., 19, home. Ron sat where they put him. Mr. Granger sat opposite and stirred his tea for a while.

"The shelf," he said at last, gravely.

"The shelf," Ron agreed, "can frankly wait."

He had a speech. He had written it on the back of a Grandmaster Sets invoice, four drafts, the final version committed to memory with the openings-study discipline the Grandmaster Set had been drilling into him since Easter, and it began Mr. and Mrs. Granger, I've come to ask — and at the kitchen table, with the heights on the larder door and her mother's eyes already bright, the entire structure of it deserted him at once, the way his structures always deserted him at the moments that mattered, leaving him with nothing but the position on the board and his own honest hands.

"I had a speech," Ron said. "It's gone. So here's just — here's the true version, which is what we do, her and me, it's sort of the family rule." He took a breath. "I've loved her since I was about thirteen and lying about it, and I've known since I was seventeen, in a tent, in the worst year of my life, that there isn't going to be anyone else, there's no board where there's anyone else. I left that tent once. Worst thing I've ever done. I walked back in and I've been walking back in every day since, and I want to do it officially, forever, with a ring, and I'm here because — " and this part came out in a rush, the part no draft had contained because he hadn't known it was the point until it was leaving him — "because she chose you. When it mattered most, with a war on, the cleverest person in our world spent the most dangerous magic of her life making sure that whatever happened to her, the two of you would be safe and happy on the far side of the world. You're what she protected. So you're who I ask. That's the whole speech. It was longer with the drafts, but it wasn't better."

There was a silence in the kitchen. Mrs. Granger had given up entirely and was crying into her tea towel with the efficiency of a woman who had planned for this. Mr. Granger stirred his tea once more, set down the spoon, and regarded Ron over the top of his glasses for a long, dentist's-chair moment.

"I read three exercise books once," he said. "Took me one night and it took ten years off me. And the thing I noticed, son — the thing I've never said to you, so sit still and have it — is that the books were full of horrors, and every single time, on every page, when it was darkest, the sentence I was dreading never came. She was never alone. Wherever it was worst, the next line was always Ron said, or Ron grabbed my arm, or — " his voice went somewhere and he went after it and brought it back — "it says here you came back. I told you that in a garden in Melbourne. It's still the whole of my opinion of you." He put out his hand. "Two conditions. You'll tell her about this visit — after, not before; she hates a secret but she'd hate missing this kitchen more. And you will never once, as long as you live, let her convince you she's fine when she isn't. She's been fine at us since she was eleven. It's the only lie she tells, and it's the only one that scares me."

"Done," said Ron, "and done," and shook it; and Mrs. Granger came around the table and kissed him on the cheek and said, "Now. About your actual plan, dear. Tell me everything, so I can fix it."

The actual plan was, by any objective measure, magnificent.

Ron had — by way of redemption for a certain evening two summers prior — re-booked the most fashionable wizarding restaurant in Britain, the one with the floating candles and the wine list in French: the private river room this time, the maître d' sworn to secrecy on pain of George, the press problem solved with a Disillusioned booking under the name R. Wazlib. There was to be a violinist. There was a plan involving the dessert course and a small box charmed to glow. He had costed it like a product launch and rehearsed it like an opening, and Mrs. Granger heard the whole thing out with her chin on her hand and said, at the end, gently, "It's lovely, dear. It's completely wrong, and I think you already know it, but it's lovely," and Ron said, "I know it's wrong, but I owe her impressive, don't I? She got the fish and chips last time," and Mrs. Granger said, "Hm," in the way of mothers leaving a thing to find its own level.

It found its level on the fourth of July, which was the night the reservation finally survived — the first two attempts having been rescheduled by Hermione herself, with mortified apologies, for reasons of statute — and it found it on the pavement outside the restaurant, at eight o'clock, where Ron stood waiting in his good robes and watched Hermione Granger come up from the Ministry side street toward him absolutely wrecked.

He knew the walk before he could see her face. He had eleven years of her walks. This was the one from third year, the Time-Turner term; the one from the tent.

"Don't say anything nice to me," she said, arriving, "or I'll cry on a public street. Gamp — " and it came out of her in a flood right there on the pavement: Odo Gamp, who had that afternoon, with two years of her registry work sitting in his in-tray for "review," quietly convened his own committee on elf affairs — his committee, hand-picked, terms of reference drafted by a Nott cousin, scheduled to report in eighteen months and pre-built to recommend nothing — and had done it smiling, in front of her, with a little speech about valuing her cataloguing. "He's going to bury it, Ron. The whole Act. He's going to bury it under a committee with my own ledgers as the headstone, and everyone upstairs will say the matter's in hand, and I sat there and thanked him, I thanked him, because eleven reformers lost their tempers in that office and I swore I wouldn't be twelve —"

Through the restaurant's golden window, the violinist was tuning. The maître d' hovered at the door with the reverent face of a man guarding a dessert course. Ron Weasley looked at all of it — the candles, the river room, the plan, four drafts and a glowing box — and then at the woman on the pavement who needed, more than any violin on earth, to finish a sentence about procedure; and he came to the best decision of his life with the clean speed of a man who had finally learned to stop calculating and play the board in front of him.

"Wrong venue," he said. "My mistake. C'mon."

They got the fish and chips from the place near the bridge, because it was open and it was theirs, and they sat on the bench on the Embankment — the bench; neither of them ever called it anything else afterward — and she ranted the whole position out of herself, Gamp and the committee and the eighteen months and the Nott cousin, while the lights came on down the river and Ron ate chips and listened the way he listened, which was completely.

And then, when she wound down: "Right. Want the move?"

"There is no move. He's procedurally bulletproof, I've checked —"

"He's not bulletproof, he's committed." Ron's voice had gone to the place it went over a board — the place a marble queen had been sharpening, nightly, at the partners' desk, since Easter. "You're looking at it like he's blocked your file. Look again. The most careful man in the Ministry just attached his name to elf reform, in writing, in front of witnesses. He thinks he's built a tomb. He's actually castled into a corner — because a committee has to meet, Hermione, it has minutes, it has witnesses you can request, it has terms of reference that can be amended by anyone Kingsley appoints, and the man who created it can't disown it without admitting what it was for. Don't fight the committee. Feed it. Send it your registry, one ledger a month, with a covering note every time — polite, public, copied to the Minister's office — until the committee either reads the gambling-debt entries into its own minutes or refuses to in writing. He hasn't buried your Act." He offered her the last chip. "He's volunteered to carry the coffin at his own funeral. Eighteen months is nothing, love. You were planning for ten years. He's accelerated you."

There was a long pause, full of river.

"Oh," said Hermione Granger, in the small precise voice of a woman watching an entire board turn over. "Oh. He has to take minutes. Ron. He has to take minutes."

"There she is," said Ron.

And it was exactly then — she would swear to it lifelong, to daughters and goddaughters and once, magnificently, to the Wizengamot in an aside that made the official record — with her mouth half-full of his last chip, her hair a ruin, her righteousness restored to full operating temperature, that face on, the one from the bathroom door with the troll behind it; the violinist three hundred yards upstream playing to an empty river room booked under the name Wazlib — that Ron Weasley slid off the bench onto one knee on the Embankment paving, produced from his inside pocket the small box he had carried since April to the documented ruin of his posture, and said:

"Hermione. I had a plan, it was massive, there was a violinist — and then you came round that corner looking like the best fight of your life, and I thought: there it is, you absolute idiot, that's the venue. It was never going to be candles. It was always going to be a bench, with the world needing fixing, and you fixing it, and me passing the chips. I want fifty more years of exactly this. Marry me?"

The ring was small, and old, and honest — a thin gold band with a single stone, bought not from a Diagon window but with Ron's designer's share of the first Grandmaster run, the first gold ever earned entirely by his own brain, banked in a separate column he had ruled himself; a fact she would not learn until the wedding toasts, and would cry about when she did. Hermione looked at it, and at him, kneeling on the pavement of the city they'd saved, holding the box the wrong way up.

"You're holding it upside down," she said, through both hands.

"I know. Doing it on purpose. Romantic."

"Yes," said Hermione Granger — "yes, obviously, yes, get up here" — and pulled him up by the lapels, and kissed him while the box went over the railing-ward and was caught, with Keeper reflexes, at the full stretch of his free arm, because some things about that family you could rely on under any conditions whatsoever; and a Muggle taxi went past and honked its blessing, and down the river all the bells of the embankment churches declined to ring, it being a Tuesday, and a quarter past nine, and nobody's business — which suited the two of them, who had conducted the entire matter, beginning to end, as what it was: a private correction to the public record, eleven years overdue.

They told the families inside twenty-four hours, in the correct order, which is to say Harry by owl that night (Tuesday's making an extra stop, don't argue, read the enclosed — and Tuesday the owl, arriving at Grimmauld Place at midnight with his second letter of the day, wore the put-upon expression of a professional absorbing scope creep), the Grangers at Sunday breakfast (where Mr. Granger said, "Took you long enough; I approved that in June," and was savaged by his wife for ruining the kitchen-table secret eleven hours early), and the Burrow at Sunday lunch, where Molly Weasley's joy registered on instruments at the Ministry, and where the date was settled before pudding in a negotiation lasting four minutes: Hermione proposed "soon"; Molly, weeping, countered "properly"; and Arthur, consulting nothing but the garden through the window, said, "The last Saturday of August, the orchard's at its best," and that was that, because nobody in that family deferred joy anymore, it being the one expense they had all agreed never again to economise on.

George's reaction deserves its own line, and gets it. He was minding the till when they came in to tell him; he heard it out, rang up NO SALE with a flourish so the drawer shot open with its little bell, announced "On the house — whole shop, one item each, wedding rate," to the eleven customers present, and then put up, in the bow window, between the Grandmaster Sets and the batteries card, a hand-lettered sign that stayed there a fortnight and was photographed for the Prophet's front page:

THE TRUSTWORTHY-LOOKING ONE IS GETTING MARRIED.

SHE SAID YES. WE CHECKED TWICE.

— MANAGEMENT.

And the flat above the shop changed hands that same month, in the shortest property negotiation in the history of Diagon Alley: George, who had rattled around in it alone for two years with the kettle and the ghosts, moved his things to a bright set of rooms over the road ("closer to the coffee, further from the stocktake, don't make it a thing"), handed Ron the other key on a Tuesday morning, and declined rent, ruling — in the voice he used when a thing was law — that Fred's half of the flat had already been left to its rightful tenant, that his own half was a wedding present, and that the only consideration he required was noise through the floorboards during business hours. "It's a good flat," he said, going down the stairs before anything could be done about anyone's face. "It just needs feet."

Chapter VIII

The First Wedding

The last Saturday of August came up golden, as Arthur had known it would, and the Burrow did the thing the Burrow did best, which was hold more than it could hold.

By eight in the morning the orchard was a fever of preparation: the marquee from Bill's wedding up and gleaming, with three years of improvements Arthur declined to explain; trestle tables down the middle of it end to end — one long table, at Hermione's specific request, no head table, no below-the-salt, everyone where everyone can reach the potatoes; golden chairs by the hundred; Mrs. Granger and Molly Weasley conducting joint operations in the kitchen with the seamless brutality of allied field marshals, a partnership formed over two Christmases and now functionally telepathic; Mr. Granger and Arthur at the orchard gate directing arrivals, each wearing the other's tradition — Arthur in a Muggle morning suit he had buttoned with reverence, Dan Granger in dress robes he kept admiring sideways in the marquee mirror; gnomes evacuated by a crack squad of cousins; Teddy Lupin, two and a third, already grass-stained, hair gone spontaneously Weasley-red in honour of the day and refusing all correction.

And up in Bill's old room, at half past nine, Ginny Weasley went to fetch the cufflinks, and found the letter.

She had been sent by the groom's faction — Ron downstairs being breathed at by George ("In. Out. In. You've faced an acromantula colony, you enormous coward, it's Hermione"), the best man's dress robes immaculate but his good cufflinks, the snitch-wing pair she'd given him at Christmas, left in the old school trunk he still kept at the Burrow because some part of Harry Potter would always keep a packed trunk somewhere safe. She knelt, threw back the lid, found the cufflink box straight away in the tray.

And saw, tucked into the torn lining of the lid — where a boy had once kept a shard of mirror and half a map — a folded square of parchment, soft at the creases, the way parchment goes when it has been taken out many times and never once sent.

She knew the hand before it was fully out of the lining. Along the outside fold, the first line showed: Gin — the real version of today.

Ginny Weasley sat back on her heels on the floor of Bill's old room, on the morning of her brother's wedding, with the house roaring happily beneath her, and held it a long moment. It was a Tuesday letter — the shape of one, the opening of one — and it was addressed to her, and it had never come; and she had been raised by Molly Weasley under one inviolable law regarding other people's post, and she heard the law, and weighed it against the address, and did the thing she would defend, cheerfully, for the rest of her life, to anyone but the man downstairs, who would not need telling.

It was dated the ninth of July, 1999 — the night of the Harpies trial; the night of the lamb and the lemon tart and her contract propped on the mantelpiece; the night, she had always privately reckoned it, before the world began. It's one in the morning at the Burrow, it said. You're asleep one floor down. It was the real version of that whole day — Gwenog's saved-up sentence, the rail above the Irish Sea, the accounting of their first year, forty-one Tuesdays, the flinch he'd gone looking for that night to be sure was gone — and she read it smiling, the way you walk a familiar road in new weather, right up until she came over the hill of the page and the road went somewhere she had never been:

I'm going to marry you. Not yet — I know the plan, the season first, first-team debut ON MERIT — but it's not a someday anymore, it's a date I haven't learned yet ... there's a village in the West Country with a gate that creaks, and a kitchen that needs to be small enough to bump into you in, and a clock wanting two hands to start with and then we'll see... I've seen it the way you see the goal before the move. Every Tuesday between now and then is just the route.

And at the bottom, under the sign-off she could have drawn from memory — Steady as Tuesday — H. — the parchment simply stopped, two years deep in its own patience, waiting in a trunk lid for a date its author had not learned yet.

A date I haven't learned yet. Ginny looked at the line for a long time. Then she looked at the window, at the orchard filling up with golden chairs, at the morning of the first wedding; and something at the back of her face lit, and stayed lit, and was still lit hours later in front of a best-man mirror, because she had just understood the whole shape of it: he was carrying the question. Somebody had to carry the when.

Downstairs, the house roared on.Downstairs, the house roared on. Somewhere below, George was straightening Ron's collar for the ninth time; somewhere across the orchard, in Ginny's own room, Hermione was standing very still while Mrs. Granger did up forty buttons and Molly cried into a handkerchief the size of a pillowcase. Ginny Weasley read the letter twice — once fast, once the way you read things you intend to keep — and then she folded it along its soft old creases, and put it back in the lining of the trunk lid, exactly where it had lain, and pressed it flat with her palm for a moment, the way the school touched the wall.

"All right, Potter," she said, to the trunk, to the house, to the rest of her life, in a voice that did not wobble because she refused it permission. "I'll carry the when."

Then she took the cufflinks downstairs and said nothing at all, which was the hardest and easiest thing she did that year; only Harry, receiving the cufflinks, looked at her face for a moment — at something lit in it, way at the back — and said, "What?" and Ginny said, "Nothing. Weddings," and kissed him in front of his best-man mirror, thoroughly enough that George, re-entering, walked straight back out announcing to the stairwell that the venue was overbooked.

The wedding was at four, in the orchard, in the long gold light.

It was small, by the standards the Prophet had hoped for, and it was everything, by the standards that counted. The little tufty-haired wizard who had married Bill and Fleur presided, older now, beaming; the guests were family, and the D.A., and one welcome-witch from Level Four sworn to secrecy, and Mrs. Prosser of Holyhead who had asked Ginny only once, with terrifying Welsh casualness, whether the brother was the same sort of quality as the sister; and Viktor Krum sent, with a courtesy that made Ron suspicious for a week, a card and a chess clock. Hermione came down the orchard on her father's arm in a dress with no train, no veil, and pockets — "I'm not spending my own wedding with nowhere to put my hands" — and Dan Granger delivered her to the front with his jaw set like a man performing the proudest demolition of his life, and shook Ron's hand, and said something nobody else caught that made Ron stand up straighter for the rest of his natural existence.

(It was, Ron told her at last, years later: "It says here you came back. Keep coming back." That's all. Your dad's the only bloke alive who can win a fight with a sentence he's already used.)

They had written their own vows, against Molly's mild scandal and with the tufty wizard's blessing. Hermione's were structured — three clauses and a provision for amendment, delivered steadily until the last line, which was "and I will never finish being surprised that the boy with the smudge on his nose turned out to be the whole of the argument," where the steadiness went, gloriously, all at once. Ron's were four sentences long, and he lost the parchment halfway through the second one, and finished from memory, which everyone present understood to be the entire point of Ron Weasley:

"I wrote it down, but — yeah. Hermione. You came after me with a troll behind the door, and you came back for me when I'd left, and you held my hand on the worst floor in England, and all I've ever worked out, in eleven years of being the slow one, is this: it's you. It was always going to be you. Every board, every game, every life — it's you. I'm done being surprised about it. I'm planning on being smug."

And there was one empty chair.

It stood at the long table through the wedding breakfast, third from the end on the groom's side, between George and the aisle — and it was not draped in black, and it bore no candle, because George Weasley had vetoed every solemn proposal in committee with mounting horror and built, instead, the only memorial his brother would have ratified. The chair was reserved with a hand-lettered card in shop gold: RESERVED — F. WEASLEY (BROTHER OF THE GROOM, FOUNDER OF THE FIRM). ARRIVING LATE. START WITHOUT HIM. KEEP YOUR HANDS OFF HIS DESSERT. On the seat sat, in pride of place, one Extendable Ear, aimed at the toasts. The best man spoke first, by custom, and kept it to seven sentences, steady until the seventh, which was: "He's the first friend I ever made, and she's the first person who ever lied to a teacher for me, and for eleven years I've had the best seat in the house, watching the two of them work out what the whole marquee already knew. To Ron and Hermione — the proof that the best things in my life all started on a train." And when George rose to speak — last of the speakers, the marquee already wrung out and refilled twice — he rested one hand on the back of that chair the whole time, easily, the way you stand with a brother, and gave four minutes of weaponised joy that took the room apart laughing, and ended like this:

"Final item of business. Some of you will have noticed we're a man light tonight, and I want to address the rumour that he'd have hated missing this. He is not missing this. He has the best seat in the house, he's had three descriptions of the dress, the Ear's been live since the vows — and I'll tell you exactly what he makes of it, because I've had twenty-two years of his opinions and I can run them without the original." He lifted his glass; the marquee lifted with him, two hundred glasses in the gold light, and the empty chair sat in the middle of it all, included, expected, late. "He'd say: about time, Ronnie. He'd say: she was always the best of us, and you were always the best investment in the shop, and I should know, I priced everything in it. And then he'd charge the happy couple for the toast." George's voice did not break; it had stopped needing to, somewhere in the spring; it just shone. "To Ron and Hermione — from both of us. It was always from both of us. Everything is."

Much later, when the lamps were low and the band had gone romantic and Mr. Granger was teaching Arthur a Muggle dance neither could survive, Harry and Ginny stood at the orchard gate with the music behind them, in the exact spot, though neither said it, where a great many of this story's hinges had swung.

"Good wedding," said Harry.

"Great wedding." Ginny had her shoes in one hand and his lapel in the other. She was looking at him with the lit thing still at the back of her face, the thing he'd been catching all day and couldn't name. "Ours'll be better."

"Will it, now." He said it lightly; his heart did not do it lightly at all. "And when's that, then?"

And Ginny Weasley — with a letter folded in a trunk lid forty feet above their heads, and the when in her keeping — the date he hadn't learned yet — two years and twelve days before a September morning this book is walking toward — stood on her bare toes, kissed him once, and gave him the only answer in her possession, which happened to be perfect:

"When it's time, Potter. You'll be the second to know."

Chapter IX

Magical Law Enforcement

The Gamp Committee on the Welfare and Regulation of House-Elves met on the first Tuesday of every month, in Committee Room C, and for the first three meetings of its existence it was exactly the instrument its architect had built: four safe wizards and a chairwoman of glacial neutrality, a schedule with eighteen months of slack in it, and terms of reference drafted to ensure that nothing it concluded could ever be mistaken for urgency.

Then the ledgers started arriving.

One per month, as promised — as threatened; Ron's plan, executed with Hermione's thoroughness, which was the most dangerous combination in Britain and would remain so for fifty years. Each ledger came with a covering note of immaculate probationary courtesy: Submitted to assist the Committee's deliberations, with an index of entries the Division's cataloguing has flagged as potentially relevant; copies to the Minister's office for the convenience of records. Each index was short. Each index was a knife. The committee could decline to read a hundred and nine ledgers; it could not, with the Minister's office cc'd and the indexes circulating, decline to read four flagged entries — and so, on the first Tuesday of October, in Committee Room C, with two reporters present (the terms of reference, amended in September on the motion of the Minister's new appointee, one P. Weasley, provided for public evidentiary sessions; nobody had objected to so transparent-sounding a clause), the chairwoman of the Gamp Committee found herself reading into her own minutes, in her own glacial voice, the entry for the 14th of June, 1849, in which a house-elf — name spelled three ways in the same paragraph — was conveyed from one estate to another in settlement of a debt arising from a wager on a cockfight.

There was a silence in Committee Room C of a quality that the Ministry's committee rooms had not produced in living memory.

"Minute it," said the chairwoman at last, because the terms of reference required it, and the quills moved, and that was the sound — Hermione said so afterward, and Percy nodded like a man hearing a chord resolve — that was the actual sound of the foundation going: not a crash, but a committee clerk's quill, entering a cockfight into the permanent record of the Ministry of Magic.

After that it went the way Percy had drawn it on a napkin a year before, move by move, almost insultingly to schedule. The committee, having minuted the indefensible, required a remedy to recommend; the only remedy in existence — complete, costed, boring, with a face-saving transition decade and a registry-perfect evidentiary base — happened to be sitting in a folder marked REGISTRY CATALOGUING: INTERIM NOTES, VOL. IV, and was tabled by its author "purely as a working draft, should the Committee find it useful"; and Odo Gamp, sixty-nine years old, master of the slow no, read the position on the board with the professional clarity of a man who had survived nine Ministers by knowing exactly when a corridor had become a corner, and executed the only move remaining, which was to adopt the entire thing as his own.

He did it beautifully. Hermione sat in the gallery of the Wizengamot on the day of the second reading and watched Odo Gamp present the Elf Welfare and Standing Act as the fruit of his Division's patient decades of registry work, the considered product of his own committee, conservative in pace and radical in nothing — a tidying, Chief Warlock, long overdue, of certain irregularities — and she watched the old guard, who would have burned her draft on the steps if her name had been on the cover, nod along to their own man tidying his own house; and her nails went white on the gallery rail, and Percy, beside her, murmured, "Steady. Let him father it. Orphaned bills die in this chamber; respectable parentage is the whole game. You'll notice he hasn't changed a clause — he couldn't; you built it so every clause holds the others up. He's not stealing your house, Hermione. He's standing in front of it telling everyone he built it, while your locks work and your floors hold and a hundred thousand beings move in. Let him have the doorstep."

The Act passed its final reading on the fourteenth of December, 2000, by a margin that the Prophet called surprising and Percy called arithmetic. The vote was not close, in the end, because nothing Hermione Granger ever sent to a vote was close; closeness was a draftsmanship failure, and she did not commit those.

She was not in the chamber when it passed. She was in the Hogwarts kitchens.
