She had cleared it with McGonagall and timed it to the minute, and so it happened that when the great clock over the kitchen hearth chimed four — the hour the result was due — Hermione Granger was sitting at the long scrubbed table below the Great Hall with a teapot, a copy of the Act, and the elves of Hogwarts going about the supper around her; and when the Ministry owl came down the chimney-vent with the single sheet, result circled, she did not read it aloud. She had thought hard about this part. She slid it instead across the scrubbed boards to the elderly elf beside her — to Pomona's Tilly, who had done most of the talking on a Friday night two years before, when a young witch had finally asked the question — and Tilly read it, and stood up on the bench, and announced it to the kitchens herself, in her own words, which were not Hermione's words and were better, and the sound the kitchens made then went up through the stone and was heard, faintly, at the staff table, where Minerva McGonagall paused mid-sentence, listened, and proposed a toast without explaining it.

Winky came to her afterward, at the quiet end of the table. Winky of the Crouches: years steadied now, by butterbeer's slow retreat and the kitchen's gruff fellowship and work that was hers to refuse — older, mended in the way that leaves seams, twisting her tea towel only a little. She had been the first name on a list under a heading that said FIRST, and Hermione had kept faith with the heading: Winky's case ran in the Act's own schedule of examples, anonymised, the bonded elf discarded for obedience, the wrongs done her now legally nameable. She stood before Hermione for a moment, working the towel.

"Is it true, what Tilly is reading?" she said. "That an elf is choosing now? Staying is choosing, and going is choosing, and wages is choosing, and an elf what chooses staying is not less than an elf what chooses going?"

"It's true," said Hermione. "It's the whole middle of it. Nobody gets to define dignity for you anymore. Not masters." She put her hand flat on the Act. "Not even me. That part took me the longest, Winky. I'm sorry it took me the longest."

Winky considered her, and the parchment, and the kitchens, with two great brown eyes that had seen the absolute worst of wizards from below their notice for a hundred seasons.

"Dobby is knowing you would get here," she said at last. "He is telling Winky so, in this kitchen, the year he is dying. 'The Miss is wrong about the how,' he is saying, 'but the Miss is the only wizard what is wrong in the right direction.'" She nodded once, sharply, settling something. "Winky is choosing to stay. Winky is choosing it with wages," — and she said the scandalous word with a small, fierce, unprecedented relish, and went back to the supper, and that, of everything that long day, was the line Hermione could not retell for years without stopping in the middle.

Kingsley sent for her in January.

The Minister's office had not grown tidier in three years, and Kingsley Shacklebolt had not grown slower; he had her sat down, tea'd, and ambushed inside four minutes. "The Being Division has a vacancy at its head," he said. "Gamp retires in April — full honours; I'm giving him an Order of Merlin, Third Class, for services to elf welfare, and before your face does that, Miss Granger, know that the citation was Percy's idea and I laughed for a full minute. You will not be taking the post."

"I — wasn't aware I'd applied, Minister."

"You didn't, and you won't, because hiding you in the Being Division stopped working the day your committee strategy decapitated the best bureaucratic infighter of his generation using only his own filing system." Kingsley leaned back. "I've watched you for two years, same as I watched you in another organisation we both remember. You don't reform departments, Hermione. You reform law — and then the departments have no choice. So: there is, as of the first of February, an Office of Magical Law Reform, inside the Department of Magical Law Enforcement, reporting to the Department Head and, with regrettable frequency, to me. It has a budget, a staff of four, a mandate that I have drafted personally and made alarming, and a door with your name on it — spelled correctly; I checked twice. The werewolf statutes alone are two years of work. The trial procedures are five. The Muggle-born Registration files are still in a basement and every page is a lawsuit waiting for a braver Ministry, which is the one I intend us to be." He slid the commission across the desk. "Magical Law Enforcement, Miss Granger. The long way in, through the front of it. I'd say welcome, but you've been running the place by memo for a year."

The first of those memos to cross the new desk — framed later, much later, beside a photograph of two Department Heads who had once flown a car into a tree — is worth setting down, because everything in the back half of this book grows out of the exchange:

M.O.M.
Office of Magical Law Reform — D.M.L.E.
From: H. Granger
To: H. Potter, Auror Office
Re: Evidentiary standards — a beginning
Date: 5 February 2001

Harry —

Official channels now exist between us, which is absurd, and I intend to abuse them.

I've spent a week with the Auror Office's evidentiary code, as far as one exists, and you should know it isn't a code; it's a stack of habits, some of them Robards' (sound), some of them inherited from Crouch's era (read the attached and feel ill). Item: confessions taken under the old emergency powers are still admissible. Item: there is no statutory right to counsel before the Wizengamot — there is a CUSTOM, which survives exactly until the first scared Ministry decides otherwise. We both lived through what "the first scared Ministry" looks like. The law held us up exactly as long as the people enforcing it were brave, and not one minute longer. That isn't law. That's luck wearing a uniform.

I want to draft an Auror Reform Bill — procedures, evidence, oversight, the works — and I want it built the way the elf Act was built: from inside knowledge, unanswerable, boring, and ten years ahead of anyone's objections. Which means I need the inside. Which means you, eventually formally, immediately informally.

Tuesday lunches? I'm told you keep Tuesdays for important correspondence.

— H.G.

M.O.M.
Auror Office — Level Two
From: H. Potter
To: H. Granger, O.M.L.R.
Re: Re: Evidentiary standards
Date: 5 February 2001 (by return)

In. Completely in. Have been compiling my own list since cadet year — Robards knows; says the day the list becomes a bill, he'll testify for it and retire the morning after it passes, "in that order, Potter, mind the order."

Tuesday lunches approved, with the standing caveat that Tuesday EVENINGS remain spoken for, in perpetuity, by a prior statute.

One condition: the bill covers cadets. Whatever we build, it has to hold for the scared nineteen-year-old in the wet heather, not just for the chamber. That's the whole test of it. If it protects him, it'll protect everyone.

— H.

P.S. — You put your OWN name on this one, Granger. The doorstep too. That's the condition and the whole condition.

Chapter X

Grandmaster

The All-England Wizarding Chess Open had been held every autumn since 1622, in a draughty guild hall behind Gringotts, in an atmosphere of profound and cherished tedium, and in October of the year 2000 it was won, as it had been won for nine of the previous eleven years, by Grandmaster Elspeth Crake, aged seventy-four, of Tutshill.

That nobody in wizarding Britain remembers it that way — that the tournament of 2000 is remembered instead as the one with the queues, the one the Wireless cleared its schedule for, the one that put a chess set on the Christmas list of every household with a child or a grudge in it — is the fault of three parties, in ascending order of culpability: a pompous customer, George Weasley, and a marble queen with strong opinions.

The customer came first. He arrived in the shop on a Saturday in September — tall, tweedy, a tournament rosette from some county event worn like a campaign medal — picked up a mid-tier Grandmaster Set, listened to its king offer him a polite assessment of his opening repertoire, and put it down with a laugh that Ron heard from the stockroom and would be able to reproduce, with rising blood pressure, for the rest of his life.

"Clever toy," the customer told Verity. "Though one does wish the joke trade wouldn't lecture. Chess is rather a serious discipline — not something one learns from a shopkeeper's novelty."

He left without buying anything. There was a silence at the till. And George Weasley, who had watched his brother quietly become the best player anyone they knew had ever faced — who had watched the marble pieces build their book on him for six months, watched the firm's own top-tier set go from coaching Ron to consulting him — took a sheet of parchment, filled in the entry form for the All-England Open in his neatest handwriting, signed it R. Weasley, shopkeeper, in letters visible from across the room, and pinned the receipt over the till next to the framed will.

"That's the marketing budget spent," he announced. "Try and lose early, Ronnie, the refund policy's shocking."

The Open ran four weekends, knockout to the last eight, and for one weekend it proceeded in its accustomed tedium; Ron won three games in the church-hall qualifying rounds in front of nine spectators and a sleeping cat. It was the second weekend that broke the dam, because the second weekend was when Lee Jordan found out.

Lee Jordan, by then the Wireless's most popular voice, had been hunting for years for what he called "the next thing nobody knows they care about," and the discovery that Ron Weasley — war hero, joke magnate, the trustworthy-looking one — was quietly carving through the country's most venerable tournament hit him, by his own account, "like a Bludger made of money." He turned up at the third-round session with a broadcast crew and no permission, charmed the guild's scandalised stewards into a "trial commentary arrangement," and proceeded to do to tournament chess what he had once done to Quidditch: he made it a story.

"— and Weasley castles, ladies and gentlemen, he castles LONG, he castles like a man who knows something the rest of us will find out in eleven moves, and Grandmaster Pinkstone has stopped touching his tea, I repeat, the tea has been ABANDONED —"

Britain, it turned out, would listen to anything Lee Jordan found exciting. By the quarter-finals the guild hall had queues; by the semi-final the WWN had cleared its Saturday schedule; Grandmaster Sets sold out nationally twice; and a generation of children who could not have told you what a fianchetto was were play-acting Ron Weasley's third-round queen sacrifice in back gardens from Penzance to Aberdeen, complete with Lee's commentary, which had rendered it, permanently, "the Weasley Wheeze: he gives you the most valuable thing on the board, and four moves later you find out why."

Through all of it, Ron prepared the way he had learned to prepare, which was at the partners' desk after closing, against the first set off the line. The marble queen had spent six months on her book of him, and her book was finished, and she had been right from the fourth move of the first game: he was better than he played when anyone was watching. Alone in the back office he played like weather. In front of a crowd, some old sixth-of-seven reflex pulled his shoulders in and made him safe, made him small, made him play for the draw the way you pass the potatoes and wait your turn — and the queen had hammered at it all autumn with the patience of stone. You've seen the win for three moves and you're checking whether you're allowed to take it. Nobody is coming to take your turn away. Take the win.

The semi-final — against a seventeen-year-old prodigy from Ilkley with ice for blood, two thousand people in the hall, and the Wireless light burning red — went forty-one moves, and at move thirty-three, in dead silence, at the moment the entire architecture of the game balanced, Ron Weasley heard his own breathing go shallow and his hand drift toward the safe continuation; and he stopped, hand in the air, for eleven seconds that Lee Jordan narrated like a penalty in a Cup final; and what went through his head, he told Hermione that night, was not chess. It was a marble voice. It was George signing shopkeeper in big letters. It was being twelve, on a giant board, telling Harry it had to be him that went on. Take the win. He played the sacrifice. The hall made a sound that the guild stewards had no precedent for, and the prodigy from Ilkley studied the board for nine minutes, stood, and offered his hand.

The final he lost.

It needs saying plainly, because what happened around it confused the country for a month: he lost the final of the All-England Open, on a Saturday afternoon in late October, to Elspeth Crake of Tutshill, nine times champion, who had been refusing draws since before his mother was born — lost it in sixty-eight moves of such ferocious quality that three foreign federations requested the transcript; lost it, crucially, after declining her draw offer at move forty, in a dead-level position, with the title genuinely available to a man content to split the point and share the crown.

He thought about the draw for a long time — long enough that Lee, on air, ran out of synonyms for "agonising." Half the hall was Weasleys. Hermione was in the front row with her fists at her mouth. A draw was history: shopkeeper holds the nine-time champion, glory all round, no risk.

"No, thank you," said Ron Weasley, politely, to Grandmaster Crake. "I'd rather find out."

And he played for the win, and the win was not there — she showed him why across twenty-eight closing moves of cold magnificent perfection, an old woman dismantling him with evident enjoyment and something that was unmistakably respect — and at the end, with the hall on its feet for both of them, Elspeth Crake shook his hand for a long moment and delivered the verdict that ran, next morning, across the back page of the Prophet and, framed, up the stairs of a certain flat above a certain shop:

"Twenty years, young man, since anyone declined my draw. They take the half-point and the headline and they go home safe, every one of them. You wanted the truth of the position more than you wanted the title." She gathered her pieces, hers, by hand, the old way. "That's not a shopkeeper's chess. That's a chess player's chess. Come to Tutshill in the spring — and bring that extraordinary set of yours. I should like to be argued with."

He came home runner-up, which the family treated as a coronation; the shop did a December that broke every record in the firm's short loud history; and the prize money — second prize, eight hundred Galleons, the largest sum Ron Weasley had ever held that was entirely, unarguably his, won with his own brain at his own board with his own name signed in big letters — went, every Knut, with George's roaring blessing, into the lease on a certain prime corner premises in Hogsmeade village that the firm had been circling for years.

WEASLEYS' WIZARD WHEEZES — HOGSMEADE BRANCH opened the first weekend of December, in the old Zonko's building, to a queue that reached the Three Broomsticks. George cut the ribbon with a Fanged Frisbee. The sign over the door carried, beneath the name, in gold letters that the two of them had argued about for exactly no minutes at all:

EST. 1996 IN A SCHOOLBAG. SITE SCOUTED 1997 BY THE MANAGEMENT (BOTH OF THEM).

"He priced this lease out of a cave, you know," George said at the opening, to nobody in particular, to everybody, his hand flat on the doorframe. "Easter of '97, on the run, wanted by the Ministry, and he's got the Prophet property pages and a quill, doing the yields on Hogsmeade retail. I told him he was mental. He said, 'Georgie, the war's temporary, footfall is forever.'" He knocked twice on the frame, gently, the way you knock on a door you have the right to. "Welcome in. Mind the step. Everything's half his."

Chapter XI

Christmas at the Burrow II

The table gained a leaf that year.

It was Arthur's doing, conducted with ceremony on the morning of Christmas Eve: the kitchen table of the Burrow — which had seated nine through two wars by a system of elbow diplomacy — drawn apart at a join nobody knew it had, and a new-planed leaf of orchard apple wood fitted into the gap, and the whole thing clamped and charmed and wiped down twice before Molly was permitted to see. He had been out in the shed with it for a week. The grain didn't match and never would, and Arthur stood with his hand flat on the pale new wood in the middle of the old dark plank like a man introducing two halves of his life, and said, "There. Room."

Room was wanted. Christmas at the Burrow in the year 2000 sat eighteen, nineteen counting Victoire asleep in her basket on the dresser, twenty counting Teddy under the table conducting operations against everyone's shoelaces: Weasleys of all marks; Grangers, second Christmas running, Mr. Granger arriving with a whiteboard of his own this year, pre-emptively, to Arthur's frank joy; Andromeda; Audrey — but Audrey deserves her own paragraph and is about to get it — and, arriving together at four o'clock with the sleet behind them and a crate of shop stock for the cousins, George Weasley and Angelina Johnson.

"Not like that," said George, to the kitchen at large, before anyone had said anything at all.

"Nobody spoke, dear," said Molly.

"You were all thinking it at a volume."

"I wasn't," offered Ron, taking the crate. "I was thinking you'd brought the good crackers. Have you brought the good crackers?"

"We brought the good crackers," said Angelina, "and it's not like that," and the two of them got through the doorway and into the warmth on the strength of the crackers, and the kitchen, which was wise, and which had watched this particular weather system forming for a year, made room and said nothing — except that Molly, later, distributing the jumpers, handed across the maroon one for Ron and the indigo for Harry and the heathered one for Hermione (third year running; Hermione cried the first year and now merely glowed), and then, without any announcement whatsoever, held out a final parcel to Angelina Johnson, soft, square, unmistakable.

The kitchen went quiet around the edges. Angelina opened it slowly. It was deep green, and the letter on the front was an A, and Angelina Johnson — who had been coming to the shop on Saturdays for a year and a half, who had laughed at the till and gone home and cried about whose laugh it was, who had loved one twin at seventeen in a way that seventeen loves and was twenty-three now and entirely lost about what name to give the thing that happened to her chest on Saturdays — looked at the A for a long moment and then at Molly, with the question she couldn't ask standing plainly in her face: do you know what you're doing, giving me this? Do you know which of them — what I — what this is?

And Molly Weasley, who missed nothing, who had buried a son and kept his hand on the mantelpiece, facing the room, came around the table and folded her hands over Angelina's on the green wool and said, quietly, underneath the noise, the first installment of a sentence she would finish properly in another chapter: "It's your letter, dear. It's only ever your own letter." Which made nothing simple, and made everything possible, and Angelina wore the jumper through dinner and all evening, and George looked at the A across the table seventeen times and was caught at it twice.

Audrey, then. Percy had managed the introduction with the air of a man presenting a budget he believed in but expected to defend: "Everyone, this is Audrey Fawcett, she's a Clerk of the Wizengamot, we met when she ruled three of my procedural objections frivolous in a single morning, please be normal" — a request the family honoured for upwards of forty seconds. Audrey turned out to be small, calm, and devastating, with the courtroom clerk's gift of saying the quiet ruling at the right volume; she absorbed the full Boxing-Day-eve onslaught of Weasley inspection without blinking, informed George that his opening three jokes were "noted for the record but cumulative," told Arthur that the Wizengamot archive held a 1907 file on the bewitchment of a Muggle threshing machine that she could perhaps arrange for him to see — at which Arthur had to briefly leave the room — and was observed, at the end of the night, helping Percy dry dishes in a silence so companionable that Molly got three Christmases ahead in her planning before the towels were folded.

And through all of it — through the carols and the crackers and Teddy's hair cycling the full spectrum at the fairy lights, through Victoire's first Christmas and Charlie's dragon stories and the whiteboard summit on the principles of the jet engine — Harry and Ginny kept almost reaching each other, and not quite.

It was nobody's fault, which was what made it hard to fight. Ginny arrived at nine on Christmas Eve, straight off the evening session, because the Harpies sat top of the league at Christmas for the first time in seventeen years and Gwenog had granted the holiday with the warning that the title race would not be observing it; she was leaving at dawn on Boxing Day for the St. Andrews fixture. Harry arrived later still, because the case — the case this book comes to in the next chapter, the one the Prophet had started calling by a name — had broken open that afternoon, and twice during dinner a silver boar walked through the wall and stood meaningfully by his chair until he went out to the cold hallway to hear it. They sat next to each other at the long table and were interrupted nine times. They got ninety seconds alone on the stairs at eleven, her hand in his collar, his forehead against hers, neither saying anything because ninety seconds was too short to start — and then Teddy arrived at a run, fleeing bedtime, and claimed asylum, and the moment laughed itself away.

They did the gifts at one in the morning, alone in the kitchen, the house asleep above them, the clock ticking all its hands at HOME — and the gifts were perfect, because they always were; that was never the trouble. Hers to him: a Muggle wristwatch, the kind with hands, engraved on the back STEADY — to wear in the field, where wizard timepieces failed around curse-work, "so that the time you keep is mine." His to her: a key. A small brass key on a plain ring, to which he would say only, "Not yet. It doesn't fit anything yet. It's a — promissory key," and watch the lit thing come up at the back of her face, the thing that had lived there since August, and not know what he was looking at, and know better than to ask.

"We're all right," Ginny said, into his shoulder, at half past one, in the question's voice.

"We're all right," Harry said, in the answer's, and both of them heard the third voice, the one underneath, the one that said: and we are also two letters a week and ninety seconds on a staircase, and the season runs to May, and the case runs to whenever it runs, and we are being so understanding, both of us, so professionally understanding, and nobody has said the word ENOUGH yet.

"After the season," she said.

"After the case," he said.

It would be hard, afterward, to convince either of them that those two sentences — reasonable, loving, each offered as a gift — were where the only real trouble of their long lives together quietly took root; but this is the book of those years, and the book was at the table, and the book saw it: the two of them holding each other in the lamplight at half past one, postponing, in perfect unison, with the best intentions in the world.

Upstairs, the clock — eleven hands now; Arthur had cut FLEUR's at last the spring Victoire came, Victoire herself keeping time on a small clock of Shell Cottage's own, one clock per household being the law of the shed — held every one of them at HOME until twenty past six on Boxing Day morning, when GINNY swung gently to TRAVELLING; and Arthur Weasley, who was already up, who was always already up, marked it the way he had marked it all that year — a small notation in a small shed notebook nobody knew about, where a father in a mended world kept the only accounts that had ever mattered to him: All eleven home, 4 hrs 49 min. Longest since August. Getting shorter.

Chapter XII

The Quiet War

The file landed on Harry's desk on the ninth of January, and it was Robards who brought it, in person, which was the first message; and he put it down without a word and pulled up the spare chair and sat, which was the second.

"Three dead," Robards said. "You'll have followed it in the Prophet, so unfollow it; half of what they've printed is wrong and the wrong half is doing damage. Edgar Roach, October — Snatcher, two confirmed sales of Muggle-born families to the Commission, never tried; list got cut short in '99 when the evidence went thin. Found dead by the method he sold them with: a tracking jinx, inverted, hunted through his own house for an hour first. Bella Withey, November — Commission clerk, processed four hundred wand confiscations; found at her desk at home, hand cursed to keep signing until the heart gave out. And Aldous Pike, the day after Boxing Day. Snatcher. The worst of the three, and the method the worst of the three, and you'll read it in the file, not hear it from me at nine in the morning." He laid his hand flat on the folder. "Somebody is working down a list, Potter. The list is people the war's justice missed — and they're being killed by exactly what they did, dressed up as exactly what they deserved. The Prophet has started calling him the Reckoner, and this morning's postbag at the press office ran four to one in his favour." Robards looked at him steadily. "It's a task force. It's yours. Build it by Friday."

"Mine?" Harry was twenty years old. There were Aurors on the floor with twenty years' service. "Sir, Savage —"

"— requested you. In writing. Third year running he's requested you for something; I've started a file." Robards stood. "Two reasons, and then I've got a budget meeting. One: you're the best read of a crime scene this office has produced since Moody had both legs and fewer opinions. Two is the real one, so look at me. Half this country agrees with the Reckoner, Potter. Quietly, at their kitchen tables, they agree. The arrest, when it comes, is going to need to be made by somebody whose right to be angrier than the killer nobody alive can question — and who chose the warrant anyway. That's the whole case. The catching is the easy half."

The team was built by Friday — Savage, Proudfoot, Sloper (third-year now, the shattered knee gone to a weather-ache and a grim pride), and a second-year Auror named Tomas Pillai, quick and careful and so visibly honoured to be picked that Savage had to take him aside about his face. The work was the work: pattern and paper, the building of the list before the killer could work down it — because the Reckoner's list could be derived, that was the horror and the handle of it; it was simply the war's unfinished docket, the cases marked insufficient, the names every Auror knew by heart and swallowed daily — and protection details on people the team had spent their short careers wishing into cells. Sloper said it once, on surveillance, at three in the morning, flatly, watching the lit window of a man whose wartime statement he had read that afternoon: "Tell me again why this one gets us all night in the cold." And Harry said the sentence he had built somewhere between a chained chair and a gorge in Yorkshire, the sentence that was, though he did not know it yet, the entire syllabus of the office he would one day run: "Because the board's only clean if it's clean for everyone. The day we pick who deserves the law, we're just him with a budget."

The ambush came in February, and it was Harry's mistake, and the file says so, because he wrote the file.

A tip, anonymous, naming the Reckoner's next target and a date: a safehouse outside Tewkesbury, a retired Snatcher under protection. The tip was good — that was the craft of it; it was ninety percent good, gold in every checkable detail, and the ten percent was the geometry. Harry set the net the way the tip's geometry invited: main force on the approaches, and the quiet back entrance — the obvious trap, therefore screened, therefore safe — held by one careful second-year, because you put your steadiest junior on the door nothing comes through.

The Reckoner came through the back door. Not at it — through it, with a collapsing-ward that brought the doorframe and half the wall's masonry down on its lone sentry, and was gone before the dust crossed the garden, the whole visit a message with a man as the parchment: I know your shapes too.

Tomas Pillai lived. That sentence got to be true by four minutes and by Sloper's field splinting, and Harry spent the night at St. Mungo's learning the other side of a corridor he knew too well — being, this time, the one in the commander's chair when Pillai's mother arrived at two in the morning with her coat over her nightdress; being the one who stood up, and met her eyes, and said the words under my command out loud to a mother, and did not soften them, because she was owed the unsoftened version. She looked at him a long time. "You're the one who stood in front of the boy in Yorkshire," she said, finally. "Jack Sloper's mother told me. So you know which door to stand in front of when it's your own." It was absolution and sentence in one, the way mothers do it, and Harry carried both out of the corridor at dawn.

What he did with them, for three weeks, was the old machinery: the silent inventory, every name from Cedric forward, now with TOMAS PILLAI freshly entered; the four-in-the-morning case reading; the conversion of guilt into hours, which had always passed for coping because it produced results. Robards let it run exactly as long as it was useful and one day longer, and then called him in, and did not offer tea, and delivered, standing at the window with his back turned, the lesson Harry repeated forty years later at other windows to other young commanders:

"You were a wall once. It was the job and you were extraordinary at it — Diagon Alley, the gorge; you put your body in the door and the door held. So your whole instinct says the wall failed in Tewkesbury, and the wall should have been you." He turned around. "Hear me, because this is the actual promotion, this conversation, not the badge that comes after: you build walls now. Net design, door coverage, who stands where — Pillai stood in that doorway because your plan was sound and the enemy was better that night, which is a sentence every commander since the founding of this office has had to learn to say without lying or dying of it. You don't get to be the wall anymore, Potter. You get to be the reason the walls are well built, and well backed, and visited in hospital by a chief who can look at their mothers. That's the trade. It's worse, and it matters more, and nobody who can't make it sits in my chair." A pause. "Pillai's first words off the potions, incidentally — Savage took them down, knew you'd want the record. He said: did the net hold everywhere else? It did. He knows what he is. Be worthy of him and stop trying to be him."

Two more things happened in those weeks, and they happened quietly, and they are the chapter's true business.

The first: on a Thursday at six, Harry went back to the sunlit room at St. Mungo's whose card had sat in his file since the summer of the war — the standing appointment he had kept faithfully through training and let lapse, somewhere in the climb, eighteen months before. The Mind Healer did not remark on the gap. They never did; it was one of the room's mercies. He went Thursday after Thursday, and it helped, the way it had helped before — slowly, sideways, like light moving across a floor.

And he didn't tell Ginny.

He had the reasons stacked like a defence brief: top of the league; the title race seventeen years in the wanting; the Quidditch Weekly profile that hadn't needed him till paragraph nine; Gwenog's old commandment, present, glad, and silent; the certain knowledge that one word — Thursdays again, it's bad enough for Thursdays again — and Ginny Weasley would be on the next connection from Holyhead with her boots still on, season be damned, because that was the wand-oath of her, that was the whole woman. He was protecting her focus. He was being, professionally, generously, understanding. And so the Tuesday letters that February — and here the book must simply show the evidence, because she kept it —

Harry20 February 2001

Gin —

All fine here. Case grinding on — paper mostly, you'd laugh at how much of the dark-wizard-catching trade is filing. Pillai's mending well, sat up Tuesday, the Healers are pleased. Kreacher's invented a soup. Tell me about St. Mungo's — sorry, St. ANDREWS — freudian slate, long week, nothing in it. Saw your goal in the Prophet, the second one, the dummy before it. Sometimes I think watching you feint is the best thing the war left me.

Boring kind of fine, honestly. Steady as ever —

— H.

— and two hundred miles west, in a salt-streaked room above the Irish Sea, Ginny Weasley read the cheerful version, twice, the way you check a silence for edges.

She was on the doorstep of Grimmauld Place nineteen hours later, boots still on, kit bag still on her shoulder, a Wednesday, mid-season, unannounced; and when Harry opened the door she held the letter up between two fingers like an exhibit and said, in a voice with no anger in it yet, which was far worse: "Freudian slate. You wrote me the cheerful version, Harry. We have one rule older than the owl, and you wrote me the cheerful version — so I'm going to come in, and Kreacher's going to make the soup, and you're going to tell me what Thursdays are for again, because I've known for three weeks, and I was waiting to see how long you'd make me know it alone."

He told her all of it at the kitchen table — Tewkesbury, the doorframe, Pillai's mother, the Thursday room, the eighteen-month gap before it — and she listened the way she had listened in a hospital chair at nineteen, all the way through, without once making it about her own fear; and then she put her hand flat on the table between them, the gesture that in their private language meant the real version, now, both of us, and made the accounting.

"Here's what I'm not going to do," she said. "I'm not going to shout at you for protecting my season, because I'd have done the same thing in your place — that's the worst part, I'd have done it exactly, I've BEEN doing it; you don't know what April looks like at the club because I've been sparing you April, we are two people sparing each other into strangers." Her voice cracked once and she drove through it, the family way. "So: amendments. One — Thursdays go in the letters. Not the contents. The fact. Four words, went Thursday, still going, and I'll know the ground I'm standing on. Two — you get April. All of it, the ugly version, starting tonight, after soup. And three —" and here she stopped, and looked at him, and the third one didn't come, because the third one was the big one, the one with a house in it and a question in it and two careers in it, the one they had each been carrying toward the other all winter like a letter with no stamp; and what she said instead, quietly, into the kitchen warmth, was the thing they both pretended for one more season was a solution and not a fuse:

"Three can wait. After the season. After the case."

The Reckoner was taken in March, in a tidy operation that the file describes as textbook because by then it was Harry writing the textbook: taken without a wand raised, in a cottage kitchen in the Fens, by a net with no gaps and no lone doors, every wall built double. She was fifty-one years old. Her name was Hester Vane, and her husband and daughter had been sold to the Commission in the winter of 1997 by Edgar Roach, for the bounty, and had not come back; and the insufficient-evidence stamp on Roach's file had been, when Harry finally held the original up to the light, signed by Bella Withey's office. She sat at her own kitchen table while they bound her, perfectly calm, and looked at the famous scar with something terribly like fellowship, and said: "You of all people. They never answered for it. You know."

"Some of them answered," said Harry. "The rest were going to — there are eleven files reopened since October, four warrants live this morning; slow is not the same as never, and I can prove it, which you've made harder." He took the warrant out and read it to her, every line, the Neville way, by the book, because the book was the point. "And you're right that I know. I know the arithmetic better than anyone you'll ever meet. I had the list, and the wand, and better reasons than yours, and a year when nobody could have stopped me." He set the warrant down on her table, between them, like the evidence it was. "That's why it's me holding this, Mrs. Vane. So that it's never once a lie when this office tells somebody: we don't get to choose. Not even him. Especially not him."

The Prophet's letters page took a month to quiet down. The team stood down in April; Pillai walked back onto the floor to applause he pretended to hate; the commendations went up the chain and one came back down with HARRY's name on it in Kingsley's own ink, and Robards put it in the file he had started, the one he had not yet told anyone the name of, though Savage had guessed, and approved, and said nothing either.

And on the last Tuesday of the case, the letter that went to Holyhead was four pages of the real version, and ended:

Went Thursday. Still going. Counting down to May — to the title, which you're going to win, and to Three, which we're going to say out loud at last, both of us, whole. I haven't forgotten that Three is waiting. Gin — I think Three might be the whole rest of it.

— H.

Chapter XIII

April the First

The first of April was the best trading day in the shop's calendar, and George Weasley worked it every year, flat out, front of house, at full wattage, because — as he told anyone unwise enough to suggest he take the day off — "it's his birthday, mate, he'd want the till singing"; and every year, from one o'clock to two, the shop closed.

It was the firm's only fixed closure. The sign was the same every year, gold on green, hung by George himself at one sharp: CLOSED 1–2 FOR THE ANNUAL GENERAL MEETING OF THE FOUNDERS. APOLOGIES. ALL DECISIONS WILL BE UNANIMOUS. What happened behind it, nobody asked and nobody knew: only that the blinds came down in the bow window, and the bolt went across, and at two o'clock the shop reopened and George was exactly as he had been, possibly more so, and the day roared on to its record-breaking close.

In the April of 2001 — the twins' twenty-third birthday; the third one George kept alone — Angelina Johnson was in the stockroom when the clock struck one.

She hadn't planned it. Saturdays were her shop days — she coached flying at the Tutshill academy in the week, and kept her Saturdays for the till and the banter and the thing she was not examining — but the first of April fell on a Sunday that year, and she had come in anyway, unasked, with cake, because some calendar arithmetic you do without admitting it; and she was halfway up the stock ladder with a crate of Decoy Detonators when the blinds came down and the bolt shot home and she realised, with the particular horror of the accidentally trespassing, that she was locked into the Annual General Meeting of the Founders.

She came down the ladder meaning to slip out the back. She got as far as the stockroom door, and stopped, because through it she could see the back office, and the back office explained the hour.

Two chairs at the partners' desk. Two butterbeers, opened, one set at the empty place. The black ledger out of its drawer, open. And George in his chair with his feet up on the desk, entirely at ease, entirely unperformed, reading aloud from a stock list in a voice she had never heard him use in company — the half voice, the voice you use on someone who knows all your timing — pausing at the gaps where the other voice went, running the old double act single-handed, not sadly, not madly, but the way you keep a language alive by speaking it: "...item, the Patented Daydream line, up forty percent on the quarter, which you'd claim was your charm-work and I'd say was my packaging, and we'd both be right, and Verity would dock us an hour for arguing— what."

He had seen her in the doorway.

There was a silence of a quality the shop had never once contained in business hours. Angelina stood with the cake tin in both hands like a shield, and did the only honest thing available, which was the truth: "I was up the ladder. I didn't know what the hour was. I'll go — George, I'm so sorry —"

"You brought cake," said George.

"It's — yes."

"He liked cake." George looked at her for a long moment, and then at the empty chair with its untouched butterbeer, and then — and she understood, later, the size of it; she understood it was the widest door he owned — he took his feet off the desk and said, "Well. Minutes of the meeting will record a quorum. Sit down, Johnson. Not that chair — that one's taken — the crate'll hold you. You can do the vote on the Daydream numbers; he always voted with me anyway, it was a rubber-stamp operation, this firm."

So Angelina Johnson sat on a crate in the back office through the closed hour of the first of April, while George Weasley conducted the Annual General Meeting of the Founders — the trading report, the new-products review, the formal reading of one page of the ideas ledger ("one page a year; rationing; he'd hate me skipping ahead"), the toast ("To absent management"), and the cake, cut in three, one slice left on the desk by the untouched bottle, "for the auditors" — and it was the strangest and most whole hour she had spent in three years, grief conducted as hospitality, the empty chair not a wound in the room but a seat at the table; and somewhere in it she laughed — at the rubber-stamp line, at the auditors — laughed properly, head back, the famous laugh, three Quidditch stands' worth of laugh in a back office.

And George told a joke.

It was a new one. That mattered, the book must be clear about why it mattered: it was not Fred's material, not the double act's back catalogue, not the museum; it was a small perfect absurdity about the Ministry's new cauldron-thickness forms, minted that moment, in his own voice alone — and Angelina laughed until the crate creaked, and wiped her eyes, and looked at him with the laugh still all through her face; and the air in the back office changed pressure, the way it does, and neither of them moved for a moment, and then both of them did.

It was a brief kiss, and a real one, and they both meant it, and that was precisely the catastrophe.

George stepped back first. Angelina would always say it was George; George would always say it was simultaneous; the crate said nothing. They stood three feet apart in the back office with the cake and the ledger and the two bottles, and the colour went out of the hour like water out of a sink.

"I keep checking whose laugh it is," said Angelina.

It came out of her in one piece, the thing under three years of Saturdays: "When I laugh at you — like that, like just then — I get home and I check. Was that George's joke or was it the old joke, was I laughing at you or at the — the echo, am I twenty-three or am I seventeen at the Yule Ball, and I can't always tell, George, that's the rot of it, you've got his face and half his timing and I loved him when I was a kid, the way you love at seventeen, a Yule Ball and a season of Hogsmeade Sundays' worth — and if I can't tell, then what am I doing to you? You'd be the understudy in your own — no. No. I'm not doing that to you. You're the best man I know. You deserve somebody with no — arithmetic."

"Right," said George. His voice had gone to a register the shop never heard either, flat and far off. "No, that's — that's sensible. We're both — it was the cake, and the hour, it's an emotional hour, well documented — you should —"

"Yes," said Angelina, already at the door, already armoured, "I should," and was gone through the stockroom, and the bell on the back door rang once, brightly, like a joke with no second voice; and George Weasley stood alone in the Annual General Meeting and looked at the empty chair, and for the first time in three years it looked empty.

He reopened at two. He worked the floor till close at full wattage, and broke the single-day record, and Verity watched him do it with her heart in her mouth, and at six he cashed up, locked the shop, and did not go home. He went to Hogsmeade — the branch was dark; he let himself in and out again, restless, a man checking a thing that wasn't there to check — and then he walked up the long lane to the castle in the April dark, and the gates knew him, and the front doors knew him (there were standing arrangements, since the rebuild, for certain visitors; McGonagall had seen to it and never once mentioned it), and George Weasley climbed alone through his sleeping school to the seventh floor, to the smooth stretch of mended wall where, sealed inside the stone of Hogwarts by his own hand and his brother's last spring, a single Whiz-bang waited out eternity; and he put his palm flat on it, where the school put theirs, and knocked twice, gently, the way you knock on a door you have the right to.

"Happy birthday, you spectacular idiot," he said. "Twenty-three. You're twenty-three today, that's the official line, I'm not having the other version where you stopped." The wall was cold and smooth and kept its own counsel, the way it had for three years of these visits — there had been more of them than anyone knew, that was the truth of the standing arrangement — and George slid down it and sat on the flagstones of the seventh-floor corridor with his back against his brother and told him about the day. The till. The record. The meeting; the quorum; the cake.

"And then she laughed, Freddie." His voice came apart on it, quietly, three years deep. "At the cauldron thing — the forms bit, you'd have hated it, too clever, no props — and it was MINE. That's what I keep — I went back through it all night on the floor, every beat, like checking stock: it was my joke, my timing, the bits of me that were never bits of us, and she laughed like the stands used to, and for one second I was just a bloke in a room making a brilliant woman laugh, and then I —" he knocked his head back against the wall, once — "then I did the other arithmetic. Same as hers. Whose laugh is it. Whose girl is — you were SEVENTEEN, the two of you, it was a dance and some Hogsmeade Sundays, and I know what you'd say, I can run you without the original, you'd say I had a loan of her for a season, you absolute weeping mess, and if you let the accounting keep you from the woman herself I will haunt the stockroom — I KNOW — but Freddie, I checked the joke. I checked whether my own laugh was mine. That's where I am. That's the state of the management."

The castle held its peace around him. Somewhere below, a stair shifted; far off, the particular four-in-the-morning settling of a very old building deciding to stand another day. And it is recorded — because she recorded it, years later, in a letter to a former student that this book has read — that Minerva McGonagall, making the high rounds in tartan and lamplight, came upon the seventh-floor corridor occupied, and stopped, and looked for a long moment at the silhouette of George Weasley asleep against the wall with his hand still flat on the stone; and conjured, without waking him, a blanket; and stood guard down the corridor's end herself until the windows greyed, on the grounds, she wrote, that the castle keeps late hours for family, and that boy had an appointment.

He went to the Burrow in the morning, because his feet took him there, the way Harry's took him to a village; and Molly Weasley needed one look at him over the garden gate — unslept, castle dust on his coat, the second of April — to put the kettle on and clear the kitchen with a glance that relocated Arthur to the shed at speed.

He told her. All of it: the meeting, the quorum he'd allowed, the joke, the kiss, the arithmetic — hers, then his, the whole double-entry wretchedness — staring at the scrubbed table the while; and Molly Weasley heard her son out to the end, and was quiet a moment, and then did the thing the chapter and the man had been waiting for. She did not reassure him. She went to the dresser, and took down from beside Victoire's basket-photograph the small framed snapshot that lived there — her brothers, Fabian and Gideon, laughing, forever young, the uncles her children knew as a watch and a war story — and set it on the table between them.

"I was thirty-one when I lost them both in one night," she said. "And I had a husband I loved past sense, and a kitchen full of children, the youngest still in arms, and do you know what I did for a year, George? I checked. Every laugh in this kitchen — was I allowed it. Every good morning — were they getting their share, wherever they were, was joy a thing I was stealing from the dead. I'd stand at that sink doing the arithmetic, just like you, just like that poor girl: whose laugh is it, who does it belong to, has it been paid for." She put her hand flat on the table, her daughter's gesture, or rather the gesture her daughter had inherited along with the rest of the iron. "And here is what your grandmother said to me, at this table, and what I am saying to you at it, and what you will one day say to somebody at it, because that's how the table works. The heart's not a chair, dear. No one is sitting in anyone's seat. It isn't musical chairs, where the living scramble for what the dead leave empty — it's this table. When somebody leaves it, you don't fill their place. You add a leaf. Your father put one in at Christmas; you watched him do it; the grain doesn't match and the table is longer. That girl isn't taking Fred's seat, George, and you aren't lending her yours. The two of you are a new leaf, badly matched, in the same long table, and Fred's place is Fred's forever, and the table —" her voice went, and she let it, because the lesson was also in the letting — "the table just gets longer, love. That's the whole secret of this family. That's the only thing I know that the war didn't. The table just gets longer."

George Weasley put his head down on his mother's kitchen table and came apart properly, at last, in the one room in the world built to bear it; and Molly held on to the back of his neck the way she had when he was small and the damage was bicycle-shaped, and said nothing further, because the sentence was finished and she knew it.

He went to Tutshill that evening. Angelina came to the door of her flat with her armour already on, and George Weasley stood on the step with his hands out of his pockets where she could see them, and said: "Two things, and then I'll go if you want. One: I checked too. The laugh, the joke, whose it was — I've been checking for three years, same sums as you, so whatever you think you're protecting me from, I'm already in it; you can't do arithmetic to a man who's already doing it. And two —" he took a breath; the April evening stood around them — "my mum says the heart's not a chair. It's a table. And tables — Angelina, the thing about our table is, it gets longer. That's all it does. Nobody's in anybody's seat. There's just — there's room. There's always been room. I'm not asking you to stop checking; I don't think people like us ever fully stop; I'm asking whether you'd rather check together, on Saturdays, and Sundays, and possibly — I want to be transparent about the scope of the proposal — at some point all the days. Slowly. With the arithmetic on the table where we can both see it."

Angelina Johnson looked at him for a long time — at George, only George, the half that had learned to be a whole, standing on her step doing the bravest accounting of his life in plain sight — and then she stepped out onto the mat and kissed him, unbrief, unambiguous, both hands in his collar, and pulled back exactly far enough to deliver the finding:

"Yours," she said. "For the record. The laugh's yours. I checked on the way down the stairs."

They took it slowly, as promised — Saturdays, then Sundays, then, by the blackberry end of summer, most of the days, to the shop's delight, the family's vast unspoken relief, and the particular fierce joy of Molly Weasley, who said nothing and knitted faster. And on the Sunday after Tutshill, the two of them walked up the long lane from Hogsmeade together in the late light, and climbed to the seventh floor, with standing arrangements and a Headmistress who saw nothing, and George introduced Angelina Johnson to the wall — formally, by name, with titles — and stood back; and Angelina put her palm flat on the smooth stone, where the school put theirs, and knocked twice, gently, the way you knock on a door you have the right to, and said, "Hello, Fred. It's me. He's all right — I'm going to see to it. Behave in there."

And from inside the wall, just at that moment — masonry settling, both of them swore forever after, just the castle settling, except that neither of them ever once believed it — there came a small, far-off, unmistakable whizz.

Chapter XIV

The Title

The league came down to the last Saturday of May, at home, against Montrose, in front of the largest crowd ever assembled in Holyhead: Harpies to win the title with a victory, Magpies — twirling their thirty-two championships like a cane — to steal it with anything else. The town had been unliveable for a fortnight. Mrs. Prosser had taken in the entire visiting press corps and was charging them double "for the atmosphere."

It would be told for decades, that match, and mostly it would be told as one minute of it; so the book will give the rest its due first. It was brutal, and it was close, and for long stretches it was going wrong: Montrose led at the hour, led by sixty at ninety minutes, and the title was visibly leaving — until Gwenog Jones, thirty-six years old, in her final season as anybody's captain, called the time-wasting foul that earned her a penalty and a blood-soaked sleeve, and the Harpies remembered who they were. Ginny scored twice in nine minutes. The stands made a noise that registered on Muggle instruments in Bangor. And at six minutes past the second hour, with the scores level and the Snitch nowhere and the whole season balanced on its edge, Ginny Weasley came up the right side onto a loose Quaffle with the Montrose Keeper stranded and the goal — the title — standing open in front of her, forty feet of empty Welsh air between her and the headline she had been building toward since she was six years old, stealing brooms in an orchard.

She didn't take it.

What she did instead — and the photographs show it, the great moving photographs that hang in the club museum and the Prophet archive and one West Country kitchen: her head turning, the half-beat of a check no defender on earth could read — was draw the last Magpie across with a shoulder-feint sold at full gallop, and play the reverse pass. The left-handed one. The pass, flat and early and perfect, release point corrected since January of the year 2000 — to Carys Pugh, sixteen seasons a Harpy, twice capped for Wales, never once a champion, retiring in four weeks, arriving on the far post at full tilt with her whole career in her face.

Carys Pugh scored the goal that won the Holyhead Harpies the league title, their first in twenty-two years, and the stadium came down, and somewhere in the middle of the gold-and-green eruption two Chasers hit each other mid-air at full speed and hung there, helmet to helmet, and what passed between them was reported by the lip-readers of three newspapers and confirmed, eventually, by both parties:

"That was my pass," said Carys Pugh, weeping.

"It was," said Ginny Weasley. "I stole it. Seemed only right to give it back."

In the directors' box, Gwenog Jones — who did not weep, the press release later clarified, but acknowledged "weather-related eye conditions" — took out a small notebook, wrote one line, and closed it. The line, framed two years later as a retirement gift, read: Gave away the title goal. Same girl I signed. Build the next decade on her.

Daily Prophet — Sport, front page27 May 2001

PUGH'S CROWN, WEASLEY'S PASS — HARPIES CHAMPIONS AT LAST

Twenty-two years of waiting ended last night in Holyhead, and it ended the way nobody scripted and everybody, on reflection, should have: with the most prolific young Chaser in Britain declining the easiest goal of her career to hand the title moment to a sixteen-season veteran playing her final home fixture. Asked afterward why, with history one shot away, she passed, G. Weasley (24 league goals, 11 assists, and the country's undivided attention) gave the answer now being chalked on club noticeboards the length of the league: "Because I'll have other Saturdays. She'd given all of hers to us already."

Harry was in the stands, eleventh row, where he had sat all season — present, glad, and silent in the press, two full years of it now, the unwritten contract honoured to the letter — and he was on the pitch within a minute of the final whistle by means he never satisfactorily explained to stadium security, and the kiss in the gold confetti made every front page in three countries, and not one of the headlines led with his name, which both of them noticed, and which felt, that night, like a thing they had built together brick by brick; and it was perfect, all of it, the parade through Holyhead and the cup held up from Mrs. Prosser's balcony and the firewhisky Gwenog rationed out with a teaspoon "because we defend this title in fourteen weeks, ladies" — perfect, all of it, for two days.

The fight happened on the Tuesday.

It happened at Grimmauld Place, in the kitchen, and it started — as the worst ones do — with tenderness: with Harry, the two of them finally, finally alone, the season over, the case long closed, taking a breath and saying, "So. After the season. It's after the season, Gin," and reaching into his pocket, and putting on the kitchen table, beside the teapot, the small brass key from Christmas.

"It fits something," he said. "It's fit something for a while. Gin — there's a house. In Godric's Hollow, behind the church, with a garden gone wild and a kitchen window on the gate, and it's been for sale since the spring before last, and it's — it's the list, Ginny, it's your whole list, the one from the wall, I found it the night I did the first vial and I've been back I don't know how many times, I know the agent's hours, I know where the clock would hang, I had a key cut to a lock I don't own yet because I — " he ran out of momentum and stood there, having handed her, he believed, the best thing he had. "I wanted it to be perfect before I told you. I wanted to have it ready."

There was a silence with the wrong shape.

"The spring before last," said Ginny.

"...Yes."

"Fourteen months." Her voice was level the way the sea is level before the weather arrives. "You found our house — our house, you just said the list, MY list — fourteen months ago. You've been visiting it. You cut a key. And in fourteen months of Tuesdays, of the real version, of the rule, the one rule —" and the weather arrived — "it didn't once occur to you that the person whose list it was might want to be IN this? That I might want to stand at that gate myself before it's all decided and gift-wrapped? You did it AGAIN, Harry — it's the cheerful letter, it's the Thursdays, it's the same — you decide what I can carry, and when I'm ready, and you call it protecting my season, and you wrap it in a bow, and I am SO TIRED of being handed finished things —"

"That is not —" and Harry's voice came up too, the real one, the one strangers never heard — "that's not fair, Gin, you KNOW why I waited — what was I supposed to do, owl you a floor plan mid title-race? 'Sorry about Montrose, also here's a mortgage'? Every week of this year was the worst possible week! There was no GOOD Tuesday for it, so I carried it, the way you carry things, which — " and here he made the fatal move, the move you make at full speed with your blood up, the accusation that is also a confession — "which is RICH, by the way, coming from you, because you think I can't tell? You've been carrying something since August — since the wedding, Ginny, I watch your face the way you read my letters, there's been a lit window at the back of it for nine months that I'm not allowed to look in, so don't you stand in my kitchen and tell me about hiding —"

"YOUR kitchen," said Ginny, very quietly, which was the most dangerous register she owned. "There it is. Your kitchen, your timing, your key."

And they stood on opposite sides of the table, the two of them, nineteen and twenty years old, champions of absolutely everything except this, breathing hard, each holding a ledger the other had just correctly named — and it could have gone several ways, that moment; they were both stubborn in the load-bearing way, both raised on last words, both terribly good in a fight; and what saved it was not wisdom, because neither of them had any just then.

What saved it was the owl.

Tuesday came through the kitchen window at his appointed hour — six o'clock, all weathers, by standing order — and found, for the first time in the long history of his commission, both of his correspondents in the same room, mid-war, with no letter to carry. He landed on the table between them, on the teapot's trivet, with the air of a colonel discovering his front line in disarray. He looked at Harry. He looked at Ginny. He looked, with profound professional disapproval, at the small brass key lying where a letter should be; and then he picked his way across the table, took the key in his beak, walked it deliberately to Ginny, dropped it in front of her, and held out his leg.

There is a delivery, said the leg, said the whole affronted bird, said the entire apparatus of their first rule standing on the trivet in feathers. I don't care what's happening. It's Tuesday. There is always a delivery. Conduct yourselves accordingly.

The fight did not survive it. It tried — Ginny got as far as "don't you DARE be adorable right now" at the owl, voice cracking down the middle — and then she was laughing and crying at once, and so was Harry, the pair of them folding into kitchen chairs across the wreckage of the worst hour of their decade; and Ginny Weasley picked up the brass key, and turned it over once, and put it down gently, and said, "Right. Real version. Both of us. All of it. I'll go first, because mine's bigger."

And she told him about the trunk.

The ninth of July; the torn lining; the real version of today; a date he hadn't learned yet — and how she had read it twice on the morning of the wedding and put it back and carried the when for both of them through nine months of choosing, every week, not to say so. Harry sat very still through all of it with the look of a man having a year of small mysteries solved in one sitting — the lit window, the you'll be the second to know, the way she'd held the promissory key at Christmas like something recognised — and when she finished he said, hoarsely, "You've known since August. You've known the whole — and you let me sweat over a floor plan —" and Ginny said, "Yes, well, apparently we're the same person, it's a disaster, Mum always said so," and that was the moment the fight officially died, on the kitchen table, between the teapot and the owl, who accepted a strip of bacon as service rendered.

The treaty got drawn up that night, and it was Ginny who named its central instrument, because she was the professional athlete and thought in fixtures. They wrote it out — actually wrote it, on parchment, because they were people of the written rule, and pinned it inside the kitchen cupboard door at Grimmauld where the tea lived, where it stayed for a year and then moved house with everything else:

THE AMENDMENT. (1) We go on both fixture boards. Us — not the leftover hours: US, scheduled first, in ink, before seasons, cases, titles, Ministries, and the reasonable demands of a grateful nation. (2) No more finished things. Plans get shared at the ugly stage. Houses, keys, Thursdays, whens: raw, early, both of us, even mid-season, ESPECIALLY mid-season. (3) The when stays hers. The question stays his. The gate, when it happens, gets paid properly. (4) The owl outranks us both. — signed, G.W. / H.P., witnessed, TUESDAY (talon mark)

And clause three meant what it said, and so the chapter ends where the summer began: with the house behind the church still wearing its weathered board; with a key that fit a lock its holders didn't own yet, hanging now on a nail by the kitchen cupboard, in plain sight, the latch undone, so to speak, for anyone who knew its history; and with Ginny Weasley, on the doorstep of Grimmauld Place at midnight, kissing Harry Potter goodbye for the off-season and saying, against his mouth, in the voice of a woman with a date in her keeping and a fixture board of her own:

"Before next season's out, Potter, you'll get your answer to a question you haven't asked yet. Keep Tuesdays free. Keep Christmas freer."

Chapter XV

England Expects

The letter arrived at Mrs. Prosser's on the first of August, by Ministry owl, in an envelope so stiff with officialdom that Tuesday, observing from his perch, declined to acknowledge the carrier at all.

The Selection Committee, England National Quidditch Side1 August 2001

to Miss G. Weasley, Holyhead Harpies Q.C.

Madam,

Following the conclusion of the domestic season, the Selection Committee has the honour to inform you of your selection to the ENGLAND NATIONAL SQUAD for the forthcoming Qualifying Tournament of the Quidditch World Cup of 2002.

You are requested to report to the national training establishment at Ilkley Moor on the 20th of August. The Committee draws your attention to the enclosed Code of Conduct, the schedule of qualifying fixtures (Bulgaria away, 6 October, is drawn first, the Committee regrets), and the provisions regarding personal correspondence at residential camp, viz., that owls are received Tuesdays and Fridays only.

The Committee is given to understand that the Tuesday provision will be the only one of seventy-one regulations in which Miss Weasley takes an immediate interest, and confirms that it was, in fact, negotiated with her circumstances specifically in mind, the Chairman's granddaughter having made the Committee's position on the matter very clear over Sunday lunch.

We are, Madam, with great expectation,

THE SELECTION COMMITTEE

She read it standing in Mrs. Prosser's hallway with her kit bag still on her shoulder, and then she read it again sitting on the stairs, and Mrs. Prosser, emerging from the kitchen to find her lodger of two years on the third step with the letter in both hands and her face undone, took one look, said, "England, is it," went back into the kitchen, and put every rasher in the house on.

The first cap came in September, a warm-up against Ireland at Exmoor: fifty thousand people, the anthems, the green and the white-and-red, and one new Chaser standing in the line with her jaw set and her eyes, the photographs show, very bright. The entire long table was in the stands — the whole apparatus of her: Weasleys by the row, Grangers, Andromeda with Teddy on her knee in a tiny England scarf and hair to match, the shop CLOSED FOR FAMILY FIXTURE, Luna in a hat of patriotic if zoologically troubling design, and one off-duty Auror in the eleventh row who stood through the whole anthem with his hand over his heart, Muggle-style, because nobody had ever told him otherwise and by now it was tradition. England beat Ireland by ninety. Ginny scored fourteen minutes in — a goal off a turnover, low and mean through a crowd, precisely the kind of goal that doesn't make galleries and absolutely makes teams — and the roar from one particular block of the stands was studied with interest by the England coaching staff, who had been warned about the family and had not fully believed the briefing.

The shirt from that first cap she had framed, and she sent it, with no note, because none was needed, to a cottage in Tutshill, where Carys Pugh hung it over the mantel and wrote back one line: Release point still a quarter-beat early under anthem conditions. Fix it before Bulgaria. — C.P.

Bulgaria, away, the sixth of October, was the fixture the whole qualifying draw bent around — because Bulgaria meant the Vratsa cauldron, sixty thousand Bulgarians in a stadium carved into a mountainside, and it meant him: Viktor Krum, twenty-five now and already a decade into legend, greatest Seeker of the age, captaining his country through one last World Cup cycle with the unhurried ferocity of a man who had everything except the only trophy that mattered. England flew into the cauldron as underdogs and flew out with a draw that felt like a coronation: 240 apiece, Krum taking the Snitch at the death to save his side after England's Chasers — and one of them in particular, the Prophet noted, "a red scribble of a woman the Bulgarian beaters never solved all afternoon" — had spent two hours dismantling the hosts in front of their own mountain. Ginny scored five. After the third, the Vratsa crowd, who know Quidditch the way valleys know weather, began applauding her goals themselves, a sound like rockfall; and after the final whistle Viktor Krum flew the length of the pitch to shake her hand, and was photographed saying something that lip-readers fought over for a week and that Ginny reported to the long table at Christmas verbatim, with the accent, twice:

"He said, 'I haff played against your husband's generation since I vas eighteen. You are the first of them I could not predict. Tell Hermy-own-ninny I am still reading.' And then he looked at the scoreboard for a bit and said, 'Ve vill meet again ven it matters more,' WHICH —" with relish — "is either a threat or the tagline of the next World Cup, and honestly I hope it's both."

(He was not yet her husband. The entire press box of two nations had made the same slip all autumn. Ginny had stopped correcting it, which Hermione noticed, and filed.)

And while one of them was conquering mountains, the other was being quietly measured for a chair.

It happened in October, on a Tuesday, in the way Robards did everything that mattered: without ceremony, sideways, over case files. Harry had brought the quarter's cold-case review to the Head Auror's office — the office running better than it had in living memory, the reform memos with Hermione now a standing Tuesday institution, the draft Bill at four hundred pages and growing teeth — and Robards heard the review out, signed where signing was wanted, and then sat back and said, conversationally, looking out the window:

"Two years, Potter. Maybe one, if my knee keeps voting the way it's been voting. When I stand up from this desk, somebody sits down at it, and I've watched every plausible somebody for a decade, and I'll tell you what I've concluded, which is that the only one who's never once looked at the chair is the one it fits."

The silence had a shape Harry didn't like. "Sir — Savage —"

"Savage refuses it annually, in writing; it's become a tradition; this year's refusal rhymed." Robards turned from the window. "I'm not asking you anything today. Today is only this: stop flinching when the office looks at you. You built the net doctrine they teach at the academy. You and Granger are drafting the laws this place will live inside for fifty years — don't insult me, the two of you have all the stealth of a parade. And in February, in a corridor at St. Mungo's, you stood in front of a mother in her nightdress and gave her the unsoftened version with your own name in it, which is the entire job, Potter, that's the whole chair, everything else is filing." He pulled the next folder toward him, interview over, in the manner of a man who had planted what he came to plant. "Go home. It's Tuesday. Mind the order: the letter, then the worrying. The letter always first."

Harry deflected it for the rest of the autumn — to Ginny in the letters (Robards said a thing; I'm ignoring it; he knows I'm ignoring it; we're both very professional about it), to Hermione at the Tuesday lunches (who said, "Mm," in a tone he recognised from revision schedules, and changed the subject with suspicious grace), to himself on the Thursday couch (where it declined to stay deflected, week on week, the way load-bearing things do).

But the chapter — and Part Two of this book — closes at the fixture board.

It hung now on the kitchen wall at Grimmauld Place, the Amendment's working instrument: a great cork board ruled into the months, Harpies fixtures in green, England fixtures in red and white, Auror rotations in blue, Reform Bill sessions in Hermione's purple ink because she had annexed a corner of it within a week of its existence ("it's the only reliable way to schedule your husband" — "he's not my husband" — "mm," said Hermione, and filed that too); and US days in gold, first, in ink, immovable, exactly as signed and witnessed by talon. And on the last Tuesday of November, with the qualifying campaign wintering and the case load light, Harry stood at the board with the next year laid out in front of him in five colours — the spring qualifiers, the Bill's committee stage, the World Cup squatting over the summer like a benevolent thunderhead, the succession question pencilled nowhere and present everywhere — and Ginny, home for the dark week of the international calendar, came and stood beside him with her tea, and looked at the gold days, and then at the small brass key on its nail, and then at December, sitting there at the board's edge, nearly empty, one word written across its fourth week in her own hand, in ink, weeks ago, unexplained and non-negotiable:

BURROW.

"Clear your board, Potter," said Ginny Weasley, in the voice of a woman whose when had nearly arrived. "Christmas is coming."

Chapter XVI

Professor Longbottom

Pomona Sprout retired at the end of the summer term of 2001, after thirty-one years, four headmasters, two wars, and approximately — the leaving card attempted the arithmetic — eleven thousand detentions' worth of dragon dung supervised; and she conducted the handover of the Hogwarts greenhouses the way she had conducted everything, which was with her sleeves rolled and her sentiment buried in the compost where it would do some good.

"Inventory," she said, on the last morning, marching Neville down Greenhouse Three with a clipboard she did not consult. "Venomous Tentacula, third generation, knows you by smell, don't let it sulk. The Mimbulus you know more about than I do, which I have never once forgiven. Puffapods want re-potting by September. The first-years will be frightened of everything including the trowels — that's correct and useful for about three weeks, after which it's a problem; your job is to know which week you're in." She stopped at the end of the aisle, where the morning came through the old glass in long green bars, and looked at him — at Professor-elect Longbottom, twenty-one years old, two years her apprentice, war hero, snake-slayer, the boy who had once melted his sixth cauldron and cried in this exact greenhouse and been handed a seedling and told to make himself useful. "I wrote to you once," said Pomona Sprout, "when half the world was still trying to make you a soldier forever. Do you remember what I wrote?"

"You wrote," said Neville, who could have recited it in his sleep, who had kept it in the band of his hat through two years of apprenticeship, "'the Aurors borrowed you. The growing things had you first.'"

"The growing things had you first." She took off her gloves — the ancient dragon-hide ones, mended past archaeology — and put them in his hands, and that was the whole ceremony, and it was enough to fog the glass. "Well. Now they have you for good, and the sword can visit on weekends. The greenhouse keys are on the hook, the kettle's wicked-tempered, and the Headmistress has my owl address for emergencies, of which there will be none, because I have left it all" — and here Pomona Sprout's voice did the thing she had buried in the compost, just once, just briefly — "in the best hands I ever taught. Don't re-pot the Puffapods late, Professor. It tells."

His name went on the door in September: PROFESSOR N. LONGBOTTOM, HERBOLOGY. He stood in front of it for a while on the first evening, alone in the corridor, a boy who had spent seven years in this castle certain he had been Sorted by mistake; and then he went in, because the Tentacula could hear him loitering and had begun to gloat.

The rest of the autumn settled around him the way the castle settles around the ones it has decided to keep. The first-years were frightened of everything including the trowels, on schedule; the seventh-years discovered that the new professor's NEWT classes were rigorous to the point of legend and oversubscribed to match; the staffroom absorbed him with the particular gentleness reserved for staff who had bled on the premises. McGonagall, presenting him at the welcome feast, permitted herself one sentence beyond the formula: "Professor Longbottom requires, I think, no introduction in this hall — though I will note, for the benefit of the first-years, that the last time he stood where he is standing now, he was holding a sword, and that you will find him very patient with anyone who feels they don't yet belong here, for the best of reasons." The applause from the older students went on long enough that Neville had to study the enchanted ceiling for a while. And in October a Howler arrived at the staff table at breakfast, scarlet and smoking, and the hall braced — and it unfolded itself and announced, in the unmistakable parade-ground voice of Augusta Longbottom, "FRANK'S SON IS ON THE STAFF. THAT IS ALL. CARRY ON," and combusted with tremendous dignity, and McGonagall remarked into her porridge that in forty years it was the first Howler of congratulation in the school's recorded history, and that it was so like Augusta to weaponise even approval.

And then there was Hannah.

The engagement of Neville Longbottom and Hannah Abbott was the least dramatic event in this entire saga, and they would both want that stated with pride. There was no plan, no violinist, no ring smuggled through a dessert course. There was the Leaky Cauldron on a Sunday night in November, after closing: Hannah doing the week's books at the corner table, Neville down from the castle as he was every Sunday, marking first-year essays on the other side of it with a mug of her cocoa going cold; the fire low, the old pub creaking around them like a ship at anchor; two people who had found each other not across a battlefield but in the long quiet after one, repotting their lives a season at a time. Hannah totted a column, ruled it off, and said, without looking up:

"We should get married."

"Yes," said Neville, dotting an i. "Obviously."
