There was a pause of perhaps four seconds, and then both of them started laughing — at the obviousness, at the four seconds, at the entire gorgeous unremarkableness of it — and Hannah came around the table and sat in his lap and ruined an essay on Bouncing Bulbs, and that was that; except that it wasn't quite, because the following weekend Neville went to see his grandmother, intending only to tell her, and Augusta Longbottom listened, nodded once as at a report of operations proceeding correctly, left the room, and came back with a small worn box.

"Your mother's," she said. "Frank gave it to her in this room. It has been waiting in a drawer for twenty years, and I am too old to approve of things waiting in drawers." And then, because she was Augusta, and because the visit Neville made every month to a quiet ward at St. Mungo's sat between them always, acknowledged and unspoken: "Take it to her first. Show her. She won't — " the old voice held its line, as it always held its line — "she won't say anything, Neville. But things reach her. The Healers agree with me that things reach her. And your mother would want it working, not waiting. She was never once a waiting sort of girl."

So the ring went first to a ward at St. Mungo's, held up gently in a quiet hour to a woman who looked at it, and at her son, and pressed a sweet wrapper into his hand with the air of an exchange completed; and then it went onto Hannah Abbott's finger at the corner table of the Leaky on the following Sunday, between the books and the cocoa, with no announcement at all — though the pub knew by Monday, and Diagon Alley by Tuesday, and the wedding was set, in the spirit of the whole affair, for "whenever the term allows, no fuss, everyone welcome"; and the Hog's Head reunion the following May would toast them at the long table, where their news was ruled, by unanimous acclaim, the year's most perfectly in-character item.

The growing things had him first. The good things, it turned out, had been growing all along.

Chapter XVII

The Vials

They did the assignment together, in the end, the way the Amendment required and the heart had been waiting for: on a Thursday evening in November, after hours, in the small chamber off the Auror Office, logged in Harry's hand in the after-hours book — purpose of review: personal — two.

The case stood open between them on the table, two dozen vials in their felt racks, the tidy exhausted handwriting waiting where it had waited four years. Ginny read every label before anything else, slowly, down the rows, the way you read a war memorial; and at the end she said, "He graded these. Look — they're in order, Harry, it's a syllabus. Easy ones first." And it was true, and it undid them both a little before a single thread was poured: Remus Lupin, dying springward into a battle, had taken the time to sequence his bequest like a course of study, gentlest material first, the hard texts at the back — one last lesson plan from the best teacher either of them had known.

They began past the beginning — the first vial visited and returned, one March night two springs before — at the second: Lily, age 15, hexing your father in front of the whole common room. He deserved it. Note how hard Sirius is laughing.

— The Gryffindor common room, red-gold and roaring with firelight, sometime deep in a seventies winter; and a fifteen-year-old girl with dark red hair to her waist standing on the hearthrug with her wand out and her colour up, delivering unto James Potter — who hung upside down in mid-air with his fringe pointing at the carpet and his ears going maroon — a public itemisation of his shortcomings so comprehensive, so beautifully structured, so devastatingly footnoted, that the common room had abandoned all pretence of homework and was scoring it openly, and Sirius Black lay full-length along the back of a sofa, howling, conducting the applause for the better clauses. The memory was Remus's: from an armchair by the fire, a book held at exactly the angle of a prefect declining official knowledge of events, shaking — Harry could feel it in the borrowed ribs — with suppressed, disloyal, fifteen-year-old laughter.

"...and FURTHERMORE," announced Lily Evans, to sustained applause from the second-years, "the next time you hex someone in the corridor to impress me, Potter, I will hang you off the Astronomy Tower by your own enchanted ego, which should hold, given it's the largest object in the castle —"

Ginny came out of the basin crying with laughter, both hands over her mouth. "She's — Harry. Harry. She's — I have given that EXACT speech. Clause for clause. I gave it to Zacharias Smith in fifth year, the Astronomy Tower line, the FOOTNOTES — " and then she stopped, because Harry was standing very still at the basin's edge with his glasses in his hand and his eyes bright and a smile on him like a man who has just been handed something he didn't know was missing; and he said, "That's her. That's — fifteen years I've had one screaming memory and a photograph that waves. And she's — Gin, she's you. She's been you the whole time. No wonder I —" and didn't finish, and didn't need to; and Ginny Weasley crossed the chamber and held on to him, and into his shoulder she said, "Well. Your dad hung upside down with his ears going purple, grinning like it was Christmas. So we know where YOU get it," and that fixed that.

The wedding was next. Your parents' wedding. Sirius loses the ring for nine minutes. I have never told anyone where it was.

It was a small wartime wedding in a garden — autumn 1978 by the light, by the dahlias, by the way every laugh had a guard posted on it — and it was nine of the best minutes either of them ever spent in a Pensieve: Sirius Black, twenty-one and criminally beautiful in dress robes, patting his pockets with rising theatricality while a vicar waited and James's face journeyed through six distinct shades of best-friend murder; the ring's eventual recovery from inside a teapot, where, Sirius maintained at wandpoint, "somebody clearly Confunded me, this is enemy action, Prongs, I am the VICTIM here"; Lily laughing so hard she had to be held up by her bridesmaid; and through it all, from the memory's quiet edge, Remus watching them the way you watch a fire in winter — Peter beaming at the front in his too-big collar; the whole doomed golden lot of them, marrying each other into history with the war standing just outside the garden hedge — and this time Harry stood inside the joy and let it be joy, with Ginny's hand in his, and when young Pettigrew's face crossed the frame she felt the flinch arrive in Harry's hand, and stay, and pass, which is all passing ever looks like.

And then, because the syllabus said so, the third vial. The small one. Lily and you, October 1981. She is teaching you to clap.

It was the sitting room at Godric's Hollow.

Harry knew it the way you know a room from a recurring dream: he had seen it exactly twice — once green-lit and ending, in a dementor's gift; once blown open to the October sky, from a gate, under snow. Here it was whole, and ordinary, and evening: a fire down to embers, toys in a basket, a cat on the windowsill, James's broom against the wall by the door, wards humming faint as a fridge — a young family's sitting room in the eleventh month of hiding, October of 1981 leaning against the windows; and on the hearthrug, cross-legged, in a jumper with a hole in one elbow, Lily Potter, twenty-one years old, was teaching her son to clap.

"— and pat. Patpatpat. Oh, very nearly! Moony, witness this, he's nearly — HARRY. Harry James. Are you clapping or are you applauding yourself, those are different — there he goes —"

The baby clapped. The baby was fat and delighted and had her eyes and his hair and clapped like the discovery of fire, shrieking, and Lily Evans Potter scooped him up off the hearthrug and stood, turning, both of them laughing in the embered light, her hair coming down, the cat unimpressed, Remus applauding from the sofa with a mug on his knee in the last good October of that world — and she danced her son in a slow circle and said into his fat delighted neck, in the voice the dementors had only ever given Harry the end of, "That's it, my love. Clap for yourself. Somebody should be cheering you every single day, and it's going to be me, it's always going to be me —"

They came up out of the basin together, by unspoken agreement, at the turn of the circle.

Neither of them said anything for a while. The chamber was dark and the silver light moved on the ceiling, and Harry Potter stood with his hands on the stone rim, not shaking this time, just full, a man with a sitting room in him now instead of only its ending; and Ginny — who had just met her mother-in-law dancing in firelight a fortnight before the world broke, who understood now where the clapping-at-everything came from, where ALL of it came from, the reckless cheering-on that the man beside her did for every single person he had ever loved — Ginny Weasley wiped her face with the heel of her hand and said, hoarsely, "She'd have been at every match. She'd have been unbearable. Banners. Mum would have had to befriend her in self-defence; they'd have run the league between them inside a year," which was exactly the right thing to say, the long-table thing, the thing that set the dead a place instead of an altar; and Harry laughed, wetly, and the assignment, the worst and best homework either of them was ever set, was — not finished; it would never quite be finished; there were vials they were saving the way you save the last of something — but done, in the way the professor had meant. Visited. Known. Carried forward instead of around.

They made the decision about the rest that same night, at the Grimmauld kitchen table, with the case between them and the fixture board glowing its five colours on the wall, and it was Ginny who proposed the instrument, and the instrument was, of course, custody: the vials marked for Teddy — Dora, for Teddy, when he asks what she laughed like, and its three companions in the back row — would go into formal joint trust: Andromeda the keeper, Harry the assignment's executor, the handover to happen not at an age but at a question, whenever Teddy asked it, because Remus had built the whole case on the principle that grief should answer the door when knocked for, not lie in wait. They wrote it up properly — they were people of the written rule — and Andromeda, receiving her copy by owl two days later, sent back a single line that went into the keepsake box: He always did set homework with the answers hidden in it. Approved. — A.T.

And at midnight, with the kettle cold and the board glowing and the small brass key hanging on its nail by the cupboard, Harry said the sentence the whole chapter had been walking toward, quietly, into the lamplight, like a man laying down a pack he'd carried so long he'd stopped feeling the straps:

"I don't want to visit the village anymore, Gin."

And Ginny Weasley looked at him for a long moment — at the man with the sitting room in him now, at the key on its nail, at December sitting on the fixture board with one word written across its fourth week in ink — and smiled the smile with the lit thing fully ablaze at the back of it, eighteen months ablaze now, and said:

"I know. Hold that thought exactly where it is. It's nearly Christmas."

The board by the church, that same midnight, two hundred miles west, stood in the frost where it had stood for two and a half years — FOR SALE, FREEHOLD, MESSRS. WITHE & PENNY — ticking gently against its post in the small wind, entirely unaware, as boards are, that its work was nearly done.

Chapter XVIII

The Toll

Christmas at the Burrow, in the year 2001, sat twenty-two — twenty-three counting Victoire, nineteen months old and mobile and a clear and present danger to every bauble below the metre line — and the table wore its mismatched leaf like a medal, and needed, Arthur observed at the carving, to be thinking about another; and the jumpers went round (Audrey's first: grey, sensible, with a small embroidered gavel that made her go quietly and completely still for a moment, which in Audrey was sobbing); and Percy stood up before the pudding, pink to the ears, and said, "A brief procedural announcement —" and got no further, because Audrey raised her left hand, ringed, and ruled, deadpan, "The motion carries," and the table came up roaring, and Molly's planning leapt three Christmases at a stroke.

And through it all Ginny Weasley sat with her secret like a Snitch in her sleeve, and waited for Christmas Eve.

"Walk with me," she said, at four o'clock, in the blue snow-light, in the voice that was not a request; and Harry, who had spent a week watching the word BURROW on the fixture board the way you watch weather coming in off the sea, put down the tea towel and got his coat.

She took his arm at the orchard gate, and turned them on the spot, and the dark swallowed the Burrow and gave them back two hundred miles south-west, in the lane by the church, in Godric's Hollow, in the falling snow.

The village was doing its Christmas Eve: gold windows, a carol leaking out of the church porch with the light, the war memorial wearing a hat of snow that became, as they passed, a hat of snow on a young family's heads. Harry had gone very quiet. The last time he had stood in this square on a Christmas Eve he had been seventeen, hunted, half-starved, listening to this same carol through this same falling snow with Hermione's arm in his and a Horcrux on his chest where a scar kept time now; and Ginny knew it — she knew the whole ledger of this place, she had been given it at the ugly stage, per the Amendment — and she walked him through the lychgate into the churchyard without a word, because some redemptions you conduct, you don't discuss.

They did the graves first. She had brought flowers out of her coat — not a wreath; wreaths were for that other Christmas Eve; these were orchard flowers, Burrow flowers, dried summer things with colour still in them — and she crouched and set them at the white marble herself, and brushed the snow off the names with her glove, and then Ginny Weasley sat back on her heels and addressed her future in-laws directly, conversationally, in the falling snow, while Harry stood behind her with his heart going strangely:

"Hello. We've met, sort of — I've seen you dance," to Lily; "I've seen you upside down with your ears going purple," to James; "it explains such a lot about your son. I'd have brought banners but it's a churchyard." The snow came down. The carol changed behind them. "I'm taking him on permanently, is the thing I came to say. You should hear it from me, here, first — properly, the way it's done. I know what he was when you left him on that doorstep and I know what he is now, and the difference is mostly him, and some of it's a castle, and a good deal of it's my mother's kitchen — but the next stretch is mine. The watch he wears says STEADY on the back. That's the contract. That's me." She stood, and dusted the snow off her knees, and put her hand in Harry's, and finished, to the stone, with the lit thing blazing: "We live two lanes from here, by the way. As of roughly tomorrow. You'll hear the gnomes."

Harry didn't manage anything for a while. The snow filled up the silence the way snow does. And then, because the Amendment said raw and early and both of us, he said the other half of it, the half he'd been carrying all autumn — the two of them walking slow under the lychgate, down the lane behind the church, while he laid it out: that he'd stalled on the cottage for a year and a half not from cold feet but from arithmetic — her life ran Holyhead to Ilkley to wherever England played; what right had he to plant her in a southern village because of his dead and his ghosts and a list off a wall — and Ginny stopped dead in the lane and started to laugh, helplessly, snow in her hair, because: "Harry. HARRY. I've been sitting on the when for sixteen months for the same backwards reason — your whole life is pointed at a chair in London, Robards has practically had you measured, and I thought: I can't name a date that lands him in the West Country when the Ministry — we're WIZARDS, you absolute — " she got hold of his coat front — "we are MAGIC, my love, the entire country is twenty minutes door to door, my commute is a fireplace, your commute is a turn on the spot, geography stopped being a reason the day you got your licence; geography is only ever sentiment for us, and the sentiment says HERE. We were each holding the door for the other. We've been standing in the doorway for a YEAR, Potter, being POLITE."

"Bit stupid," said Harry, grinning now, snow on his glasses.

"Catastrophically stupid. Champions of absolutely everything except this." She let go of his coat, and smoothed it, and her voice changed gear, went down into the once-a-year register; and Harry realised where in the lane they had stopped. "Right," said Ginny Weasley. "Come on. There's a toll to pay."

The kissing gate stood at the churchyard's far corner under its own small roof of snow, dark old wood in the last blue light, creaking county for three hundred years of Godric's Hollow farewells; their gate, by four years of custom — the toll paid in its only coin every visit since a summer evening in 1998, the rule older than almost all their others. Ginny went through first, and turned, and held the gate closed between them, the way the toll was claimed; and Harry leaned in to pay it —

— and she pulled back an inch, and shook her head, and said, with the snow coming down and her whole heart in plain sight at last, sixteen months of carried when arriving all at once at its appointment:

"Toll's gone up."

A beat. The carol, far off. The gate creaked under her glove.

"It's time, Harry," said Ginny, steady as anything, steady as a Tuesday. "It's time. Ask me."

And Harry Potter — who had carried the question since a hospital chair in the March of 1999; who had written it down at one in the morning at her mother's table and filed it in a trunk lid for the day the name in her story was so completely hers that his couldn't dent it; who had cut a key and held a thought and kept, with everything in him, to the deal neither of them had ever once said aloud until a kitchen fight in May — went down on one knee in the snow at the kissing gate, in the village where his name began, and took from his inside pocket the small worn box that had lived there since the week the key was cut, and asked.

"Ginny Weasley," he said, and his voice went and he let it, because the lesson of the table was in the letting, "you told me once that grief queues. You never told me joy does too — that it just stands there, patient, holding its place in the line, for years, until you're ready to be this happy. I've been queuing since I was sixteen and a common room smelled like you. Marry me. Take the list — the village, the small kitchen, the clock, the gnomes — take the whole of it, and the toll with it: every gate, every time, forever. Marry me."

"Yes," said Ginny — no footnotes, no comedy, the famous voice gone to a whisper that the snow nearly took — "yes, obviously, yes" — and he stood, and she hauled him the last inch by the collar, and the toll was paid, at length, with interest, under the little roof of snow, while the gate creaked its three-hundred-year-old blessing under their hands; and then she held out her left glove and he worked it free and the ring went on in the cold — a thin old band, a single clear stone, and inside it, she would find engraved when she looked by lamplight that night, one word: STEADY — and Ginny Weasley laughed and cried and shook the snow off her hair, and looked at her hand, and then at the man, and delivered the line the family would request annually forever after:

"Well, Potter. You'll be pleased to know the question survived the wait. Lovely structure. Strong finish. The queue thing — " her voice betrayed her completely — "the queue thing was a foul, you wrote that to MAKE me cry —"

"Worked, though."

"Everything you do works eventually, it's the most annoying thing about you. Come on." She laced her cold fingers through his and pulled him on down the lane, toward the bend, toward the low stone wall and the wild garden and the FOR SALE board ticking in the snow-wind; and the two of them stood at the cottage gate — at the gate, the creaking one, the one that had never once been theirs to creak — and Ginny put her ringed hand flat on the top bar, and Harry put his over it, and they pushed it open together, six inches, just to hear it; and it creaked like the Burrow's, like home, like the answer to a postscript written two and a half years before — you're going to laugh at how completely you already described it — and Ginny laughed exactly as predicted, on schedule, on the nose, and said, "Boxing Day. The agent. First thing. Withe AND Penny, both of them, I want the whole firm."

They walked back into the Burrow kitchen at seven, snow-covered and lit like windows, into the full roaring Christmas-Eve crowd of it — and Ginny peeled off her left glove, and said nothing at all, and reached past George for the cocoa.

It took four seconds. It was Molly who detonated — mid-sentence to Andromeda, cup halfway down to its saucer, a scream of pure joy that Victoire would attempt to reproduce for months — and then the kitchen went up entire, chairs over, dogs imaginary and otherwise barking three gardens away, George demanding to know whether the QUESTION had been UNANIMOUS, Percy beginning a procedural inquiry into precedence of announcement ("we were FIRST, Audrey, by SEVEN HOURS — "), Audrey ruling herself out of order, Hermione and Ron arriving at Ginny simultaneously from opposite sides of the table; and Arthur Weasley stood up quietly in the middle of the eruption and went to the dresser, and stood for a while in front of the family clock with his hands behind his back — the clock with its eleven hands, its mended HOME, its whole long arithmetic of survival — looking at it the way a craftsman looks at a job that has just, gladly, gotten bigger; and Molly, surfacing from the second round of weeping, found Teddy Lupin at her elbow, three and a half and turquoise with occasion, demanding to know what everyone was shouting about, and told him: "Harry and Ginny are getting married, my darling," and Teddy considered this with enormous gravity and replied, "I know," in the tone of a person whose intelligence had been insulted, "obviously," and was given pudding for it.

Much later, at the orchard gate, in the cold and the quiet, with the kitchen still golden and loud behind them — their spot; the book has stopped pretending it's a coincidence — Harry turned the new weight on Ginny's hand over in his and said, "August. You said, at Ron's wedding — you'd carry the when, and I'd be the second to know." The snow had stopped. The stars were doing their winter best over Devon. "So who was first?"

And Ginny Weasley looked up at the owl-loft window, where a put-upon professional silhouette sat watch over the wreckage of the evening's discipline, and grinned the grin that won league titles.

"Tuesday. Obviously. I told him in October — somebody had to mind the schedule." She pulled Harry's arm around her, and put her ringed hand over his heart, over the scar that kept time, and added, in the voice of a woman whose every list was coming true on the nose: "He's been insufferable about it. Checks my hand every delivery. You're marrying into a very strange family, Potter."

"Steady, though," said Harry, into her hair.

"Steady as anything," said Ginny. "It's on the ring."

Chapter XIX

Keys

The offices of Messrs. Withe & Penny, Diagon Alley & Godric's Hollow, est. 1622, opened at nine on Boxing Day, and at one minute past nine the bell went, and Mr. Penny — the Godric's Hollow partner, a small round wizard with half-moon spectacles and the unhurried air of a firm that had outlasted eleven Ministries and one Statute of Secrecy — looked up from his ledger at the couple in the doorway, snow on their shoulders, ring two days old, and said, with enormous satisfaction, to the back room: "Withe. Get the file. It's happened."

"We have," he explained, producing a folder soft with age while a gaunt partner emerged carrying tea things with the speed of a man who had also been waiting, "declined four offers on the cottage. Two from London speculators, one from a gentleman who wished to demolish the apple tree for reasons we did not dignify with minutes, and one, memorably, from a wizarding heritage consortium proposing — " he consulted the file with distaste — "a themed experience. The vendor's instructions, Mr. Potter, Miss Weasley, have been in this folder since the spring of the year 2000, when a young man stood at the gate one March night for upwards of an hour and was observed — this is a village, you understand; observation is the local industry — and the vendor, old Mrs. Hollybush, who taught half this village its letters and is enjoying her retirement at her sister's in Penzance, amended her instructions the very next week to read, and I quote: 'The house will wait for the boy. Houses know. Charge him fairly and not a Knut over, and tell him the kitchen flue draws best if you're kind to it.'" Mr. Penny beamed over his spectacles. "Mr. Withe has had a book running on which season you'd come since the year before last. He had Christmas. He's insufferable."

The conveyancing took three weeks, which Withe & Penny apologised for as scandalous haste; the price was fair and not a Knut over; and on a cold blue Saturday in late January, Harry Potter stood at the cottage gate with the deed in his coat and took from his pocket the small brass key that had hung on a nail for a year — cut, he had finally confessed under the Amendment's full disclosure provisions, from the agent's pattern during one of his unauthorised vigils, "which Mr. Penny says is technically attempted burglary and also, his words, the most romantic thing to happen in that office since the Statute" — and put it in the lock of the front door, and turned it.

It fit. Of course it fit. It had always been going to fit; that was the entire nature of the key.

What followed was a spring of work, and the book will keep only the load-bearing scenes.

The kitchen stayed small. This required defending: the builder — a capable wizard from Tinworth with strong views — walked the ground floor on the first morning, knocked the wall between kitchen and parlour twice, and pronounced, "Have this out and you'd double the space. Everyone's doing it. Big kitchen, island unit, room to breathe—" and got no further, because Ginny Weasley put her hand flat on the condemned wall like a woman defending a hippogriff and said, "The wall stays. Big kitchens are for staff. Small kitchens are for families — I want the one where everyone's in the way, where you can't make the tea without touching somebody. That's not a flaw in the floor plan. That's the floor plan." The builder looked at her, and at the wall, and at Harry — who shrugged with his whole heart — and said, "Right you are," and ever afterward told the story as evidence that the famous couple were the only sane clients he'd ever had.

Kreacher came to inspect in February, and his verdict ran to three days and a written report. The elf of Grimmauld Place was very old now, and entirely transformed and entirely unchanged: he had, since the passage of a certain Act, presented his employers with terms of service drafted with the assistance of "the Granger office" — wages he mostly declined to spend, two days' holiday he spent cleaning, and a clause, inserted at his own insistence, binding him to the House of Black's silver "in perpetuity, whatever establishments master keeps." The cottage he toured in skeptical silence, white towel immaculate, opening cupboards like a health inspector with a grudge; and at the end he stood in the small kitchen, looked out the deep-silled window at the gate, and delivered, to the window rather than to them, the closest thing to a blessing in his repertoire: "It is a poky house. Mistress Black would have called it a gamekeeper's hovel." A long pause. The towel got a precise quarter-turn. "Kreacher's mother served in a house like this, before the family rose. She said small rooms hold their people better. Kreacher will maintain the establishment Tuesdays and Fridays. The silver stays at Grimmauld Place. The flue," he added, going, "draws best if you're kind to it. Kreacher has spoken to it already."

The ruin they saw to in March, and they did it properly, with Withe & Penny and the village council and a deed of trust that Hermione drafted overnight as a wedding present's first installment: the destroyed house on the lane — Harry's by inheritance, untouchable by long magic, the sign at its gate still rising for any wizard who passed, still flowering with messages, It's over. Sleep well. among them in a familiar hand — conveyed in perpetuity to a memorial trust, its garden to be tended, its wildness curated, never rebuilt and never tidied into a monument either; "a kept ruin," the deed said, in Hermione's exact words, "that the cost should stay visible, two lanes from the proof that it was paid." Harry signed it at the gate itself, on Mr. Penny's portable desk, with half the village standing quietly by in the manner of villages; and an old woman he didn't know touched his sleeve afterward and said, "Knew your mum. She'd be glad you're not living in it, love. She'd be glad you're living near it. There's a difference, and it took you the right number of years to find it," which Harry repeated to the Thursday room the following week as the best summary of fifteen years of his interior life ever produced by a stranger at a fence.

And the clock — the clock was Arthur's, and the clock was the chapter's heart.

He summoned Harry to the shed on a Sunday in April, with the particular formality he reserved for the transmission of mysteries, and Harry arrived to find the workbench cleared of plugs and batteries and laid out instead with brass sheet, jeweller's saws, files in graded ranks, and the Weasley family clock itself, brought out from the kitchen, its eleven hands stilled and patient in the shed light.

"You'll have wondered," said Arthur, "where the hands come from." He took up the oldest — MOLLY, worn gold, lettering nearly loved away — with the gentleness other men keep for newborn things. "You don't buy a hand for a clock like this, Harry. There's no shop. The clock won't take a bought hand — Fabian and I tried once, as an experiment, with a catalogue hand; it fell off by morning and the clock sulked for a week. It has to be cut. By hand, by someone who — " he set MOLLY back with the others — "the clockmakers say by someone with standing, which is their way of not saying the true thing, which is: by someone who loves the person the hand is for, badly enough to ruin three sheets of brass getting the letters right. That's the enchantment. That's the whole enchantment. Everything else is just telling the time." He pushed a sheet of brass across the bench, and a saw, and a paper pattern in his own careful draughtsman's hand. "Molly's mother taught me, the spring before we married, in this — well, in a shed very like this one. So. Your house wants a clock, and the letter, I'm told —" his eyes did the thing Weasley eyes did — "the letter specified two hands to start with. And then we'll see."

They worked the whole Sunday. Harry ruined three sheets of brass, on schedule, which Arthur received each time with profound satisfaction ("good — it's counting") — and somewhere in the fourth hour, filing the curve of a G with his shoulders learning the ache of it, Harry understood what the shed was actually for, and had always been for: that Arthur Weasley had cut BILL the autumn Bill was born, and CHARLIE, and all of them, each hand a Sunday like this one; that he had cut HARRY and HERMIONE in the first summer after the war, and hung them on the clock in front of the whole family, so that two children the war had tried to unhouse would be told, out loud, by a kitchen wall, that they were HOME; that the shed was where this family did its theology. By the last light they had two hands, true and balanced and shining: GINNY, with a serif on the G that had cost a whole sheet; HARRY, slightly crooked at the R, "which stays," ruled Arthur, "a hand should have one flaw, it's how the clock knows it's real — and besides," holding it up to the window light, deadpan as his daughter, "the R is crooked in exactly the way you fly. The clock will appreciate the accuracy."

The clock itself they chose in Diagon — small, dark-cased, ticking gravely — and the cottage's chimney breast received it in the small kitchen, where the wall had stayed, in the spot Harry had marked with his eye on a dark March night two years before; and the two hands went on, and swung, both of them, to HOME, and held there, quivering slightly, like things that had been waiting to be allowed to say it.

They moved in on the first Saturday of May — two days after the fourth reunion, the long table at the Hog's Head having toasted the house, and a certain two-day-old promotion, in advance — and the moving was done correctly, by the now-codified rite of this family: which is to say there was a Muggle van, hired, driven at twenty-six miles an hour down the West Country lanes by Mr. Granger with Arthur navigating rapturously beside him; and the two fathers carried the kitchen table in together, complaining about the doorframe in joyful concert — the table Arthur had built them over the winter in secret, oak and orchard apple, sized exactly to the small kitchen, seating six in close fellowship; and underneath, where you'd find it only if you were small, or looking, two spare leaves stored on runners, waiting, and burnt into the underframe in pokerwork Arthur's verdict and prophecy and house-blessing in one: ROOM. And at the end of it, boxes everywhere, the clock ticking, the gnomes already reconnoitring the wild garden with the air of refugees who had heard good things, Dan Granger stood in the middle of the poky perfect kitchen with his hands on his hips, surveyed the chaos entire, and pronounced the rite's closing words:

"Well," he said. "That's that, then."

And Tuesday the owl — who had conducted his own inspection of the premises a full week prior and filed his findings (a deep displeasure with the curtains; a grudging approval of the mouse situation) — sailed in through the kitchen window at six o'clock precisely, by standing order, all weathers, and took up station on the deep sill, on a perch that had been bolted there before the bed was even assembled, brass plate already screwed home, the household's true priorities legible to any caller:

TUESDAY. ALL HOURS. CLAUSE 4. He outranks us both.

Chapter XX

The Open Eye

Gawain Robards retired on the second of May, 2002 — sooner than even his own pessimism had scheduled it; the knee, he explained, had called a snap election — and he chose the date deliberately, "so the office never forgets what the job was for", and the handover took place where he had conducted every important act of his tenure: standing up, in the operations room, at the great slate board where the live cases ran in columns of chalk.

The whole floor was assembled. Kingsley had come down from Level One and stood at the back with the air of a man attending the end of something he'd built. And Robards, sixty-one, knee long since outvoted, ran his eye down the board's columns one last time — the open eye of the Auror Office, watched by it every working day for nine years of rebuilding — and gave the shortest leaving speech in departmental memory:

"When I took this office, its cells were full of innocent people and its best officers were dead or worse. The instruction I gave myself was: leave it so that the next man can be chosen for the right reasons. That's done. The rest is in the files." He picked up, from the ledge of the slate board, the piece of chalk — there was only ever one piece of chalk; its rationing was the office's oldest joke and deepest ritual — and held it out. "Savage. The floor recognises your annual statement."

And Savage — grizzled, vast, senior-most by a decade — stood, and produced a parchment, and read aloud, to the assembled force, with relish, his now-traditional refusal of the Head's chair, which that year ran: "I, Auror Savage, being of sound mind and bad knees, do for the ninth time decline the chair, the headaches, and the budget meetings; and being asked this year to also propose my alternative, I name the one we'd all follow through a collapsing ward in the dark, and have, because we did, and he went first. The motion is Potter. Look around the room if you want the seconders." He sat down to the sound of an entire floor of Aurors knocking their fists once on their desks, the office's oldest yes.

Harry Potter was twenty-one years old, and would remain, a century later, the youngest Head Auror in the department's history, and the appointment was — the Prophet notwithstanding — the least controversial in its history too, because the floor had made it, in plain sight, fist by fist. He came up to the board because Robards beckoned, and was handed the chalk, and stood there a moment, throat working, in front of the columns of live cases and the rows of his colleagues and the open eye of the job; and his first act as Head of the Auror Office — before the speech he barely managed, before Kingsley's handshake, before any of it — was to take from his pocket a small card he had carried for a year, and pin it to the top corner of the slate board, where it stayed, re-pinned by every Head who followed him, for as long as there was a board:

"Did the net hold everywhere else?" — T. Pillai, second-year, St. Mungo's, Feb. 2001.

"That's the standard," Harry said to the floor, when he could. "That's the whole standard. I work for him, and so do you. Columns one through four, nothing's changed by this except the handwriting — Proudfoot, your warrant came through, take Pillai, he's earned the door. Constant vigilance. Get on with it." Which was three sentences of Moody, one of Robards, and one entirely his own, and which the floor received as the correct liturgy, exactly, and got on with it; and Robards, leaving an office for the last time with his hat in his hand, paused at the door beside Kingsley and said, not quietly enough, with nine years of satisfaction in it: "Mind the order, Minister. The standard first, then the sentiment. He's got the order right. He'll do."

Five weeks later, the Auror Reform Bill came to its final reading.

It was four hundred and sixty pages, three years in the drafting, and unanswerable — built the way its architects built everything, from inside knowledge, boring on purpose, ten years ahead of every objection; statutory counsel before the Wizengamot; evidentiary standards with the Crouch-era rot cut out by name; oversight with teeth; and running through the whole of it like a watermark, the cadet clause, the wet-heather clause, the one its co-author had made the price of his participation on day one. It went to the chamber over two names, equal type, as demanded in a memo of February 2001 and never afterward renegotiated: H. Granger, Deputy Head, Department of Magical Law Enforcement — the promotion had come at Easter, to nobody's surprise and Rita Skeeter's visible despair — and H. Potter, Head, Auror Office. It passed by the margin arithmetic predicted. And Gawain Robards, retired five weeks, kept the promise made over a desk years before: he testified for it on the Monday, in a civilian's hat, magnificently; and the morning after it passed he sent each of its co-signers the same owl, one line: In that order, mind. Told you the order mattered. — G.R.

And the first of April that year — for the chapter must end at the shop, where all the best ledger-lines of this family are entered — fell on a Monday, and the sign went up at one o'clock as it went up every year: CLOSED 1–2 FOR THE ANNUAL GENERAL MEETING OF THE FOUNDERS. ALL DECISIONS WILL BE UNANIMOUS.

Only this year, the meeting had a quorum of three by standing invitation: Angelina in her own chair now, at the partners' desk, beside the one that was taken; the cake hers, by tradition; the minutes — there were minutes now; Audrey had drawn up a form as an engagement present and George had wept laughing and adopted it — recording one page of the ideas ledger read aloud ("rationing"), the trading report ("obscene; he'd be furious he missed it"), and the toast to absent management. And under ANY OTHER BUSINESS, George Weasley rose, in the back office of the shop he had built twice, on his brother's twenty-fourth birthday and his own, and said: "Item. The management observes that it has been in love with the flying coach for — Verity's checked the stockbook — three years, four months, and a number of Saturdays it declines to disclose for tax reasons. The management further observes that today is the first of April, and that if the management proposed on ANY OTHER DATE, a certain absent partner would never forgive the wasted material — the JOKES, Angelina, think of the anniversary jokes, decades of them, 'she said yes and we checked twice,' 'married on April Fools', it's a SEAM, it's a MINE, he'd haunt me bald if I wasted it —" and by this point he was on one knee with the ring out and his voice had quit the joke entirely and arrived somewhere bare and steady — "and the management is asking. Properly. Whose laugh it is, we settled. This is the rest of it. All the days, Angelina. Marry me on the silliest, truest day of my year, and every anniversary, we'll close from one to two, and there'll be cake, and three chairs, and it'll be the two of us and him, the whole firm, forever. Unanimous?"

And Angelina Johnson looked at him — and then, deliberately, at the empty chair with its untouched butterbeer, taking the third vote in the only meeting in Britain where the dead kept a seat and a say — and lifted her glass to it, and said, "Unanimous," and the motion carried; and from the front of the shop, where Verity had her ear shamelessly to the office door with eleven customers shushed and craning behind her, there went up a cheer that rattled the Pygmy Puffs; and the sign that appeared in the bow window at two o'clock, when the shop reopened seven minutes late for the first time in its history, read:

THE FUNNY-LOOKING ONE IS GETTING MARRIED.

SHE SAID YES. THE VOTE WAS UNANIMOUS.

(ALL THREE OF US.)

— MANAGEMENT.

Chapter XXI

The World Cup

Gwenog Jones left the Holyhead Harpies in June, after eighteen years, with the league trophy retained, a testimonial match that reduced Wales to a standstill, and a press conference of characteristic length. "You're leaving at the top," said the man from the Prophet, meaning it kindly.

"I'm leaving FOR the top," said Gwenog Jones. "Different direction." And she stood up, and put on her coat, and was unveiled forty minutes later in London as the new Head Coach of the England national side, with a World Cup eight weeks away and a single sentence of manifesto: "I've spent three years coaching the best player at this tournament. I went where she's pointed."

The Quidditch World Cup of 2002 was held in the high desert of Patagonia, in a stadium raised for the month out of red rock and old magic, seating two hundred thousand under a sky so big the veterans of the '94 final kept stopping to look at it; and England's run through it passed, even at the time, directly into legend — the group stage swept; Japan beaten in a quarter-final of such violence that the match report was cited in two disciplinary hearings and one marriage proposal; Ireland, the old kings, outlasted in a semi-final that took six hours and finished under desert stars. And on the last Saturday of August, in front of the largest crowd in the history of the game, England met Bulgaria in the final, and the world got the match it had been promised in a mountain cauldron ten months before: ve vill meet again ven it matters more.

It mattered more.

The final of 2002 ran four hours and eleven minutes and is generally held to be the finest match ever played; this book, having a wedding to get to, will keep to its own business, which is one Chaser and one Seeker. The Chaser was twenty-one years old, played the entire second hour with two cracked fingers nobody learned about until Tuesday, scored eleven of England's forty-nine goals, made nine more, and at the height of it, leading a counterattack three hundred feet over the Patagonian desert with sixty thousand Bulgarians howling for her blood, played a left-handed reverse pass of such geometry that the Bulgarian Keeper is on record, years later, still maintaining it should have been illegal — while in a box high in the red rock, a retired Welshwoman in a Tutshill cottage's borrowed hat rose to her feet with the rest of the world, watching her pass, the pass, win a World Cup final, and observed to nobody: "Quarter-beat early. Under anthem conditions. We'll speak Tuesday."

And the Seeker was twenty-six, and the greatest who ever lived, and a hundred and seventy points down with the light going; and every soul in the stadium above the age of twelve knew exactly what was coming, because they had seen it before, in another final, in another country, when he was eighteen: Viktor Krum rose off his mark like the closing of an argument, flew the greatest forty seconds of pursuit the game has ever recorded, and caught the Snitch — caught it knowing, caught it because the match was lost either way and the Snitch was his, the one thing in the game no one had ever taken from him — and ended his last World Cup as he had ended his first, fist in the air, on his own terms, twenty points short. England, 490; Bulgaria, 470. The desert came down.

He flew to her before he flew to his own bench — that was noted everywhere, and meant everything in the language of that sport — and they shook hands at the centre of the burning stadium, the new world and the old, and the lip-readers got it cleanly this time:

"It mattered more," said Viktor Krum. "And you vere still impossible to predict. Both things. I am — " and the great dour face did the thing it did perhaps twice a decade — "I am glad, Veasley, that the game goes to such hands. Look after it."

Harry watched the Cup lifted from the England family box, where he was signed in — by his own request, in his own hand, in defiance of every protocol available to the Head of the Auror Office of Great Britain — as equipment; and the photograph of Ginny Weasley with the World Cup overhead and her broken hand hidden behind it ran on every front page on six continents, and not one of them needed his name by the third paragraph, and most never got there, and he kept the Prophet's edition all his life, not for her picture but for the small print of the caption, which described him, where he could just be made out at the rail, mid-roar, as "an unidentified supporter."

The peace was made at the victory banquet, at two in the morning, over sixty-four squares.

It was Krum who proposed it. He appeared at the England table — the whole long apparatus of her was there; the Federation had given up and issued the Weasleys their own liaison — carrying, under one arm, a flat familiar case, and stopped in front of Ron, and said, without preamble: "You haff never liked me. It is the most sensible position any Englishman has ever taken and I haff alvays respected it. Also I read the transcript of your final against Crake — sixty-eight moves; you declined her draw; this I respected more. So." He set the case on the table and opened it: a chessboard, travel-worn, magnificent; and beside it he placed, from his pocket, a chess clock that Ron recognised, because it had arrived by owl two years before with a wedding card that had made him suspicious for a week. "I send the clock hoping for this game eventually," said Viktor Krum. "I am a patient man. Ask anyone. Ask the Snitch."

They played until four. Hermione sat beside them the whole time and pretended to read, which fooled nobody, least of all the two players, who were both showing off and both knew it and had, by the second hour, begun to enjoy each other with the suddenness of a dam going; and at some point in the third hour, deep in a position the spectators had given up on, Ron offered the draw — courteously, correctly, the position dead level — and Viktor Krum looked up from the board with the ghost of a mountain smile and gave him back his own legend:

"No, thank you. I'd rather find out."

Neither of them ever told the score. It became, in fact, the running joke of a friendship that lasted fifty years and two continents — the result is sealed; ask the clock; the clock is also sealed — and all that was ever entered into the family record was Hermione's testimony: that they shook hands at seven minutes past four in the morning, that both of them were laughing, and that Viktor said, putting the board away, "Tell your children the truth about Quidditch and lie to them about chess; it is the correct order of mysteries"; and that her husband and the first boy who ever took her to a dance walked out of that banquet hall with the desert dawn coming up, arguing happily about an endgame, even.

Gwenog Jones found Ginny at the very end of the night, on the stadium rim, with the Cup sitting between them on the red rock and the sky doing its enormity.

"Right," said the most decorated woman in the history of British and Irish Quidditch, coach of the world champions, in the voice she had used across a desk five years before at a trial. "Accounting. I signed a seventeen-year-old who stopped scoring the moment matches were won, and I wrote one line in a notebook, and tonight the line pays out in full, so you get to hear it from me before you read it in my memoirs: build the next decade on her. The decade's yours now, Weasley. The club's, the country's. I'm sixty-three games from the end of my deal and then it's somebody's job to coach YOUR generation's successors, and I'll tell you a secret —" she looked out at the desert, eighteen years of Saturdays in her face — "it had better be you, eventually. Different direction. Top's not a place, girl. It's a relay."

"Noted," said Ginny Weasley, world champion, twenty-one, with a wedding in a week and her whole sky coming up gold over the rocks. "Filed. Pencilled for about 2020." She picked up the Cup, and grinned the grin. "First, though — I've got a fixture. It's been on the board two years. In ink."

Chapter XXII

The Long Table

The seventh of September, 2002, came up golden over Devon, as the last Saturdays of summer at the Burrow were contractually obliged to do; and the orchard wore the marquee, and the marquee wore the morning, and the whole long apparatus of two families moved through the rituals it had been rehearsing, the participants would later agree, for approximately eleven years.

The morning kept its small legends. Molly Weasley was discovered at six a.m. having already cried twice, "to get ahead of it," a strategy she abandoned by nine as fiscally unsound. The bride flew at dawn — once around the orchard on the old Cleansweep from the broom shed, veil-less, world champion's hands easy on the grain, "to settle the air," a tradition she invented on the spot and her daughter would keep — and was observed from the kitchen window by her father, who said nothing and noted the lap in a small shed notebook, under an entry that read only: All hands home today. All of them. Every one. The groom, at the cottage two hundred miles south-west, was talked through his collar by his best man via two-way mirror, lost the ring for nine minutes — it was in the teapot; nobody ever explained it; Ron maintained to his grave it was "enemy action, mate, it's HEREDITARY" — and arrived at the orchard gate at eleven with his hair doing what it did, which the bride had specified in writing: clause one: do NOT let them flatten it. I'm marrying the hair too.

Teddy Lupin, four and a half, bore the rings, and the rings survived, which is the most that can be said. He came down the aisle between the golden chairs with terrible four-year-old gravity, cushion held out like evidence, hair a respectful formal black for upwards of eleven seconds — at which point he saw Harry waiting at the front, and joy got the better of the metamorphosis, and the black went turquoise, then Weasley-red, then a celebratory cycling rainbow that no amount of grandmotherly signalling from the second row could arrest; and the congregation gave up its composure early, which set the tone, and Teddy delivered the rings at a run for the last ten feet and was caught one-armed by the Head of the Auror Office and held there for the whole of the ceremony, because some security details you don't delegate.

Dudley Dursley sat at the back, in a carefully chosen grey suit, enormous and upright and braced — the coffee tradition was four years old now, first Thursdays, a fixed point both men pretended was casual, but a wizarding wedding was new water entire; and the family had been briefed, which is to say Molly Weasley had him by the arm before the seating finished, installed him beside Andromeda ("she's the calmest of us, dear, and she's seen everything"), loaded a plate into his hands on principle, and returned at intervals through the day to reload it, adopting him by force of catering in the manner of her people. He shook Ginny's hand in the receiving line and managed, "He's — yeah. He's all right, isn't he. Congratulations," which from a Dursley, regarding a Potter, was the Gettysburg Address; and Ginny, who had the whole file, stood on her toes and kissed his cheek and said, "Thanks for the coffees, Big D. They count," and moved on before anything had to be done about his face.

The little tufty-haired wizard — third Weasley wedding; he had begun, he confided, to feel like staff — raised his wand at noon, and the orchard went quiet, and the vows were short, because the two of them had agreed that the long version was already on file, four hundred letters deep.

"Everything I am arrived by owl," said Harry Potter. "Eleven years old, a letter I wasn't allowed to read, and everything since — the school, the war, the family, you — it all came down out of the sky with feathers on, and I've learned to just stand still and let it land. You're the best thing the post ever brought me, Gin. I vow to write back. Every Tuesday. Forever. The real version, always — and I vow to remember, on the bad days, what you taught me at a funeral the summer the war ended, sixteen and braver than the whole of me: that grief queues, and that there is always, always a next Tuesday."

"I've loved you since I was ten and lying about it," said Ginny Weasley, "which my brother will claim is family precedent, and he's right, and we're not giving him the satisfaction of eye contact. Harry. I put my elbow in the butter dish for you. I waited out the noble years, I read forty-one Tuesdays of the real version, I carried your when for two years in a trunk lid — and here's my vow, and it's the one off the back of the watch, and it's the only one I've got, because it's all of them at once: steady. Steady when it's loud, steady when it's dark, steady when the whole sky's coming down out of the weather with feathers on. You watch the door, my love. I'll watch you. Steady as Tuesday. Longer than that. But it's the biggest unit of measurement we've got."

(The officiant gave them a moment, because the congregation needed one; the congregation included, by then, the officiant.)

The wedding breakfast was one long table — of course it was; it ran the length of the marquee and out the open end into the orchard sun, built of every table the Burrow owned and three borrowed from the village and Arthur's two new leaves and, at its far sunlit end, a small oak-and-apple table for six brought up that morning in a Muggle van from a kitchen in Godric's Hollow, because Ginny had insisted their own table be in it, "so it learns the family early"; and third from the end on the groom's side stood the chair. The card, in shop gold, read: RESERVED — F. WEASLEY (UNCLE OF THE BRIDE, FOUNDER OF THE FEAST). LATE, AS USUAL. THE EAR IS LIVE. MIND WHAT YOU SAY ABOUT THE HAIR. And the Extendable Ear lay coiled on the seat, aimed at the toasts, and nobody at that table found it strange, because the table had long since learned the family theology: you don't fill the place. You add a leaf.

The toasts ran golden through the afternoon. Ron's was ninety seconds and perfect — "Eleven years ago a skinny bloke with broken glasses sat down in my train compartment, and I thought, this one needs feeding, and I have never once been wrong about anything since, thank you, raise your glasses" — and Mr. Granger, promoted by acclamation to Toastmaster General of both families, did ten minutes on the engineering tolerances of the Burrow that had Arthur weeping into Molly's shoulder; and then, at the golden end of the afternoon, against all tradition and to nobody's surprise, the bride stood up.

"I'm told the bride doesn't traditionally speak," said Ginny Weasley, world champion, to the long table and the orchard and the September light. "The bride has never once been traditional, and the bride has a delivery to make. Bear with me. This one's been in the system a while."

And she raised one hand, and snapped her fingers, and out of the orchard, out of the blue, on schedule to the second — because she had drilled it with him for a week, because he was a professional, because he had been waiting four years for a commission worthy of his commission — came Tuesday: the owl, the colonel, the witness to the whole of it, sailing the full length of the long table at wing-stretch, over the candles and the cake and two hundred upturned faces, to land in front of Harry Potter with his leg held out; and on the leg, folded small, soft at the creases, three years patient: a letter.

"The ninth of July, 1999," said Ginny, while Harry untied it with hands that had stopped being steady, while the table went silent as snow. "One in the morning, my mum's kitchen table, the night the Harpies signed me. He wrote me the real version of that day — the rule was always the real version — and then he wrote one paragraph more, and lost his nerve, bless him, on the grounds that I was seventeen and the headline would eat my season. So he filed it. In the lining of his trunk lid, where he keeps —" her voice did what it did, and she let it, the family way — "where he keeps the things he isn't finished with. And on the morning of Ron and Hermione's wedding, two years and twelve days ago, I went up to fetch his cufflinks, and I found it, and I read it twice, and I put it back, and I have carried what it said every day since, and not told a living soul except the owl. Read it to them, Potter. It's the only letter Tuesday never carried. He's owed the delivery."

And Harry Potter stood up at his own wedding, at the long table, in the September light, and read aloud, in a voice that went and was let go, the last paragraph of a letter from a twenty-year-old at a kitchen table at one in the morning — I'm going to marry you... it's not a someday anymore, it's a date I haven't learned yet... there's a village in the West Country with a gate that creaks... every Tuesday between now and then is just the route — and the long table came apart entirely: Molly past all strategy; George on his feet bellowing "SINCE RON'S WEDDING? You sat through TWO YEARS of him agonising about FLOOR PLANS—"; Hermione with her face in Ron's shoulder; Andromeda's hand at her mouth; Aberforth, who had shut the Hog's Head for the day on the grounds that "the whole clientele's in Devon anyway," raising a glass alone at the orchard end; and Ginny Weasley took the letter gently back out of her husband's hands, and folded it along its soft old creases, and held it up to the table like the exhibit it was, and finished the toast:

"The date I hadn't learned yet," she said, "was today. It was always today. To my husband — who writes the real version; to the owl — who outranks us both; and to this family, this whole impossible table — which taught me the only thing I know that the war didn't." She raised her glass; the orchard raised with her, two hundred glasses in the long light, and an Extendable Ear on an empty chair caught every word and carried it wherever such things are carried. "The table just gets longer. Look at it. Look what it does. — Dumbledore's Army, the Order of the Phoenix, Weasleys, Grangers, Potters, Lupins, Tonkses, Dursleys, the management both present and absent —"

"ALL PRESENT," roared the long table, the old answer, the whole answer; and Luna Lovegood, four seats down, serene under a hat that had opinions, was heard to amend it, precisely, gladly, into the ringing after: "All present. Plus," counting the table with one finger, unhurried, "rather a lot, now. It does keep happening. I find it's best to just keep setting places."

There was one more piece of news in that golden afternoon, and it arrived the way the family's news always arrived now, which is to say through official channels behaving unofficially. Kingsley Shacklebolt — guest, not Minister, he had insisted, in lilac robes magnificent enough to constitute a security risk — drew Hermione quietly to the orchard's edge during the dancing, and handed her a parchment with a seal she knew, and said, "Hipworth's retirement crossed my desk Thursday. The commission's drawn. It wants only your yes, and Monday's announcement — though I am reliably informed," with the slow Kingsley smile, "that this family has a tradition regarding the timing of announcements at weddings, and I have decided to be traditional." And so it was that the youngest Head of the Department of Magical Law Enforcement in the history of the Ministry of Magic learned of her appointment at twenty-two, barefoot on September grass, with cake in her hand, at the wedding of the boy whose troll she'd once lied about; and she told Ron first, under the apple trees, and Ron Weasley whooped so loudly that the band stopped, and then told the table himself, at volume, standing on a chair — "OI. MY WIFE RUNS MAGICAL LAW ENFORCEMENT NOW. As you were." — and Audrey was heard to rule the announcement procedurally irregular and the motion carried regardless, and Mr. Granger had to be fetched a chair, and somewhere in the roar Hermione Granger, Head of the DMLE, mouthed across the chaos to Harry Potter, Head of the Auror Office, Tuesday lunches stand, and he raised his glass: the two of them, eleven and twelve when a troll cornered them into history, now jointly holding the law of magical Britain, in a marquee, covered in confetti, while a four-year-old with rainbow hair conducted the band.

Dennis Creevey took the photograph at six o'clock, when the light went to honey: the whole table, the whole length of it, orchard end to marquee end — the couple at the centre mid-laugh; Teddy airborne; the Cup (smuggled in by the England liaison "for the table, not the bride") catching the sun; George's hand on the back of a reserved chair; Luna's hat migrating; one grey-suited Muggle laughing unguardedly at last between Andromeda and a plate Molly had just reloaded — and it hangs today where the long-table photographs all hang, in the back room of the Hog's Head, where Aberforth nailed up the first one in the year 2000 muttering that it was a pub, not a gallery, and has added a hook every year since, muttering; and the row runs on down that smoke-browned wall, year after year, reunions and weddings and christenings-after-parties, faces arriving and faces greying and the table in every frame a little longer than the last, so that regulars who know no better ask what the series is, and Aberforth tells them it's the same photograph, taken annually, of the only thing his brother ever got completely right: people keep showing up.

They slipped away at half past six, while the party stood no chance of noticing — that was the tradition they chose to found, leaving the long table loud and lit behind them, the dancing going on into the Devon dark without its cause — and turned on the spot at the orchard gate, their spot, the book admits it freely now; and the dark swallowed Devon and gave them back two lanes from a church, at a low stone wall, at a gate that creaked.

The kitchen was small, and the evening came through the deep-silled window in long gold bars, and nobody was in the way, which would take getting used to. On the chimney breast the new clock ticked gravely, two hands — one with a serif that had cost a whole sheet of brass, one with a crooked R that stayed — both pointing, steady, quivering slightly, at HOME. On the sill, on a perch bolted down before the bed was built, a put-upon professional in feathers stood his post, off the clock since noon, pretending not to watch them come up the path. Under the window, Arthur's table for six waited with its two spare leaves on their runners and one word burnt underneath where you'd find it only if you were small, or looking; and the gnomes were singing something unrepeatable down by the apple tree, and the wild garden stood golden to the wall, and the ruin two lanes away kept its long watch over the cost of all of it, sleeping well.

They made sandwiches. That is the last domestic fact this book has to report, and it reports it with satisfaction: the Potters of Godric's Hollow, world champion and Head Auror, married five hours, still in most of their wedding clothes, stood hip to hip in a kitchen too small on purpose and made cheese sandwiches at half past six in the evening, because they had each eaten nothing at their own wedding and both refused to be the one to say so first; and they ate them at their own small table, in their own small kitchen, in the village where his name began and her list came true, with the clock keeping its two-handed time above them and a whole long loud table of family carrying on without them under the Devon stars, two hundred miles and twenty minutes and no distance at all away.

"Well," said Ginny Weasley Potter, with her mouth full, surveying the boxes, the gold light, the gnome-loud garden, the entire rest of it — and delivered the rite's closing words, inherited from a dentist in a doorway, three years and one chapter ago:

"That's that, then."

And the clock ticked on into the evening, two hands at HOME and room on its face for more; and on the sill the owl tucked his head under his wing, on duty, off the clock, steady; and the table — their table, the family's table, this whole long account's table — sat in the gold light with its leaves waiting on their runners, doing the only thing that table ever did, which is the only thing this book ever had to say:

It got longer.
